### [1] [Multi-level context Modeling for consistent expert selection in Mixture-of-Experts](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16427)
*Shuhan Huang,Naifan Zhang,Yuanbo Tang,Yang Li,Wai Kin Victor Chan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces MCF-MOE, a Mixture-of-Experts framework that improves expert routing by using richer, multi-level token representations, leading to more consistent routing and better performance on language tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing MoE routers base expert selection on shallow or layer-local token representations, causing unstable and semantically inconsistent routing across layers. This limits expert specialization and the overall effectiveness of MoE scaling. The authors aim to understand expert selection from a representation standpoint and remove the bottleneck caused by context-incomplete token features used for routing.

Method: They propose Multi-level Context Fusion MoE (MCF-MOE), which builds context-aware token representations for routing by fusing information from (1) cross-layer semantic aggregation (deeper, high-level semantics across layers) and (2) local token-level interactions (fine-grained, within-layer context). These fused, richer representations are then used by the router to decide which experts each token should be sent to. The framework is applied within Transformer-based MoE architectures and compared against strong baseline routing schemes.

Result: On language modeling and language understanding benchmarks, MCF-MOE achieves better downstream task performance and improved routing consistency metrics compared to strong MoE baselines. The experiments show that experts specialize more reliably when provided with the multi-level context fusion representations, validating the proposed design.

Conclusion: Context incompleteness in routing representations is a key limitation for current MoE models. By explicitly enhancing token representations with multi-level contextual information through MCF-MOE, expert routing becomes more stable, semantically consistent, and effective, resulting in performance gains across benchmarks. The study underscores that achieving contextual completeness in routing features is crucial for unlocking the full potential of MoE architectures.

Abstract: Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) enables efficient scaling of Transformer models by routing tokens to a small subset of experts. However, existing routers typically condition expert selection on shallow or isolated token representations, which often produce unstable and semantically inconsistent routing decisions across layers. In this work, we revisit expert selection from a representation perspective and identify context incompleteness as a key bottleneck limiting effective expert specialization. To address this issue, we propose Multi-level Context Fusion MOE (MCF-MOE), a framework that constructs context-aware representations by integrating complementary signals from cross-layer semantic aggregation and local token-level interactions, enabling more informative and consistent expert selection. Experiments on language modeling and understanding benchmarks demonstrate that MCF-MOE consistently improves routing consistency and downstream performance over strong MoE baselines, highlighting the importance of contextual completeness in expert routing. The code is available at https://anonymous.4open.science/r/MCFMOE.

</details>


### [2] [RIMS: Preference Optimization via Smoothed Multi-pair Aggregation for Small-Scale LLM Retrieval-Augmented Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16431)
*Pei Tian,Zihan Dong,Tianci Liu,Linjun Zhang,Haoyu Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: RIMS introduces a new preference optimization framework that makes small language models more robust and effective for RAG under noisy retrieval, using soft aggregation over multiple preference pairs and self-generated preference data.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Small language models are desirable in constrained environments but are fragile when retrieval returns noisy or spurious evidence. Existing preference-based methods either keep only the single hardest preference pair, wasting supervision, or treat many pairs as independent, losing global structure and limiting data efficiency. There is a need for a preference optimization strategy that better utilizes all available pairwise signals while remaining compatible with small, open models and robust to noisy retrieval.

Method: RIMS has three stages: (1) Generate chain-of-thought preference data by having the target SLM itself produce candidate rationales/answers and applying rejection sampling, avoiding reliance on larger proprietary models. (2) Replace hard argmin/argmax selection over preference pairs with a differentiable soft aggregation operator that smoothly weights all pairs, preserving margin-aware discrimination while keeping gradients from every pair. (3) Apply preference optimization using this smoothed objective on top of several alignment algorithms, with theoretical analysis showing a bounded approximation error and improved gradient alignment to the ideal oracle objective compared to hard selection.

Result: On four multi-hop QA benchmarks with noisy retrieval, RIMS consistently improves Exact Match and F1 over strong state-of-the-art baselines across multiple small model backbones, demonstrating better robustness and answer quality in RAG settings.

Conclusion: Soft, differentiable aggregation of multiple preference pairs, combined with self-generated chain-of-thought preference data, enables more efficient and theoretically grounded preference optimization for small language models in RAG. RIMS yields provably better gradient alignment and empirically superior performance under noisy retrieval, offering a practical, open implementation for robust multi-hop QA.

Abstract: Small-scale language models (SLMs) are attractive for retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) in resource-constrained settings, but their limited capacity makes them highly sensitive to noisy or spurious retrieved evidence. Existing preference-based methods such as RoseRAG select only the hardest single preference pair via hard argmin/argmax, discarding the remaining signal; others treat multiple pairs as independent binary comparisons, resulting in low data utilization. We propose RIMS, a three-stage preference optimization framework comprising (1) synthetic chain-of-thought preference data generation via rejection sampling using the target SLM itself without relying on proprietary models, (2) a differentiable soft aggregation mechanism that replaces hard selection with a smooth operator, preserving gradient signal from all preference pairs while retaining the discriminative structure of margin-aware selection, and (3) preference optimization with the smoothed objective applied to multiple alignment algorithms. We theoretically show that the smoothed approximation admits a controllable error bound and that smooth aggregation yields provably tighter gradient alignment to the oracle objective than hard selection. Experiments on four multi-hop question answering benchmarks show that our approach outperforms state-of-the-art baselines across multiple SLM backbones, achieving consistent gains in Exact Match and F1 under noisy retrieval conditions. Our implementation is available at https://github.com/tptrix29/RIMS.

</details>


### [3] [Committed Before Reasoning: Behavioral Reproduction and Preliminary Activation-Level Evidence of Answer Pre-Commitment in an Open-Weight LLM](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16451)
*Heejin Jo*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: This paper investigates a minimal probe showing that chat models often commit to an answer first and then rationalize it, using a simple car-wash scenario where models wrongly recommend walking instead of driving.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand and expose a core failure mode of chat models: they sometimes produce answers that contradict task premises and then generate reasoning to justify those incorrect answers, raising concerns about faithfulness and reliability of model reasoning.

Method: The authors use a simple, controlled prompt about going to a car wash that logically requires driving. They run multiple rollouts on Qwen3-8B under different system prompts and thinking vs. non-thinking modes. They then probe hidden activations using a pretrained, training-free activation oracle to read out whether the model is internally leaning toward "walk" or "drive" at specific token positions, comparing against neutral baselines and controlling for lexical content.

Result: (1) Behaviorally, the model almost always recommends walking, even though only driving satisfies the premise, and this persists even with a large thinking budget. (2) Activation-level probing shows that internal representations lean toward "walk" before the answer is emitted, even in many cases where the final answer is "drive"; these effects persist after controlling for lexical bias and simple text recovery. (3) Methodologically, they show that question wording alone can substantially change oracle read-outs, implying that negative activation-probing results require per-wording positive controls to be interpretable.

Conclusion: The study provides preliminary but suggestive evidence that in this simple setting, the model tends to commit early to an incorrect answer and then rationalize it, and that activation-probing must be carefully controlled for wording effects. It highlights limitations of current "thinking" modes and underscores the need for better methods to ensure that generated reasoning is faithful to the model's actual decision process.

Abstract: Chat models sometimes commit to an answer and then produce reasoning that justifies it rather than deriving it -- even when the answer contradicts a task premise. We study a minimal probe: "I want to wash my car. The car wash is 100 meters away. Should I walk or drive?" Only drive works (the car must be at the car wash), yet models overwhelmingly recommend walking. (1) Behavioral reproduction: on Qwen3-8B across five system-prompt conditions (210 rollouts), the wrong commitment occurs in 85-100% of sampled rollouts per condition and 100% of greedy rollouts, in both thinking and non-thinking modes; a 4,096-token thinking budget does not repair it. (2) Preliminary activation-level evidence: probing hidden states with a pretrained, training-free activation oracle (no task-specific probe training) at positions before the answer text is emitted, "walk" read-outs exceed a neutral-context baseline (68% vs. 17%; walk-committing rollouts p=.005, drive-committing rollouts p=.005, Fisher exact) -- notably, rollouts that eventually answer drive also read as walk-leaning before commitment (5/6). The oracle's default on unrelated content is "drive" (83%), so the read-outs are not lexical bias; stratifying by literal walk/drive occurrence shows they are not text recovery either (spans containing "drive" still read out walk; in balanced lexical fields, per-rollout walk-majorities beat a per-prompt neutral baseline 15/22 vs. 1/8, p=.01; drive-committing rollouts 6/6, p=.002). Samples are small and the within-rollout positional gradient is not significant (p=.34); we frame these results as preliminary. (3) Methodological: with fixed oracle, activations, and positions, question wording alone moves a positive control from 2/16 (open question) to 11/16 (closed); negative oracle results are uninterpretable without per-wording positive controls.

</details>


### [4] [Encoding EEG Signals to Examine Human-Like Next-Word Prediction Behaviour in Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16549)
*Boi Mai Quach,Binh T. Nguyen,Cathal Gurrin,Graham Healy*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper tests whether better next-word prediction by language models implies better alignment with human brain activity during reading, using EEG-based ERP signals.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although large language models achieve human-like performance on next-word prediction, it is unclear whether this translates into capturing the neural and cognitive processes of human reading. Neuroscience shows word predictability affects brain responses, but we lack fine-grained tests of how different LMs correspond to these signals.

Method: They compare humans and multiple language models using two information-theoretic predictors: top-1 prediction accuracy and word surprisal. These measures are used as regressors to model EEG-derived event-related potentials (ERPs) recorded during reading, focusing on ERP components linked to different stages of linguistic and semantic processing, and analyzing effects across word types (e.g., open-class words).

Result: Surprisal, but not simple top-1 prediction, explains variance in language-related ERP signals, particularly for open-class, semantically rich words. Increased LM scale and computational resources do not monotonically improve alignment between model-derived predictors and human ERPs.

Conclusion: Next-word prediction accuracy alone is not a sufficient indicator of cognitive plausibility. Surprisal-based measures offer a more meaningful link between LMs and human neural processing, and simply scaling models larger does not guarantee closer convergence to human-like linguistic processing as measured by ERPs.

Abstract: Language models (LMs) are trained to excel at predicting the next word in the sequence given prior context, and humans also share this predictability in reading comprehension. Neuroscience research reveals that next-word predictability influences brain response, as recorded at millisecond resolution using electroencephalography (EEG). While our evidence indicates that advanced LMs achieve accuracies closely aligned with human performance at the next-word prediction task, this raises the question: Does higher prediction accuracy necessarily mean that these models adequately capture the cognitive signals associated with human reading comprehension? Here, we generate regressors for both humans and LMs based on two information measures, including top-1 prediction and surprisal, to predict event-related potential (ERP) elicited from EEG recordings which reflect different stages of cognitive processing during reading. We argue that modelling ERP patterns offers fine-grained analysis of the cognitive plausibility of various LMs during reading. Our results indicate that only surprisal potentially correlates with language-processing ERPs, especially for open-class words with high semantic content. Moreover, our findings challenge the assumption that scaling LMs with increased parameters and computational budgets will consistently lead to improved convergence with human-like linguistic processing.

</details>


### [5] [NOWJ@COLIEE 2026: Adaptive Pipelines for Legal Retrieval and Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16603)
*Thuong-Hieu Ngo,Hoang-Trung Nguyen,Huu-Dong Nguyen,Xuan-Bach Le,Le-Dung Nguyen,Quang-Thanh Tran,Ha-Thanh Nguyen,Thi-Hai-Yen Vuong*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper reports the NOWJ team’s multi-task system for COLIEE 2026, detailing specialized pipelines for each legal NLP task and showing how combinations of dense retrieval, reranking, and LLM-based reasoning are applied end-to-end.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To build strong, task-specific yet conceptually unified systems for all COLIEE 2026 tasks, leveraging recent advances in dense retrieval, reranking, and large language models to improve performance on legal case retrieval, entailment, and judgment prediction.

Method: For Task 1, a four-stage pipeline: candidate filtering → dense retrieval with multiple embedding models → cross-encoder reranking with fine-tuned generative models and MLP classifiers → adaptive cutoff prediction. For Task 2, BM25 filtering, T5 reranking, and LLM-based entailment verification with a consensus ensemble. For Task 3, a retrieval-augmented generation setup with dense retrieval, attention-based reranking, and few-shot LLM reasoning. For Task 4, a dynamic routing framework that predicts query difficulty and sends instances either to a few-shot solver or a zero-shot chain-of-thought solver. For the Pilot Task, a combination of hierarchical transformers, CRF layers, argument relation mining, and probabilistic argumentation graph reasoning for judgment prediction.

Result: The abstract does not give numerical scores, but indicates that the team successfully built and applied tailored pipelines for all five COLIEE tasks, integrating retrieval, reranking, and reasoning components into competitive systems.

Conclusion: A modular, multi-stage approach that blends dense retrieval, cross-encoder and attention-based reranking, and LLM-based reasoning can be effectively adapted to diverse legal NLP tasks, from retrieval and entailment to judgment prediction.

Abstract: This paper presents the methodologies and results of the NOWJ team's participation across all five tasks of the COLIEE 2026 competition. For Task 1 (Legal Case Retrieval), we propose a four-stage pipeline comprising candidate filtering, dense retrieval with complementary embedding models, cross-encoder reranking via fine-tuned generative rerankers and MLP-based pairwise classification, and adaptive per-query cutoff prediction. For Task 2 (Legal Case Entailment), we combine BM25 filtering, T5-based reranking, and LLM-based entailment verification with consensus ensemble. For Task 3 (Statute Law Retrieval and Entailment), we adopt a retrieval-augmented generation framework with dense retrieval, attention-based reranking, and few-shot-prompted LLM reasoning. For Task 4 (Legal Textual Entailment), we introduce a dynamic routing pipeline that classifies query difficulty and dispatches cases to either a balanced few-shot solver or a structured zero-shot chain-of-thought solver. For the Pilot Task (Legal Judgment Prediction), we combine hierarchical transformers with CRF layers, argument relation mining, and probabilistic argumentation graph reasoning.

</details>


### [6] [From Memory to Skills: Evidence-Grounded Co-Evolution Governance for Long-Horizon LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16621)
*Bo Tang,Yang Zhang,Guomian Zhuang,Wenqiang Wei,Gaoyang Zheng,Lindong Xie,Yanchao Tan,Feiyu Xiong,Qingyu Yang,Edward Chung,Zhiyu li*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: MSCE is a training-free framework that turns an LLM agent’s past experiences into explicit, callable skills and calibrated memory, enabling better long-horizon performance without extra training.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most long-horizon LLM agents use memory only as passive context: they retrieve old trajectories and paste them into the prompt, which does not turn experience into reusable capabilities and scales poorly. The paper aims to build a framework where experience actively evolves into skills and structured knowledge, improving sample efficiency, transfer, and reliability over time.

Method: They propose MSCE, which organizes agent experience into (1) grounded step traces, (2) reusable procedural policies (skills), and (3) declarative environmental cognition. When the system detects that a learned level-2 (L2) policy has consistent positive gain, it crystallizes it into a callable skill that stores evidence links, applicability conditions, decision guidelines, verification rules, and a reliability estimate. Additionally, they introduce reflection-weighted value backfilling: the agent performs dense self-reflection along a trajectory and uses these local reflections to help propagate sparse terminal rewards back through the trace, yielding calibrated value estimates that guide which memories and skills to keep, refine, or discard.

Result: On benchmarks EvoAgentBench and LoCoMo, MSCE-based agents achieve significantly better performance than state-of-the-art baselines that either only augment skills or rely on memory retrieval, and they show strong cross-domain transfer and the ability to keep improving over long periods of interaction.

Conclusion: Structuring experience as both evolving memory and executable skills, with evidence-calibrated valuation via reflection-weighted backfilling, allows LLM agents to develop more robust, transferable, and continually improving capabilities in long-horizon tasks without additional gradient-based training.

Abstract: Existing memory systems for long-horizon LLM agents often retrieve prior traces as passive context rather than converting them into executable capabilities. In this paper, we propose MSCE, a training-free Memory--Skill Co-Evolution framework that organizes agent experience into grounded step traces, reusable procedural policies, and declarative environmental cognition. MSCE crystallizes evidence-backed L2 policies with positive estimated gain into callable skills that retain evidence links, applicability boundaries, decision guidance, verification rules, and reliability estimates. It further introduces reflection-weighted value backfilling, which propagates sparse terminal feedback through dense local self-reflections to produce evidence-calibrated trace values for governing memory and skill evolution. Experiments on EvoAgentBench and LoCoMo demonstrate that MSCE significantly outperforms state-of-the-art skill-augmented and memory-driven agent baselines, exhibiting strong cross-domain transferability and lifelong-evolution capabilities.

</details>


### [7] [OpenLanguageModel: Readable and Composable Small-Language-Model Pretraining for Education and Research](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16669)
*Tavish Mankash,Vardhaman Kalloli,Keshava Prasad,Deepan Muthirayan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Introduces OpenLanguageModel (OLM), an open-source PyTorch library that makes building, pretraining, and researching small language models transparent and modular.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most language-model codebases are complex, opaque, and hard to adapt for teaching, research ablations, or small-scale pretraining. There is a need for a clear, modular, and educationally oriented framework that keeps the internal mechanics of language models visible while still handling practical concerns like tokenization, datasets, optimization, and hardware execution.

Method: Design a PyTorch library where model architectures are expressed in a readable, compositional way (with modules like Block, Residual, Repeat, Parallel) and directly map to diagrams. Integrate this model layer with tokenizers, various dataset backends (including streaming), training utilities (optimization, mixed precision, callbacks, checkpoints), and hardware-aware runners for CPU, single-GPU, and multi-GPU setups. Provide presets for common architectures, detailed documentation, and an AutoTrainer that configures training for the available machine. Validate by implementing GPT‑2, training on FineWeb‑Edu, running ablations by swapping components, and benchmarking scaling behavior and correctness against reference implementations.

Result: The authors implement 27 presets across nine model families and demonstrate a full workflow: translating GPT‑2 from diagram to code, training on FineWeb‑Edu, and swapping an attention component with minimal code changes. Validation shows close alignment with independent reference implementations, strong weak-scaling efficiency (90.6% on four GPUs for a 348M-parameter model), and encouraging early usability feedback indicating that architecture edits remain compact and understandable.

Conclusion: OLM successfully provides a transparent, modular, and practical framework for building and pretraining small language models. It bridges educational clarity and research flexibility with real-world training features and efficient multi-GPU performance. Released under the MIT license and distributed via PyPI, GitHub, and a dedicated documentation site, it aims to support both teaching and architecture-level LM research.

Abstract: OpenLanguageModel (OLM) is an open-source PyTorch library for building and pretraining small language models while keeping their machinery visible. In OLM, model code reads like the architecture: components are ordinary modules, while Block, Residual, Repeat, and Parallel describe how they are wired. The resulting model can move unchanged from a teaching notebook to a complete pretraining run or a research ablation. OLM connects this readable model layer to tokenizers, local and streaming datasets, optimization, mixed precision, callbacks, checkpoints, and hardware-aware CPU, single-GPU, and single-node multi-GPU execution. We demonstrate the full path by tracing GPT-2 from diagram to code, launching a FineWeb-Edu training script, replacing one attention component, and letting AutoTrainer configure the available machine. The package includes 27 presets across nine familiar model families and documentation that progresses from LM fundamentals to architecture research. Validation shows close agreement with independent reference implementations, 90.6% four-GPU weak-scaling efficiency for a 348M-parameter workload, compact architecture edits, and positive early usability results. OLM is MIT-licensed and available through PyPI, GitHub, and its documentation site.

</details>


### [8] [SpecLA: Efficient Speculative Decoding for Linear-Attention Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16673)
*Zhibin Wang,Xuying Han,Zhaohua Yang,Fuliang Liu,Xue Li,Rong Gu,Sheng Zhong,Chen Tian*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: SpecLA speeds up decoding for stateful linear-attention language models by adapting speculative decoding to their recurrent state structure, achieving up to 1.70x speedup.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Linear-attention models avoid the large and growing KV cache of Transformers by using recurrent states, but autoregressive decoding with these models is still sequential and expensive because it processes one token at a time. Existing speculative decoding methods assume Transformer-style KV caches and do not handle the recurrent dependencies and state-updating constraints of linear attention. There is a need for a speculative decoding framework specifically designed for stateful linear-attention models to gain speedups while respecting their state structure.

Method: The paper introduces SpecLA, a speculative decoding runtime tailored to stateful linear-attention models. SpecLA (1) verifies draft token chains and trees using topology-aware GPU kernels that respect recurrent dependencies; (2) stores compact factors during verification so that it can reconstruct and update only the states along the finally accepted trajectory, without recomputing everything; and (3) integrates a drafter that combines confidence pruning with an EAGLE-style, target-aligned proposal mechanism to preferentially generate draft token sequences that are likely to be accepted, reducing wasted verification work.

Result: On an NVIDIA H100 GPU using a public GDN-1.3B linear-attention model as the target, SpecLA delivers up to a 1.70x end-to-end decoding speedup compared to standard autoregressive decoding, demonstrating that speculative decoding can be effectively adapted to stateful linear-attention architectures.

Conclusion: Speculative decoding can be made efficient for stateful linear-attention models by carefully handling recurrent state dependencies, selective state updating, and drafter-target alignment. SpecLA’s topology-aware verification, compact state recovery factors, and confidence-pruned drafting yield substantial speedups, indicating a viable path to faster inference for linear-attention LMs without the traditional KV cache.

Abstract: Linear-attention models replace the growing KV cache with recurrent states, but autoregressive decoding still reads, updates, and writes these states one token at a time. Speculative decoding can reduce this cost by verifying several draft tokens in one target pass, yet existing speculative systems are designed for Transformer KV caches. For stateful linear-attention targets, verification must follow recurrent dependencies across chains and branches, acceptance must update only the accepted state trajectory, and the drafter must avoid submitting candidates that waste stateful verification work. This paper presents SpecLA, a speculative decoding runtime for stateful linear-attention models. SpecLA verifies chains and trees with topology-aware kernels, stores compact factors produced during verification to recover accepted states, and uses confidence pruning plus a target-aligned EAGLE-style drafter to feed useful candidates to the verifier. On an NVIDIA H100 with a public GDN-1.3B target, SpecLA achieves up to 1.70x end-to-end speedup over autoregressive decoding.

</details>


### [9] [Are Arithmetic Heuristic Neurons Form-Invariant? A Mechanistic Analysis of Symbols, Text, and Code in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16693)
*Sharath Naganna,Tanvir Ahmed Sijan,Uddipta Kalita*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies whether the neurons that implement arithmetic in Llama-3 models are the same across different input formats (symbolic math, word problems, and code), and finds a shared, format-invariant neuron-level circuit whose activation state explains many failures.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although large language models can perform arithmetic, they often behave inconsistently across equivalent problem formulations (e.g., equations vs. word problems vs. code). It is unknown whether such inconsistencies are due to separate internal mechanisms for each format or a single shared mechanism that is sometimes improperly activated. Prior work indicates arithmetic is implemented via sparse “heuristic” MLP neurons, but it is unclear if these heuristics generalize across formats. The paper aims to clarify the internal structure of arithmetic computation and the source of cross-format failures.

Method: The authors analyze three Llama-3 models and focus on addition and subtraction tasks presented in three formats: symbolic expressions, natural language word problems, and Python code. They use a two-stage mechanistic interpretability pipeline: (1) attribution patching to identify which MLP neurons are most causally responsible for arithmetic behavior in each format, and (2) activation patching to test the functional role of these neurons by swapping or ablating activations. They then look for the intersection of important neurons across formats, define this as a shared arithmetic circuit, and perform targeted interventions to test its necessity and sufficiency. Finally, they transfer neuron activations from successful runs in one format to failed runs in another to see if this rescues performance.

Result: They discover a compact set of MLP neurons that are consistently important for arithmetic across symbolic math, word problems, and Python code in Llama-3. Interventions show that this shared set of neurons is necessary and sufficient for arithmetic in late layers. When they transplant the activation patterns of these shared neurons from a correct-format execution to an incorrect-format execution, they fix the majority of previously incorrect predictions, with recovery rates exceeding 97% for addition and subtraction. Furthermore, the neurons fall into similar heuristic families across formats, indicating stable roles rather than format-specific reuse.

Conclusion: Arithmetic in Llama-3 is largely mediated by a common, sparse circuit of heuristic neurons that is reused across different input formats. The main reason models fail on some formulations but not others is not that they use different circuits, but that the same circuit can end up in different activation states. This suggests that improving reliability on arithmetic across formats may require better control of activation states and routing into this shared circuit, rather than training entirely new mechanisms for each format.

Abstract: Large language models often succeed on one formulation of a problem while failing on an equivalent formulation. Whether these failures arise from distinct internal circuits or different activation states of a shared circuit remains unknown. Recent mechanistic interpretability studies suggest that arithmetic in LLMs emerges from a "bag of heuristics," encoded by a sparse set of MLP neurons that represent distinct arithmetic strategies. We investigate whether arithmetic heuristic neurons are form-invariant across symbolic arithmetic, natural language word problems, and Python code in three Llama-3 models. In each format, we identify arithmetic heuristic neurons using a two-stage pipeline combining attribution patching and activation patching. A compact set of neurons is shared across all three formats, and targeted interventions show this shared circuit is both necessary and sufficient for late-layer arithmetic computation. Transferring the shared neurons' activations from a successful execution in one format to a failed execution in another recovers most incorrect predictions, exceeding 97% for addition and subtraction, indicating that cross-format failures arise from activation states rather than distinct circuits. Moreover, shared neurons consistently belong to the same heuristic families across formats, demonstrating that arithmetic computation in LLMs is largely form-invariant at the neuron level.

</details>


### [10] [Though Language Models Err While They Strive: Conformal Prediction for Self-Correcting Scientific Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16704)
*Mingqiao Mo,Yunlong Tan,Hao Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Scientific Feasibility Control (SFC), a framework that enforces scientific validity in LLM-generated technical reasoning using graph-structured conformal prediction and stepwise validation, improving both accuracy and scientific soundness on multiple benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs often generate scientifically incorrect or physically impossible statements during technical reasoning, and these early errors can cascade through later steps, making the entire solution unreliable. Existing methods typically check claims independently, ignoring logical dependencies between steps, and lack formal guarantees on scientific validity. The paper is motivated by the need for a principled way to enforce scientific feasibility with statistical guarantees while preserving or improving task performance.

Method: The authors propose Scientific Feasibility Control (SFC), which decomposes scientific reasoning into atomic units that must satisfy "absolute-coherent-factuality": each unit must be individually consistent with physical laws and coherently supported by prior context. They model the reasoning process as a graph of approximate deducibility relations, capturing logical dependencies between claims. Using a conformal prediction framework, they perform real-time validation of each atomic unit. When a scientific violation is detected, the system dynamically branches to alternative generation paths that are consistent with the verified context. This graph-structured, stepwise validation yields sets of scientifically feasible reasoning trajectories with formal coverage guarantees.

Result: On benchmarks including PhyX multimodal physics, MATH, ScienceQA, and ARC Challenge, SFC achieves higher reasoning accuracy and significantly better scientific validity than baseline models. In particular, it attains 50.1% accuracy on the PhyX physics reasoning benchmark, outperforming DeepSeek-R1 (49.8%) and GPT-4 (45.8%). It also achieves 91.7% scientific validity with conformal coverage guarantees at an alpha = 0.10 confidence level and reduces scientific law violations by 73% across multiple underlying LLM architectures.

Conclusion: SFC provides a principled, graph-structured conformal prediction framework for enforcing scientific feasibility in LLM reasoning. By validating atomic reasoning steps for both factual correctness and logical coherence, and dynamically branching when violations occur, SFC mitigates error cascades and improves both accuracy and scientific soundness. The approach offers formal statistical guarantees on scientific validity and generalizes across tasks and model architectures, suggesting a promising direction for deploying LLMs in safety-critical scientific domains.

Abstract: Large language models frequently violate fundamental scientific principles when generating technical content, undermining their reliability in scientific applications. We introduce Scientific Feasibility Control SFC, a graph-structured conformal prediction framework that provides statistical guarantees for scientific reasoning validity through progressive absolute-coherent-factuality validation. Our approach decomposes scientific reasoning into atomic absolute-coherent-factuality units requiring both individual correctness against physical laws and logical substantiation from preceding context, addressing the cascade effect where early scientific errors contaminate subsequent reasoning steps. Unlike independence-based methods that treat claims in isolation, SFC models logical dependencies as approximate deducibility graphs and operates through real-time validation with dynamic branching when scientific violations are detected, the system branches to alternative generation paths using verified context as foundation. We demonstrate SFC across established scientific reasoning benchmarks including PhyX multimodal physics, MATH, ScienceQA, and ARC Challenge, achieving 50.1 percent accuracy on PhyX physics reasoning, substantially outperforming recent reasoning models including DeepSeek-R1 49.8 percent and GPT-4 45.8 percent while providing 91.7 percent scientific validity with formal conformal coverage guarantees at alpha equals 0.10 confidence level and reducing scientific law violations by 73 percent across multiple model architectures.

</details>


### [11] [JOR-Bench: Japanese Operations Research Benchmarks for Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16777)
*Yuu Jinnai*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: JOR-Bench is a set of Japanese-language benchmarks for testing how well LLMs can formulate and solve operations research problems, enabling cross-lingual evaluation against existing English benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most benchmarks for evaluating LLMs on operations research tasks are in English, limiting the understanding of model capabilities in other languages such as Japanese. There is a need for standardized, solver-independent benchmarks to systematically test whether OR problem formulation and solving abilities transfer across languages and to uncover any language-specific weaknesses or behaviors.

Method: The authors construct JOR-Bench by translating five existing English OR benchmarks—IndustryOR, MAMO Complex LP, NL4OPT, OptiBench, and OptMATH—into Japanese, yielding 1,319 problems covering multiple OR domains (LP, MIP, non-linear programming, combinatorial optimization). They pair each Japanese problem statement with an expected numerical answer, making the benchmark solver-independent. They then evaluate seven LLMs (both multilingual and Japanese-focused) on both English and Japanese versions. For the main comparison, they standardize execution via the Python interface to OR-Tools to ensure comparable and reproducible results with open-source tooling, and they conduct error analyses to investigate cross-lingual behavior differences.

Result: Overall, strong multilingual LLMs show nearly language-neutral performance in OR formulation and solving: the average accuracy difference between English and Japanese is only -0.3 percentage points. However, error analysis reveals nuanced cross-lingual discrepancies. In particular, in some domains with Japanese prompts, models occasionally misinterpret the task pragmatically and output decision-variable values rather than the requested objective value, highlighting subtle language-specific disambiguation failures.

Conclusion: JOR-Bench demonstrates that leading multilingual LLMs can largely transfer their OR problem-solving capabilities from English to Japanese with minimal performance loss. At the same time, the benchmark exposes fine-grained cross-lingual issues, such as pragmatic misinterpretation of what quantity to output. JOR-Bench thus provides a standardized, solver-independent resource for evaluating and comparing LLMs’ operations research abilities in Japanese and for studying cross-lingual robustness in mathematical and optimization reasoning.

Abstract: We present JOR-Bench, a collection of five Japanese-language benchmarks for evaluating the ability of large language models (LLMs) to formulate and solve operations research (OR) problems. Each benchmark is a Japanese translation of an existing English benchmark: IndustryOR, MAMO Complex LP, NL4OPT, OptiBench, and OptMATH, covering 1,319 problems spanning linear programming, mixed-integer programming, non-linear programming, and combinatorial optimization. JOR-Bench is a solver-independent benchmark that can be used with any solver or programming language, and consists of pairs of Japanese problem statements and expected numerical answers. We evaluate seven LLMs, including multilingual general-purpose models and Japanese-specialized models, on both the original English and the new Japanese versions, and compare performance across languages. For the main evaluation, we standardize execution with the Python interface to OR-Tools to make model outputs comparable and reproducible with open-source software. Our results show that OR formulation ability is largely language-neutral for strong multilingual models; the overall average accuracy difference between English and Japanese is only $-0.3$ pp. Yet error analysis reveals subtle cross-lingual differences, including a pragmatic disambiguation failure in some domains that causes models to output decision-variable values instead of the objective value when the prompt is in Japanese.

### [12] [Cascading versus Joint Modeling for Hierarchical Offensive Language Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16790)
*Ruixing Ren,Junhui Zhao,Xiaoke Sun*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper compares cascaded and joint multi-task models for hierarchical offensive language detection, showing cascaded models are more accurate but more expensive in parameters and latency, and that loss design based only on class-imbalance intuition is suboptimal.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To systematically compare two main modeling paradigms—cascaded decomposition and joint multi-task modeling—for fine-grained offensive language detection, and to clarify how best to handle severe class imbalance, both of which were previously understudied and unsupported by controlled experiments.

Method: The authors design a three-level cascaded detection system, customize the training and class-imbalance handling strategy for each subtask, and conduct an ablation study to identify the best loss configurations. They also build a joint multi-task model with a shared encoder as an architectural control to measure accuracy, parameter count, and inference latency. End-to-end cascade evaluations quantify error propagation, particularly from the first-stage filter.

Result: The cascaded system achieves macro-F1 scores of 0.795, 0.716, and 0.557 on the three subtasks. The ablation study shows that intuitive, imbalance-severity-based loss configurations are suboptimal, and reconfigured losses improve both performance and stability. Around 20% of pipeline errors are attributed to the first-stage filter and are unrecoverable by later stages. Compared to the joint multi-task model, the cascade yields higher accuracy in all subtasks, including a 7.1 macro-F1 gain on the most imbalanced one, but uses about 3× parameters and 1.67× inference latency.

Conclusion: Cascaded architectures offer a measurable accuracy advantage over joint multi-task models for hierarchical offensive language detection, especially under severe class imbalance, but at a substantial cost in model size and inference speed. Optimal performance requires empirically tuned class-imbalance handling per subtask rather than relying on heuristic intuition, and first-stage errors critically limit overall cascade performance. The work provides a quantified trade-off that can guide deployment decisions.

Abstract: Fine-grained offensive language detection organizes labels into a hierarchical structure, for which two modeling paradigms exist: cascaded decomposition and joint multi-task modeling. Prior work rarely provides a direct, controlled comparison of the two paradigms in terms of accuracy, parameter count, and inference latency, and rarely verifies whether a chosen class-imbalance handling strategy is actually optimal. This paper proposes a three-level cascaded detection system whose training strategy is customized per subtask, together with two verification mechanisms. First, a controlled ablation study determines the best class-imbalance handling strategy for each subtask. Second, a joint multi-task model with a shared encoder is trained as an architectural control, yielding real measurements along the dimensions of accuracy, parameter count, and inference latency. Experiments show that the cascaded system attains macro-F1 scores of 0.795, 0.716, and 0.557 on the three subtasks of the official test set. The ablation study reveals that configuring the loss function purely by imbalance-severity intuition is suboptimal; reconfiguring based on the ablation results improves both performance and stability. End-to-end cascade evaluation shows that roughly one-fifth of the errors in the cascade pipeline originate from the first-stage filter and cannot be corrected by subsequent stages. Relative to the joint multi-task model, the cascaded architecture achieves higher accuracy on all three subtasks, with a 7.1-point macro-F1 gain on the most severely imbalanced subtask, at the cost of three times the parameters and 1.67 times the inference latency. Together, these results establish an explicit, quantifiable trade-off between the accuracy advantage of cascaded architectures and their deployment cost.

### [13] [Diagnosing Correctness Probes under Self-Judgement Confounding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16799)
*Yi-Long Lu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that hidden-state readouts in language models more reliably track the model's own self-judgment of correctness than objective correctness, so transferability of such readouts does not by itself prove they encode true correctness.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prior work uses linear probes on hidden states to predict whether a model’s answer is correct and often assumes that transferred readout directions reflect an internal notion of objective correctness. However, model self-judgment (whether it ‘believes’ its answer is right) and ground-truth correctness usually align, making it unclear whether these probes capture true correctness or just the model’s confidence/self-endorsement. The authors aim to disentangle these possibilities by constructing situations where self-judgment and objective correctness systematically disagree.

Method: The authors build special “conflict cases” where, by design, the model’s self-judgment (SJ) says one answer is better while the external objective correctness (OC) label says another is better. They then estimate separate linear directions in hidden-state space associated with SJ and OC using factorial designs. They analyze which direction better explains rank ordering of answers in these conflict cases and test how these directions transfer across tasks (math vs factual recall) and benchmarks (MMLU, TruthfulQA), across layers, model sizes (up to 14B parameters), and with various controls (likelihood, sequence length, null directions).

Result: In conflict cases, standard correctness-labeled probes tend to rank incorrect but self-endorsed responses above correct but self-rejected responses, indicating that the learned signal tracks SJ more than OC. Across four instruction-tuned models, the SJ-associated direction consistently transfers above chance across domains (math ↔ factual), while the OC-associated direction has a below-chance point estimate in the conditions where it should reflect OC. This asymmetry arises in mid-to-late layers and is robust to controls. Similar patterns appear on MMLU and binary TruthfulQA even without fitting directions on those targets, suggesting the effect is general.

Conclusion: The dominant, most transferable component of many “correctness” readouts actually reflects model self-judgment rather than objective correctness. Therefore, observing that a hidden-state direction transfers across tasks is insufficient evidence that it encodes objective truth or correctness; it may instead encode internal confidence or self-endorsement. This complicates interpretations of linear probes and related techniques for auditing or steering language models using hidden-state semantics.

Abstract: Hidden-state readouts can predict whether language-model outputs are correct, but objective correctness (OC) usually agrees with the model's own self-judgement (SJ), leaving the decoded signal semantically ambiguous. We construct conflict cases in which OC and SJ predict opposite readout orderings. On high-confidence disagreements, conventional correctness-labelled contrasts often rank incorrect/self-endorsed responses above correct/self-rejected responses, following SJ rather than OC. We estimate factorial SJ- and OC-associated directions and evaluate their polarity across mathematical reasoning and factual recall. Across four instruction-tuned models up to 14B parameters, the SJ-associated direction transfers above chance in both cross-domain directions for every model, whereas the OC-associated direction has a below-chance point estimate for the expected OC ordering in every corresponding condition. This transfer asymmetry develops across middle-to-late layers, persists under answer-likelihood, sequence-length, and null-direction controls, and extends to MMLU and binary TruthfulQA without target-domain direction fitting. Across the studied models and diagnostic subsets, the most reliably transferable component preserves SJ-associated polarity. Transferability alone therefore does not establish objective-correctness semantics.

</details>


### [14] [Schema-Constrained Document-Level Event Argument Extraction with Lightweight LLM Fine-Tuning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16808)
*Roberto Pietrantuono,Antonio Guerriero,Pouya Sattari*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates whether mid-sized open LLMs can reliably perform schema-constrained, document-level event argument extraction on MAVEN-ARG, using role-set prompting, LoRA fine-tuning with a JSON interface, and deterministic decoding plus strict post-processing, achieving state-of-the-art performance over prior GPT baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Document-level event argument extraction is difficult due to long-range trigger-argument dependencies, cross-sentence context, and strict role schemas, which cause boundary errors and role inconsistencies. Existing systems, including GPT-based ones, struggle with these issues, and it is unclear whether smaller, open models can perform this task reliably under schema constraints.

Method: The authors use mid-sized open LLMs and: (i) inject schema role sets directly into prompts to enforce role compliance; (ii) apply parameter-efficient supervised fine-tuning (LoRA), training the models via the same JSON-only interface that is used at inference; and (iii) employ deterministic decoding plus a post-processing pipeline that validates JSON, discards invalid roles, removes duplicate arguments, and aligns predicted spans to the correct location in the document window. They evaluate on the MAVEN-ARG benchmark with the official evaluator.

Result: Fine-tuned mid-sized open LLMs, particularly Phi-4 (14B), surpass previously reported GPT-based baselines on MAVEN-ARG across mention-level, entity-coreference-level, and event-coreference-level evaluations. The best model achieves 42.39% F1 at the event-coreference level, demonstrating competitive performance for document-level, schema-constrained EAE.

Conclusion: Mid-sized open LLMs, when augmented with schema-aware prompting, LoRA-based supervised fine-tuning using a JSON interface, and deterministic, schema-validating post-processing, can perform reliable, document-level schema-constrained event argument extraction. This approach offers a strong, reproducible open alternative to larger proprietary GPT models, as evidenced by state-of-the-art results on MAVEN-ARG and publicly released code.

Abstract: Event Argument Extraction (EAE) converts documents into structured event records by identifying argument spans and assigning them schema-defined roles. Document-level EAE is challenging due to long-range dependencies between triggers and arguments, cross-sentence context, and strict role constraints, which often lead to boundary errors, uncertainty in roles, and inconsistencies with restricted schemas.
  In this paper, we study whether mid-sized open LLMs can perform schema-constrained EAE reliably at the document level on MAVEN-ARG. Our approach combines (i) role-set injection in prompts for schema compliance, (ii) parameter-efficient supervised fine-tuning (LoRA) using the same JSON-only interface used at inference, and (iii) deterministic decoding with post-processing that validates JSON, filters invalid roles, de-duplicates arguments, and aligns spans to the document window. Under the official MAVEN-ARG evaluator, fine-tuned mid-sized open models outperform previously reported GPT baselines across mention, entity-coreference, and event-coreference evaluations; our best model (Phi-4, 14B) reaches 42.39\% F1 at the event-coreference level. Code to reproduce experiments is publicly available at https://github.com/dessertlab/EAE/.

### [15] [Group Entropy-Controlled Policy Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16850)
*Guangran Cheng,Chengqi Lyu,Songyang Gao,Wenwei Zhang,Kai Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Group Entropy-Controlled Policy Optimization (GEPO), a method that improves reinforcement learning alignment of large language models on heterogeneous task mixtures by conditioning advantage shaping on group-wise entropy statistics, leading to better-balanced exploration and performance across tasks than GRPO and other entropy-control methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In RL-based alignment of LLMs, entropy control is used to balance exploration and exploitation, typically with global or token-level regularization. However, training often occurs on mixtures of heterogeneous tasks (e.g., math, coding, general instruction following) whose optimal exploration levels differ. Under a single policy, this induces distinct entropy regimes per task group, making a single global entropy coefficient inadequate. Moreover, GRPO-style normalized advantages become entropy-dependent, so advantage values across different prompt groups are not statistically comparable, which can bias learning towards certain groups and harm cross-task performance. A more nuanced, group-aware entropy control mechanism is needed to maintain appropriate exploration per task while preserving fair and effective credit assignment across groups.

Method: The authors propose Group Entropy-Controlled Policy Optimization (GEPO), a lightweight extension of GRPO. They partition prompts into groups (e.g., by task or domain) and estimate a group-level entropy from existing grouped samples. Using this group entropy, GEPO performs entropy-conditioned asymmetric advantage shaping: in groups with low entropy (indicating over-exploitation), it downscales positive advantages to prevent the policy from collapsing; in groups with high entropy (indicating under-exploitation or excessive randomness), it attenuates negative advantages to avoid prematurely suppressing exploration. The shaping thresholds and scaling factors are adaptively computed from historical entropy statistics for each group, requiring no extra model parameters and minimal modification to GRPO. This results in per-group, entropy-aware modulation of learning signals while reusing standard RLHF/GRPO infrastructure.

Result: On two different base LLMs and thirteen benchmarks covering mathematics, physics, science QA, code generation, and general instruction following, GEPO consistently surpasses GRPO and other recent entropy-control techniques. The method yields balanced improvements across heterogeneous tasks rather than boosting some at the expense of others. Experimental analyses indicate that GEPO maintains more appropriate, task-specific entropy trajectories during training, avoiding both over-exploitation in simpler or highly rewarded tasks and under-exploration in harder domains, which translates into better overall alignment performance.

Conclusion: GEPO demonstrates that entropy control for RL-based LLM alignment benefits from being group-aware rather than globally or token-wise uniform, especially when training on heterogeneous task mixtures. By using group-level entropy statistics to asymmetrically reshape advantages, GEPO corrects the entropy-induced bias of normalized advantages in GRPO and restores comparability of learning signals across prompt groups. This yields more stable and balanced cross-task learning, improves benchmark performance, and preserves suitable exploration levels within each task group, all with a lightweight extension that can be easily integrated into existing GRPO-style pipelines.

Abstract: Entropy control has become an effective tool in reinforcement learning (RL) of large language models (LLMs), helping balance exploration-exploitation trade-off during alignment process. Such RL paradigm is often conducted on mixtures of heterogeneous tasks, which induce distinct entropy regimes under the same policy, making global or token-level entropy regulation insufficient to corresponding heterogeneous needs of exploration. This heterogeneity further makes GRPO-style normalized advantages induce an entropy-dependent bias, making advantage signals across prompt groups statistically non-comparable. To address this issue, we propose Group Entropy-Controlled Policy Optimization (GEPO), a lightweight extension to GRPO that uses group entropy, estimated from existing grouped samples to perform entropy-conditioned asymmetric advantage shaping. GEPO attenuates positive advantages in low-entropy groups to reduce over-exploitation, and negative advantages in high-entropy groups to preserve exploration, with adaptive thresholds derived from historical entropy statistics. Extensive experiments on two base models across thirteen benchmarks spanning mathematics, physics, science, code generation, and instruction following show that GEPO consistently outperforms GRPO and recent entropy-controlled methods, delivering balanced cross-task improvements while preserving task-specific exploration levels throughout training.

</details>


### [16] [Trace-Based On-Policy Distillation for Masked Diffusion Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16872)
*Haolin Ren,Ziyang Huang,Chenhao Yuan,Jun Zhao,Kang Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose TOPD, a teacher-guided, on-policy distillation method that improves reasoning for diffusion LLMs without RL or dense off-policy masking, achieving RL-level performance with far less compute.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Reasoning-oriented post-training for diffusion LLMs is hard: SFT needs dense supervision on states that don't match the model's actual denoising trajectory (off-policy), and RL needs sparse rewards or value models and is compute-intensive. There is a need for a method that provides dense, well-aligned supervision for diffusion models while avoiding reward estimation and reducing compute.

Method: TOPD (trace-based on-policy distillation) supervises a diffusion LLM on its own denoising trajectory. They 1) sample on-policy diffusion trajectories from the target dLLM, 2) for each partially denoised state, query a strong teacher model to obtain token distributions, and 3) train the target with a token-level Reverse KL loss so that its token decisions along that trace match the teacher’s. This provides dense teacher guidance but stays on-policy with respect to the diffusion process.

Result: On math reasoning benchmarks, applying TOPD to SDAR-4B-Chat yields MATH500 accuracy comparable to an RL-trained model (TraDo-4B-Instruct), improving scores by +5.7 (static eval) and +4.5 (dynamic eval) over the original model. It reaches this performance with 4× fewer rollout rounds than RL, corresponding to an estimated 96× reduction in model-compute required to hit that accuracy.

Conclusion: Trace-based on-policy distillation is an effective alternative to RL for reasoning post-training of diffusion LLMs: it preserves dense teacher supervision, aligns with the model’s own denoising dynamics, and achieves RL-level performance on math reasoning with significantly reduced computational cost.

Abstract: Diffusion large language models (dLLMs) are a promising alternative to autoregressive generation. However, reasoning-oriented post-training for dLLMs remains challenging. Supervised fine-tuning (SFT) for dLLMs requires dense but often off-policy masked states, while reinforcement learning (RL) relies on sparse rewards or value modeling. This paper proposes \textbf{trace-based on-policy distillation (TOPD)}, a teacher-supervised framework that transfers reasoning ability to a target dLLM without reward estimation. The key idea is to supervise a dLLM on its own denoising trajectory, focusing on the trace-aligned token decisions that form the final response. Specifically, TOPD samples on-policy diffusion trajectories from the target dLLM, obtains teacher token distributions from a teacher model on the corresponding partially denoised states, and updates the target dLLM with a token-level Reverse Kullback-Leibler (Reverse-KL) objective. This design preserves dense teacher supervision while aligning training with the model's own denoising states. On mathematical reasoning benchmarks, TOPD enables SDAR-4B-Chat to match the MATH500 accuracy of its RL-trained counterpart TraDo-4B-Instruct, with gains of +5.7 under static evaluation and +4.5 under dynamic evaluation. Compared with the RL-trained counterpart, TOPD achieves this with 4$\times$ fewer rollout rounds, corresponding to an estimated 96.0$\times$ to-accuracy model-compute speedup.

</details>


### [17] [Real-World Evaluation of an AI Agent Drafting Translational Impact Summaries](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16989)
*Mohammad Arvan,Amber E. Osterholt,Bailee Rue,Yuvaneswaren Ramakrishnan Sureshbabu,Krishna Riteshkumar Patel,Rebecca T. Feinstein,Bethany C. Bray,Niranjan S. Karnik*

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: CTSA programs must regularly document the real-world impact of their scholars, but manually assembling a complete, cross-platform impact record for each scholar is extremely time-consuming (about 15 hours per scholar) and does not scale to entire cohorts. There is a need for a more efficient, systematic, and comprehensive method to gather and summarize impact evidence, including non-traditional outputs that are often missed by routine processes.

Method: The authors developed a human-in-the-loop AI agent that searches across platforms, compiles sourced evidence on each scholar’s outputs, and drafts one-sentence impact summaries framed by the Translational Science Benefits Model (TSBM). They integrated this tool into the impact-reporting workflow for one CTSA hub and tested it on 10 KL2/K12 scholars. Two independent staff members coded each of the 507 AI-generated findings as accept, edit, or reject. The main outcome was the unanimous usable rate (both raters accepted or edited a finding). They also assessed inter-rater reliability (Cohen’s kappa), compared the agent’s profile discovery recall with human search, characterized the types of impact identified across TSBM domains and non-scholarly categories, and collected user ratings of synthesis accuracy and usefulness.

Result: The AI agent achieved a unanimous usable rate of 81.7%, meaning that in most cases both reviewers either accepted or edited the AI’s findings rather than rejecting them. Reviewers spent a median of 14 minutes per scholar to finalize dossiers, compared with an estimated 15 hours needed for fully manual dossier assembly. Inter-rater agreement on usable versus reject decisions was moderate (Cohen’s kappa = 0.43). In a profile discovery study, the agent’s recall was similar to that of human searching. The evidence gathered by the agent covered all four domains of the TSBM, and roughly one-third of findings were in non-scholarly impact categories that typical reporting processes often overlook. Staff rated the agent’s synthesis accuracy 4.5/5 and its usefulness 4.8/5.

Conclusion: A human-in-the-loop AI agent can effectively act as the first-pass author of research impact records for CTSA scholars. By shifting staff effort from manual data collection and writing to reviewing and refining AI-generated content, the approach makes cohort-level impact reporting feasible while still maintaining quality and capturing a broad range of impact types, including non-traditional outputs.

Abstract: Introduction. Clinical and Translational Science Award (CTSA) programs must document their scholars' research impact, but assembling each scholar's record by hand takes staff an estimated 15 hours and does not scale to a full cohort. An artificial intelligence (AI) agent could serve as a tool to gather scholar data across platforms and disciplines.
  Methods. We built a human-in-the-loop AI agent that assembles a dossier of sourced evidence for each scholar and drafts one-sentence Translational Science Benefits Model (TSBM) impact summaries for staff review. We evaluated it in the impact-reporting workflow of one CTSA hub across 10 career-development (KL2/K12) scholars. Two evaluation staff independently coded all 507 findings as accept, edit, or reject; the primary measure was the unanimous usable rate, defined as the share both accepted or edited.
  Results. Both reviewers accepted or edited 81.7% of the agent's findings. Reviewers each spent a median of 14 minutes per scholar, replacing an estimated 15 hours of manual assembly. Inter-rater agreement was moderate (Cohen's kappa 0.43 on the usable-versus-reject decision). A profile discovery study found the agent's recall close to human search. The agent's impact evidence spanned all four TSBM domains, and about a third of the reviewed findings fell in non-scholarly categories that routine processes tend to miss. Reviewers rated synthesis accuracy 4.5 and usefulness 4.8 on a 5-point scale.
  Conclusions. A human-in-the-loop AI agent can serve as the first-pass author of a scholar's impact record, shifting staff from collecting and writing to reviewing, and making cohort-scale impact reporting feasible.

### [18] [Learning from Synthetic Data without Model Collapse in Iterative Instruction Tuning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17043)
*Xiaonan Luo,Yue Huang,Kehan Guo,Ping He,Chuan Zou,Ting Hua,Xiangliang Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies model collapse in synthetic-data-based instruction tuning of LLMs and proposes KITE, a framework that uses failure-guided data generation and uncertainty-based curation to yield more stable, per-iteration improvements.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs are increasingly trained on synthetic (model-generated) data, their performance can degrade—a phenomenon known as model collapse, characterized by narrowed coverage and accumulated bias. Most prior work focuses on bounding or measuring this degradation in aggregate. However, in realistic iterative model evolution, practitioners care that each new model version actually improves on its predecessor. This requires a finer-grained, actionable understanding of where collapse happens, so that data collection and curation can be targeted to the right regions of the task/knowledge space.

Method: The authors study collapse in the specific setting of self-improving instruction tuning with synthetic data. They first empirically analyze how performance changes across skills, showing that collapse is not uniform but manifests as polarization: strong skills are reinforced while weak skills degrade further. Based on this finding, they introduce KITE (Knowledge-boundary Instruction Tuning via Exploration), a two-stage training framework. In stage one, the system performs failure-guided data generation, explicitly exploring areas where the model currently fails or is uncertain. In stage two, it applies boundary-aware uncertainty curation to select synthetic instructions and responses near the model’s knowledge boundaries, using uncertainty signals to prioritize informative, diverse, and challenging examples for instruction tuning.

Result: Across several benchmarks and multiple open-source LLM backbones, KITE produces more consistent and stable performance gains over successive training iterations compared to strong synthetic-data baselines. Rather than reinforcing only already-strong abilities, KITE better mitigates the polarization effect and reduces the collapse of weaker skills, leading to more balanced and reliable improvement.

Conclusion: Model collapse under synthetic self-training is better understood as a polarization of capabilities rather than a simple uniform degradation. By explicitly targeting the model’s failure regions and knowledge boundaries during synthetic data generation and curation, it is possible to obtain more stable, iteration-by-iteration improvements. The proposed KITE framework operationalizes this idea and empirically outperforms existing synthetic instruction-tuning approaches, suggesting that boundary-aware, uncertainty-driven exploration is a promising direction for safer self-improvement of LLMs.

Abstract: Model collapse is a central challenge in learning from synthetic data: as later-generation large language models (LLMs) are trained on an increasing proportion of model-generated data, performance can degrade due to narrowed coverage and accumulated bias. Existing work mainly studies how to bound this degradation. In iterative model evolution, however, the more meaningful objective is to ensure that each successive model improves over its predecessor, which requires diagnosing collapse at a granularity that is actionable for data curation. We study this problem in synthetic data self-improving for instruction tuning. We show that collapse in this setting is not simply uniform performance degradation, but can appear as a polarization of competence, where synthetic training reinforces already strong skills while further degrading weak ones. Motivated by this observation, we propose KITE (Knowledge-boundary Instruction Tuning via Exploration), a two-stage framework that combines failure-guided data generation with boundary-aware uncertainty curation. Experiments across several datasets and multiple open-source LLMs show that KITE yields more stable improvement than strong synthetic-data baselines.

### [20] [Scope3Trace: Evidence-Based Identification and Extraction of Scope 3 GHG Emissions from Sustainability Reports](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17122)
*Siyuan Zheng,Yifan Duan,Chao Xue,Flora D. Salim*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Scope3Trace, a framework to reliably and transparently extract Scope 3 (and other) greenhouse gas emissions data from heterogeneous ESG and sustainability reports using an evidence-grounded, multimodal, LLM-assisted pipeline.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Scope 3 GHG emissions form the bulk of corporate carbon footprints but are hard to analyze systematically because disclosures are sparse, report formats are highly variable (PDFs, tables, scanned documents), and current automated extraction methods often lack explicit evidence links or require extensive manual annotation and verification. There is a need for a scalable, accurate, and traceable way to extract emissions data from real-world ESG reports to support climate analysis, benchmarking, and decision-making.

Method: The authors design Scope3Trace, an evidence-grounded information extraction framework that operates end-to-end on real-world ESG and sustainability reports. It includes: (1) a document processing pipeline that collects PDFs and applies OCR parsing; (2) LLM-assisted page localization to find relevant sections and LLM-assisted table reconstruction from complex layouts; (3) a hybrid rule-plus-LLM extraction system to pull out organization- and building-level Scope 3 emissions data, combined with explicit evidence-grounded verification that links each extracted value back to its source text or table. On top of this, they construct a dual-level, evidence-grounded, multimodal dataset of organization-level Scope 3 disclosures across heterogeneous reports.

Result: Scope3Trace can reliably extract Scope 1–3 emissions totals and category-level Scope 3 disclosures from diverse sustainability reports, with high accuracy reported for both the numeric values and their associated evidence locations. The framework successfully handles heterogeneous document formats (including scanned PDFs and complex tables) and supports the construction of a high-quality, evidence-linked dataset of emissions disclosures.

Conclusion: Scope3Trace demonstrates that an integrated, evidence-grounded, LLM-assisted pipeline can make large-scale, traceable extraction of Scope 3 GHG emissions from real-world ESG reports feasible and accurate. The framework improves transparency and reliability over prior approaches, and the resulting dataset and methods provide a foundation for more robust corporate emissions analysis and downstream climate applications.

Abstract: Scope 3 greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions account for the majority of corporate carbon footprints, yet remain difficult to analyze at scale due to sparse disclosures, heterogeneous report document formats, and limited evidence traceability. Existing approaches typically rely on large language models to extract emissions information from ESG reports, but often lack explicit evidence grounding or depend on costly manual annotation and verification to ensure extraction reliability. To address these challenges, we propose Scope3Trace, an evidence-grounded information extraction framework designed to extract interpretable and traceable Scope 3 emissions information from real-world ESG and sustainability reports. The framework integrates a document information extraction pipeline that performs PDF collection and OCR parsing, LLM-assisted page localization and table reconstruction, and hybrid rule-LLM extraction of organization- and building-level emissions disclosures with evidence-grounded verification. Building upon this framework, we further contribute a dual-level, evidence-grounded, multimodal dataset comprising organization-level Scope 3 disclosures extracted from heterogeneous sustainability reports. Scope3Trace enables reliable extraction and transparent integration of heterogeneous sustainability disclosures, achieving high accuracy in extracting Scope 1-3 totals and category-level disclosures from sustainability reports.

TL;DR: First large-scale, systematic evaluation of large language models for the low-resource Kyrgyz language using a mixed native and carefully translated benchmark suite.

This issue is particularly pronounced for less-resourced languages such as Kyrgyz, where reliable natively authored evaluation data are scarce. KyrgyzLLM-Bench comprises two natively authored datasets$-$KyrgyzMMLU and KyrgyzRC$-$together with carefully translated and manually post-edited versions of WinoGrande, HellaSwag, BoolQ, and TruthfulQA. Across families and tasks, model rankings transfer broadly from English to Kyrgyz on WinoGrande and BoolQ, and to a lesser extent on MMLU, while HellaSwag exhibits a substantial English-Kyrgyz performance gap consistent with translation-induced plausibility shifts. Few-shot prompting improves several open-source models on reading comprehension but behaves inconsistently for proprietary models on translated tasks.
