### [22] [Auditing Question-Order Effects in Large Language Models with the QQ Equality: Mechanism Characterization and a Saturation Caveat](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17219)
*Pilsung Kang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper adapts a quantum-inspired question-order consistency test (the QQ equality) into an audit tool for checking whether LLM token-level response patterns behave like human survey judgments, and finds that, for a tested model, forced-binary next-token probabilities are too saturated (near-deterministic) to support meaningful QQ-based audits.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Human survey answers show systematic question-order effects that are well captured by a simple projective quantum model, which predicts a specific equality (QQ) among certain joint response probabilities. As LLMs are increasingly used in settings analogous to surveys or sequential questioning, the authors want a principled, pre-registered way to test whether LLMs exhibit similar structured order effects and contextuality, and to understand what kinds of internal mechanisms could or could not pass such tests. They also want to know whether commonly accessible next-token probability interfaces of autoregressive LLMs are informative enough to support these audits in practice.

Method: The authors (1) analyze, in a general probabilistic framework, which classes of sequential decision kernels over binary answers will satisfy the QQ equality and related contextuality constraints, identifying conditions like equality of certain transition mismatch rates and a cross-symmetry condition for a polarity- and position-dependent repetition family; (2) show closure properties such as order-matched mixing preserving QQ satisfaction; (3) translate Contextuality-by-Default’s rank-2 criterion into an inequality in ‘audit coordinates’, namely |q_QQ| ≤ OSS, where OSS is an order-sensitivity score built from marginal order effects; and (4) build a concrete, pre-specified audit pipeline for LLMs that only exposes next-token log-probabilities, including counterbalancing of item order, worst-case robustness envelopes, spot checks for sampling consistency, and a saturation diagnostic to detect near-deterministic outputs. They then run a pilot audit on an open-weight instruction-tuned LLM under two different prompt framings using forced-binary next-token probabilities as proxies for survey responses.

Result: Theoretical results: (a) For marginal-independent kernels, satisfying the QQ equality is equivalent to all four mismatch transition rates being equal, a condition that subsumes the 2D rank-1 projective quantum model with a fixed measurement pair when only the state varies; (b) for a polarity- and position-dependent repetition family, the authors derive an exact cross-symmetry condition characterizing when QQ holds and provide closed-form expressions for violations; (c) they prove that behavioral mixtures that respect question order preserve QQ satisfaction; and (d) they recast the Contextuality-by-Default rank-2 condition as the inequality |q_QQ| ≤ OSS in their audit coordinate system. Empirical results: the pilot on an instruction-tuned open-weight LLM shows that all pre-specified “health gates” for the audit procedure are formally passed, yet the model’s forced-binary next-token distributions are highly saturated: 17 out of 18, and 7 out of 8 item pairs in the two framings are near-deterministic. Under these conditions, no item pair can be certified as residually contextual, and the QQ-based audit essentially becomes uninformative.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that while the QQ equality and related contextuality criteria can be rigorously characterized and turned into a principled, pre-registered audit framework for sequential judgments, in practice, for the tested instruction-tuned LLM and prompt settings, the available forced-binary next-token log-probabilities are too saturated to meaningfully assess distributional QQ behavior. Therefore, the authors recommend always including pre-specified saturation diagnostics whenever next-token distributions are interpreted as analogues of human survey-response distributions; otherwise, QQ-style or contextuality audits may falsely appear to pass or fail simply because the model’s outputs are almost deterministic rather than because of genuine structured order effects.

Abstract: Human survey respondents exhibit question-order effects that satisfy the QQ (quantum question) equality, an a priori, parameter-free prediction of the projective quantum question-order model. We develop the QQ equality into an audit criterion for sequential judgments of autoregressive large language models (LLMs). Theoretically, we characterize which mechanism classes satisfy it robustly: marginal-independent kernels satisfy QQ iff all four mismatch transition rates coincide (a class containing the 2D rank-1 projective model with a fixed measurement pair under state variation); a polarity- and position-dependent repetition family is characterized by an exact cross-symmetry condition with closed-form violations; QQ-satisfying behaviors are closed under order-matched mixing; and the rank-2 Contextuality-by-Default criterion translates into audit coordinates as $|\qQQ|\le\OSS$, where $\OSS$ (the order-sensitivity score) totals the order sensitivity of the two marginals. Methodologically, we develop a pre-specified, audit-logged pipeline applicable to any model exposing next-token log-probabilities; it combines worst-case robustness envelopes, sampling-consistency spot checks, full label counterbalancing, and a saturation diagnostic. Empirically, in a first-signal pilot on an open-weight instruction-tuned model under two framings, all pre-specified health gates passed, yet 17/18 and 7/8 item pairs, respectively, were saturated (near-deterministic), and no item was certified residually contextual. Forced-binary next-token log-probabilities were thus inadequate for distribution-level QQ audits under the tested model and prompting conditions; we recommend pre-specified saturation diagnostics whenever next-token distributions are treated as survey-response distributions.

*Cory Massaro*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper examines statistical patterns in n-grams of LLM-generated text to understand how its style and semantics differ from human writing.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: Analyze the statistical distribution of n-grams in LLM-generated text, then qualitatively inspect frequently occurring n-grams to identify stylistic deficiencies and relate higher-order n-grams to semantic content.

Result: The author finds simple, consistent, and distinctive patterns in the n-gram distributions of LLM-generated text, revealing recognizable stylistic shortcomings compared with human text; higher-order n-grams are shown to carry semantic signals.

Conclusion: LLM-generated text has identifiable n-gram-based statistical signatures linked to stylistic deficiencies, and because higher-order n-grams correlate with meaning, style and semantics in LLM output cannot be cleanly separated.

Abstract: Prior work on LLM-generated text has demonstrated quantitative and qualitative departures from text produced by humans. LLM-generated texts differ from human writing in style, resulting in a characteristic textual "feel," while the semantic range of LLMs is much restricted compared to that of humans. In this contribution, I note simple but consistent patterns in the statistical distribution of n-grams within LLM-generated text. Via qualitative analysis of these n-grams, I reveal deficiencies in LLM style. Because higher-order n-grams correlate to semantic content, I conclude that questions of style and semantics are not cleanly separable.

Abstract: This paper describes TalTech's submissions to the Beyond Transcription Challenge (BeTraC), which requires generating SOAP notes directly from long doctor-patient conversation recordings, without intermediate transcription. ### [25] [AI_LectureNote: A Retrospective Pilot Study of a Post-ASR Workflow for English-Script Rendering and Semantic Drift in Korean-English Medical Lectures](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17237)
*Kyeongeon Lee,Donghoon Chang,Seungryeol Baek,Taehong Kim,Wonjun Yang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents and pilots AI_LectureNote, a post-ASR workflow that converts Korean-English medical lecture transcripts into more readable study notes while restoring Latin-script medical terms, and shows it improves script rendering but can introduce semantic and polarity errors.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In Korean medical lectures that mix Korean and English terminology, automatic speech recognition often outputs Latin-script medical terms as Korean phonetic transliterations, making transcripts hard to read and study from. Existing evaluations tend to focus on word or character error rates, not on how well technical terms are rendered or whether meaning and sentiment (e.g., polarity like positive/negative findings) are preserved when fixing scripts. The authors want a workflow that makes historical lecture transcripts more readable and linguistically faithful for learners, while systematically characterizing the new kinds of errors that this post-processing may introduce.

Method: The authors design AI_LectureNote, a historical post-ASR pipeline that takes speech-to-text outputs from Korean-English medical lectures and rewrites them into study-style transcripts, specifically restoring Latin-script forms of medical terms instead of Korean phonetic spellings. They retrospectively test this on four lectures recorded by the authors, under five different conditions involving two ASR front-ends: a direct whisper-1 path and a 3-minute chunked gpt-4o-transcribe path. They then compare raw vs post-processed outputs on multiple dimensions: English-script rendering rate of medical terms, semantic faithfulness to reference sentences, and correct handling of polarity cues. Errors such as semantic drift and polarity failures are annotated for 282 reference sentences and 101 polarity-cue rows, respectively, by a single annotator. They also compute Jaccard overlaps of failure sets across front-ends to explore differences in error patterns.

Result: Post-processing substantially improved the macro English-script rendering rate of medical terms: from 0.39 to 0.71 for the whisper-1 outputs and from 0.26 to 0.65 for the chunked gpt-4o-transcribe outputs. However, this gain in surface rendering did not guarantee meaning preservation. In the two post-processed conditions, the authors found semantic drift in 34 and 36 out of 282 reference sentences, and polarity failures in 11 and 13 out of 101 polarity-cue rows. Cross-input comparison revealed that polarity-failure cases were more stable across front-ends (Jaccard 0.60; 9 shared out of 15 unioned failures) than general semantic-drift cases (Jaccard 0.23; 13 shared out of 57 unioned drifts), suggesting differing error structures linked to the ASR source and post-processing.

Conclusion: The pilot demonstrates that while AI_LectureNote effectively restores Latin-script medical terms and improves surface readability of Korean-English lecture transcripts, it can introduce nontrivial semantic and polarity errors. Because these error types do not always co-occur, the study argues that evaluations should disentangle multiple dimensions: surface transcript accuracy, term-script rendering quality, consistency of script choice at the chunk level, and preservation of medical meaning (including polarity). The findings are exploratory, limited to a single annotator and a small set of lectures, but they highlight concrete failure modes and offer a framework for more nuanced assessment of post-ASR rewriting systems for bilingual technical lectures.

Abstract: AI_LectureNote is a historical, readability-oriented post-ASR workflow for Korean-English medical lectures. It rewrites speech-to-text output into study transcripts while restoring Latin-script medical terms rather than Korean phonetic transliterations. We retrospectively evaluate the workflow on four author-recorded lectures across five conditions. In this pilot, post-processing raised the macro English-script rendering rate from 0.39 to 0.71 on the whisper-1 path and from 0.26 to 0.65 when applied to 3-minute chunked gpt-4o-transcribe output. However, English-script rendering did not imply semantic faithfulness: the two post-processed conditions showed semantic drift in 34 and 36 of 282 reference sentences and polarity failures in 11 and 13 of 101 polarity-cue rows. A descriptive cross-input comparison suggested different candidate failure patterns: polarity-failure sets overlapped more strongly across front-ends (Jaccard 0.60; 9 shared of 15 unioned failures) than general semantic-drift sets (Jaccard 0.23; 13 shared of 57 unioned drifts). This single-annotator pilot documents concrete failure modes rather than population rates and supports evaluating surface accuracy, term-script rendering, chunk-level script consistency, and medical-meaning preservation separately.

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: EvolvingWorld is a framework and benchmark for simulating how characters and worlds co-evolve over long interactive literary narratives, using an open-schema world model, character agents, and LLM-based evaluation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most existing interactive literary systems either imitate fixed personas or generate isolated scenes, and therefore fail to capture the dynamic, long-term co-evolution of characters and worlds. There is a need for a framework that treats literary simulation as a continuous process where character traits, relationships, and world states persist and change over time, and that can work across diverse fictional settings without rigid, hand-crafted schemas, along with appropriate datasets and evaluation protocols.

Method: The authors define literary simulation as a long-horizon process in which characters repeatedly interact, scenes progress, and both character and world states are persistently updated. They design an open-schema framework composed of two coupled modules: (1) a Character Agent that handles multi-character role-play and updates persistent character profiles, and (2) an LLM-based World Model that maintains global, location-level, and entity-level states and drives scene progression. On top of this architecture, they specify 7 trainable tasks covering scene initialization, interaction generation, and state updates. They then construct a dataset from 57 books, yielding 138,596 supervised training samples and 222 test snapshots, and propose a trajectory-level LLM-as-Judge evaluation protocol with 10 dimensions and 20 metrics to assess long-horizon simulation quality.

Result: Using the constructed dataset and evaluation protocol, experiments demonstrate that EvolvingWorld can better maintain persistent and coherent character arcs and world states across long narrative trajectories compared to prior approaches. The model shows improved performance on the 7 defined tasks and higher scores under the proposed multi-dimensional LLM-as-Judge evaluation, indicating more consistent long-horizon literary simulation.

Conclusion: EvolvingWorld provides an effective open-schema framework and benchmark for character and world co-evolution in interactive literary worlds. By modeling narratives as long-horizon processes with persistent state tracking and by coupling character agents with a global world model, it improves coherence and persistence in literary simulations. The released dataset, tasks, and trajectory-level evaluation protocol establish a foundation for future research on long-term, interactive narrative generation and world modeling.

Abstract: This paper introduces EvolvingWorld, a framework and benchmark for character and world co-evolution in interactive literary worlds. Existing systems either treat interactive literary simulation as static persona imitation or isolated scene generation, failing to capture how characters and worlds evolve together over time. To address this, EvolvingWorld models literary simulation as a long-horizon process where characters interact, scenes progress, and character and world states are persistently updated. Unlike prior systems relying on fixed schemas, EvolvingWorld adopts an open-schema framework to support simulation across diverse literary worlds. The framework consists of two coupled modules: a Character Agent for multi-character role-play and persistent profile evolution, and an LLM-based World Model for global and location/entity-level state maintenance and scene progression. Based on this architecture, we formulate 7 trainable tasks for scene initialization, interaction generation, and state update. We construct a dataset from 57 books, producing 138,596 supervised training samples and 222 snapshots for testing. Furthermore, we introduce a trajectory-level LLM-as-Judge evaluation protocol spanning 10 dimensions and 20 metrics. Experiments show that EvolvingWorld can improve long-horizon simulation by effectively maintaining persistent, coherent character and world development.

### [27] [Should Missing Modalities Always Be Necessary to Repair for Multi-modal Sentiment Analysis?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17262)
*Yubo Gao,Haotian Wu,Xiaoyu Xu,Yibo Yan,Hong Chen,Ruoshui Peng,Fei Pan,Puay Siew Tan,Zhuoran Gao,Yonghua Hei,Jie Zhang,Xuming Hu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper questions the default practice of always repairing missing modalities in multimodal sentiment analysis and proposes SIEVE, a method that learns when repairing is actually necessary for each sample, improving performance and approaching an oracle upper bound.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most multimodal sentiment analysis methods with missing modalities assume that all missing modalities should be reconstructed or repaired before prediction. However, this assumption is rarely challenged: it is unclear whether adding or repairing modalities truly helps every sample. The authors perform an oracle analysis and find that full-modality input is only optimal for a minority of samples, and every subset of modalities is preferred for some samples. This indicates that the usefulness of a modality is sample-dependent and that unnecessary repair can even hurt performance. This motivates a new framework that explicitly decides, for each sample, whether repairing missing modalities is worthwhile.

Method: The authors propose SIEVE (Sufficiency-Informed Evidential ValvE), a dual-branch framework that integrates a decision mechanism on whether to repair missing modalities. SIEVE has: (1) a direct prediction branch that operates on the available modalities without repair, and (2) a repair branch that uses any chosen repair backbone to reconstruct or infer missing modalities and then predicts sentiment. During training, SIEVE compares per-sample losses from the two branches and uses the loss gap as an empirical signal of sufficiency—how adequate the direct branch is without repair. This signal is fed into an evidential gate that jointly models sufficiency and its epistemic uncertainty, learning to route each sample either through the direct branch or the repair branch. SIEVE is repair-agnostic, acting as a plug-and-play decision layer on top of existing explicit or implicit repair modules without altering their architectures.

Result: Experiments on standard multimodal sentiment analysis benchmarks CMU-MOSI and IEMOCAP show that SIEVE consistently improves performance across different rates of missing modalities when combined with various representative repair backbones. It demonstrates that learning when to repair can yield better overall accuracy than always repairing. Furthermore, SIEVE’s performance approaches the per-sample upper bound achievable by an oracle that always selects the better of the direct or repair branches, suggesting that its routing mechanism is highly effective.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that the conventional repair-first assumption in multimodal sentiment analysis under missing modalities is suboptimal: not every missing modality should be repaired, as modality utility is sample-dependent. By introducing SIEVE, which learns a sufficiency- and uncertainty-aware routing decision between direct and repair-based prediction at the sample level, the authors show consistent gains over existing repair methods and performance close to an oracle dual-branch upper bound. This establishes selective, sample-wise repair as a more principled and effective paradigm than unconditional repair, while remaining compatible with a wide range of existing repair modules.

Abstract: Existing methods for multimodal sentiment analysis (MSA) under missing modalities usually follow a repair-first paradigm. We revisit this assumption and ask: \emph{should every missing modality be repaired?} A per-sample oracle analysis shows the answer is not always: full-modality input is optimal for only a small fraction of samples, and every modality subset is preferred by some samples. These results suggest that adding or repairing modalities may not always improve prediction, and that the utility of each modality is sample-dependent. Building on this finding, we propose \textbf{S}ufficiency-\textbf{I}nformed \textbf{E}vidential \textbf{V}al\textbf{vE} (\textbf{SIEVE}) that turns ``whether to repair'' into an explicit, learnable decision at the sample level. SIEVE compares a direct prediction branch with a repair branch, derives an empirical sufficiency signal from their per-sample loss gap, and routes each input through an evidential gate that jointly models sufficiency and its epistemic uncertainty. SIEVE is repair-agnostic: it operates as a plug-and-play decision on top of any explicit or implicit repair module, without modifying its internal design. Experiments on CMU-MOSI and IEMOCAP show that SIEVE consistently improves representative repair backbones across evaluated missing rates, and approaches the per-sample dual-branch achievable optimum.

</details>


### [28] [Debate-on-Graph: Reliable and Adaptive Reasoning of Large Language Model on Uncertain Knowledge Graph](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17266)
*Peiji Yu,Xin Chen,Tianxing Wu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Debate-on-Graph (DoG), a framework that uses uncertain knowledge graphs and multi-agent debate to reduce hallucinations and improve reliability of LLM-based question answering, achieving state-of-the-art results on multiple QA benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large language models perform well on many NLP tasks but often hallucinate or lack up-to-date/specific knowledge in question answering. Knowledge graphs can help, but real-world KGs contain noise and errors. Existing KG-enhanced LLM methods generally treat all retrieved triples as equally reliable, which may propagate or amplify noise and worsen hallucinations. Uncertain knowledge graphs, where each triple has an associated confidence score, offer a way to distinguish more- from less-reliable facts, but prior work has not fully explored how to systematically integrate such uncertainty into LLM reasoning for QA. The paper aims to provide a principled framework that leverages these uncertainty signals to retrieve cleaner, more relevant evidence and guide LLM reasoning more reliably.

Method: The authors propose Debate-on-Graph (DoG), a two-stage framework combining uncertain knowledge graphs (UKGs) with multi-agent LLM debate. First, they design a heuristic search algorithm specialized for UKGs that navigates the graph using both structural signals (e.g., connectivity, paths related to the question entities) and triple-level confidence scores. This search extracts a question-relevant subgraph that emphasizes highly confident, relevant triples while filtering out low-confidence or irrelevant edges, thereby reducing noise in the evidence provided to the LLM. Second, they introduce a Multi-Agent Debate mechanism: multiple LLM agents are prompted with the question and the extracted UKG subgraph, then they generate candidate answers and supporting arguments. Agents engage in adversarial debate, challenging each other’s answers using the graph-based evidence; through several rounds of critique and refinement, the system converges on a final answer that best aligns with both the UKG evidence and internal consistency. The debate is adaptive, allowing the agents to request or focus on different portions of the subgraph as needed to resolve conflicts or uncertainties.

Result: On four benchmark QA datasets, DoG is empirically evaluated against strong LLM reasoning baselines and KG-based methods. Across these benchmarks, DoG consistently improves answer accuracy, achieving state-of-the-art performance. The results show that using UKG-aware retrieval significantly reduces the influence of noisy or incorrect triples compared to standard KG retrieval, and that the multi-agent debate further increases answer reliability beyond single-agent or non-debate settings. Ablation studies indicate that both components—the UKG-specific search and the debate mechanism—contribute meaningfully to performance gains. Qualitative analyses suggest that DoG produces answers that are better supported by explicit graph evidence and exhibits fewer hallucinations or unsupported claims.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that uncertain knowledge graphs, when properly leveraged, are an effective way to mitigate hallucinations and noise amplification in LLM-based question answering. By combining a UKG-tailored search that extracts high-confidence, question-relevant subgraphs with a multi-agent debate mechanism, Debate-on-Graph (DoG) enables more reliable, adaptive reasoning and sets new state-of-the-art results on multiple QA benchmarks. The work highlights the importance of modeling uncertainty in external knowledge sources and of structured, adversarial reasoning processes among LLM agents, and suggests future directions such as improving the search heuristics, scaling to larger graphs and models, and applying the framework to other reasoning-intensive tasks beyond QA.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have demonstrated remarkable capabilities in natural language processing. However, LLMs often suffer from hallucinations and lack of relevant knowledge when dealing with question answering (QA) tasks. To mitigate these issues, knowledge graphs (KGs) have been utilized to enhance LLM reasoning. Nevertheless, KGs often contain noise and errors, while existing KG-enhanced LLM approaches are generally unable to identify and filter such noisy and erroneous content, which can instead amplify hallucinations and pose challenges for reliable reasoning. Uncertain knowledge graphs (UKGs), which associate each triple with a confidence score to quantify uncertainty, offer a promising direction to address this challenge. Compared with prior work, we investigate how to leverage UKGs to support LLMs for QA. We propose Debate-on-Graph (DoG), a new framework that enables LLMs and UKGs to collaborate adaptively for reliable reasoning. Specifically, we first design a heuristic search algorithm tailored for UKGs to extract reliable and question-relevant subgraphs, thereby reducing noise and errors in retrieved knowledge. We then introduce a Multi-Agent Debate mechanism, which yields reliable answers through adaptive adversarial debates, aiming to fully exploit the knowledge in UKGs while preserving the reliability of retrieved evidence. Extensive experiments on four benchmark QA datasets show that DoG achieves state-of-the-art performance over existing LLM reasoning methods and KG-based baselines, while enabling reliable and adaptive reasoning. Our code is available at https://github.com/seucoin/Debate-on-Graph.

</details>


### [29] [Safety That Does Not Transfer: Cross-Lingual Clinical Correctness Drift in Deployable Medical Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17270)
*Anthonio Oladimeji Gabriel,Dimeji Olawuyi,Toba Ajayi,Temilola Aderemi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates whether clinical safety performance of large language models (LLMs), validated in English, transfers to Hausa in low-resource health settings, finding major safety degradation for locally deployable small models but not for a frontier model.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most safety studies of LLMs are in English and on large frontier systems, whereas real-world use in low-resource health settings involves small, locally run models and consultations in local languages like Hausa. It is unknown whether safety established in English persists in these contexts, and whether any failures stem from language, clinical content, or model class. This gap poses a concrete risk to patient safety where health burdens are high and regulation is limited.

Method: The authors constructed matched English-Hausa question pairs for three high-burden conditions in northern Nigeria (malaria, sickle cell disease, tuberculosis). Questions targeted four use cases: factual knowledge recall, emergency triage, a leading question that invites a contraindicated action, and a claim about traditional remedies. Six LLMs were tested: five small locally deployable models (4–9B parameters, including two med-finetuned variants) and one frontier model. A total of 128 model responses were independently scored by two fluent Hausa speakers, blinded to each other, against Nigerian national treatment guidelines for clinical correctness and potential harm. Inter-rater agreement was quantified (Cohen’s kappa) and discrepancies, especially on harm judgments, were analyzed.

Result: For locally deployable models, mean clinical correctness dropped sharply from 1.57 in English to -0.03 in Hausa on a -1 to 2 scale (2 = correct, -1 = actively harmful). In contrast, the frontier model’s mean score declined only slightly, from 2.00 in English to 1.75 in Hausa, and it produced no responses judged harmful in either language. Performance degradation was consistent across malaria, sickle cell disease, and tuberculosis scenarios. Inter-rater agreement on correctness was substantial (kappa = 0.70), whereas initial agreement on harm was low (kappa = 0.22), prompting further qualitative analysis of how human raters perceive and classify harmfulness.

Conclusion: Clinical safety performance does not reliably transfer from English to Hausa for small, locally deployable LLMs, even when clinical knowledge appears adequate in English. Since a frontier model performs safely in both languages, the deficit is not inherent to Hausa or to the clinical topics, but to the smaller model class typically used in low-resource settings. This raises safety concerns about deploying quantised small LLMs for clinical use in local languages and underscores the need for language- and model-specific safety evaluation, more robust multilingual training, and context-appropriate regulation and deployment practices.

Abstract: Safety evaluation of large language models is conducted predominantly in English and predominantly on frontier systems. Neither condition describes how such models are encountered in low-resource health settings, where small quantised systems are run locally and queried in local languages. We ask whether clinical safety established in English transfers to Hausa, and whether any failure is attributable to the language, the clinical task, or the class of model that low-resource deployment admits. Matched English-Hausa question pairs were built for three conditions of high burden in northern Nigeria: malaria, sickle cell disease, and tuberculosis, probing knowledge recall, emergency triage, a leading question inviting a contraindicated action, and a traditional-remedy claim. Six models were evaluated: five locally deployable systems of 4-9 billion parameters, two medically fine-tuned, and one frontier system. All 128 responses were scored against Nigerian national treatment guidelines by two fluent Hausa speakers working independently and blind to one another. Among locally deployable models, mean clinical correctness fell from 1.57 in English to -0.03 in Hausa, on a scale where 2 denotes a correct answer and -1 an actively harmful one. The frontier model moved from 2.00 to 1.75 and produced no response judged harmful in either language. Drift was consistent across all three conditions. Inter-rater agreement was substantial for clinical correctness (kappa = 0.70); agreement on harm was initially poor (kappa = 0.22) and is examined in detail. Because a frontier model answers the same questions competently in Hausa, the deficit is a property neither of the language nor of the clinical material, but of the deployable tier.

</details>


### [30] [Team DACTYL at PAN 2026: Bayesian Data Mixing and Empirical X-risk Minimization for AI-text Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17382)
*Shantanu Thorat*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies why AI-text detectors often fail on out-of-distribution data and shows that carefully curated multi-dataset training plus calibrated large language models can yield strong, robust detection performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: AI-generated text detectors tend to perform well on in-distribution data but degrade notably on out-of-distribution texts, likely due to overfitting and shortcut learning. Naively mixing datasets does not reliably fix this and can even worsen shortcut reliance. There is a need for principled ways to curate training data and build detectors that generalize across diverse distributions, such as those in shared tasks like PAN 2026.

Method: The authors first fine-tune BERT-tiny models equipped with Bayesian classification heads to act as selectors, choosing texts from three different datasets that should form a consolidated, higher-quality training set. Using this curated training data, they then train two large-scale detectors—a DeBERTa-V3-large model and a ModernBERT-large model—via empirical risk minimization (X-risk). Finally, they build an MCGrad model that takes the ModernBERT-large predictions and calibrates them, enhancing reliability and performance. They evaluate all models on the PAN 2026 benchmark using five metrics (AUROC, F1, C@1, Brier score, and F0.5u).

Result: The DeBERTa-V3-large classifier, trained on the curated dataset, achieves a mean score of 0.882 across the five evaluation metrics on the PAN 2026 test set. ModernBERT-large performs substantially better with a mean score of 0.96. The MCGrad calibration model applied to ModernBERT-large attains the highest mean score of 0.974, placing second on the official PAN 2026 leaderboard and indicating strong out-of-distribution detection performance.

Conclusion: Thoughtful curation of training data, using lightweight Bayesian selector models, can significantly boost the out-of-distribution robustness of AI-generated text detectors. Combined with strong base architectures like DeBERTa-V3-large and ModernBERT-large and post-hoc calibration (MCGrad), this approach yields competitive performance on the PAN 2026 benchmark. The public release of the trained ModernBERT-large and DeBERTa-V3-large models on Hugging Face supports reproducibility and facilitates further research on robust AI-text detection.

Abstract: Existing research shows that AI-generated text detection classifiers achieve strong in-distribution (ID) performance but do not maintain the same performance on out-of-distribution (OOD) texts, suggesting overfitting to dataset-specific features. However, combining different training datasets doesn't always improve performance and, in some cases, can even encourage shortcut learning. To address this issue, we fine-tune BERT-tiny models with Bayesian classification heads to select texts across three different datasets to use as a consolidated training set. We trained three different classifiers: fine-tuned DeBERTa-V3-large and ModernBERT-large classifiers via empirical X-risk minimization, and an MCGrad model that calibrates the predictions from the ModernBERT-large classifier. The DeBERTa-V3-large-large classifier achieves a mean score of 0.882 on the PAN 2026 test set across five metrics: AUROC, $F_1$, C@1, Brier score, and $F_{0.5u}$. ModernBERT-large achieves a score of 0.96 while MCGrad achieves the best score of the three with a mean score of 0.974, ranking second on the leaderboard. Our results highlight that careful dataset curation can lead to strong OOD performance. We release our ModernBERT-large and DeBERTa-V3-large models at https://huggingface.co/collections/ShantanuT01/panclef-2026 .

### [31] [The Librarian Who Refused to Code: Model-Dependent Identity Enactment in LLM Code Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17420)
*Shayell Aharon Salomon,Noam Israel,Ido Safruti,Amir Shaked*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper empirically evaluates how different biographical personas in system prompts affect code-generation behavior in large language models under controlled, pre-registered conditions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Biographical personas are commonly used in system prompts to steer model behavior, yet their concrete effects—especially on code generation quality and output characteristics—have not been rigorously measured in a controlled, pre-registered experimental setting. The authors aim to fill this gap and determine whether personas provide reliable quality benefits or instead introduce model-specific behavioral biases.

Method: The authors conducted a controlled experiment with four prompt conditions (no persona, two engineer personas—a minimalist and a thorough engineer—and a research librarian persona). They tested 12 code-generation tasks on two frontier models (Claude Opus and GPT-5.5), with five runs per prompt-cell, resulting in 480 completions. They applied pre-registered mixed-effects analyses to provider-reported token counts and conducted post-hoc analyses on visible-character output length and correctness, also examining special behaviors such as disclaimers and refusals to provide code.

Result: Persona effects differed substantially between the two models. A significant condition-by-model interaction was found for provider-reported token counts, mirrored qualitatively by visible-character length. On Claude Opus, the minimalist engineer persona reduced visible output by about 30% without improving correctness, while the thorough engineer persona increased output length, again without correctness gains. The librarian persona triggered frequent in-character disclaimers and a notable number of no-code responses, which reduced mean correctness from 0.92 to 0.67. GPT-5.5 did not show these disclaimer or refusal behaviors and had several completions truncated due to length limits. Overall, personas did not consistently improve code correctness and sometimes harmed it, depending on the model and persona.

Conclusion: Biographical personas function more as model-dependent behavioral-policy biases than as general-purpose quality improvements for code generation. Their effects vary across models and can change output length and style without reliably improving correctness—and may even reduce correctness in some conditions, as seen with the librarian persona on Claude Opus. The authors emphasize the need for careful, model-specific evaluation of persona use and provide their data and analysis artifacts for further study, while noting that full end-to-end rescoring requires access to an unreleased task harness.

Abstract: Biographical personas are widely used in system prompts, but their effects on code generation are rarely evaluated under controlled, pre-registered conditions. We tested four prompt conditions (no persona, two engineer personas, and a research-librarian persona), 12 code-generation tasks, two frontier models, and five runs per cell (480 completions). Persona effects differed between the two tested models. Under the pre-registered mixed-effects analysis, the condition-by-model interaction was significant for provider-reported output tokens; a post-hoc visible-character measure showed the same qualitative pattern. Six GPT-5.5 completions were length-capped and are reported separately. On Claude Opus, the minimalist engineer persona reduced visible output by 30% (33% in provider tokens) without improving correctness, while the thorough engineer persona increased output without a correctness gain. In an exploratory post-hoc analysis, the librarian persona elicited in-character disclaimers in 55 of 60 Opus responses and 12 genuine no-code responses, lowering mean correctness from 0.92 to 0.67. GPT-5.5 produced neither behavior in its 59 non-truncated responses. These results are consistent with personas acting as Model-Dependent behavioral-policy biases rather than universal quality interventions. We release raw completions, derived scores, analysis artifacts, a pre-registration document, and an execution gate log; end-to-end test-based rescoring requires an unreleased task harness.

### [32] [How Reliable Are Multimodal Signals of Conversational State? Evidence from Remote Dyadic Collaborative Tasks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17452)
*Tahiya Chowdhury*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates multimodal conversational features for measuring cognitive load and power, using a three-dimensional framework (predictive accuracy, cross-task generalizability, test-retest reliability) on the AVCAffe dataset, and finds that only interaction features are truly reliable across contexts, while linguistic and acoustic features have important limitations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In conversational analysis and conversational AI, researchers need behavioral features that reliably reflect conversational states (like cognitive load or power) across different tasks and speakers, not just in narrow lab settings. However, many commonly used features (linguistic, acoustic, interactional) may perform well in one task but fail to generalize or be stable over time. The paper is motivated by this gap: to systematically assess which feature types actually provide robust, context-independent signals for conversational states and to challenge standard evaluation practices that may overestimate feature stability.

Method: The authors design a three-dimensional evaluation framework that examines (1) predictive accuracy (how well features predict labels such as cognitive load or power), (2) cross-task generalizability (performance when models trained on some tasks are tested on other tasks), and (3) test-retest reliability (stability of feature-behavior relationships across repeated measurements). They apply this framework to three feature families—interactional (e.g., floor dominance, turn-taking patterns), acoustic (prosodic and vocal features), and linguistic (lexical / textual features)—extracted from dyadic video-conference conversations in the AVCAffe dataset, involving 53 dyads performing 9 collaborative tasks. They further introduce speaker normalization / control to separate conversational state information from static speaker identity in acoustic features, and compare performance on predicting cognitive load asymmetry and power roles, including task-level aggregation analyses.

Result: The study finds that (a) no single feature family is best on all three evaluation dimensions; (b) linguistic features achieve the highest predictive accuracy for cognitive load within tasks but their performance collapses when evaluated across tasks, indicating reliance on task-specific vocabulary; (c) acoustic features appear reliable at first, but their reliability falls to nearly zero once speaker identity is controlled, showing that standard prosodic features primarily encode who is speaking rather than their conversational state; (d) interaction features are the only ones that remain reliable under speaker normalization and across tasks; and (e) classification of conversational power roles stays near chance level regardless of feature family and evaluation setting, suggesting that aggregated task-level behavior is not sufficient to infer power roles in this dataset.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that: (1) there is an inherent trade-off between predictive accuracy and generalizability for linguistic features in conversational state modeling; (2) conventional acoustic feature evaluations are misleading unless speaker identity is explicitly controlled, since much of their apparent stability stems from static speaker traits; (3) interactional features (such as floor dominance) are the only genuinely robust indicators of conversational states like cognitive load asymmetry across tasks and speakers; and (4) future work on conversational systems should treat speaker normalization and multi-dimensional evaluation (accuracy, cross-task generalizability, and reliability) as necessary requirements for selecting robust multimodal features, rather than relying on single-dimension performance metrics.

Abstract: Measuring conversational states such as cognitive load and conversational power from multimodal behavior requires characteristic features that are not only predictive but also reliable across task contexts. We present a three-dimensional evaluation framework assessing predictive accuracy, cross-task generalizability, and test-retest reliability, applied to interactional, acoustic, and linguistic features extracted from dyadic conversations during collaborative tasks performed over a video-conferencing platform (AVCAffe dataset; 53 dyads, 9 tasks). Our results show that no single feature family dominates all three dimensions. Linguistic features show the highest predictive accuracy for cognitive load but collapse under cross-task evaluation, revealing sensitivity to task-specific vocabulary. Additionally, acoustic reliability, often reported as evidence of feature stability, degrades once speaker identity is controlled, confirming that standard prosodic features measure vocal characteristics rather than conversational state. Interaction features provide the only genuinely reliable signal, unchanged after speaker normalization. Interestingly, classifying power role remained near chance baseline across all conditions, indicating limitations of task-level aggregated behavior for predicting power role in conversation. Our findings reveal three insights: (1) linguistic features predict best but generalize poorly across task contexts; (2) acoustic reliability collapses to near-zero once speaker identity is controlled, challenging standard evaluation practice; and (3) interaction features provide the only genuinely reliable signal, with floor dominance predicting within-dyad cognitive load asymmetry. These results argue for speaker normalization and multi-dimensional evaluation as prerequisites for context-aware, robust multimodal feature selection in conversational systems.

### [33] [Multilingual Sentence Embeddings for Linguistic-Integrated Reliability Audit](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17466)
*Ummugul Bezirhan,Ji Yoon Jung,Matthias von Davier*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper tests whether multilingual sentence embeddings can replace English translations in a reliability auditing method (LiRA) and finds they can, without hurting reliability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current multilingual assessment systems depend heavily on translating student responses into English for scoring and quality control, which is costly, error-prone, and can fail for some responses. The authors want to know if using multilingual sentence embeddings directly in the original languages can preserve or improve the reliability of scoring while avoiding translation failures.

Method: They use Linguistic-Integrated Reliability Auditing (LiRA) on 11 constructed-response items from PIRLS. For each item, they compare reliability estimates obtained via the traditional workflow (translating responses into English) to estimates obtained by applying LiRA to multilingual sentence embeddings of the native-language responses. They run this comparison across three different multilingual embedding models and track how well embedding-based estimates match translation-based estimates and how many responses can be recovered that were previously dropped due to failed translations.

Result: Native-language multilingual sentence embeddings closely replicated the reliability estimates that were originally based on English translations. Moreover, because embeddings do not require successful translation, they allowed inclusion of responses that had been excluded due to translation failure, without degrading the overall reliability metrics.

Conclusion: Multilingual sentence embeddings are a viable replacement for translated-English input in LiRA-based reliability auditing for multilingual assessments. They can maintain reliability comparable to translation-based methods while reducing data loss from translation failures, suggesting a more efficient and potentially fairer approach to quality control in large-scale international assessments.

Abstract: Multilingual assessment systems commonly rely on translation for scoring and quality-control processes. We evaluate whether multilingual sentence embeddings can replace translated English input for Linguistic-Integrated Reliability Auditing (LiRA) across 11 PIRLS constructed-response items and three embedding models. Native-language embeddings reproduced translation-based reliability estimates closely while recovering responses excluded after translation failure, with no meaningful change in reliability.

### [34] [Token-Level Off-Policy Learning for Faithful Generation Under Distribution Shift](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17524)
*Zitong Huang,Gustavo Lucas Carvalho,Deqing Fu,Robin Jia*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They introduce TOPL, a new off-policy post-training method that treats learning as predicting which tokens in a response are correct, leading to better generalization in summarization and machine translation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing off-policy post-training methods struggle when training directly on off-policy generated text, often harming generalization and robustness. The authors want a way to leverage off-policy data while avoiding these pitfalls, and to improve cross-dataset and cross-task generalization in faithful generation tasks like summarization and translation.

Method: They define Token-Level Off-Policy Labeling (TOPL), where post-training is formulated as a token-level correctness prediction problem. The model is trained to distinguish good vs. bad tokens in responses using token-level labels, rather than optimizing entire sequences. They compare TOPL to various sequence-level and token-level baselines, apply it to summarization and translation, run ablations to isolate the importance of token-level signals, and analyze the learned LoRA adapters as linear classification heads/steering vectors.

Result: On document summarization across 11 datasets, TOPL shows strong out-of-distribution generalization, outperforming a range of sequence- and token-level baselines. It also transfers well to machine translation, indicating its benefits generalize beyond summarization. Ablations show that using token-level learning signals is crucial, as sequence-level variants do not achieve similar gains. Analysis of LoRA adapters reveals that they behave like linear classifiers and steering vectors.

Conclusion: TOPL is an effective off-policy post-training paradigm that reframes learning as token-level correctness prediction, leading to better OOD generalization and transfer across faithful generation tasks. Its advantages stem from token-level supervision, and the resulting model updates are interpretable in terms of classification and steering behavior.

Abstract: We propose Token-Level Off-Policy Labeling (TOPL), an off-policy training paradigm that reframes post-training as a token-level correctness prediction task. Our key intuition is that by training the model to distinguish good and bad tokens in a response, we naturally guide the model towards generating good tokens, while avoiding the pitfalls that come with directly training the model to generate off-policy tokens. Experiments on document summarization tasks show that TOPL achieves strong out-of-distribution generalization across 11 datasets against a diverse set of sequence-level and token-level baselines. We further demonstrate that TOPL transfers effectively to machine translation, suggesting that its benefits generalize across different faithful generation tasks. Through ablation studies, we confirm that our token-level learning signal is critical to good performance; sequence-level analogues do not confer similar benefits. Finally, we show that TOPL induces interpretable model updates: the LoRA adapters learned through TOPL function as linear classification heads and steering vectors.

### [35] [Oracle Gap and Signal Fidelity: A Fixed-Pool Diagnostic for Test-Time Collaboration](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17531)
*Jie Hu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper analyzes when training-free test-time collaboration methods actually help LLM reasoning, decomposing their gains into measurable components and showing that benefits are limited by oracle gap and verifier signal fidelity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although test-time collaboration strategies (self-consistency, best-of-N, verifiers, etc.) are widely used to boost LLM reasoning, their improvements are inconsistent and can be negative. Practitioners lack a principled way to predict when these methods will help or hurt before deployment. The authors want to understand and quantify the conditions under which training-free collaboration is beneficial, and to replace vague "multi-agent" intuitions with a precise, measurable framework.

Method: The authors reframe collaboration as a candidate-selection problem over a fixed pool of model-generated candidates. They analytically decompose the net accuracy gain of a selector/verifier into: (1) recoverable mass (oracle gap), i.e., the maximum possible improvement given the pool; (2) verification-signal coverage, i.e., how many items the signal applies to; (3) conditional selection quality, i.e., how often the selector chooses correctly when it has a signal; and (4) harm to already-correct outputs. They then empirically measure these quantities on benchmarks (LiveCodeBench, MATH Level-5 hard subjects, GPQA-Diamond) comparing different selectors: public-test verifiers, generated-test verifiers, LLM-based selectors, self-consistency, and symbolic answer-equivalence selectors.

Result: Across datasets, the best attainable gains are first constrained by the oracle gap and then by the fidelity of the verification signal, measured via candidate-level agreement (e.g., MCC) between verifier verdicts and ground truth labels. On LiveCodeBench, a strong public-test verifier (MCC 0.825) yields a sizable +8.14pp accuracy gain over a single-sample baseline, while a weaker generated-test verifier (MCC 0.248) gives a modest +2.70pp gain and is comparable to LLM selectors, but with much lower harm (near-zero vs. 4.69% harm). On MATH, a symbolic answer-equivalence selector outperforms self-consistency by +4.67pp, whereas LLM-based selectors actually reduce performance. On GPQA-Diamond, the intrinsic oracle gap is very small (recoverable mass 3.03%) and most candidate pools (87.54%) contain answer-identical candidates; using a weaker model shrinks the oracle gap even further, indicating that oracle gap depends jointly on task, model capability, and sampling configuration.

Conclusion: Training-free test-time collaboration is not universally beneficial; its potential is bounded by the oracle gap of the candidate pool and the quality and coverage of the verification signal. The authors’ decomposition provides both conceptual clarity—viewing collaboration as candidate selection—and a practical diagnostic workflow: before deploying collaboration, estimate the oracle gap and empirically measure signal coverage, fidelity, and harm. Only if these metrics indicate sufficient headroom and reliable verification should one invest in more complex collaborative schemes.

Abstract: Test-time collaboration, including self-consistency, best-of-N selection, critic models, and verifier pipelines, is often credited with broadly improving LLM reasoning, yet its gains are uneven and sometimes negative. We ask when training-free collaboration should be expected to help. For a fixed candidate pool, we decompose a selector or verifier's net gain into measurable factors: recoverable mass, verification-signal coverage, conditional selection quality, and harm to already-correct outputs. This reframes collaboration as a candidate-selection problem rather than as an intrinsic property of a multi-agent topology. Across LiveCodeBench, MATH Level-5 hard subjects, and GPQA-Diamond, gains are bounded first by the oracle gap and then by signal fidelity, which we measure directly as candidate-level agreement between verifier verdicts and official labels. On LiveCodeBench, a public-test verifier (MCC 0.825) gains +8.14 percentage points (pp) over a first-sample baseline; a generated-test verifier (MCC 0.248) improves by +2.70pp and is not statistically distinguishable from an LLM selector, but operates at near-zero harm versus the selector's 4.69% harm rate. On MATH, a symbolic answer-equivalence selector beats self-consistency by +4.67pp, while LLM selectors are negative. On GPQA-Diamond, recoverable mass is only 3.03% and 87.54% of candidate pools are answer-identical; a weaker model's pools shrink both further, suggesting that oracle gap is a joint property of task, model, and sampling configuration. Our framework yields a practical pre-deployment diagnostic: estimate the oracle gap, then measure coverage, signal fidelity, and harm before investing in collaboration.

</details>


### [36] [Tokenizing Crosslingual Homographs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17689)
*Rotem Brillant,Yuval Pinter*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how multilingual tokenizers handle cross-lingual homographs/false friends and proposes a simple language-cue-based tokenizer modification that modestly improves machine translation and makes tokenization more language-aware.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multilingual language models use shared subword vocabularies across languages, which saves capacity but can cause problems when identical surface forms have different meanings or usage in different languages. Current tokenizers often treat such cross-lingual homographs in a language-agnostic way, potentially harming downstream tasks like machine translation. The authors aim to understand this limitation and explore whether encoding language information directly at the tokenizer level can mitigate it.

Method: They analyze several tokenization algorithms (BPE, UnigramLM, and the context-sensitive SaGe tokenizer) focusing on how they segment cross-lingual homographs and false friends. They then introduce a simple intervention: inserting lightweight language cues at the tokenizer level by replacing the initial characters of shared-vocabulary words with language-specific characters during vocabulary construction, thereby reducing cross-language identity of tokens. They evaluate this both intrinsically via tokenizer-level statistics and extrinsically via English-to-X machine translation experiments across multiple languages and evaluation sets.

Result: Intrinsic analysis shows that BPE and UnigramLM typically tokenize cross-lingual homographs in a largely language-agnostic way, while SaGe exhibits more language-specific divergence; after applying their language-cue intervention, these differences between tokenizers largely disappear. In downstream English-to-X machine translation, the proposed cues lead to modest quality improvements in several setups, particularly with BPE tokenization, although gains are not uniform across all target languages and test sets.

Conclusion: The study concludes that current shared-vocabulary tokenizers insufficiently distinguish cross-lingual homographs, which can be partially addressed through simple language-aware modifications at the tokenizer level. While the observed gains in machine translation are modest and inconsistent, the results indicate that injecting lightweight language information during tokenization is a viable and promising direction for improving multilingual language models and warrants further exploration and refinement.

Abstract: Multilingual language models rely on shared subword vocabularies to represent multiple languages within a limited number of token units. While such sharing is often useful, it can also create cases in which identical surface forms are treated too uniformly across languages, even when their meanings or usage differ. We investigate this limitation through cross-lingual homographs and false friends, and examine whether introducing language information earlier in the tokenization process can improve their treatment. We propose a simple tokenizer-level intervention based on language cues: language-specific characters replacing initial characters of shared-vocabulary words, reducing common identity during vocabulary construction. In intrinsic analysis, we find through tokenizer-level statistics that BPE and UnigramLM often treat cross-lingual homographs in a largely language agnostic way, whereas the context-sensitive SaGe tokenizer diverges more strongly; our intervention removes this gap. In downstream English-to-X machine translation, our cues yield modest improvements in several settings, especially under BPE, although the effect is not consistent across all languages and evaluation sets. Overall, the findings suggest that adding lightweight language information at the tokenizer level is a promising direction for further exploration.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors design AEGIS, an exploratory framework that combines span-level toxicity detector outputs with frozen language model generators. Abstract: Span-level rationales are often assumed to improve controllability in text detoxification, but it remains unclear when such guidance helps and when it introduces trade-offs. We present Awareness-Enhanced Guidance for Iterative Safeguard (AEGIS) as an exploratory framework for studying span-guided multilingual detoxification across English, Mandarin Chinese, and Korean. AEGIS combines span-level detector outputs with frozen generator backbones, allowing harmful spans, intensity labels, and target attributes to be provided as structured guidance during rewriting. Rather than claiming state-of-the-art detoxification performance, we analyze how span guidance affects the balance between toxicity reduction and meaning preservation across generator families, model scales, and languages. Our results suggest that span-guided detoxification is conditionally useful: explicit rationales change the trade-off between toxicity reduction and meaning preservation, but their effects depend strongly on the generator backbone and the linguistic context. These findings highlight both the promise and the limitations of span-level control signals for multilingual detoxification.

### [38] [C$^2$KV: Compressed and Composable KV Cache Reuse for Efficient LLM Inference](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17715)
*Chuheng Du,Junyi Chen,Hanlin Tang,Kan Liu,Tao Lan,Lin Qu,Chaoyue Niu,Shengzhong Liu,Guihai Chen,Fan Wu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: C^2KV is a framework that compresses and reuses key-value caches for long-context LLM inference to cut both computation and memory/transfer cost without hurting quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long-context LLM applications incur high inference costs not only from repeated prefill computation but also from storing and accessing very large KV caches. Existing KV reuse approaches mainly optimize computation and ignore the KV memory/transfer bottleneck, and naive KV compression combined with non-prefix reuse harms accuracy. The paper aims to provide a way to do compressed, non-prefix KV reuse that is accurate and efficient.

Method: They propose C^2KV, which learns a compressed, position-agnostic manifold for KV caches so that segments can be modularly reused and concatenated. A small sidecar Extractor module with learnable compression tokens and structured attention generates compressed KV representations from the base model’s activations, without changing the frozen base LLM. They design the representation and attention flow to be composable and position-agnostic, and use a joint compression-concatenation co-training objective so that what is learned during compression matches how KVs will be reused at inference time.

Result: On multiple long-context benchmarks and model families, C^2KV substantially cuts KV storage and transfer overhead and enables up to 17× speedup in long-context inference while maintaining generation quality comparable to the non-compressed, non-reuse baseline.

Conclusion: Jointly optimized compressed, position-agnostic KV representations enable accurate non-prefix KV reuse while drastically reducing memory and bandwidth costs. C^2KV shows that carefully designed extraction and co-training can make KV compression and reuse practical for long-context LLM serving without degrading output quality.

Abstract: Long-context inference is central to modern large language model (LLM) applications such as retrieval-augmented generation and multi-document reasoning. To mitigate the growing inference cost, recent work has explored key-value (KV) cache reuse to reduce redundant prefill computation. However, existing reuse methods primarily focus on computation savings and overlook a critical bottleneck in long-context LLM serving: the cost of storing and accessing large KV caches. While KV compression appears to be a natural complement, naively combining compression with non-prefix KV reuse often leads to severe accuracy degradation. In this work, we propose C$^2$KV, a unified framework for non-prefix KV reuse that jointly optimizes KV extraction and inference-time concatenation. C$^2$KV learns a composable and compressed KV cache manifold that is explicitly designed to be position-agnostic. Our approach introduces a lightweight sidecar Extractor with learnable compression tokens and a structured attention flow, enabling modular KV representations that can be flexibly reused and concatenated without modifying the frozen base model. We further employ a compression-concatenation co-training strategy to align extraction-time representations with their downstream reuse behavior. Extensive experiments across multiple long-context benchmarks and model families demonstrate that C$^2$KV significantly reduces KV cache storage and transfer costs, achieving up to 17$\times$ inference speedup under long contexts, while preserving generation quality.

</details>

*Daniel Vodička,Jakub Šmíd,Pavel Král,Christophe Cerisara*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates several large language models for citation function classification, sets new state-of-the-art results on the ACL-ARC dataset, and introduces a new, richer citation function dataset (AC3).

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors systematically compare five models (Mistral 7B, Orca 2-7B, LLaMA 3.1-8B, Falcon 7B, SciBERT) under zero-shot, few-shot, and fine-tuning setups on the ACL-ARC dataset. This study presents one of the first comprehensive evaluations of multiple state-of-the-art (SOTA) large language models (LLMs) for citation function classification, achieving new SOTA results on the ACL-ARC dataset. We systematically compare five models (Mistral 7B, Orca 2-7B, LLaMA 3.1-8B, Falcon 7B, and SciBERT) across zero-shot, few-shot, and fine-tuning approaches. Our fine-tuned Falcon 7B model achieves a 73.3% macro F1 score on ACL-ARC, representing a significant improvement over previous methods. Additionally, we introduce AC3, a novel dataset featuring a seven-category annotation scheme that distinguishes between neutral acknowledgments and explicit evaluative stances (more opinion-oriented citations - criticizing, complimenting, contradicting). The dataset is implemented across four context extraction variants to systematically evaluate the impact of contextual scope on classification performance. To our knowledge, this is one of the first studies dedicated to comprehensive model comparison for citation function classification, addressing a gap identified in recent surveys.

### [40] [When to Use Extra Context: Evidence-Grounded Terminology Adaptation for Simultaneous Speech Translation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17766)
*Zeyu Yang,Satoshi Nakamura*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces EGTA, a lightweight, evidence-grounded terminology adaptation framework that selectively leverages document-level context to improve terminology recovery in simultaneous speech translation of technical talks, yielding better BLEU, XCOMET, and term recall without full-model fine-tuning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In simultaneous speech translation of technical talks, having access to the broader document context should, in principle, improve translation quality. However, naively injecting the entire document context into each streaming segment is too coarse and inefficient. Diagnostic experiments show that most of the benefits from context come from better recovery of paper-specific terminology, not general semantic improvements. This creates a need for a more targeted, terminology-focused way to exploit document context that works under streaming/latency constraints and does not require heavy model retraining.

Method: The authors propose EGTA (Evidence-Grounded Terminology Adaptation), a framework that: (1) constructs a document-specific terminology memory from the talk/paper; (2) conditionally selects a compact set of candidate terms based on the current streaming state; and (3) adapts both the ASR/speech-side and decoder-side decision spaces using only these selected terms. EGTA is designed to be flexible and can be plugged into cascaded, end-to-end, and generation-only simultaneous ST pipelines. Crucially, it operates without full-model fine-tuning, instead guiding the model’s predictions via the dynamically maintained terminology memory and selection mechanism.

Result: On the MCIF-dev evaluation set for ACL technical-talk SimulST, the EGTA-RG variant improves BLEU by +1.05 (En→Zh) and +0.59 (En→De), XCOMET-XL by +0.019 and +0.006, named-entity recall by +79% and +73% relative, and acronym recall by +0.099 and +0.171, respectively. These gains persist across different latency settings, especially for XCOMET-XL, named-entity recall, and acronym recall. External validation on ACL60/60-dev confirms consistent improvements in terminology recall without any extra fine-tuning. Control experiments with shuffled memories and activation audits suggest that the performance gains are specifically due to alignment with correct paper-specific evidence, not just the presence of additional generic context.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that document-level context in simultaneous speech translation should be exploited in a targeted, terminology-centric manner rather than by uniformly adding large contexts. EGTA’s evidence-grounded terminology adaptation provides a practical, plug-and-play way to improve terminology fidelity—especially named entities and acronyms—across various SimulST architectures and latency regimes, without costly full-model fine-tuning. The authors’ analyses indicate that the improvements stem from precise alignment with paper-specific terminology evidence, supporting EGTA as an effective and efficient approach to document-context utilization in technical-talk translation.

Abstract: Extra context is valuable for simultaneous speech translation of technical talks, but injecting the entire document context into every streaming segment is often too coarse. Through diagnostic experiments, we find that context gains mainly come from paper-specific terminology recovery rather than uniform semantic enhancement. We therefore propose EGTA, an Evidence-Grounded Terminology Adaptation framework that builds a document terminology memory, selects compact candidate terms conditioned on the current streaming state, and adapts ASR/speech-side and decoder-side decision spaces using only the selected terms. EGTA can be instantiated in cascaded, end-to-end, and generation-only SimulST settings without full-model fine-tuning. We evaluate EGTA on an ACL technical-talk SimulST evaluation suite consisting of MCIF-dev and ACL60/60-dev. On MCIF-dev, EGTA-RG improves BLEU by +1.05/+0.59, XCOMET-XL by +0.019/+0.006, named-entity recall by +79\%/+73\% relative, and acronym recall by +0.099/+0.171 on En$\rightarrow$Zh and En$\rightarrow$De. Across MCIF-dev latency settings, EGTA consistently improves XCOMET-XL, named-entity recall, and acronym recall. External validation on ACL60/60-dev further shows consistent terminology-recall gains without additional fine-tuning. Shuffled-memory controls and activation audits provide evidence that the improvements are tied to paper-specific evidence alignment rather than generic context prompting.

In this context, Spanish speech understanding under realistic acoustic conditions has received particularly little attention. We introduce ESCUCHA, the first Spanish speech understanding benchmark designed to evaluate LALMs across heterogeneous acoustic conditions and reasoning abilities. ESCUCHA comprises 1,000 human-curated questions paired with audio, totaling 162.9 hours sourced directly ``from the wild'' rather than drawn from existing datasets, with durations ranging from a few seconds to over 80 minutes. The benchmark emphasizes reasoning, spanning 9 perceptual and 10 reasoning categories, and it captures linguistic diversity through multiple Spanish accents and non-normative speech. ESCUCHA further includes multi-audio questions, spoken questions, and audio instructions, and it flags which questions support open-ended evaluation. Benchmarking several state-of-the-art multimodal and speech models reveals substantial performance gaps relative to trained humans.

### [42] [When a Name Is Not a Name: A Benchmark Dataset and Distilled Reasoning for Culturally Entangled Bangla Homographs in Low-Resource LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17828)
*Md. Asaduzzaman Shuvo*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a new Bangla benchmark and task for disambiguating culturally entangled homographs (words that can be both common nouns and personal names) and shows how prompting and distillation can reduce models’ bias toward dominant common-noun meanings.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Modern language models often lack deep cultural grounding, especially for low-resource languages like Bangla. Many Bangla words function both as common nouns with rich cultural meaning and as personal names, and models systematically prefer the common-noun reading, leading to misinterpretations. Existing benchmarks and evaluations underrepresent this kind of culturally grounded ambiguity, so it is unclear how to measure or mitigate such biases.

Method: The authors define the task of Culturally Entangled Homograph (CEH) disambiguation and construct a Bangla benchmark of 1,516 expert-verified sentences containing 3,032 labeled occurrences where the same word appears twice in a sentence with two different readings. Each occurrence is annotated with a culturally grounded category (e.g., name vs. common noun) and an explanation of the reasoning. They then evaluate both open- and closed-source language models, including a Bangla-specific model, under various prompting strategies. They explore contrastive chain-of-thought prompting to encourage explicit reasoning and experiment with distilling cultural explanations into smaller models (1–3B parameters) so they learn the reasoning process rather than just labels.

Result: Across all evaluated models, they find a dominant-meaning bias: systems default to the common-noun interpretation and often ignore the name sense. The Bangla-specific model performs particularly poorly under all prompting schemes, demonstrating that language-specific pretraining alone does not ensure cultural grounding. Contrastive chain-of-thought prompting substantially reduces this bias without additional training. When cultural explanations are distilled into small models, these models learn to reason about the correct reading, reducing dominant-meaning bias from up to 100% to below 5%. After distillation, the previously weak Bangla-specific model becomes the strongest performer on the benchmark.

Conclusion: The study shows that cultural grounding is not automatically achieved by language-specific pretraining and that modern LLMs systematically favor dominant common-noun senses over personal-name readings in culturally entangled homographs. By introducing the Bangla CEH benchmark, the authors provide a targeted way to measure this issue. They demonstrate that contrastive chain-of-thought prompting and explanation distillation can effectively teach models to perform culturally informed word sense disambiguation, drastically mitigating dominant-meaning bias and improving performance, even for small and previously underperforming models.

Abstract: Many Bangla words are at once personal names and culturally loaded common nouns, "Maya" is both a girl's name and a word for affectionate compassion. Choosing the right reading demands cultural knowledge that is scarce in the pretraining data of modern language models. We introduce Culturally Entangled Homograph (CEH) disambiguation and build a Bangla benchmark of 1,516 expert-verified sentences (3,032 labelled occurrences) in which one word appears twice with two distinct readings, each labelled with a culturally grounded category and an explanation of the reasoning behind it. Across open- and closed-source models, we find a systematic dominant-meaning bias: models default to the common-noun sense and overlook the name. A Bangla-specific model fails under every prompting regime we test, showing that language-specific pretraining alone does not confer cultural grounding. We further show that contrastive chain-of-thought prompting can sharply reduce this bias without training, and that distilling cultural explanations teaches small (1-3B) models to reason toward the correct reading rather than memorise labels, cutting dominant-meaning bias from as high as 100% to under 5% and turning the failed Bangla-specific model into our strongest system. Dataset and code are available at https://github.com/ashuvo25/BanglaCEH.
