### [43] [Zero Hallucination, by Construction: Hallucination-Aware Layered Oversight for Trustworthy Enterprise AI](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17883)
*Bogdan Raduta,Horia Velicu,Alexandru Preda,Serban Chiricescu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper argues that zero hallucination should be enforced at the system level, not expected from the base LLM, and proposes HALO, a six-layer assurance architecture to contain and manage hallucinations in enterprise AI agents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Enterprises distrust AI agents primarily due to hallucinations—confident but untrue outputs. Existing fixes like waiting for better models, adding faithfulness judges, or using retrieval pipelines are insufficient because LLMs are inherently capable of unsupported generation. There is a need for a systematic, engineering-focused approach that treats hallucination as a failure mode to be controlled rather than eliminated.

Method: The authors design HALO (Hallucination-Aware Layered Oversight), a multi-layer assurance architecture. HALO combines: (1) grounded generation only over retrieved, approved content; (2) constrained, deterministic execution to limit where errors can occur; (3) multi-signal verification using both an LLM judge and evidence-based checks against source texts; (4) calibrated abstention mechanisms so the system declines to answer when grounding is weak; (5) full traceability of retrievals, tool calls, and generations; and (6) continuous oversight to detect drift, trigger alerts, regenerate agents, and statistically validate improvements. They apply this architecture to a regulated claims-extraction use case.

Result: HALO demonstrates that hallucinations can be treated as a containable and monitorable failure mode in a real, regulated workload (claims extraction). Each layer contributes to detecting, constraining, or mitigating hallucinations, and the multi-signal, evidence-based verification improves confidence in outputs compared to relying solely on model self-reported certainty or a single judge.

Conclusion: Zero hallucination should be understood as a property enforced by the surrounding system, not an innate property of an LLM. By layering grounded generation, constraints, multi-signal verification, abstention, traceability, and continuous oversight, HALO provides a practical assurance framework that enables enterprises to deploy AI agents more safely in high-stakes, regulated environments.

Abstract: Enterprises will not deploy AI agents they cannot trust, and the most-cited reason for distrust is hallucination: confident, fluent output that is simply not true. The common response is to wait for a model that does not hallucinate. We argue that this is the wrong target. Large language models are, by construction, capable of generating unsupported text, and no amount of scale removes the possibility; a faithfulness judge bolted onto a raw model catches some errors but still ships others, and even well-curated retrieval pipelines have been shown to fabricate citations. We reframe the goal: "zero hallucination" is not a property a model possesses but a property a system enforces. We present HALO (Hallucination-Aware Layered Oversight), an assurance architecture which treats hallucination as a containable failure mode rather than an eliminable one. HALO composes six layers of defense: grounded generation over retrieved, approved content; constrained, deterministic execution that bounds where the model can err; multi-signal verification that scores every output for groundedness and hallucination using both an LLM judge and evidence-based checks against the source text; calibrated abstention, so the system declines rather than guesses when grounding is insufficient; total traceability of every retrieval, tool call, and generation; and continuous oversight that detects drift, alerts on threshold breaches, and closes the loop by regenerating and statistically validating improved agents. We detail each layer, give particular attention to evidence-based confidence (which verifies extractions against the source document rather than trusting the model's self-reported certainty), and illustrate the architecture on a regulated claims-extraction workload

### [44] [DeLIVeR: Decomposed Learning for Information-grounded Veracity Recognition via Reinforced Knowledge Graph Exploration](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17935)
*Cong Hoan Nguyen,Thomas Hoang,Hieu Minh Duong,Long Nguyen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: DeLIVeR is a reinforcement-learning-based framework that improves LLM fact-checking by planning decomposed questions to retrieve precise evidence from knowledge graphs, yielding large F1 gains over prior methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Automated fact-checking with LLMs is hampered by "query brittleness": small changes in how evidence is requested can cause retrieval to fail, especially for complex, multi-hop claims. Existing RAG or KG-based methods often rely on static query templates or shallow reasoning, which limits both accuracy and transparency. The paper is motivated by the need for a more robust, strategic, and explainable way to explore knowledge graphs and gather evidence, so that LLMs can reliably judge claim veracity.

Method: The authors introduce DeLIVeR (Decomposed Learning for Information-grounded Veracity Recognition), which reframes evidence retrieval as a strategic exploration problem over knowledge graphs. A Planner LLM takes an input claim and decomposes it into a set of targeted sub-questions. These questions guide traversal of structured KGs to collect high-precision evidence. The Planner is trained using Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO), a reinforcement learning algorithm. The reward function encourages structurally diverse question paths through the KG and maximizes final verdict accuracy (correct fact-check labels). The overall system integrates this planner-driven KG exploration with a verifier to output final truthfulness judgments and explanations.

Result: On three benchmark datasets for fact-checking—LIAR, FEVER, and PolitiFact—DeLIVeR substantially outperforms state-of-the-art baselines, including HippoRAG2. Using the Qwen2.5-7B model within their framework, the authors report peak F1-scores of 83.73 on LIAR, 84.57 on FEVER, and 79.70 on PolitiFact. These scores constitute improvements of about 10–15 percentage points in F1 compared with HippoRAG2, indicating a strong gain in both precision and recall for claim verification tasks.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that treating fact-checking evidence retrieval as a reinforced, question-planning process over knowledge graphs addresses query brittleness and multi-hop reasoning gaps. DeLIVeR’s Planner LLM, optimized with GRPO and diversity/accuracy-focused rewards, enables more effective and explainable exploration of KGs. This results in significantly better performance than prior methods and yields an auditable, transparent reasoning trail for misinformation detection. The authors suggest that reinforced question planning offers a promising direction for robust, verifiable fact-checking with LLMs.

Abstract: Automated fact-checking remains a challenge for Large Language Models (LLMs) due to "query brittleness" in traditional retrieval systems. We propose DeLIVeR (Decomposed Learning for Information-grounded Veracity Recognition), a framework that treats evidence retrieval as a reinforced strategic exploration task. DeLIVeR utilizes a Planner LLM to decompose complex claims into targeted question sets, which are used to traverse structured Knowledge Graphs (KGs) for high-precision evidence. We optimize the Planner's policy using Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO) with a reward system prioritizing structural diversity and verdict accuracy. Our evaluation on LIAR, FEVER, and PolitiFact shows that DeLIVeR significantly outperforms state-of-the-art baselines. Using Qwen2.5-7B, our framework achieved peak F1-scores of 83.73, 84.57, and 79.70 respectively, representing a 10-15% improvement over HippoRAG2. By shifting to a reinforced question-planning strategy, DeLIVeR effectively bridges multi-hop reasoning gaps and provides an auditable, transparent path for verifiable misinformation detection.

### [45] [What Transfers Under Source Shift? Definitions, Examples, and Fine-Tuning for Climate Disclosure Classification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17952)
*Guosheng Li,Fenghui Ren,Bin Liu,Chuan Yu,Kaiying Ji,Lin Yue,Jun Shen,Sasa Qian*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how well common LLM adaptation strategies (definitions, examples, fine-tuning) transfer for climate disclosure classification when the text source changes (e.g., from reports to earnings calls), finding that simpler strategies often transfer more reliably than complex, high-performing in-source ones.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Climate-related text is spread across heterogeneous sources such as annual reports, press releases, and earnings calls. Classifying climate disclosures is important for analysis and regulation, but models are usually evaluated on a single source, so it is unclear whether adaptation techniques that work well in one domain still work under source shift. Understanding which LLM adaptation strategies are robust when the underlying text source changes is crucial for building dependable climate disclosure classifiers.

Method: The authors cast climate disclosure classification as a cross-source adaptation problem. They evaluate three adaptation strategies—using label or task definitions, providing few-shot examples, and parameter-efficient fine-tuning—across eleven open- and closed-source LLMs. They use two corpora that share the same label set but originate from different sources (e.g., different document types). They compare in-source performance versus cross-source performance, examining variants such as similarity-based example retrieval, randomly selected few-shot examples, and LoRA fine-tuning, and analyze how definition granularity affects transfer.

Result: All three types of adaptation (definitions, examples, fine-tuning) improve performance on average when transferring across sources. However, strategies that yield the largest gains when training and testing on the same source—such as similarity-based retrieval for example selection and LoRA-based fine-tuning—lose most of their advantage when the test source changes. Randomly selected few-shot examples, though weaker in-source, tend to preserve their relative benefit more robustly across sources. Definitions show the most consistent transfer benefits, provided that their level of detail aligns well with the target texts.

Conclusion: The effectiveness of LLM adaptation strategies for climate disclosure classification is highly sensitive to shifts in data source. Strong in-domain methods like similarity-based retrieval and LoRA fine-tuning do not necessarily remain superior under cross-source conditions, whereas simpler approaches—especially well-matched task definitions and even randomly chosen few-shot examples—can be more stable. Consequently, under source shift, simpler adaptation strategies are often a safer and more reliable choice than complex, heavily optimized ones.

Abstract: Climate disclosure classification is a fundamental task for analysing corporate climate disclosures, yet such disclosures appear in many different sources -- annual reports, press releases, and earnings calls -- that differ in length, purpose, and writing style. Existing evaluations are mostly conducted within a single source, leaving open whether common LLM adaptation strategies remain effective under source shift. We reframe climate disclosure classification as a cross-source adaptation problem and study three widely used adaptation strategies -- definitions, examples, and fine-tuning -- across eleven open- and closed-source LLMs, using two corpora that share the same label space but come from different sources. We find that all strategies bring positive cross-source gains on average, but the strongest in-source strategies are not the strongest cross-source ones: similarity-based retrieval and LoRA fine-tuning gain most in-source but lose most of that advantage under source shift; randomly selected few-shot examples, a weaker in-source baseline, retain their advantage more reliably; definitions transfer most consistently, though only when their granularity matches the target text. Across these strategies, when the source changes, simpler is often safer.

### [46] [An Early Warning of Emerging Biosecurity Risks in Frontier LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18056)
*Zhida He,Xia Hu,Baichen Le,Chunxiao Li,Jiajia Li,Lijun Li,Chaochao Lu,Jing Shao,Youbang Sun,Hua Tang,Xiang Wang,Xiao Wang,Xiaoyu Wen,Tong Wu,Jia Xu,Peng Yu,Shu Yu,Jie Zhang,Qiaosheng Zhang,Yi Zhang,Xing-Ming Zhao,Tianhang Zheng,Ziyuan Zhou*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Intern-BioBreaker, a specialized model and experimental framework to red-team biological risks of advanced LLMs, showing that current safeguards fail to prevent models from generating operational and sequence-level harmful biological outputs that can be realized in the lab.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As frontier LLMs are integrated into scientific workflows, particularly in biology, their ability to generate increasingly capable and detailed scientific outputs may outpace existing safety measures. There is growing concern that alignment and content filters focused on text may not adequately address the risk that models could be induced to provide actionable guidance for sensitive biological tasks or design harmful biological sequences. The field lacks a systematic, rigorous way to test, quantify, and validate these biological risks across models, including both open and proprietary systems, and to link model outputs to real-world, physical consequences.

Method: The authors develop Intern-BioBreaker, a bio-focused red-teaming model, and embed it in a computational-to-physical evaluation pipeline. Intern-BioBreaker is used to generate targeted jailbreak prompts aimed at eliciting operationally useful guidance for safety-sensitive biological tasks and at inducing models to output potentially harmful biological sequences. They evaluate multiple frontier models (open and proprietary) under this red-teaming regime, measuring task-level attack success rates. For sequence-level outputs, they select representative cases, synthesize the DNA, express the constructs in host systems, and verify the resulting proteins using orthogonal assays, including assessments of receptor-binding affinity and inferred pathogenic potential. This end-to-end pipeline links model prompts and outputs to wet-lab realization and functional characterization.

Result: (i) Intern-BioBreaker substantially outperforms baseline attack methods and exposes widespread jailbreak vulnerabilities across many frontier LLMs, with some models reaching near-saturated or 100% attack success rates on targeted biological tasks. (ii) In detailed sequence-level case studies, GPT-5.5 can be prompted to design modified viral candidate sequences that exhibit pathogenic potential; the corresponding proteins are predicted or shown to have even stronger receptor-binding affinity than reference sequences, implying elevated infection potential. (iii) Wet-lab experiments confirm that at least some model-generated biological designs are experimentally realizable: DNA based on these outputs can be synthesized, expressed in host cells, and yield proteins consistent with the model’s intended designs, demonstrating that the risks extend beyond text and into physically manifested biological agents or components under controlled conditions.

Conclusion: The study demonstrates a significant disconnect between current text-oriented safeguards and the real biological risks posed by powerful scientific LLMs. Existing alignment techniques and filters can be systematically circumvented by a specialized red-teaming model, leading to operationally useful and potentially harmful biological guidance and designs that can be instantiated in the lab. The authors argue for more robust and domain-specific biological red-teaming, stronger nucleic acid synthesis screening, and upgraded safety mechanisms that evolve with model capabilities to mitigate these emerging biosecurity risks.

Abstract: Frontier large language models (LLMs) are increasingly integrated into scientific workflows, yet their growing biological capabilities may outpace current safeguards. To assess the biological risks of frontier models, we develop Intern-BioBreaker, a specialized bio-red-teaming model, together with an integrated computational-to-physical framework that couples model-level stress testing with wet-lab validation. Within this framework, Intern-BioBreaker generates targeted jailbreak prompts to test whether aligned models can be induced to provide operational guidance for safety-sensitive biological tasks or produce sequence-level outputs with potentially harmful properties. Selected sequence outputs are then carried forward for DNA synthesis, host expression, and orthogonal protein verification to assess whether model-generated designs can yield the intended biological products. Our evaluation reveals a concerning gap between text-level safeguards and the risks posed by capable scientific models: (i) Intern-BioBreaker outperforms baseline attack models and reveals widespread bio-risk jailbreak vulnerabilities across both open-weight and proprietary frontier LLMs, with several targets reaching near-saturated or 100% task-level attack success rate (ASR); (ii) in sequence-level case studies, GPT-5.5 can be induced to generate modified viral candidate sequences with pathogenic potential; the corresponding translated proteins may exhibit even stronger receptor-binding affinity and thus enhanced infection potential; and (iii) end-to-end verification shows that selected model-generated biological designs are not merely textual artifacts, but can be physically realized under controlled experimental settings. These findings underscore the need for stronger biological red-teaming, nucleic acid synthesis screening, and safety mechanisms that keep pace with model capabilities.

*Supryadi,Irfan,Julianti,Darren Keanly Martin,Jayvin Fernando,Yuqi Ren,Deyi Xiong*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

For real-world deployment in Indonesia, models should reflect local values, in this case Pancasila, the state philosophy that shapes public life. There was no rigorous, dilemma-focused, Indonesian benchmark derived from realistic situations to test this, so it was unclear whether existing LLMs actually capture Indonesian value priorities.

Method: The authors construct Pancasila-Dilemmas, a dataset of 1,834 questions sourced from Indonesian news and mapped to the five pillars of Pancasila: Religion, Humanity, Unity, Democracy, and Social Justice. Each item is formulated as a dilemma scenario to stress-test value trade-offs. Native speakers proofread the data, and five diverse Indonesian annotators provide reference answers. They then evaluate 50 LLMs (both closed- and open-source) on this dataset and compute two metrics: Probability Match Score (PMS), measuring how closely a model’s preference distribution matches human label distributions, and Max-Vote Agreement Score (MVAS), measuring agreement with the majority human choice.

Result: Across all 50 models, performance is modest: no model exceeds 0.5 on PMS or 0.72 on MVAS. When broken down by Pancasila value category, models perform especially poorly on dilemmas labeled under Religion and Unity, indicating systematic weaknesses in these value domains. The dataset is released publicly for further research and benchmarking.

Conclusion: Existing LLMs, including strong closed-source models, are not well aligned with Indonesian Pancasila-based values, particularly in Religion and Unity-related dilemmas. Pancasila-Dilemmas offers a realistic, country-specific benchmark that reveals this gap and can be used to guide future alignment work and fine-tuning for the Indonesian context.

Abstract: The value alignment of large language models (LLMs) is crucial for ensuring responses align with human intention and value preferences. However, most evaluations of value alignment focus on Western or universal values, while assessments grounded in the value systems of specific countries remain scarce. In this paper, we introduce Pancasila-Dilemmas, an evaluation dataset of 1,834 questions derived from Indonesian news, classified by 5 values of Pancasila: Religion, Humanity, Unity, Democracy, and Social Justice. This dataset reflects daily life in Indonesia, making it suitable for measuring the value alignment of LLMs deployed for Indonesia. To ensure a more rigorous evaluation, we choose scenarios containing dilemmas. The dataset is proofread by native speakers and answered by 5 diverse Indonesian citizens. We evaluate 50 closed- and open-source LLMs on our dataset. Results reveal that all evaluated LLMs achieves less than 0.5 Probability Match Score (PMS) and 0.72 Max-Vote Agreement Score (MVAS). Compared by each values, LLMs mostly struggle in Religion and Unity dilemma cases. This highlights a significant gap in capturing Indonesian values. The dataset is publicly available at https://github.com/tjunlp-lab/Pancasila-Dilemmas.

</details>

*Taylor Arnold,Nicolas Ballier,Artem Saloev*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper compares silent gaps in speech between US sitcoms and AI-generated podcasts, analyzing how they differ by speaker gender and production setting.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand how silence is used in different types of produced spoken media (traditional TV comedies vs. synthetic podcasts) and whether there are systematic differences related to speaker gender and production context.

Method: The authors analyzed 30 US situational comedies and 51 synthetic podcasts created with Google NotebookLM. They automatically detected speakers’ genders using a fundamental-frequency (pitch) threshold estimated in Praat and then compared patterns of silence gaps (e.g., frequency, duration, distribution) across gender and between the two media types.

Result: They found measurable differences in the characteristics of silence gaps between sitcoms and synthetic podcasts and differences associated with the gender of the speaker, though the abstract does not specify the direction or magnitude of these differences.

Conclusion: Silence gaps are structured differently in human-produced TV comedies and AI-generated podcasts and vary by speaker gender. This suggests that both production setting and speaker characteristics shape the temporal organization of spoken media and that synthetic audio may differ systematically from naturalistic media in its use of silence.

Abstract: This study investigates silence gaps in two kinds of audiovisual material. We analysed thirty US situational comedies and fifty-one synthetic podcasts generated with Google NotebookLM. Gaps were compared across speaker gender, assigned from a fundamental-frequency threshold estimated in Praat, and across production settings.

### [49] [VDAR-Router: Adaptive LLMs Routing via Verbalized Query Difficulty Analysis Retrieval](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18098)
*Yu-Chien Tang,Jun-Chen Hung,Wen-Chih Peng,An-Zi Yen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces VDAR-Router, a difficulty-aware, retrieval-based framework for routing queries to large language models to improve the cost-performance trade-off without additional training.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As large language models are increasingly deployed in real systems, it becomes essential to minimize deployment cost while maintaining strong performance. Existing LLM routing methods typically choose models using surface-level features such as query semantics or embedding similarity, which can overlook how difficult a query actually is. This can lead to sending easy queries to expensive models or hard queries to weak models, resulting in suboptimal cost and quality. The authors aim to design a routing approach that explicitly accounts for query difficulty to make more economical and effective model selection decisions.

Method: The authors propose VDAR-Router, a training-free, difficulty-aware routing framework. For each incoming query, the system first asks an LLM to produce an explicit difficulty analysis describing characteristics related to how challenging the query is. This analysis is then used as a key to retrieve similar historical examples that have already been processed by different candidate models, yielding a set of records with known model performances and costs. Using these retrieved examples, VDAR-Router estimates the suitability of each candidate model for the current query. Finally, it applies a reward function that jointly encodes model performance and computational cost to select the model that best satisfies the target cost-performance trade-off.

Result: On three benchmark datasets, VDAR-Router outperforms existing routing baselines across a range of cost-performance trade-offs. It more effectively assigns queries to cheaper or stronger models depending on inferred difficulty, achieving lower cost for the same performance level or higher performance under the same budget. Empirical analyses show that difficulty-aware retrieval yields more relevant historical examples than similarity-based retrieval alone, and leads to more accurate predictions of which model will perform best on a given query.

Conclusion: The study concludes that explicitly modeling query difficulty and using it as a retrieval signal is an effective strategy for LLM routing. VDAR-Router, by combining difficulty analysis, retrieval over past cases, and a cost-aware reward function, can make better model selection decisions than methods relying only on surface semantics or embeddings. The authors argue that difficulty-aware, retrieval-based routing offers a practical, training-free way to improve the economic efficiency of multi-model LLM systems and that explicit query analysis also enhances interpretability of routing decisions.

Abstract: Large language models are increasingly used in practical systems, making efficient model selection important for reducing deployment cost. LLM routing has emerged as a practical solution for allocating each input query to an appropriate model under a desired cost-performance trade-off. Existing routing methods often estimate model suitability from the surface semantics or embedding similarity of the input query. However, such methods may ignore the underlying difficulty of a query, leading to suboptimal routing decisions. To address the challenge, we propose VDAR-Router, a difficulty-aware retrieval-based routing framework. For each input query, VDAR-Router first generates an explicit difficulty analysis. It then retrieves historical examples with similar difficulty profiles. Based on the retrieved records, it estimates candidate model suitability and selects the model using a reward function that considers both performance and cost. Experiments on three datasets show that VDAR-Router consistently achieves better cost-performance trade-offs than existing baselines. These results demonstrate the effectiveness of difficulty-aware retrieval for training-free LLM routing. Case studies further show that explicit query analysis helps retrieve more relevant examples and supports more reliable routing decisions.

</details>


### [50] [How Does Alignment Tuning Shape Representations of Sycophancy and Related Cue-Induced Biases in LLMs?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18114)
*Prakhar Gupta,Terry Jingchen Zhang,Florent Draye,Bernhard Schölkopf,Zhijing Jin*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that many cue-induced biases in aligned LLMs correspond to clear, causal directions in hidden states that are installed mainly during alignment tuning, and that steering along these directions can partially debias model outputs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs often change otherwise-correct answers when prompts contain irrelevant cues such as hints, mislabeled examples, or fake prior turns. This raises concerns about robustness and trustworthiness, but it is unclear where in the model this susceptibility resides, how it is represented, and whether it can be systematically controlled. The paper aims to localize and characterize these biases inside model representations.

Method: The authors study five LLM families and seven types of benchmarked cue-induced biases (BCT). For each bias type, they extract a single “bias direction” in the model’s hidden state space. They then validate this representation through three approaches: (1) probing to see if hidden states encode cue-induced bias; (2) leave-one-dataset-out transfer to test whether the learned direction generalizes across datasets within the same bias; and (3) causal interventions, where they directly steer activations along or against the bias direction to examine effects on model behavior.

Result: They find that pretrained base models show little cue-induced bias and their activations contain no strong cue-specific signal beyond the question content, whereas alignment-tuned models exhibit strong susceptibility. In aligned models, each bias corresponds to a coherent, approximately one-dimensional direction that predicts and causally controls biased behavior across model families. These directions are decodable and can be used to steer models toward less biased answers. However, biases remain representationally distinct: directions for different biases do not collapse into a single common subspace, and the degree of cross-bias entanglement varies by model family rather than bias type.

Conclusion: Cue-induced biases in LLMs mainly arise from alignment tuning and are encoded as distinct, causally influential directions in hidden-state space rather than as a single generic flaw. Because these directions can be identified and manipulated, they offer a principled handle for analysis and partial debiasing, though different biases require separate treatment and may interact in model-specific ways.

Abstract: Modern LLMs are alarmingly susceptible to surprisingly simple immaterial changes of input prompts: a casual hint, an incorrectly labeled few-shot example, or a fake prior assistant turn often flips an originally correct answer. We study where this susceptibility, spanning sycophancy and related cue-induced biases, lives inside the model. Across five model families and seven BCT bias types, we extract a per-bias direction from hidden states and triangulate it through three measures: probing, leave-one-dataset-out transfer, and causal intervention. The susceptibility is largely installed by alignment tuning rather than pretraining: pretrained base models barely cave to these biases, and their activations carry no cue-specific signal beyond question content. Within aligned models, each bias becomes a single coherent direction that we can both decode and steer along, recovering the unbiased answer across every family we test. The biases stay representationally distinct, however: cross-bias entanglement is model-specific rather than a property of the bias category, and even behaviorally similar biases occupy different directions. The same intervention also serves as a modest debiasing tool, recovering a meaningful share of bias-induced errors while preserving most correct answers across all instruct families. Cue-induced bias is therefore best understood not as a single flaw in LLMs but as a family of distinct, causally active directions that alignment tuning installs.

</details>


### [51] [VEHBench: A Stage-Local Diagnostic Benchmark for LLM-Assisted Vibration Energy Harvester Design](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18181)
*Depeng Su,Yuyu Luo,Guobiao Hu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Introduces VEHBench, a diagnostic benchmark for evaluating LLM-assisted design of vibration energy harvesters (VEHs) across multiple workflow stages, using an analytical physical oracle over 763 tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need for a benchmark that is native to engineering workflows and can reveal stage-specific strengths and weaknesses of LLMs in physical design tasks.

Method: The authors create VEHBench, a benchmark consisting of 763 tasks derived from VEH literature and grounded in a physics-based analytical oracle. VEHBench is structured around four design roles that mirror real engineering activities: (1) specification triage (interpreting and prioritizing design requirements), (2) verifier-guided search (iterative refinement informed by a physical verifier), (3) corrupted-state recovery (repairing partially incorrect or degraded design states), and (4) policy-conditioned selection (choosing designs under given constraints or policies). They then evaluate multiple LLMs on these tasks to analyze model behavior across roles and stages.

Result: Experiments show that LLM performance varies significantly across the four design roles: no single model is best at all stages of the VEH design workflow. Different models exhibit distinct response-control and behavioral patterns when interacting with the analytical verifier and under different task conditions. This highlights strong stage dependence of LLM capabilities in verifier-grounded engineering workflows.

Conclusion: VEHBench offers an engineering-native, stage-aware benchmark for diagnosing and comparing LLMs in verifier-grounded physical design tasks, specifically for VEH design. It enables more nuanced evaluation, selection, routing, and improvement of engineering LLMs by exposing how their capabilities differ across key workflow stages rather than only at the final artifact level. The benchmark is publicly released to support further research and development.

Abstract: Battery-free Internet of Things (IoT) requires iterative design of vibration energy harvesters (VEHs) under coupled physical constraints, while LLMs are emerging as interface layers for engineering workflows. However, existing engineering benchmarks primarily assess final artifact validity, offering limited insights into how LLMs behave across different stages of coupled physical design. We introduce VEHBench, an engineering-native diagnostic benchmark for LLM-assisted VEH design, featuring 763 literature-grounded tasks scored by an analytical physical oracle. VEHBench evaluates four design roles: specification triage, verifier-guided search, corrupted-state recovery, and policy-conditioned selection. Experimental results reveal that LLM capability is strongly stage-dependent: no single model consistently dominates the entire workflow, and response-control profiles expose distinct behavioral patterns across design roles. VEHBench thus provides a stage-aware foundation for evaluating, selecting, routing, and improving verifier-grounded engineering LLMs. The benchmark artifact is available at https://huggingface.co/datasets/AnonymousVehbench/vehbench

### [52] [PPL-Factory: Task-Aware and Budget-Aware Data Selection from Language Modeling to Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18199)
*Hang Zhang,Warren J. Gross*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes PPL-Factory, a task-aware and budget-aware perplexity-based framework to select the most informative training samples for LLM fine-tuning, achieving better or comparable performance with as little as 1–10% of the data.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Fine-tuning large language models is computationally expensive, and not all training samples are equally useful. Existing data selection methods often rely on fixed, heuristic criteria (e.g., quality, diversity, trace length) that are not robust across different tasks, and standard perplexity-based selection ignores task-specific objectives, especially for reasoning tasks. There is a need for a simple, generalizable, and model-aware way to select informative data that respects data budgets and task differences.

Method: The authors introduce PPL-Factory, a data selection framework that uses task-aware perplexity scores to estimate the difficulty and informativeness of individual samples, distinguishing between language modeling and reasoning task objectives. It then applies data budget-aware criteria to choose which samples to keep under a given subset size, effectively ranking and filtering training data based on these perplexity-derived scores for efficient fine-tuning.

Result: On reasoning benchmarks such as GSM8K and MATH, PPL-Factory outperforms other state-of-the-art data selection methods using only 1% of the training data. With 10% of the training data, models fine-tuned with PPL-Factory-selected samples achieve higher accuracy than full-data fine-tuning by 0.9 points on GSM8K and 4.8 points on MATH, demonstrating strong data efficiency and performance gains.

Conclusion: Task-aware and budget-aware perplexity-based data selection is an effective and practical strategy for efficient LLM fine-tuning, enabling substantial reductions in training data and computation while maintaining or even improving downstream task performance. PPL-Factory provides a simple and interpretable way to generalize data selection across different reasoning tasks.

Abstract: Not all training samples contribute equally to large language model fine-tuning. Selecting informative training samples can reduce the computational cost while preserving downstream performance. Many existing data selection methods rely on indirect heuristics, such as data quality, diversity or reasoning trace length. However, the effectiveness of these fixed criteria is task-dependent and difficult to generalize across diverse downstream tasks. Perplexity-based data selection provides a simple and model-aware solution to estimate the sample difficulty, but existing approaches typically score the entire training sequence and ignore the difference in learning objectives of language modeling and reasoning tasks. In this paper, we propose PPL-Factory, a simple and interpretable data selection framework that combines task-aware perplexity-based scores and data budget-aware selection criteria. Experiments on GSM8K demonstrate that PPL-Factory outperforms other state-of-the-art data selection methods using only $1\%$ of the training set. With $10\%$ of the data, PPL-Factory exceeds full-data fine-tuning accuracy by 0.9 on GSM8K and 4.8 on MATH. Overall, our results demonstrate that task-aware and budget-aware perplexity-based selection provides an effective and applicable approach for efficient fine-tuning.

### [53] [SWE-Pruner Pro: The Coder LLM Already Knows What to Prune](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18213)
*Yuhang Wang,Yuling Shi,Shaoqiu Zhang,Jialiang Liang,Shilin He,Siyu Ye,Yuting Chen,Kai Cai,Xiaodong Gu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces SWE-Pruner Pro, a method that prunes tool-output code lines directly inside a coding agent using its internal representations, saving tokens while preserving or improving task performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Coding agents working with long tool outputs (e.g., code, logs, diffs) suffer from high token costs and latency. Existing context pruning methods like SWE-Pruner require an external classifier, adding complexity and overhead. The authors observe that the agent’s own internal states already encode information about which lines of tool output are relevant, motivating a more integrated, efficient pruning approach.

Method: SWE-Pruner Pro adds a lightweight prediction head on top of the agent’s internal representations while it reads tool outputs. For each line of tool output, the head maps the agent’s hidden states to a binary keep-or-prune label. The model uses a length-aware embedding conditioned on the number of lines in the tool output to inform these decisions. Pruning thus happens inside the agent itself rather than via a separate model. The approach is evaluated on two open-weight LLM backbones over four multi-turn coding benchmarks.

Result: Across benchmarks, SWE-Pruner Pro reduces both prompt and completion tokens by up to 39% while maintaining overall task performance and keeping inference overhead bounded. On MiMo-V2-Flash, it not only preserves but improves performance: SWE-Bench Verified resolve rate increases by 3.8%, and long-context Oolong accuracy improves by 2.2 percentage points.

Conclusion: Exploiting an agent’s own internal representations is an effective strategy for context pruning in coding agents. By integrating a small pruning head directly into the agent and using length-aware embeddings, SWE-Pruner Pro achieves substantial token savings with minimal overhead and can even improve downstream task quality on challenging long-context benchmarks.

Abstract: Pruning long context for coding agents has been a vital technology for efficient context management. While existing context pruning methods such as SWE-Pruner realize this by attaching a separate code classifier, we find the agent itself encodes internal representations indicating the relevance of code context when reading tool output. Based on this finding, we propose SWE-Pruner Pro, which prunes tool outputs directly inside the agent. Concretely, a small head turns the agent's own internal representations into a keep-or-prune label for each line, with a length-aware embedding keyed to each tool output's line count. Across two open-weight backbones and four multi-turn benchmarks, SWE-Pruner Pro saves up to 39% of prompt and completion tokens while preserving task quality, with bounded inference overhead. Notably, on MiMo-V2-Flash SWE-Pruner Pro additionally raises the SWE-Bench Verified resolve rate by +3.8% and the long-context Oolong accuracy by +2.2 points.

### [54] [It's Not What You Say, It's How You Say It: Evaluating LLM Responses to Expressions of Belief](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18232)
*Kevin Du,Clara Kümpel,Michelle Wastl,Alex Warstadt*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how different ways users express beliefs influence whether LLMs follow user-provided context or rely on prior world knowledge, building a typology of belief expressions and benchmarking 16 models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are often given user beliefs in prompts, but it is unclear when models should treat these beliefs as true or instead rely on their own stored knowledge. Moreover, expressions of belief appear in many linguistic forms (e.g., presuppositions, evidential markers, varying levels of certainty and tone), which may differently bias LLM behavior. Understanding these effects is important both for safety/robustness and for effective prompt design.

Method: The authors construct a typology of user expressions of belief based on four linguistic dimensions: form (e.g., assertion, presupposition), evidentiality (source or evidence of belief), epistemic stance (certainty/uncertainty), and tone (e.g., neutral, emphatic), yielding 17 fine-grained EoB types. They then pair these EoBs with factual world-knowledge statements to create controlled belief-query pairs that hold content constant while varying only linguistic framing. Using this benchmark, they systematically evaluate how 16 LLMs (across architectures such as Llama3, Qwen3, Gemma3; parameter scales from 1B to 30B; and base vs. instruction-tuned stages) choose between following the contextual belief or their prior knowledge, and analyze response patterns statistically.

Result: Across models, there are consistent differences in how often they accept user beliefs versus stick with prior knowledge. Larger and instruction-tuned models are generally less likely to follow contextually provided beliefs than smaller and base models, indicating stronger reliance on stored knowledge or safety biases. The study also finds that some EoB types (specific combinations of form, evidentiality, stance, and tone) have significantly higher persuasive power, systematically pushing models to adopt the contextual belief more often than others.

Conclusion: Linguistic framing of user beliefs has systematic and measurable effects on LLM behavior, influencing whether models prioritize prompt context or prior knowledge. Model architecture, size, and training stage modulate this sensitivity. These findings inform both practical prompt engineering—by highlighting belief expressions that more effectively steer models—and the design of more robust models that can better calibrate when to accept contextual beliefs versus rely on their own knowledge.

Abstract: Users frequently express their beliefs to large language models (LLMs). In some situations, the LLM should accept these contextual beliefs as true. In others, they should stick to their prior knowledge. Notably, users' expressions of belief (EoBs) can take linguistically diverse forms - using presuppositions, evidential and certainty markers, or varied tones - each of which may have a different persuasiveness over the LLMs. We introduce a typology to systematically evaluate how different EoBs affect whether models follow context versus prior knowledge. The typology is grounded in four linguistically motivated dimensions: form, evidentiality, epistemic stance, and tone, spanning 17 fine-grained types. By pairing these EoBs with world knowledge facts, we generate controlled EoB-query pairs that isolate the effect of linguistic variation. Using this benchmark, we evaluate 16 LLMs that differ in architecture (Llama3, Qwen3, Gemma3), scale (1B-30B parameters), and training stages (base vs instruct). We identify meaningful variations in response behavior across these axes, e.g., that bigger models and instruction models tend to be less context-following than smaller models and base models. We further identify specific EoBs that statistically significantly persuade LMs more consistently than others. Our work reveals systematic patterns in how linguistic framing affects LLM context integration, with implications for prompt engineering and model robustness.

### [55] [Automated Discovery Has No Universally Superior Harness](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18235)
*Akshat Gupta,Jermaine Lei,Alexander Lu,Gopala Anumanchipalli,Leshem Choshen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper empirically dissects and evaluates components of autonomous LLM discovery harnesses, showing no single fixed harness generalizes well and advocating adaptive, problem-specific harness selection guided by early performance signals.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing autonomous discovery systems like OpenEvolve and TTT-Discover are treated as monolithic, general-purpose recipes and often evaluated with too few trials, making it hard to know which design choices actually matter and whether improvements generalize across models and tasks. There is a need to understand the contribution of individual harness components, quantify variance, and develop more reliable, generalizable methods for harness selection and compute allocation.

Method: The authors decompose OpenEvolve-style evolutionary search and TTT-Discover into their constituent components (archives, parent selection, exploration strategy, budget allocation, etc.), recombine them into 30 budget-matched harness variants, and evaluate these across 12 model–problem pairs. They run over 3.1M LLM rollouts and use repeated-trial statistical analysis to compare harnesses, examine generalization across conditions, study the relationship between early and final performance, and design an adaptive budget allocation scheme that starts many harnesses, prunes poorly performing ones early, and reallocates compute to better performers.

Result: No single harness configuration dominates across all model–problem combinations; harness performance is highly problem- and model-dependent, with OpenEvolve variants often underperforming simpler baselines. Early performance during a discovery run is strongly predictive of final outcomes. Using this, an adaptive allocation strategy that launches multiple harnesses, discards weak ones early, and reallocates remaining budget to stronger ones yields better performance than either picking a single harness in advance or using a static ensemble without adaptation. The authors also release all run pools and null distributions as reusable statistical baselines.

Conclusion: Discovery harness design should be treated as a tunable hyperparameter rather than a fixed recipe, as no universal best harness exists across tasks and models. Online adaptation, guided by early-run performance, is an effective strategy for leveraging compute more efficiently and improving discovery outcomes. The released datasets and baselines can serve as infrastructure for more rigorous, statistically grounded evaluation of future harness designs, encouraging the field to move away from single-shot, low-trial comparisons of monolithic systems.

Abstract: Autonomous discovery systems such as OpenEvolve and TTT-Discover are often used as general-purpose harnesses. However, in practice these are composite systems combining several design choices about archives, parent selection, exploration, and budget allocation into a single recipe. Because discovery runs are expensive and inherently stochastic, existing harnesses are often compared using too few independent trials to distinguish key methodological improvements from run-to-run variance. We systematically decompose OpenEvolve-style evolutionary search and the TTT-Discover search harness into its constituent components and systematically evaluate 30 budget-matched harnesses across 12 model-problem pairs using more than 3.1 million LLM rollouts and repeated-trial statistical analysis. Our results show that discovery harnesses have a generalization problem: No fixed harness is reliably superior across the evaluated model-problem pairs, and variants of OpenEvolve generally underperform simpler alternatives. Thus, harness choice is better viewed as a hyperparameter rather than as a universal recipe, and should be tailored to the specific problem and underlying model. We also find that early discovery progress predicts final performance, and use this property to present a budget-matched adaptive-allocation experiment that starts multiple harnesses, prunes weak partial runs, and reallocates compute to stronger survivors, outperforming both commitment to a randomly sampled fixed harness and a non-adaptive harness ensemble. Together, these results motivate shifting from fixed harness selection to online adaptation guided by early performance. We release all run pools including baseline null distributions for every model-problem pair as reusable statistical infrastructure against for future harness proposals.

</details>

### [56] [Rater State Bias in RLHF Preference Data: An Audit Framework](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16195)
*Elena Kopteva,Vitaliy Hlynianyi-Zhuk*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes that annotators’ emotional or cognitive states during labeling can systematically bias RLHF preference data, and it introduces definitions, predictions, and an audit framework to detect and study this bias.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: RLHF relies on human preference data to align models, assuming labels only reflect comparative output quality. However, annotators work over time and often under stress, so their changing emotional states may systematically influence which outputs they prefer. This creates a structured confound—distinct from random noise or simple disagreement—that can propagate through reward models and RL policies, potentially distorting aligned behavior. The authors want to formalize this concern and make it empirically testable.

Method: The paper is primarily conceptual and methodological rather than experimental. It: (1) Formally defines rater state shift (changes in annotator state over time), rater state confound (when labels encode both output quality and rater state), and correlated rater state bias (shared patterns across annotators under similar conditions). (2) Introduces the notion of “survival level emotional authenticity” as a specific, operationalizable response pattern characterized via lexical, pragmatic, discourse, and safety-related features. (3) Analyzes, at the level of data aggregation and reward modeling, how correlated rater state bias can persist through majority voting or other aggregation and influence learned reward signals. (4) Derives five falsifiable predictions and corresponding effect size thresholds to guide an initial empirical audit. (5) Proposes an audit protocol and pilot study design that can be run on publicly available instruction-tuned models, without needing access to proprietary training logs.

Result: Because the work is positioned as hypothesis and framework development rather than a full empirical study, there are no large-scale quantitative results. The main outputs are: (i) a set of precise definitions around rater state shift and its associated confounds, (ii) the concept and operationalization outline of survival level emotional authenticity as a measurable response pattern, (iii) a theoretical analysis showing pathways by which correlated rater state bias can pass through aggregation and into reward models, (iv) five specific, testable predictions plus proposed effect size thresholds that an audit should be able to detect if the bias is present, and (v) a concrete protocol and pilot study plan for auditing existing instruction-tuned models for these effects.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that rater state shift is a plausible, structured, and testable source of bias in RLHF preference data that is distinct from random noise or mere annotator disagreement. Because rater states can be systematically correlated across annotators and time, their influence can survive standard aggregation procedures and contaminate learned reward signals and downstream policies. They advocate treating rater state as an important variable in RLHF system design and propose that their definitions, predictions, and audit protocol provide a practical starting point for empirically investigating and mitigating this bias in current and future RLHF pipelines.

Abstract: We identify a structured confound in Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback (RLHF). Pairwise preference labels are intended to reflect the compared outputs, but they may also reflect the rater's state during annotation. Under sustained stressful or distressing conditions, raters' preferences may shift over time. As a result, preference data can encode rater state alongside judgments about response quality. These shifts differ from ordinary disagreement or random label noise. They are state dependent, can be shared across annotators working under similar conditions, and can propagate through reward modeling and policy optimization. We therefore propose rater state shift as a plausible and testable source of structured bias in RLHF preference data. This paper develops a hypothesis and an audit framework for studying this source of bias. We define rater state shift, rater state confound, and correlated rater state bias. We also define survival level emotional authenticity as a measurable response pattern using lexical, pragmatic, discourse, and safety related features. We analyze how correlated rater state bias can survive aggregation and enter learned reward signals. We derive five falsifiable predictions and effect size thresholds for an initial audit. Finally, we present an audit protocol and pilot study plan that can be applied to publicly available instruction tuned models. We do not infer the training history of any specific deployed model. Our goal is to isolate a plausible and testable source of structured bias in RLHF preference data.

</details>


### [57] [Design and Validation of a Lightweight 1D CNN for Affective Touch Classification in Soft Plush Companions](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16196)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

However, soft bodies deform in complex ways and have many tactile sensing channels, making affective touch recognition difficult, especially on small embedded hardware. There is also a lack of standardized, reusable datasets and streamlined development tools for this niche.

Method: The authors created an open-source MATLAB framework to design, train, and validate compact deep-learning models for affective touch recognition. They collected a FAIR-compliant dataset of 1326 labeled tactile gesture sequences from 25 participants (children to adults) interacting with a soft, sensorized toy. Result: A compact 1D dilated CNN with about 13.2k parameters achieved 75% accuracy on a held-out test set and 85% mean accuracy in leave-one-subject-out cross-validation. Real-time PC-based simulation with live sensor streaming showed that the CNN can recognize nuanced, socially meaningful touches that a previous heuristic system missed, while high-force negative interactions are better caught by simple threshold rules.

A hybrid pipeline that combines instantaneous heuristic filtering for obvious high-force events with CNN-based classification for nuanced gestures is recommended for embedded deployment. The released dataset and MATLAB framework provide a practical, reusable foundation for future research and hardware integration, which will be detailed in subsequent work.

Theoretical inference-time analysis shows that quantized deployment requires 3.2 MMAC per window, compatible with 20 Hz real-time operation on the target microcontroller. PC-based real-time simulation with the physical toy streaming sensor data demonstrates that the CNN resolves subtle social touches that the previous heuristic system failed to detect, whereas high-force negative interactions are captured more reliably by trivial threshold-based logic. The resulting hybrid inference pipeline - instantaneous heuristic filtering followed by CNN-based nuanced gesture classification - is proposed as the embedded deployment strategy. The study demonstrates that emotionally meaningful, privacy-preserving touch interpretation is computationally feasible for direct embedding within soft therapeutic companions, with hardware integration addressed in a forthcoming study.

### [58] [Some Large Language Models Exhibit Consistent Risk Attitudes](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16197)
*Bowen Sun,Rui Min,Yuxi Wang,Brian Odegaard,Qi Wang,Jing Du*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper investigates how large language models translate perceived risk into decisions, revealing that they have stable, measurable risk attitudes that differ systematically from humans.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While AI systems are increasingly used in high-stakes, uncertain environments, we lack tools to quantify how they turn risk assessments into concrete actions. Most current evaluations focus on accuracy or calibration, not on the underlying risk preferences that shape decisions, which are crucial for safety and alignment in open-ended deployment settings.

Method: The authors design a cross-domain evaluation framework that separates an agent’s context-specific risk beliefs from its final categorical choices. They apply this framework to six representative LLMs and 100 human participants on three types of tasks: spatial navigation, clinical triage, and financial allocation. Using regression-based models, they estimate each agent’s mapping from belief about outcomes to decisions and derive measures of risk sensitivity and risk attitude bias.

Result: They find that most LLMs show (1) strong consistency within each task domain, indicating a stable belief-to-decision mapping; (2) stable relative ordering of risk attitudes across different task domains, suggesting a persistent style of risk-taking or risk-aversion; and (3) a narrower, more constrained range of risk attitudes than the more diverse human participants, indicating convergence toward a limited subset of possible risk profiles.

Conclusion: Risk attitude emerges as a stable, quantifiable behavioral trait of LLMs that had not been systematically characterized before. This provides a baseline for evaluating and aligning AI decision-making under uncertainty and suggests that future work should investigate where these intrinsic dispositions come from and how they can be shaped or controlled in high-stakes applications.

Abstract: As artificial intelligence systems are deployed in open-ended, high-stakes settings, a critical dimension remains unmeasured: how perceived risk is translated into action. We test whether large language models (LLMs) exhibit systematic and consistent risk attitudes under uncertainty. We introduce a cross-domain framework that decouples contextual risk belief from categorical decision, and apply it to six representative LLMs and 100 human participants across spatial navigation, clinical triage, and financial allocation tasks. Using regression models, we extract each agents belief-to-decision mapping and quantify risk sensitivity and risk attitude bias. We find that most tested LLMs exhibit (i) robust intra-task consistency, indicating stable mappings from contextual belief to risk decision within a fixed task domain; (ii) cross-domain rank-order stability, preserving relative risk posture across tasks; and (iii) a convergence toward a restricted risk-attitude distribution relative to the broader human baseline. These results reveal risk attitude as a stable and previously uncharacterized dimension of LLM behavior, establishing a foundation for evaluating and aligning AI systems in open-ended decision-making and motivating further investigation into the origins of these intrinsic behavioral dispositions.

Result: The survey yields a structured taxonomy of GNN-based link prediction methods, mapping recent advances to specific encoder architectures and application domains. The paper also compiles and organizes known challenges and gaps in the current literature.

The proposed taxonomy and discussion of challenges and future directions are intended to guide subsequent research toward more effective, versatile, and scalable GNN-based link prediction methods.

We propose an innovative taxonomy that categorizes recent advancements based on techniques and applications. From a technique perspective, we focus on key GNN encoder architectures, including GCN-based, GAE-based, GAT-based, and GFormer-based methods, discussing their strengths and limitations. From an application perspective, we highlight prominent use cases of link prediction in knowledge graphs and recommendation systems, demonstrating their real-world impact. In addition, we examine the current challenges and discuss promising future directions.

### [60] [PlanFlip: Attacking Multi-Agent LLM Systems via Planning-Phase Prompt Injection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16199)
*Yuhang Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how the planning component in multi-agent LLM systems is a vulnerable attack surface and proposes a taxonomy of prompt-injection attacks on planners, evaluates them across frontier LLMs, and introduces defenses based on heterogeneous model checking.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-agent LLM architectures typically use a Planner to break a high-level goal into sub-tasks for other agents (Executors, Critics). While prior work often focuses on securing individual agents or execution-time behavior, the authors observe that the planning stage itself is a single point of failure: if compromised, it can corrupt all subsequent sub-tasks. Existing defenses like keyword filters and homogeneous multi-agent redundancy may be insufficient. Therefore, they aim to systematically characterize and evaluate planning-phase prompt injection attacks and understand how model capability, pipeline homogeneity, and reasoning style affect vulnerability, while exploring effective defenses.

Method: The authors propose PlanFlip, a framework of four planner-focused prompt-injection attacks: (PF-1) GoalSubstitution, which covertly replaces the original user goal; (PF-2) PriorityInversion, which flips or distorts task priorities; (PF-3) ContextPollution, which injects misleading or irrelevant content; and (PF-4) RoleConfusion, which alters role boundaries or instructions. These attacks are crafted to masquerade as benign tool outputs to evade simple filters. They evaluate nine frontier LLMs in multi-agent configurations over 3,479 episodes, measuring attack success rate (ASR), stealth (whether the Critic flags misalignment), and StepShift (changes in plan structure). They also compute semantic deviation between intended and attacked plans using independent human judges. Finally, they design two defenses: GoalAnchorCheck (D1), which detects deviations from the original goal, and CrossAgentConsensus (D2), where heterogeneous models cross-check each other, and compare these against same-backbone (homogeneous) baselines.

Result: Experiments show: (1) More capable models are more vulnerable in this setting: GPT-5 exhibits the highest attack success rate (ASR = 0.68), challenging the idea that stronger models are automatically more secure. (2) Homogeneous pipelines (where Planner and Critic share the same backbone) suffer a correlated-agent blind spot: models like GPT-4o and Llama-3.3-70B have nearly zero ASR by naive metrics yet achieve perfect Stealth (1.00) and non-zero StepShift, meaning attacks significantly restructure plans while the same-backbone Critic wrongly reports alignment. Independent judges confirm noticeable semantic deviation (-0.20 to -0.32) with high inter-rater agreement (r = 0.943). (3) Reasoning-augmented models such as DeepSeek-R1 are robust in this regime, showing StepShift = 0.00 across all attacks. For defenses, GoalAnchorCheck and CrossAgentConsensus attain detection rates up to 1.00 and outperform homogeneous same-backbone baselines in 15 of 16 evaluation cells.

Conclusion: The study concludes that the planning phase is a critical and underappreciated attack surface in multi-agent LLM systems. Prompt-injection attacks targeted at planners can flip or distort entire task decompositions and propagate errors throughout the pipeline, especially when attacks are disguised as realistic tool outputs. Model capability does not guarantee safety, and homogeneous multi-agent designs with shared backbones create correlated blind spots where Critic agents fail to notice compromised plans. In contrast, reasoning-focused models and heterogeneous, cross-model defenses like GoalAnchorCheck and CrossAgentConsensus significantly improve robustness. The overarching takeaway is that architectural diversity, not mere redundancy within a single backbone, is essential for securing multi-agent LLM planning.

Abstract: Multi-agent LLM systems increasingly rely on a Planner to decompose goals into sub-task sequences that downstream Executor and Critic agents execute and audit. We identify the planning phase as a critical attack surface: a single injection into the Planner's context achieves cascade amplification, corrupting all downstream sub-tasks simultaneously. We introduce PlanFlip, a framework comprising four planning-phase prompt injection attacks -- GoalSubstitution (PF-1), PriorityInversion (PF-2), ContextPollution (PF-3), and RoleConfusion (PF-4) -- each disguised as plausible tool outputs to evade keyword filters. Evaluating nine frontier LLMs across 3,479 episodes, we uncover three findings: (1) capability amplifies vulnerability -- GPT-5 achieves the highest attack success rate (ASR = 0.68), contradicting the assumption that stronger models are inherently more secure; (2) homogeneous pipelines exhibit a correlated-agent blind spot -- GPT-4o and Llama-3.3-70B show ASR near 0 yet Stealth = 1.00 and StepShift > 0, with attacks restructuring plans while the same-backbone Critic reports alignment (two independent judges confirm -0.20 to -0.32 semantic deviation, r = 0.943); (3) reasoning-augmented models resist injections -- DeepSeek-R1 achieves StepShift = 0.00 across all attacks. We propose GoalAnchorCheck (D1) and CrossAgentConsensus (D2), achieving detection rates up to 1.00 and outperforming same-backbone baselines in 15 of 16 cells. Our key insight: heterogeneous model diversity is a security prerequisite for multi-agent systems; redundancy within a homogeneous backbone provides no protection against planning-phase attacks.

</details>


### [61] [Deterministic Replay for AI Agent Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16200)
*Rasheed Mudasiru*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduces agrepl, a CLI tool enabling deterministic replay of AI agent executions by capturing and replaying all external interactions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: AI agent systems using LLMs and external tools are highly non-deterministic due to sampling variance and external environment noise, making debugging, observability, and reproducibility difficult because existing platforms only log executions but cannot truly replay them in isolation.

Method: Define an agent execution model and a request-key matching function K(s), then intercept all external communications at the transport layer via a MITM proxy, serializing these into structured traces that can be deterministically replayed in a fully isolated environment; introduce a noise-aware diff algorithm that treats some HTTP header differences as noise and others as signal; implement the system as a Go-based CLI tool.

Result: Across five workloads with 250 replay instances, agrepl achieves perfect replay fidelity (F = 1.0) and reduces median per-step latency by 98.3% during replay compared to live execution.

Conclusion: Deterministic replay of AI agent executions is practical and effective when all external interactions are captured at the transport layer and carefully matched during replay; agrepl provides a developer-friendly, reproducible debugging and analysis workflow for LLM-based agent systems and is made widely accessible as an open-source, single-binary MIT-licensed tool.

Abstract: AI agent systems that couple large language models (LLMs) with external tools and APIs are inherently non-deterministic: LLM sampling variance, external API state, CDN infrastructure headers, and execution-environment noise collectively prevent any prior agent run from being faithfully re-executed. Existing observability platforms capture execution logs but cannot reproduce a run in isolation. We present agrepl, a developer-first CLI framework for deterministic replay of agent executions. agrepl intercepts all external interactions at the transport layer via a man-in-the-middle (MITM) proxy, serialises them as structured execution traces, and replays them in a strictly isolated environment with zero outbound network access. We formalise the agent execution model, define the request-key matching function K(s), and prove the determinism invariant. We introduce a noise-aware diff algorithm classifying HTTP header divergence into signal and noise tiers. Empirical evaluation across five workloads (n = 250 replay instances) demonstrates replay fidelity F = 1.0 and a median per-step latency reduction of 98.3%. agrepl is implemented in Go, ships as a single static binary, and is released under the MIT licence.
  Keywords: AI agents, deterministic replay, LLM debugging, reproducibility, MITM proxy, execution tracing, record/replay systems.

### [62] [Generative Ontology Induction: Domain-Agnostic Schema Discovery from Document Corpora Using Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16201)
*Sergei Sergienko*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes Generative Ontology Induction (GOI), a framework that automatically learns a structured, exportable ontology blueprint from example documents and evaluates it using a new Node Coverage Score, showing high structural coverage across diverse domains.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Ontology construction is a major bottleneck for knowledge-intensive AI because current automated methods either rely on rigid predefined schemas, work only in narrow domains, or output unstructured representations that are hard to plug into downstream systems. The authors want a domain-agnostic, automated way to derive a rich, structured ontology directly from example corpora while ensuring that the induced structure is both expressive and machine-usable.

Method: They propose Generative Ontology Induction (GOI), which learns a generative blueprint comprising entities, dimensions, properties, relationships, and constraints from example documents and represents it as a typed graph with six node types and seven edge types, exportable in YAML/JSON. They also define a new evaluation metric, Node Coverage Score, that measures how many of the true structural ontology nodes (classes, properties, dimensions) appear in generated outputs. They conduct controlled generative validation across four diverse ontologies: a software services invoice schema, a job description ontology, a pain-management clinical visit record ontology, and a professional services contract ontology.

Result: In experiments, GOI-driven generation achieves 95–100% coverage of the structural backbone (core ontology nodes) across all four ontologies, while a simpler generic three-field template baseline attains 97.8% coverage only for the familiar invoice schema but drops sharply in coverage for the more complex or unfamiliar ontologies (to 52.2%, 62.2%, and 78.3% for job description, pain-management, and professional services contract ontologies, respectively). The results indicate that GOI matches or exceeds baselines on familiar schemas and substantially outperforms them on more specialized domains.

Conclusion: GOI provides a domain-agnostic, generative approach to automatically inducing structured ontologies from examples and exporting them in a graph-based representation suitable for downstream use. The high Node Coverage Scores across varied ontologies, independent of the model’s prior familiarity with the document type, suggest that GOI reliably captures the structural backbone of target domains and overcomes the limitations of generic, schema-agnostic templates.

Abstract: Ontology engineering remains a critical bottleneck in knowledge-intensive AI systems. Existing automated approaches either depend on predefined schemas, operate within narrow domains, or produce unstructured outputs unsuitable for downstream pipelines.
  We introduce Generative Ontology Induction (GOI), a domain-agnostic framework that induces a generative blueprint - entities, dimensions, properties, relationships, and constraints - from a corpus of examples and exports it as a typed graph (six node types, seven edge types) in YAML/JSON. We introduce the Node Coverage Score, a novel evaluation metric that measures the fraction of structural ontology nodes (classes, properties, and dimensions) appearing in generated outputs.
  A controlled generative validation on four contrasting ontologies - a familiar Software Services Invoice schema, a custom Job Description Ontology, a confidential Pain-Management Clinical Visit Record Ontology, and a Professional Services Contract & Statement of Work Ontology - shows that GOI-prompted generation covers 95-100% of the structural backbone in every case; a generic three-field template holds at 97.8% on the invoice schema but drops to 52.2% on the Job Description Ontology, 62.2% on the Pain-Management ontology, and 78.3% on the Professional Services Contract ontology. The structural coverage holds regardless of how familiar the document type is to the model.

These gains show that even small models can become strong specialists on the benchmark when given an efficient adaptation pipeline.

The benchmark emphasizes symbolic precision, constrained formatting, extraction, and short-horizon semantic decision making under a strict one-letter output protocol. A shared parameter-efficient fine-tuning pipeline then adapts a subset of models using 4-bit NF4 quantization with DoRA/LoRA-style adapters on an NVIDIA L4-class budget. In base evaluation, Qwen Coder 3B leads at 75.67% strict accuracy, followed by Qwen2.5 1.5B at 67.10%, Qwen3.5 2B at 64.98%, and Granite 3.3 2B at 64.61%. On the shared 108-example held-out fine-tuning split, adaptation improves Qwen Coder 3B by +26.85 points, SmolLM2 1.7B by +25.92, Qwen2.5 1.5B by +19.44, SmolLM2 360M by +10.18, and SmolLM2 135M by +5.55. Across ranking, topic-level heterogeneity, difficulty strata, failure composition, efficiency frontiers, and topic-conditioned transfer, the same conclusion recurs: a disciplined workflow of benchmark construction, cross-model evaluation, and low-cost specialization already makes a subset of sub-3B models viable as local experts for structured niche workloads.
