### [64] [Masked Diffusion Language Models are Strong and Steerable Text-Based World Models for Agentic RL](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16204)
*Darshan Deshpande*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces steerable text-based world models for RL, showing that masked diffusion language models yield more coherent, diverse, and grounded simulated environments than larger autoregressive LMs, and that these models can substantially improve RL training and transfer across multiple environments without environment-specific fine-tuning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As RL agents improve, fixed hand-designed environments and sparse rewards cease to be informative and often cause mode collapse, especially in long-horizon, tool-using tasks. There is a need for scalable, diverse, and controllable training environments. While text-based world models can simulate such environments, standard autoregressive language models are limited by left-to-right generation that makes it difficult to jointly condition on global structures like tool schemas, prior dialogue turns, and desired outcomes. The authors aim to formalize and improve text-based world modeling so that environments can be easily steered and scaled for training advanced agents.

Method: 1) Formalize text-based world modeling as a steerable transition-dynamics problem, decomposing each step into initial state, task context, tool schemas, domain rules, and steering directives. 2) Curate a large dataset of 239k grounded state-action trajectories drawn from nine open-source text environments and twelve model families. 3) Compare autoregressive language models with masked diffusion language models (MDLMs), where MDLMs perform bidirectional, anchor-aware denoising over text states instead of left-to-right generation. 4) Develop a plug-and-play GRPO-based RL training framework that uses deterministic checks on simulated states to compute rewards. 5) Evaluate zero-shot transfer to three out-of-distribution environments (ScienceWorld, ALFWorld, AppWorld) using three different agent backbones, and perform behavioral/adversarial analyses and human evaluation on realism, outcome correctness, and usefulness for training.

Result: Masked diffusion language models outperform autoregressive LMs that are up to four times larger in parameter count, providing better coherence, groundedness to tools and rules, and higher rollout diversity at similar inference cost. Using the proposed GRPO training framework with these world models yields strong zero-shot transfer: across three unseen environments and three agent backbones (1.2B–7B parameters), agents achieve up to 47 percentage points absolute improvement over baselines that lack environment-specific fine-tuning. Additional analyses show clearer behavioral failure modes under adversarial conditions and positive human judgments of the realism, correctness, and training utility of the simulated environments.

Conclusion: Steerable text-based world models built with masked diffusion language models can serve as powerful, scalable simulators for RL, overcoming key limitations of autoregressive models such as left-to-right bias and mode collapse. By structuring environment dynamics around global anchors (states, tools, rules, directives) and integrating them into a plug-and-play GRPO framework with deterministic checks, the approach enables substantial zero-shot transfer gains across diverse, previously unseen environments. The open-sourced datasets, models, and framework provide a foundation for future work on controllable, diverse, and efficient RL training via text-based simulation.

Abstract: Recent growth in reinforcement learning (RL) has surfaced a need for diverse, specialized training environments. Hand-curated environments with fixed task and reward difficulties become ineffective signals as model performance improves, and sparse rewards over long horizons induce mode collapse on specific workflows or tool structures. World models that simulate environment states have matched pure rollout performance, making them promising for scaling diversity on-demand. However, autoregressive (AR) world models suffer from a left-to-right bias preventing conditioning on globally interdependent state anchors such as tool schemas, prior turns, and expected outcomes. We (i) formalize text-based world modeling as a steerable transition-dynamics problem decomposed into initial state, task context, tool schemas, domain rules, and steering directives, and (ii) curate 239,403 grounded state-action trajectories spanning nine open-source environments and twelve frontier model families. We compare AR LMs and masked diffusion language models (MDLMs), showing MDLMs, via bidirectional anchor-aware denoising, achieve better coherence, groundedness, and empirically validated rollout diversity than LLMs over 4x their parameter size, at comparable inference latency. We introduce a plug-and-play GRPO training framework with deterministic state checks, and perform zero-shot transfer ablations on three OOD environments (ScienceWorld, ALFWorld, AppWorld) across three 1.2B-7B agent backbones (LFM2.5, Qwen3, Mistral), achieving up to 47% absolute gains over baselines without environment-specific fine-tuning. We further conduct behavioral analysis of failure modes under adversarial scenarios and human evaluation on realism, outcome correctness, and training utility. We open-source our work to encourage research in this direction.

### [65] [It Takes 8 Tokens: Weak-to-Strong Off-Policy RL via Auxiliary Branches](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16205)
*Dayu Wang,Jiaye Yang,Weikang Li,Jiahui Liang,Liwei Qian,Xin Pei,Jizhou Huang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes W2SPO, an off‑policy RL method where a weak auxiliary model injects short trajectory segments into a stronger target model’s reasoning to better explore solution space and improve verifiable-reward training efficiency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing verifiable‑reward RL methods for LLM reasoning (e.g., GRPO-style approaches) rely on contrasting multiple self-generated rollouts. On hard reasoning problems, these rollouts become semantically redundant and fall into the same incorrect reasoning basins, providing little reward contrast and limiting policy improvement. The authors aim to address this support-limited exploration bottleneck and to make reasoning RL both more effective and more computationally efficient.

Method: They introduce a weak-to-strong learning paradigm called W2SPO. A weaker, cheaper auxiliary model generates short reasoning segments (often ~8 tokens) that are inserted at intermediate states of the stronger target model’s trajectories. From these diverted states, the target model continues the reasoning to completion. The RL updates are applied only to the short inserted segments, using the final verifiable rewards (e.g., problem solved correctly or not) as feedback. This is an off-policy RL scheme, since the auxiliary model’s behavior generates the segments that are then used to train the target policy.

Result: On mathematical reasoning benchmarks with 4B-scale models, W2SPO outperforms evaluated post-trained baselines and vanilla GRPO given the same sampling budget. Specifically, under equal sampling, Pass@1 improves from 62.3% (GRPO) to 64.2%, and training is 3.55× faster, indicating both accuracy and efficiency gains.

Conclusion: Leveraging a weak auxiliary model to diversify intermediate reasoning states can alleviate the support-limited exploration problem in verifiable‑reward RL for LLMs. Short auxiliary branches effectively expand local exploration support, leading to stronger reasoning policies, better performance, and substantial training speedups compared to standard self-rollout-based methods like GRPO.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards has emerged as a standard approach for enhancing reasoning in large language models, which typically optimizes the policy by contrasting multiple self generated rollouts. However, we identify a critical support limited bottleneck in this paradigm: on challenging reasoning tasks, the target model's samples often exhibit semantic redundancy, converging into the same erroneous "reasoning basins" that offer negligible reward contrast for policy updates. In this paper, we propose to overcome this limitation through a weak to strong learning paradigm, where a policy's exploration is informed by a weaker but computationally efficient auxiliary model. We introduce W2SPO, an off policy RL method that injects short auxiliary segments often as brief as 8 tokens into intermediate target model trajectories and the target model then completes the reasoning path from these diverted states. Policy updates are restricted to these short inserted segments based on final verifiable rewards. Empirically, W2SPO achieves superior performance among evaluated 4B scale models on mathematical reasoning benchmarks, outperforming evaluated post trained baselines. Compared with vanilla GRPO under the same sampling budget, W2SPO improves Pass@1 from 62.3% to 64.2% while achieving a 3.55 times training speedup. These results suggest that weak auxiliary branches can induce stronger target reasoning policies by expanding local exploration support.

### [66] [PPO-HSC: An Exploratory Reinforcement Learning Framework Based on Wide-Area Policy Coverage Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16206)
*Yujie Shen,Haowen Chen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: An RL fine-tuning framework (PPO-HSC) for LLMs that adds a novelty-aware reward to mitigate mode collapse, increasing solution diversity without hurting accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard RL fine-tuning methods like RLVR and vanilla PPO tend to over-exploit a small set of high-reward trajectories, causing mode collapse: LLMs repeatedly generate similar solutions and lose exploratory behavior and coverage over the broader solution space. This harms reasoning robustness and creativity, especially in tasks like math and code where many distinct valid solutions exist. The paper aims to preserve and encourage exploration and diverse reasoning while still leveraging reinforcement learning from verifiable rewards.

Method: They modify PPO by adding a High-order Sampling Coverage (HSC) reward term. During training, they maintain a dynamic library of previously discovered, verified-valid trajectories (solutions). For each new trajectory, they measure its semantic similarity to the library and assign a higher reward when it is both valid and low in similarity, encouraging "low-similarity yet high-validity" reasoning. This HSC reward is differentiable and integrated into PPO’s objective, subject to a plausibility constraint that ensures generated trajectories remain structurally rational (not random or syntactically broken).

Result: On mathematical reasoning benchmarks (GSM8K, SVAMP) and code generation tasks, the method yields more diverse solutions and better coverage of the state space, while keeping or improving accuracy and syntax correctness compared with state-of-the-art RL baselines. In other words, it mitigates mode collapse without sacrificing performance on the main tasks.

Conclusion: Incorporating a novelty- and coverage-based reward (HSC) into PPO for LLM fine-tuning effectively addresses mode collapse from standard RLVR/RL procedures. PPO-HSC encourages exploration of new, valid reasoning patterns, improving diversity and coverage of solutions while maintaining or exceeding existing accuracy and syntactic quality, and offers a practical way to balance exploitation of known good solutions with structured curiosity in LLM training.

Abstract: This paper introduces PPO-HSC (Proximal Policy Optimization with High-order Sampling Coverage), an exploratory reinforcement learning framework designed to address the "Invisible Shackles" of mode collapse in Large Language Model (LLM) fine-tuning. While standard Reinforcement Learning from Verifiable Rewards (RLVR) effectively reinforces high-reward trajectories, it often leads models to over-optimize known solutions, sacrificing curiosity and the ability to explore broader solution manifolds. To overcome this, PPO-HSC incorporates a High-order Sampling Coverage (HSC) reward that incentivizes the discovery of "low-similarity yet high-validity" reasoning patterns. By maintaining a dynamic trajectory library of verified unique solutions, the framework provides a differentiable signal that rewards semantic novelty while ensuring structural rationality through a plausibility constraint. Empirical evaluations on mathematical reasoning (GSM8K, SVAMP) and code generation tasks demonstrate that PPO-HSC significantly enhances solution diversity and state-space coverage while maintaining or surpassing the accuracy and syntax integrity of state-of-the-art RL baselines.

### [67] [JUMP: Single-Pass Membership Inference on Fine-Tuned Diffusion Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16207)
*Yeachan Jun,Albert No*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes JUMP, a new single-pass membership inference attack tailored to discrete diffusion language models that significantly outperforms the prior SAMA attack while using far fewer queries.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Membership inference attacks reveal whether a specific data point was used to train a model, which is a key privacy risk. Discrete diffusion language models (dLLMs) have special any-order, parallel decoding capabilities that change the attack surface relative to autoregressive LLMs. Existing attacks like SAMA do not fully exploit these properties and are query-inefficient, motivating a method that is both more powerful and more efficient for fine-tuned dLLMs.

Method: The authors design JUMP (Joint Uncertainty-Guided Mask Probing), a scoring-based membership inference attack. First, they use the any-order property of dLLMs to identify token positions where a reference model has low confidence, since these are likely to be most informative for distinguishing members from non-members. Then, leveraging parallel decodability, they jointly mask all the selected low-confidence positions and perform a single masked prediction query to both the target and reference models. JUMP then computes a clipped reconstruction-gap statistic between target and reference token probabilities over the selected positions, using this as the membership score. This requires one selector pass and one scoring pass per model, rather than many randomized masks as in SAMA.

Result: On fine-tuned LLaDA-8B-Base models evaluated across six MIMIR benchmark domains, JUMP raises the mean ROC-AUC of membership inference from 0.82 (SAMA) to 0.90. It also yields stronger detection performance at low false-positive rates, all while using dramatically fewer queries—only a single selector and a single scoring query per model—demonstrating both improved effectiveness and efficiency over SAMA.

Conclusion: JUMP shows that the structural properties of discrete diffusion language models can be directly exploited to build stronger and more efficient membership inference attacks. By guiding mask selection with reference-model uncertainty and jointly probing those positions in one pass, JUMP outperforms prior loss-mimicking approaches like SAMA. This underscores both the heightened privacy risks for fine-tuned dLLMs and the need for defenses that account for their any-order, parallel decoding behavior.

Abstract: Membership inference attacks (MIAs) test whether a candidate example appeared in a model's training data. We study MIAs for fine-tuned discrete diffusion language models (dLLMs), where membership means inclusion in the target model's fine-tuning set. Unlike autoregressive language models, dLLMs allow an attacker to choose arbitrary mask sets and obtain token distributions for all masked positions in parallel. The prior dLLM attack, SAMA, follows a natural loss-mimicking strategy by averaging reconstruction signals over many randomly sampled masks, but it uses the any-order interface only as randomization and requires many target/reference queries. We propose JUMP (Joint Uncertainty-Guided Mask Probing), a single-pass scoring attack that exploits both distinctive properties of dLLMs: any-order decodability is used to select low-reference-confidence positions, and parallel decodability is used to score all selected positions through one joint masked query per model. JUMP masks the selected positions jointly and computes a clipped target/reference reconstruction-gap statistic. On fine-tuned LLaDA-8B-Base across six MIMIR domains, JUMP improves mean ROC-AUC from 0.82 to 0.90 over SAMA and substantially improves low-FPR detection, while requiring only one selector pass and one scoring pass through each of the target and reference models.

</details>


### [68] [ColGraphRAG: Late-Interaction Evidence Retrieval for Multimodal GraphRAG](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16208)
*Seonok Kim*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper improves multimodal question answering over graphs by swapping the image-ranking module to a late-interaction multi-vector retriever, which yields better use of visual evidence on MultimodalQA.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In graph-grounded multimodal QA, performance strongly depends on which nodes (text, tables, images) are retrieved into the reasoning graph. For images, existing systems often use bi-encoder single-vector similarity, which can miss fine-grained patch- or token-level visual details required for accurate alignment with questions. This can lead to discarding relevant visual evidence before reasoning even starts. The authors want to test whether a more expressive, fine-grained similarity model for images can improve retrieval and downstream QA, without changing the rest of the graph-based pipeline.

Method: They keep the overall graph-grounded QA pipeline fixed: offline graph construction, retrieval for text and tables, structured information extraction, and downstream reasoning. The only change is to the visual candidate-ranking step for graph-linked image nodes. Instead of using a single-vector bi-encoder similarity for images, they use a late-interaction, MaxSim-style multi-vector scoring in the spirit of ColBERT/ColPali, which preserves patch- or token-level structure. They plug this new image retriever into the existing system and evaluate its impact on retrieval metrics and QA accuracy on the MultimodalQA benchmark, comparing questions where visual evidence is crucial vs text-dominant questions.

Result: On MultimodalQA, replacing the image-ranking operator with the MaxSim-style multi-vector scorer improves retrieval metrics for graph-linked image candidates and yields gains in downstream QA accuracy. The improvements are larger on questions where visual evidence is important, while the effects on text-dominant questions are mixed (some gains, some neutral or small changes).

Conclusion: The results suggest that more expressive, late-interaction multi-vector similarity for graph-linked images helps ensure that relevant visual evidence is included in the evidence graph, leading to better QA when images matter. This provides mechanism-level evidence that visual evidence inclusion via better image retrieval is beneficial. However, the authors note that broader validation across datasets and more detailed graph-level diagnostics are needed to fully understand and generalize these findings.

Abstract: Graph-grounded multimodal question answering organizes text, tables, and images in a structured evidence graph, yet end-to-end accuracy depends on which multimodal assets are ranked highly enough to enter downstream reasoning; for graph-linked images, single-vector bi-encoder similarity can discard patch- and token-level structure needed for fine-grained alignment. We evaluate replacing the visual candidate-ranking operator over graph-linked image nodes with late-interaction MaxSim-style multi-vector scoring in the ColBERT/ColPali lineage, while keeping offline graph construction, text- and table-side retrieval, structured extraction, and downstream reasoning unchanged. On MultimodalQA, this change is associated with improved retrieval-stage point estimates for graph-linked image candidates and downstream QA gains, with larger movement where visual evidence matters most and mixed trends on text-dominant questions; we interpret the pattern as mechanism-level evidence for graph-linked visual evidence inclusion, while broader validation and finer graph-level diagnostics remain important future work.

### [69] [Shapley Context Pruning: A Cooperative Game Perspective for Context Reranking and Pruning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16209)
*Yanqiao Chen,Dongsheng Hou,Yuhan Rui,Zhen Cao,Yepang Liu*

Main category: cs.AI

Method: The authors model context selection as a cooperative game where sentences are players, and their contributions are measured via a Shapley-value-inspired value function. They use a Deep Sets architecture to approximate a permutation-invariant value function over sentence embeddings from a pre-trained language model, trained with a pairwise margin ranking loss. Monte Carlo sampling is employed to approximate Shapley-style values efficiently during training and inference, with formal error bounds and sample complexity guarantees for preserving Top-K rankings.

Result: Through experiments on supporting-sentence recall, Needle-in-a-Haystack tests, long-context QA, and multi-hop reasoning, along with ablations on embedding quality and attribution strategies, SCP shows competitive or improved downstream QA performance compared to strong baselines while enabling effective context pruning.

Conclusion: Shapley Context Pruning provides an interpretable, theoretically grounded, and scalable framework for context reranking in RAG systems. By combining Deep Sets with Monte Carlo approximations of game-theoretic importance scores, it can prune context efficiently without significantly hurting, and sometimes improving, QA performance across multiple long-context and multi-hop benchmarks.

Abstract: Context reranking and pruning have become essential for improving the efficiency of modern Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) systems, yet an interpretable and unified framework remains underexplored. Previous work has primarily emphasized lexical retrieval, cross-encoder architectures, model distillation, and Low-Rank Adaptation (LoRA), mostly relying on heuristic loss functions and empirical attribution. This paper presents Shapley Context Pruning (SCP), a novel framework for context reranking that establishes a cooperative-game-theory perspective for importance attribution by modeling the context as a cooperative game. Balancing the trade-off between fine-grained and coarse-grained representations, we employ a Deep Sets architecture to approximate a permutation-invariant value function at the sentence level, utilizing pre-trained language models as sentence embedders and optimizing via a pairwise margin ranking loss. To ensure practical scalability without sacrificing mathematical rigor, we leverage Monte-Carlo sampling for efficient training and inference, providing formal theoretical error bounds and sample complexity guarantees for preserving Top-K subset rankings. Furthermore, we conduct comprehensive experiments-spanning supporting-sentence recall, Needle-in-the-Haystack (NIAH) evaluations, long-context QA, and multi-hop reasoning-alongside rigorous ablation studies on embedding quality and attribution strategies. The model achieves competitive downstream QA performance against robust baselines.

We introduce a taxonomy that clarifies relationships among existing approaches along three axes: verification paradigm (formal versus probabilistic), temporal scope (step-wise versus multi-step), and guarantees strength. Beyond taxonomy, we unify underlying theoretical foundations, make implicit assumptions and limitations explicit, and identify emerging directions.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM agent memory systems use flat, unstructured storage and LLM-based classification, which harms multi-hop/temporal reasoning, is too slow/expensive, and allows silent accumulation of contradictory memories.

Method: MOSAIC introduces (1) entity-typed graph-based memory with semantic classification to preserve relational structure, (2) hash-accelerated dual-path retrieval using locality-sensitive hashing instead of LLM-based classification for fast lookup, and (3) active conflict detection during memory write that checks new information against graph neighbors to update or delete contradictory entries.

Abstract: LLM agents augmented with persistent memory can recall past interactions, but existing systems suffer from two limitations: flat, unstructured storage loses relational context needed for multi-hop and temporal reasoning, and reliance on expensive LLM-based classification makes them impractical for latency-sensitive deployment. Without mechanisms to validate new information against stored knowledge, these systems silently accumulate contradictions. We present MOSAIC (Memory-Organized Structured Agent for Information Collection), a structured, conflict-aware long-term memory framework for LLM agents that is substantially more accurate and efficient. MOSAIC introduces three key capabilities: (1) entity-typed graph storage with semantic classification preserving relational structure across events, personas, and relationships, enabling multi-hop and temporal reasoning over conversation history; (2) hash-accelerated dual-path retrieval replacing LLM-based classification with locality-sensitive hashing, achieving near-instantaneous lookup with negligible accuracy loss; and (3) active conflict detection at save time that cross-references new information against existing graph neighbors, triggering updates or deletions for contradictory entries. Evaluated on LoCoMo (long-conversation QA), HaluMem, and a novel clinical-guideline error compounding test, MOSAIC achieves 89.35% accuracy on LoCoMo (+27.21 pp over the best baseline), best HaluMem-Medium extraction F1(86.77%) and HaluMem-Long extraction F1 (85.84%), best QA correctness on both Medium and Long (73.10%, 70.75%), and detects 66% of injected factual conflicts-4.7 times higher than the best baseline (14%)-while hash-accelerated retrieval keeps average search latency at 0.58 s per question.

### [72] [Symbolic Augmentation Closes a Canonical-Equivalence Blind Spot in Neural Fact-Checkers](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16212)
*Genpei Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies numerical and unit-based hallucination errors in LLM-generated scientific summaries and proposes a symbolic-data augmentation method to make a neural verifier robust to different but equivalent numeric expressions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large language models often hallucinate or alter numerical values and measurement units in scientific summarization, potentially reversing the meaning of scientific claims. Existing neural fact-checkers struggle to reliably detect these typed-quantity errors, especially when numbers or units are expressed in different but physically equivalent forms.

Method: The authors define a five-class taxonomy of typed-quantity errors and build a 1500-item benchmark from PMC and arXiv texts, annotated by two LLMs with adjudication. They fine-tune a ModernBERT encoder on this benchmark and run four probing experiments that reveal poor robustness to canonical-equivalent rewrites (e.g., Celsius vs Kelvin). To address this, they introduce Symbolic Augmentation: a training-time framework that uses a symbolic verifier’s modules in reverse to generate label-preserving augmented training examples that rewrite quantities into equivalent forms. They also test adding symbolic features as encoder inputs and using symbolic silver labels generated by a teacher model.

Result: The fine-tuned ModernBERT baseline achieves strong in-distribution performance (macro-F1 = 0.899) but suffers a drastic drop in accuracy (to 36.5%) on canonical-equivalent quantity rewrites. Symbolic Augmentation dramatically improves robustness to such rewrites (to 98.2% accuracy) while slightly increasing in-distribution macro-F1 from 0.899 to 0.902. The augmented encoder matches the performance of a stronger closed-frontier LLM without additional inference cost and improves transfer to the SciFact-Open benchmark (binary macro-F1 from 0.791 to 0.828). Two negative findings are that directly feeding symbolic features into the encoder brings no gains, and scaling up training with symbolic silver labels degrades performance when teacher labels are noisy.

Conclusion: Typed-quantity hallucinations by LLMs are a serious but structurally characterizable failure mode. Training a neural verifier with Symbolic Augmentation—using symbolic systems to generate equivalence-preserving numeric and unit rewrites—substantially improves robustness to physically equivalent expressions and yields better generalization than simply injecting symbolic features or noisy symbolic labels. The work argues that the most effective integration point between symbolic and neural components is at training time via data augmentation, not at inference time or via auxiliary inputs.

Abstract: Large language models hallucinate numbers and units when summarizing scientific text, a failure mode that can silently invert a scientific claim. We recast the detection of such errors as typed verification: we introduce a five-class typed-quantity error taxonomy and a 1500-item benchmark, rewritten from PMC and arXiv sources and labeled by two independent LLM annotators with adjudication (Krippendorff's alpha = 0.882). A ModernBERT encoder fine-tuned on this benchmark reaches macro-F1 = 0.899, far above any off-the-shelf neural fact-checker, yet four probes expose a sharp structural blind spot: on canonical-equivalent rewrites of physically equivalent quantities (e.g., 95°C and 368.15 K) its accuracy collapses to 36.5%. We propose Symbolic Augmentation, a training-time framework that runs the modules of a symbolic verifier in reverse to generate label-preserving augmented training data. The augmentation lifts canonical-equivalence robustness to 98.2% while slightly improving in-distribution accuracy (macro-F1: 0.899 to 0.902); the augmented encoder matches a closed-frontier LLM at no inference cost and transfers to an external benchmark (SciFact-Open binary macro-F1: 0.791 to 0.828). Two negative results sharpen the claim: symbolic features as auxiliary encoder inputs add nothing, and symbolic silver labels scale negatively under teacher noise. Together these results identify training-time augmentation as the right integration point between symbolic and learned components.

### [73] [SelKV: Selective KV Cache Merging with Per-Token Merge-or-Drop and Attention Compensation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16213)
*Soumia Bouyahiaoui,Manel Kara laouar,Aicha Boutorh,Mohamed Hadj Ameur*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: A training-free framework compresses LLM KV caches via similarity-aware token merging plus an attention compensation bias, cutting cache to 25% with near-lossless or better performance and 3.3x speedup on long contexts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs suffer from KV cache memory and latency that grow linearly with context length, which becomes a bottleneck for long-context inference. Existing token-merging compression reduces memory but merges tokens indiscriminately, harming representation quality and causing attention sag where merged tokens get disproportionate attention mass. There is a need for a compression method that is both efficient and faithful to the model’s original behavior, without retraining.

Method: The paper introduces a dual-component, training-free KV cache compression framework. (1) A soft cosine gate uses value-vector similarity to adaptively decide which tokens to merge, suppress, or discard, thereby preserving semantic fidelity by avoiding merges of dissimilar tokens. (2) An attention-ratio compensation mechanism computes, from prefill-time attention statistics, a decoding-time logit bias that corrects the softmax imbalance created when merged tokens receive the same attention mass as unmerged tokens. The method is applied during inference only, with no model fine-tuning.

Result: On LongBench (16 English datasets), the method retains only 25% of the KV cache yet matches or surpasses representative one-shot token-merging baselines under compression. It is particularly strong on grouped-query attention (GQA) models, where it maintains near-lossless generation quality. On complex multi-document QA tasks, the compressed model even outperforms the full-cache baseline and achieves a 3.3x decoding speedup at 100k-token context lengths.

Conclusion: Similarity-aware merging plus attention-ratio compensation enables effective, training-free KV cache compression for LLMs, substantially reducing memory and improving speed without sacrificing—and sometimes improving—task performance. This shows that careful control of which tokens are merged and how their attention is rebalanced can overcome key limitations of prior indiscriminate token-merging approaches.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) generate text autoregressively, relying on a key-value (KV) cache whose memory footprint grows linearly with context length, creating a major bottleneck. Recent compression methods mitigate this cost via token merging; however, these approaches often rely on indiscriminate aggregation, which degrades representations and introduces attention sag, a mismatch where merged tokens receive the same softmax mass as individual tokens despite encoding multiple inputs. We propose a training-free, dual-component framework for KV cache compression that addresses these limitations. First, a soft cosine gate adaptively modulates merging decisions based on value-vector similarity, suppressing or discarding dissimilar tokens to preserve semantic fidelity. Second, we introduce an attention-ratio compensation mechanism that applies a decoding-time logit bias derived from prefill attention statistics, correcting the softmax imbalance induced by merging. Evaluated on LongBench (16 English datasets) while retaining only 25% of the KV cache, our framework achieves strong compressed performance against representative one-shot baselines. It is especially robust on the evaluated grouped-query attention (GQA) models, maintaining nearlossless generation quality. Furthermore, the method outperforms the full-cache baseline on complex multi-document QA tasks and delivers a 3.3x decoding speedup at 100k tokens.

### [74] [RAIL Guard: Closing the Evaluation-to-Remediation Gap in Responsible AI for LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16215)
*Sumit Verma,Pritam Prasun,Pritish Kumar*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose RAIL Guard, a closed-loop guardrail system for LLM agents that iteratively fixes unsafe or low-quality outputs instead of just blocking them, significantly improving safety convergence with manageable utility trade-offs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current guardrail systems mostly act as binary blockers: they classify content as safe or unsafe and reject unsafe generations. This wastes computation, forces users to restart from scratch, and does not help repair otherwise useful but flawed outputs. Organizations need more nuanced, measurable, and repair-oriented mechanisms that can both evaluate and remediate LLM outputs across multiple responsible-AI dimensions, including for tool-using agents.

Method: They design RAIL Guard, a responsible AI pipeline that evaluates LLM outputs along eight explicit dimensions and then enters an evaluate–rewrite–reevaluate loop for any failing dimensions. They test multiple remediation strategies (e.g., closed-loop automatic correction, block-and-retry, feedback-driven self-repair) with four frontier LLMs. They run three experiments: large-scale evaluation on 4,276 text outputs; 6,400 agent tool-call scenarios with pre-tool-call checks; and analysis of which dimensions are inherently fixable vs. structurally constrained (e.g., transparency, accountability, inclusivity).

Result: Closed-loop remediation reaches 96.9% convergence on required safety/quality thresholds versus 49.1% for a simpler block-and-retry baseline, though the strongest method reduces task utility by 22.3%. A lighter feedback-driven self-repair approach attains 86.6% convergence on dimensions that are actually fixable, with no statistically significant reduction in utility (p=0.177). Pre-tool-call evaluation reduces unsafe tool executions in agents by 33% (p=0.007) with no reduction in overall task completion. They also empirically show that some dimensions (transparency, accountability, inclusivity) frequently fail and do not improve much under remediation, implying the need for deeper architectural changes.

Conclusion: Iterative, closed-loop guardrails that can both evaluate and repair LLM outputs perform substantially better than binary block-and-retry systems, especially for agentic tool use, while retaining most utility when designed carefully. However, some responsible-AI dimensions appear structurally difficult to fix through post-hoc rewriting alone, suggesting that model and system architecture changes are necessary. RAIL Guard operationalizes this insight and is released as open-source SDKs for practical deployment.

Abstract: Existing guardrail systems for large language model agents operate as binary classifiers that block unsafe content, leaving organizations to discard failing outputs and retry from scratch. We introduce RAIL Guard, a closed-loop responsible AI pipeline that evaluates LLM outputs across eight measurable dimensions and iteratively remediates failing outputs through an evaluate-rewrite-reevaluate loop. We evaluate the pipeline across three experiments on four frontier LLMs and 4,276 content outputs plus 6,400 agent tool-call scenarios. Closed-loop remediation achieves 96.9% convergence versus 49.1% for block-and-retry, though the highest-convergence method reduces utility by 22.3%; feedback-driven self-repair achieves 86.6% convergence on fixable dimensions with no significant utility loss (p = 0.177). Pre-tool-call evaluation reduces unsafe agent executions by 33% (p = 0.007) with zero impact on task completion. We identify a key distinction between fixable dimensions that respond to remediation and structural dimensions (Transparency at 93.0%, Accountability at 92.8%, and Inclusivity at 82.5% failure) that require architectural rather than algorithmic solutions. The system is available as open-source SDKs.

### [75] [Generalist AI Control: Towards Multi-purpose Adaptive Algorithms](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16313)
*Klinsmann Agyei,Pouria Sarhadi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a single neural-network-based “generalist controller” that can control many different SISO dynamical systems (with different orders and dynamics) as well as system-specific LQI controllers, and even generalizes to new conditions not seen in training.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Conventional control design requires separate, system-specific controllers, often redesigned when system order, dynamics, or operating conditions change. This is inefficient and doesn’t scale to large families of systems. The authors aim to build a single, reusable controller that can handle many different dynamical systems without per-system tuning or architectural changes, moving towards more general-purpose control policies.

Method: They design a learning-based controller built on a novel dynamic state-space representation implemented via attention mechanisms with masking, so the same network can process states of different dimensions. Systems are identified by assigning a system tag, allowing one shared model for multiple plants. Training uses 314,630 simulated demonstrations from 25 diverse SISO systems (stable/unstable, minimum-/non-minimum-phase, linear/nonlinear, and from different application domains). The architecture uses multi-scale temporal processing and a mixture-of-experts module to learn cross-system control strategies in a single shot (joint training across all systems).

Result: In simulations, the single generalist controller matches the performance of dedicated LQI controllers across all tested systems, including difficult non-minimum-phase and unstable systems. It also generalizes to conditions not present in the training data, such as actuator saturation, noise, disturbances, and new reference trajectories.

Conclusion: A single learned “generalist controller” can effectively control a broad set of SISO dynamical systems with varying orders and dynamics, without system-specific design or tuning. The work shows that attention-based, masked state-space representations plus mixture-of-experts can produce robust, cross-system control policies that approach classical LQI performance and remain effective under unseen operating conditions, marking meaningful progress toward general-purpose control within a defined family of systems.

Abstract: Traditional controllers are designed for specific systems and do not transfer across different system orders and dynamics. We present a Generalist Controller, a learning-based controller capable of controlling systems of varying orders and dynamics. The approach introduces a novel dynamic state-space representation using attention mechanisms with masking, enabling a single neural network, trained in one shot, to handle systems with different dimensions without architectural modifications by assigning a system tag to each system. We generated 314,630 demonstrations from 25 diverse systems, including stable, unstable, minimum-phase, and non-minimum-phase dynamics, spanning linear and nonlinear systems from autonomous underwater and aerospace vehicles to mechanical systems and chemical processes. The model learns cross-system control strategies through multi-scale temporal processing and a mixture-of-experts architecture. Simulation results demonstrate that the proposed generalist controller achieves comparable performance to system-specific LQI controllers across all tested systems, including challenging cases such as non-minimum-phase and unstable dynamics, whilst generalising to unseen operating conditions including actuator saturation, noise, disturbance, and reference trajectories not encountered during training. This work represents a significant step towards generalist control policies within a defined family of dynamical systems, demonstrating effective control across a range of single-input single-output (SISO) systems of varying order and dynamics using a single learned policy without system-specific tuning.

### [76] [LaCache: Exact Caching and Precision-Adaptive Inference for Diffusion Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16339)
*Xingru Chen,Zelang Liang,Yongjia Ma,Jiqing Zhan,Shuling Yang,Lian Wen,Kun Zhan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: LaCache is a training-free acceleration framework for diffusion-based LLMs that removes redundant computation via caching and FP8 mixed precision, yielding large speedups without hurting accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Semi-autoregressive decoding in diffusion-based LLMs recomputes the whole sequence at each denoising step, even though large parts of the sequence (prefix and masked suffix) stay unchanged within a block. This operator-level redundancy leads to unnecessary computation and latency, limiting the practical efficiency and scalability of DLLMs. The paper aims to reduce this redundancy without retraining or sacrificing output quality.

Method: The authors introduce LaCache, a training-free acceleration framework composed of two main components. First, Lossless State Memoization (LSM) stores and reuses intermediate states that do not change across denoising steps: (i) EmbedCache for token embedding outputs, (ii) RoPECache for token-wise rotary positional encoding pre-attention states, and (iii) FACache for online softmax statistics within FlashAttention. These caches allow the model to skip recomputation for tokens whose states are invariant within a SAR block. Second, to address the memory-bandwidth overhead introduced by caching and to speed up computation further, they design a per-group FP8 quantization scheme for FFN layers. This scheme is adapted to the step-dependent activation distributions that arise during the diffusion process, enabling aggressive mixed-precision compression while keeping numerical behavior close to full precision.

Result: On DLLM benchmarks, LaCache alone yields about 1.3× end-to-end speedup over the baseline diffusion LLM with standard SAR decoding, without degrading accuracy. When LaCache is integrated with other existing DLLM acceleration approaches, the combined system achieves up to 40.2× end-to-end speedup while preserving task performance at a level comparable to the original model.

Conclusion: Loss-aware caching of invariant intermediate states plus carefully designed FP8 mixed precision can substantially reduce redundant computation in diffusion-based LLMs with no retraining and minimal accuracy impact. LaCache demonstrates that operator-level memoization and quantization, tailored to the diffusion process, can significantly improve practical inference efficiency and can be complementary to other acceleration techniques.

Abstract: Diffusion-based Large Language Models(DLLMs) enable parallel generation via Semi-Autoregressive (SAR) decoding in text generation. However, current methods suffer from severe operator-level redundancy: they recompute the entire sequence during denoising steps, ignoring that the prefix and masked suffix remain invariant within a block. We propose LaCache, a training-free acceleration framework that alleviates this redundancy through lossless caching and mixed precision. Specifically, LaCache employs Lossless State Memoization (LSM) by caching three types of intermediate results: (i) EmbedCache for embedding outputs, (ii) RoPECache for token-wise pre-attention states, and (iii) FACache for the online softmax statistics within FlashAttention. These caches allow the model to skip redundant computation on unchanged tokens without altering the output. To further alleviate memory-bandwidth bottlenecks, LaCache inegrates a per-group FP8 quantization strategy for FFN layers, tailored to step-dependent activation distributions across the diffusion process. Experiments demonstrate that LaCache alone achieves approximately 1.3X end-to-end speedup over vanilla DLLM. When combined with existing acceleration methods, LaCache reaches up to 40.2X end-to-end speedup while maintaining comparable task accuracy.

### [77] [Interactive Task Alignment as a POMDP](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16412)
*Andy Dai,Zexue He,Zhenyu Zhang,Alex Pentland,Jiaxin Pei*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces and evaluates a benchmark and framework for measuring how well language models identify and align with users’ true intended tasks when requests are ambiguous, showing that current models significantly underperform humans.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing benchmarks mostly test language models on clear, fully specified instructions, but real users often have vague, evolving, or inconsistent goals. This gap means we lack rigorous ways to measure and improve how models handle ambiguity and negotiate task understanding with users. The authors aim to formalize and benchmark this “task alignment” ability to better reflect real-world assistant use.

Method: The authors (1) define task alignment as inferring a latent user task from partial, evolving signals and formalize it as a POMDP; (2) propose a general framework that converts standard fully specified tasks into underspecified, multi-turn interactions using a user simulator; (3) validate the simulator against real human behavior through a user study; (4) instantiate this across domains such as shopping, coding, and professional work; (5) systematically evaluate multiple language models on these interactions; and (6) study the impact of post-training methods like supervised fine-tuning and reinforcement learning on task alignment metrics.

Result: Across domains, language models frequently mis-handle ambiguous user requests: they start executing tasks too early, fail to ask useful clarifying questions, and do not successfully converge on the user’s true goal. Under task ambiguity, models correctly recover the user’s intended task only 22–32% of the time, whereas humans reach about 48% in comparable settings. Post-training with supervised fine-tuning and reinforcement learning improves performance but does not close the gap with human behavior.

Conclusion: Task alignment—resolving ambiguity and converging on a user’s real goal through interaction—is a distinct capability where current language models are substantially weaker than humans. Even strong post-trained models remain limited in their ability to manage uncertainty, ask clarifying questions, and avoid premature action, indicating that better interaction-oriented training and evaluation are needed for reliable AI assistants and agents.

Abstract: Current benchmarks for language models primarily evaluate execution on fully specified tasks. However, real user tasks are often ambiguous. Users arrive with incomplete, exploratory, or even inconsistent goals, requiring the assistant to first determine the intended task before carrying it out. We study this problem as task alignment: the ability to align with a user on their intended task. We introduce a general framework for converting specified tasks into underspecified interactions, formalized as a POMDP in which the model must infer a latent task from partial and evolving user intent. We validate our user simulator post hoc with a human user study. Across shopping, coding, and professional work settings, we find that while models often perform well once the task is specified, models still struggle with task alignment: current models act prematurely, interact ineffectively, and fail to resolve ambiguous requests. Models on average recover the user's intended task only 22-32% of the time under ambiguity. In a human study in the same setting, humans reach 48%, outperforming all evaluated models. We show that post-training with supervised fine-tuning and reinforcement learning improves task alignment, but models still lag behind humans in resolving uncertainty through interaction. Together, our results suggest that current models still lack key interaction abilities required for reliable agency.

### [78] [When to Plan: Learning to Select Between Reactive Control and Deliberative Planning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16421)
*Adam Labiosa,Josiah P. Hanna*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a reinforcement-learning-based meta-reasoning policy that decides when to use a fast reactive controller versus slower model-based planning based on the reactive policy’s uncertainty, and show it works well on motion planning and navigation tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Humans flexibly switch between fast, habitual reactions and slower, deliberative planning, but most artificial agents either rely purely on reactive policies that can fail out-of-distribution, or on computationally expensive planning used uniformly. There is a need for agents that can autonomously decide when to react quickly and when to invest extra computation in planning, so they can be both efficient and robust. The authors aim to learn this meta-reasoning ability rather than hand-designing heuristics for when to plan.

Method: They formalize reactive control as a policy mapping observations directly to actions, trained with RL or imitation learning. They then define an uncertainty score for this reactive policy and use it as input to a learned meta-reasoning policy trained via reinforcement learning. At each decision point, the meta-policy chooses whether to act using the cheap reactive controller or to invoke more expensive decision-time, model-based planning. The learning objective balances task performance with the computational cost (latency) of planning. The approach is evaluated in motion planning and navigation domains.

Result: In navigation and motion planning environments, the learned meta-reasoning policy successfully uses the reactive policy when it is confident and effective, and calls the planner mainly in states where the reactive policy is likely to fail. This yields agents that maintain strong performance while reducing planning calls compared with always-planning baselines, and are more robust than purely reactive agents. As the reactive policy becomes stronger over training, the meta-policy automatically shifts behavior to rely more heavily on reactive control.

Conclusion: Learning a meta-reasoning policy conditioned on reactive-policy uncertainty allows agents to adaptively allocate computation between fast reactive control and slower planning. This leads to efficient and robust decision-making that can naturally transition toward mostly reactive behavior as the underlying reactive policy improves. The work demonstrates a practical way to endow RL agents with a learned analogue of human-like switching between habitual and deliberative control.

Abstract: It has long been recognized that humans have the ability to switch between fast, reactive decision-making and slower, deliberative planning. In this paper, we study the question of how to learn this ability, known as meta-reasoning, in artificial agents. We model reactive decision-making as a policy that directly maps state observations to actions. Such policies can be trained with reinforcement learning (RL) or imitation learning, but may generalize poorly outside of their training distribution. Alternatively, model-based decision-time planning is more likely to produce good actions across a broader set of states but requires additional computation time, which delays acting. In this work, we introduce an RL method for training a meta-reasoning policy that allocates computation by conditioning on a reactive-policy uncertainty score. This score enables it to predict when the reactive policy is likely to perform poorly and when planning is needed. We conduct an empirical study on motion planning and navigation environments, showing that this design enables the meta-reasoning policy to learn when the reactive policy provides a good-enough action versus when decision-time planning is needed. Additionally, we show that our design enables the meta-agent to shift toward fully reactive control as the reactive policy improves.

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To reassess Berkeley’s historical role in computing and AI by showing that his real ambition was to use symbolic logic as an engineering language for machines that sense, remember, act over time, and adapt to their environment, and to connect this with Heiserman’s later work as a still-promising but under-explored architecture for robotic cognition.

Method: Conceptual and historical analysis of Berkeley’s writings and projects—especially his book "Symbolic Logic and Intelligent Machines"—reading them as a coherent program that links logical form, digital circuitry, control mechanisms, and intelligent machine behavior, and then comparing and extending this line of thought with David L. Heiserman’s designs for adaptive, memory-based robot architectures.

Result: The analysis shows that Berkeley systematically defined intelligent machines operationally (via inputs, outputs, memory, control, states, events) and grounded symbolic logic in hardware realizations and temporally extended, environment-coupled behavior, rather than in purely abstract symbol manipulation. It also shows that Heiserman’s work continues this trajectory, emphasizing adaptive, creature-like robots built on memory, confidence measures, and generalization mechanisms.

Conclusion: Berkeley’s and Heiserman’s contributions should be reinterpreted not as merely historical curiosities but as articulations of an architectural paradigm for embodied robotic cognition, in which symbolic logic functions as a design language tightly linked to hardware, control, and adaptive behavior. This paradigm remains under-tested and could inform contemporary research on robot architectures that integrate logical structure with embodied, temporal, and environmental engagement.

Abstract: Edmund C. Berkeley is usually remembered as a writer who helped connect symbolic logic to computing machinery. That description is correct, but incomplete. Read across Berkeley's machine-oriented writings and projects, the central concern is broader: to show that symbolic logic can serve as a practical design language for machines that acquire information, retain it, respond appropriately to changing conditions, and organize their behavior over time. This paper takes Symbolic Logic and Intelligent Machines as the principal text in that effort, while treating it as representative of a larger Berkeley program that links logical form, circuitry, control, and intelligent behavior. On this reading, Berkeley does not merely treat intelligence as abstract symbol manipulation. He repeatedly defines intelligent machines in operational terms, ties logic to hardware realization, and describes machine behavior through the coordinated interaction of inputs, outputs, memory, calculation, control, states, and events. His treatment of robots and machine activities accordingly moves beyond static logical form toward temporally extended, environment-coupled behavior. David L. Heiserman's machine-intelligence work extends this program toward adaptive creature architectures built around memory, confidence, and generalization. Taken together, Berkeley and Heiserman can be read not as exhausted historical episodes but as contributors to a still under-tested architectural approach to embodied robotic cognition.

### [80] [SEER: Supervised Learning to Control Energetic Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16523)

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Constraint Programming relies on propagation to prune the search space, but stronger propagation often requires much more computation. The best propagator for a given constraint and instance can vary and may change during search, making static or purely dynamic heuristics insufficient. There is a need for a smarter way to decide when the cost of a powerful propagator is justified by its pruning benefit.

Method: They propose an approach that uses a machine-learned oracle function to decide, at run time, whether to execute a complex propagator for a given constraint. They design the oracle to be flexible and easily embeddable in modern solvers. Concretely, they concentrate on the Energetic Reasoning propagator as a case study, define classification features, train ML models, and empirically evaluate prediction accuracy and behavior within the solver.

Result: Experiments show that the learned oracle can achieve high prediction accuracy in deciding when to run Energetic Reasoning. The study identifies effective classification features and uncovers practical challenges and pitfalls in training and deploying such an oracle in a solver context.

Conclusion: It is feasible to construct a machine-learning-based oracle to control the use of expensive propagators such as Energetic Reasoning. The approach can be integrated into existing solvers and has the potential to maintain strong pruning while reducing computational overhead, though several highlighted issues must be addressed for robust, real-world deployment.

Abstract: One of the main strengths of Constraint Programming is the ability to reduce the search space via propagation. However, propagation is a double-edged sword, with more pruning power coming at the price of larger computation time. For each problem constraint, the best propagator depends on the specific instance and may change at search time. In the literature, Machine Learning (ML) techniques and activity-based heuristics have been applied respectively for choosing (statically) the propagators for a batch of problems and to adapt (dynamically) the propagation strength. We propose to merge those efforts by using an oracle function, obtained via ML, to decide whether to run complex propagators for a target constraint. A combination of design choices makes the approach flexible and easy to embed in state-of-the-art solvers. In this paper, we focus on investigating the feasibility of building an oracle for the Energetic Reasoning propagator. Our experiments show that high prediction accuracy can be obtained, provide suggestions for classification features, and highlight important issues to address when building such an oracle.

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how to optimally schedule human oversight within long, multi-step AI workflows and proposes a principle for where to place review stages.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a tension between the need for human oversight to ensure quality and the desire to leverage AI’s efficiency. The authors observed that well-placed human oversight in long workflows can improve satisfaction and reduce rework and token use, but there is little formal understanding of how to place these oversight points optimally.

Method: The authors formalize the problem of scheduling human oversight stages within a multi-step AI workflow. They introduce assumptions about costs, error dynamics, and intervention effects, and then analytically derive a scheduling principle—called the nonuniformity principle—characterizing optimal oversight placement. They then test this principle empirically in two representative AI agent workflows: AI-assisted literature review writing and AI-based website construction.

Result: The main theoretical result is the nonuniformity principle: in an optimal schedule, the gaps between oversight stages are non-decreasing along the workflow (i.e., oversight is denser earlier and becomes sparser later, or at least not denser later than earlier). Empirically, they show that schedules following this principle lead to higher user satisfaction and reduced unnecessary rework and token consumption in literature review writing and website building tasks compared to alternative oversight patterns.

Conclusion: Optimal human-AI coworking in long AI workflows should use strategically placed, non-uniform oversight rather than evenly spaced or ad hoc reviews. Placing more frequent human checks earlier and allowing longer gaps later can balance quality assurance with efficiency, improving outcomes while conserving human time and computational resources. The nonuniformity principle offers a general guideline for designing oversight schedules in AI agent systems across tasks.

Abstract: As generative AI is increasingly applied to automate multi-step and high-stake workflows, human judgment and involvement remain essential for ensuring the quality of AI-generated outputs. In practice, while it is desirable for human experts to provide oversight on AI regularly, often by reviewing intermediate outputs, giving feedback, making corrections, and steering subsequent steps, such oversight is constrained by the time and resources that humans can afford. This creates a tension between the need for human oversight and AI's efficiency in delivering more output with less intervention. An important but underexplored question, then, is how to optimally engage humans in human-AI coworking. This work was originally motivated by our empirical observation that in long AI workflows, human oversight often improves user satisfaction while reducing unnecessary rework and token consumption. From there, we formulate the problem of where to place oversight stages in human-AI coworking. Under reasonable assumptions, we then develop the nonuniformity principle, which states that the optimal schedule places oversight stages with non-decreasing gaps along the workflow. We empirically validate this principle in two common AI agent workflows: writing literature reviews and constructing websites.

A global semantic codebook unifies these into a shared vocabulary of canonical atomic propositions, placing every modality and observation into one interpretable space that spans fine grained facts to high level concepts and composes into richer ones. This brings interpretability with reasoning, cross-modal understanding and retrieval, and compositionality that enables complex multimodal understanding, rich data curation and complex structured retrieval. ### [83] [Exact Network Surgery: Functional Invariance and Gradient Plasticity in Reactive Computational Graphs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16568)
*Abdallah Khemais*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose and validate an exact, in-place method to insert residual blocks into a running neural network without changing its behavior, while keeping new parameters trainable.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing function-preserving network growth methods like Net2Net or progressive stacking either accept small numerical deviations or require rewriting and retraining the whole program/graph. There is a need for a principled way to grow networks during training, in-place, with bit-exact function preservation and immediate trainability of new components, suitable for reactive/dynamic graph systems.

Method: They formalize "Exact Network Surgery" for inserting gated residual blocks into a live computational graph. They introduce and analyze Gradient Shadowing gates (parameter alpha) initialized at zero on a new randomly initialized branch. They prove three main results: (1) an identity-morphism theorem ensuring the gated residual block behaves exactly like the identity map at insertion under certain floating-point assumptions; (2) a structural-locality theorem for a reactive invalidation engine that guarantees only the downstream cone of the insertion point is recomputed, preserving all other node values and optimizer states; and (3) an escape-from-initialization proposition showing the zero-initialized gate still receives non-zero gradient at insertion, allowing the new branch to start learning. They also characterize a degenerate saddle configuration (zero output projection plus zero gate) where gradients are identically zero. They implement these ideas in NeuroDSL, a reactive Julia-based computation graph engine, and run controlled experiments to test numerical exactness, gradient behavior, cost scaling, and reproducibility across restarts.

Result: Empirically, network grafting via Exact Network Surgery is bit-exact for all tested logits (0 mismatches out of 1600). The Gradient Shadowing gate alpha moves away from zero at the first optimizer step, and branch gradients become active at the second step, matching theoretical predictions. The intentionally degenerate configuration shows gradients exactly zero for 600 steps, confirming the saddle-point analysis. The computational cost of surgery scales linearly with the size of the downstream cone (correlation r = 0.9992), while the overhead of graft-plus-invalidation bookkeeping remains nearly constant (~0.75 ms) regardless of insertion depth. Training continues in a way that is bit-identical even across an actual process restart.

Conclusion: Exact Network Surgery enables in-place, bit-exact insertion of residual blocks into live neural networks while preserving both function and optimizer state, and ensuring the new parameters can start learning immediately except in a clearly characterized degenerate case. Their NeuroDSL implementation empirically confirms the theoretical guarantees on numerical exactness, locality of recomputation, gradient behavior, scalability of surgery cost, and deterministic resumption. This provides a rigorous foundation for dynamic, structure-changing training workflows in reactive graph systems.

Abstract: Function-preserving network growth techniques such as Net2Net and progressive stacking expand a model's capacity without destroying its learned function, but existing formulations either tolerate numerical perturbations or require a full rebuild of the training program. We formalize Exact Network Surgery: the in-place insertion of a residual block into a live computational graph such that (i) the network function is preserved -- bit-exactly under explicit floating-point hypotheses -- and (ii) inserted parameters remain trainable immediately after insertion. We prove an identity-morphism theorem for gated residual blocks, a structural-locality theorem showing that a reactive invalidation engine recomputes exactly the downstream cone of the insertion point, leaving every other node's value and optimizer state untouched, and an escape-from-initialization proposition showing that the Gradient Shadowing gate alpha, initialized at zero over a randomly initialized branch, receives a generically non-zero gradient at insertion time. We identify a degenerate configuration -- zero-initialized output projections combined with a zero gate -- that is an exact saddle point gradient descent cannot escape. Every claim is validated on the reference implementation in NeuroDSL, a reactive graph engine in Julia: grafting is bit-exact on every logit tested (0 mismatches out of 1600); the gate escapes zero at the first optimizer step and unlocks branch gradients at the second, exactly as predicted; the degenerate configuration exhibits gradients identically zero for the entire 600-step run; surgery cost tracks downstream cone size with r = 0.9992 while graft-plus-invalidation bookkeeping is constant (about 0.75 ms) across insertion depths; and training resumes bit-identically across a real process restart. A flagged preliminary appendix reports first single-seed observations on post-insertion gate dynamics.

### [85] [Just A Rather Very Intelligent Spoken Agent](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16610)
*Chen Chen,Zhehuai Chen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduces JarvisBench, a benchmark for evaluating always-on, Jarvis-style mediators that sit between users and long-horizon AI agents, focusing on both task performance and user interaction quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long-horizon agents can handle complex workflows but offer poor, opaque interaction: users give a single initial instruction and then receive sparse, selective updates, making it unclear what the agent is doing or when to intervene. There is no standard way to keep the agent continuously reachable and explainable to users. The authors aim to fill this gap by defining and measuring the value of an always-on mediating layer that enables real-time, trace-grounded, user-friendly oversight and guidance.

Method: They propose JarvisBench, a benchmark with two tracks: (1) an agent-collaboration track that measures how mediation affects downstream task completion, and (2) a user-interaction track that measures understandability, responsiveness, and accessibility of ongoing execution. They instantiate this benchmark with a modular reference “Jarvis” mediator that supports real-time interaction and is evaluated on 34 text-only WildClaw tasks run in the OpenClaw environment, using various LLMs as worker agents and as the mediator’s core model.

Result: Experiments with several state-of-the-art LLM worker agents (GPT-5.5, Claude Opus 4.7, Gemini-based, and GPT-based) show that Jarvis-style mediation can give trace-grounded answers to user queries during execution and can improve task performance when user guidance is injected at well-chosen points. Performance, however, varies significantly with the choice of LLM used as the mediator brain.

Conclusion: An always-on mediation layer can materially improve both transparency and performance in long-horizon agent workflows, but its effectiveness is highly dependent on the underlying LLM. JarvisBench offers a structured way for the community to study and improve this “missing middle layer” between users and autonomous agents, motivating further research on mediator architectures and training strategies.

Abstract: Long-horizon AI agents are becoming increasingly capable, yet their interaction with users remains surprisingly thin. In most workflows, users give an initial instruction, receive only selective textual updates, and lose a clear sense of what the agent is doing or when to step in. This leaves a missing part in the current agent ecosystem: an always-on Jarvis-style mediator that keeps the agent continuously reachable to the user. Such a mediator should support real-time spoken interaction with the user, answer questions without interrupting the worker, proactively report progress or confusion, and inject user guidance back into the agent's execution when useful. In this work, we introduce JarvisBench, a benchmark for measuring the dual value of mediation in long-horizon agent workflows. JarvisBench contains two complementary tracks: an agent-collaboration track that measures whether mediation improves downstream task completion, and a user-interaction track that measures whether mediation makes ongoing execution more understandable, responsive, and accessible to users. We instantiate the benchmark with a modular reference Jarvis prototype and evaluate it on 34 text-only WildClaw tasks executed in OpenClaw. Preliminary results with GPT-5.5, Claude Opus 4.7, Gemini-based, and GPT-based worker agents suggest that Jarvis-style mediation can provide trace-grounded responses to user questions and improve task performance when sparse user guidance is injected at appropriate moments. The results also show that effectiveness depends strongly on the mediator's LLM brain, highlighting both the promise of this missing middle layer and the need for broader community effort. Demo page https://cchen1436.github.io/jarvis

Method: The authors build a modular pipeline where: (1) a Text-Aligned Neural Surrogate (TANS) uses cross-attention to align clinical text embeddings with a continuous 3D lattice-density field that defines the orthosis geometry; (2) a Graph Neural Network surrogate predicts plantar stress in real time as a stand-in for computationally expensive Finite Element Analysis; and (3) the system is trained/anchored on the PicoFoot-5K anthropometric dataset to ensure anatomical plausibility and fit. A Text-Aligned Neural Surrogate (TANS) uses cross-attention to project clinical-text embeddings onto a continuous lattice-density field, while a Graph Neural Network (GNN) surrogate predicts plantar stress in real time as a substitute for Finite Element Analysis (FEA). ### [87] [TopoTuner: Topological Finetuning of Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16637)
*Abdulkadir Erol,Yash Mahajan,Vepaul Hariprashad,Baha Rababah,Santu Karmaker,Cuneyt G. Akcora,Mubarak Shah*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: TopoTuner is a topology-guided fine-tuning method that selectively freezes attention projection matrices in LLMs, achieving performance comparable to full fine-tuning while updating only 1–2% of parameters and outperforming LoRA in most settings.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Full fine-tuning of large language models is effective but computationally expensive because it updates all parameters. Parameter-efficient methods like LoRA reduce trainable parameters but do not explicitly determine which pretrained components should be updated versus frozen. There is also a desire for reusable adaptation patterns that can transfer across tasks and datasets. The paper aims to systematically identify which attention projections actually need updating, using a principled geometric/topological criterion, and to reuse this knowledge across domains.

Method: The authors propose TopoTuner, which views each attention projection matrix as a set of row vectors (a row cloud) and applies tools from topological data analysis. They compute persistence diagrams for these row clouds and use Wasserstein distances between diagrams to capture how the topology of each projection changes during fine-tuning. Based on the observed task-specific topological drift on a source dataset, TopoTuner learns a freezing profile that specifies which projection matrices should be updated and which can be frozen. This profile is then transferred to new, out-of-domain datasets for efficient fine-tuning across tasks such as question answering and sentiment analysis.

Result: On LLaMA-3.1-8B, Mistral-7B-v0.3, and Qwen3-8B-Base, TopoTuner achieves performance competitive with full fine-tuning while updating only 1–2% of the model parameters. It outperforms LoRA in 7 out of 9 model–dataset combinations, despite potentially modifying up to 39.57% of projection parameters when needed. In addition, it reduces training time by about 20.4% compared to full fine-tuning and 5.5% compared to LoRA on average.

Conclusion: TopoTuner demonstrates that topology-guided selective freezing of attention projection matrices can provide a parameter-efficient alternative to full fine-tuning, often surpassing LoRA while updating far fewer parameters. The success of transferring freezing profiles across tasks suggests that task-induced topological drift in attention projections is reusable, opening a new line of research on sharing fine-tuning behaviors and profiles between different datasets and tasks.

Abstract: Full fine-tuning remains a strong way to adapt pretrained LLMs, but it updates all weights and can be expensive. LoRA reduces the number of trainable parameters, but it does not directly answer which pretrained components should be trained and which can be frozen during adaptation. We introduce TopoTuner, a topology-guided fine-tuning framework for selective freezing of attention projection matrices. \method treats each projection matrix as a row cloud and uses Wasserstein distances between persistence diagrams to measure how its topology changes during fine-tuning.
  TopoTuner learns a reusable freezing profile from a source dataset and transfers it to efficiently fine-tune models on out-of-domain datasets, evaluating whether task-specific topological drift generalizes across question answering and sentiment analysis tasks.
  Across LLaMA-3.1-8B, Mistral-7B-v0.3, and Qwen3-8B-Base, TopoTuner is competitive with full fine-tuning while training only 1-2\% of the model parameters, and outperforms LoRA in 7 out of 9 model-dataset settings, which can change up to 39.57\% of the projection parameters. Along with minimized updates, TopoTuner reduces training time by 20.4\% relative to full fine-tuning and 5.5\% relative to LoRA on average. TopoTuner opens a new direction for reusable freezing profiles, where fine-tuning behavior learned on one dataset can be shared across multiple tasks.
