<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most hallucination detection methods work only at inference time and struggle when the model produces the same wrong answer consistently, causing low semantic entropy and undetected hallucinations. The authors want a training-time solution that makes hallucinations more detectable without changing the detection algorithm itself.

Method: They propose diversity-oriented fine-tuning strategies aimed at increasing generative diversity, particularly for hallucinated outputs, so semantic-entropy-based detectors get higher entropy signals. They implement two variants: one via Supervised Fine-Tuning (SFT) and one via Direct Preference Optimization (DPO), and run extensive experiments comparing model behavior pre- and post-fine-tuning.

Result: After applying their fine-tuning methods, models are less likely to produce low semantic-entropy responses when they hallucinate, which improves the recall/effectiveness of semantic-entropy-based hallucination detection. Performance becomes better than or comparable to state-of-the-art detection approaches.

Conclusion: Diversity-oriented fine-tuning is an effective way to make hallucinations more detectable by existing semantic-entropy-based methods, yielding improved detection without requiring architectural changes and achieving competitive or superior performance to prior work.

Abstract: Existing hallucination detection methods are typically conducted at the inference stage, without making any modifications to the model itself. In this paper, we are interested in exploring fine-tuning strategies that enhance the detectability of hallucinations in the resulting model. Focusing on semantic-entropy-based detection, we observe that many erroneous outputs remain undetected because the model produces nearly identical incorrect answers across multiple runs. To address this, we propose diversity-oriented fine-tuning to encourage more varied generations. We introduce two specific strategies: one based on Supervised Fine-Tuning (SFT) and the other on Direct Preference Optimization (DPO). Extensive experiments are conducted to evaluate our approach and analyze the behavior of the models before and after fine-tuning. We find that after adopting our fine-tuning methods, the models become less likely to produce low semantic entropy responses for hallucinated answers, thereby improving the effectiveness of hallucination detection, eventually yielding results better than or comparable with state of the art methods. The code will be publicly released.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To participate in eRisk 2026 Task 1, which evaluates systems that conversationally assess depression severity in LLM personas without asking direct, sensitive questions, and to test the hypothesis that algorithmic supervision can allow a weaker open-source model to match or outperform a stronger paid model in this setting.

Method: The authors design a three-stage pipeline evolution: (1) a monolithic single-model prototype doing both interviewing and scoring; (2) a multi-agent architecture that separates the conversational interviewer from a BDI-II scoring agent, coordinated by an orchestration layer; and (3) a hybrid system that swaps the paid GPT-5-nano interviewer for an open-source Gemma 27B model. To compensate for Gemma's weaker reasoning and instruction-following, they add (a) a precomputed dialogue tree to standardize interview openings and follow-ups, (b) a reliability-weighted consensus aggregation mechanism inspired by the Weaver framework to combine multiple assessments, and (c) a cluster-based imputation procedure to infer unasked symptoms. They run this pipeline in three fully automated submissions over 20 personas.

Result: They submit three runs: Run 1 (paid baseline with GPT-5-nano interviewer) and Runs 2 and 3 (hybrid with Gemma 27B plus the new algorithmic components). The best hybrid configuration, Run 3, attains an ADODL score of 0.9063, ranking 3rd among all complete runs and placing DS@GT 2nd among 21 teams overall. It also surpasses the paid baseline Run 1 (0.8841 ADODL) while using about one-quarter of the per-persona API cost.

Conclusion: The experiments demonstrate that an open-source LLM, when embedded in a pipeline with strong algorithmic supervision—dialogue tree guidance, consensus aggregation, and symptom imputation—can match or even outperform a stronger proprietary model in conversational depression screening tasks, while substantially reducing costs. This supports the authors' central hypothesis and suggests a practical path toward cost-effective, high-performing mental health assessment systems based on open models.

Abstract: We describe DS@GT's submission to the eRisk 2026 Task 1 challenge on conversational depression screening, in which systems interview LLM personas that simulate individuals with varying depression profiles and produce a Beck Depression Inventory II (BDI-II) score plus four key symptoms per persona, without directly asking sensitive mental health questions. Our pipeline evolved through three stages: a monolithic single-model prototype to start off, a baseline multi-agent architecture that separates conversational interviewing from BDI-II scoring under a coordinating orchestration layer, and a final hybrid configuration that replaces the paid GPT-5-nano interviewer with the open-source Gemma 27B. To offset the model's weaker reasoning and instruction-following, the hybrid adds three algorithmic components: a precomputed dialogue tree that standardizes interview openers and follow-ups, a reliability-weighted consensus aggregation inspired by the Weaver framework, and a cluster-based imputation step for unprobed symptoms. We submitted three fully automated runs across all 20 personas, with Run 1 from the paid baseline and Runs 2 and 3 from the hybrid. Hybrid Run 3 achieved an ADODL of 0.9063, ranking 3rd among all complete-submission runs and placing DS@GT 2nd among the 21 teams overall, while outperforming our paid baseline Run 1 (0.8841) at roughly one-quarter of the per-persona API cost. These results support our central hypothesis that with sufficient algorithmic supervision, a weaker open-source model can compete with a stronger proprietary model in the conversational interviewer role. Our source code is available at https://github.com/dsgt-arc/erisk-task1-2026.

### [90] [Tractable Query Answering under Epistemic Confidentiality Policies in DL Ontologies (extended version)](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16715)
*Lorenzo Marconi,Daniela Rieti,Riccardo ROsati*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a new, more efficient and confidentiality-preserving semantics (MPV) for Controlled Query Evaluation over DL-Lite_R ontologies, overcoming intractability and indistinguishability issues of existing semantics, and validates it with an implementation and experiments.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing Controlled Query Evaluation (CQE) semantics (GA- and IGA-entailment) for protecting confidential information in ontology-based data access are computationally intractable even for lightweight DL-Lite_R TBoxes, and, when extended with Epistemic Dependencies (EDs) as policies, the IGA semantics fails to guarantee indistinguishability, a key security property. There is thus a need for a semantics that both preserves confidentiality (including indistinguishability) and admits tractable query answering.

Method: The authors formally analyze query answering for Boolean unions of conjunctive queries under GA- and IGA-entailment in the presence of DL-Lite_R TBoxes and Epistemic Dependencies, proving intractability and the violation of indistinguishability by IGA. They then introduce a new CQE semantics based on minimal policy violation (MPV), which selectively allows only those query answers that minimally conflict with the confidentiality policy. They prove formal relationships between MPV and existing semantics (sound approximation) and analyze its computational properties, especially data complexity for DL-Lite_R ontologies. Finally, they implement the framework and empirically evaluate its feasibility using an OWL 2 QL benchmark.

Result: They show that CQE with GA- and IGA-entailment over DL-Lite_R ontologies is computationally intractable in general, and that, when using Epistemic Dependencies as policies, IGA-entailment does not satisfy the indistinguishability property. For the proposed MPV semantics, they prove that it is a sound approximation of GA/IGA semantics that does satisfy indistinguishability, and that query entailment under MPV for DL-Lite_R ontologies is decidable in polynomial time with respect to data complexity. Their implementation and experimentation on an OWL 2 QL benchmark demonstrate the practical feasibility of the MPV-based CQE framework.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that the minimal policy violation (MPV) semantics offers a promising alternative for Controlled Query Evaluation over DL-Lite_R ontologies, reconciling confidentiality (including indistinguishability) with tractable query answering. MPV soundly approximates existing semantics while enabling polynomial-time data complexity and is practically viable, as evidenced by the prototype implementation and experimental evaluation on OWL 2 QL benchmarks.

Abstract: We study Controlled Query Evaluation (CQE), a declarative approach to confidentiality-preserving data access, in the context of Description Logic (DL) ontologies, and for confidentiality policies expressed through Epistemic Dependencies (EDs). We first address the problem of answering queries (specifically, Boolean unions of conjunctive queries) under known semantics for CQE (GA- and IGA-entailment). Our results show that if the TBox is expressed in $\text{DL-Lite}_{\mathcal{R}}$, CQE is computationally intractable in general. Moreover, in the presence of EDs, the IGA semantics has recently been proven not to satisfy an important confidentiality preservation property known as indistinguishability. With the goal of defining computationally easier and confidentiality-preserving forms of CQE, we introduce a new semantics for CQE, based on the notion of minimal policy violation (MPV). We show that the new semantics provides a sound approximation of the previous ones, while satisfying the indistinguishability property. We also prove that, in the case of $\text{DL-Lite}_{\mathcal{R}}$ ontologies, query entailment under the MPV semantics can be decided in polynomial time in data complexity. Finally, we present a software implementation of our framework that we used to evaluate the feasibility of this new approach using an existing benchmark for OWL 2 QL.

### [91] [RECON: Benchmarking Agent Memory for Compositional Reasoning over Long Contexts](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16716)
*Mihir Shriniwas Arya*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduces RECON, a benchmark to test long-context, memory-intensive compositional reasoning of LLM agents across large narrative case files.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM-based agents are increasingly used in settings where they must maintain and reason over long-term memory across extended contexts and interactions. Existing benchmarks mostly test simple fact retrieval or detection of fact changes, but they do not deeply evaluate how agents reason about the downstream implications of those changes in complex, multi-hop narrative settings. There is a need for a benchmark that stresses both retrieval and reasoning over very long, obfuscated narratives and tests realistic memory-like tasks.

Method: The authors construct RECON, a benchmark consisting of 24 long narrative case files across three realistic domains: criminal, medical, and financial. Each case file is 50k–100k tokens long and is designed as an obfuscated narrative that requires compositional reasoning. They define six memory-intensive task types for evaluation: (1) reconstructing multi-hop evidence chains, (2) propagating cascading invalidations when some evidence changes, (3) resolving source conflicts, (4) counterfactual reasoning, (5) satisfying complex temporal constraints, and (6) temporal fact retrieval. LLM agents (with and without specialized memory architectures, including an Oracle upper bound) are evaluated on these tasks to measure how well they retrieve and reason over the long contexts.

Result: The benchmark shows that current LLM architectures struggle significantly with this form of long-context, compositional memory reasoning. The best-performing non-Oracle system achieves only 22.4% accuracy across tasks, indicating failures both in retrieving pertinent information from long narratives and in reasoning correctly over that information. Retrieval and reasoning each emerge as distinct, significant bottlenecks.

Conclusion: RECON exposes serious limitations of current LLM-based agents in handling memory-intensive, long-context reasoning that is required for realistic applications such as personal assistants and enterprise copilots. The low performance, even for state-of-the-art systems, suggests that advances are needed in both memory/retrieval mechanisms and reasoning capabilities to handle cascading updates, conflict resolution, and temporal reasoning over very long contexts.

Abstract: Large language models and LLM-based agents are widely used as personal chat assistants, enterprise copilots, and autonomous workflow agents. In all these applications, memory (the ability to retain, access, and reason over information accumulated over long contexts and multiple interactions) plays a crucial role in determining the reliability of any agent. We introduce RECON (Reasoning over Extended Contexts with Obfuscated Narratives), a benchmark for evaluating compositional reasoning over long contexts. RECON spans 24 case files across three domains (criminal, medical, and financial), each ranging from 50k to 100k tokens, and tests agents on six memory intensive tasks: reconstructing multi-hop evidence chains, propagating cascading invalidations, resolving source conflicts, counterfactual reasoning, satisfying temporal constraints, and temporal fact retrieval. Recent memory benchmarks evaluate whether agents can retrieve scattered facts or detect if a fact has changed whereas RECON evaluates what happens after the change, whether agents can trace which downstream conclusions are affected, which survive through independent support, and how alternative timelines would have unfolded. Our evaluation reveals substantial limitations across current architectures: even the strongest non-Oracle system reaches only 22.4% Accuracy, with retrieval and reasoning each surfacing as challenges.

### [92] [Constraint-Anchored Reasoning Traces](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16727)
*Zehua Cheng,Wei Dai,Jiahao Sun*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces CART, a neuro-symbolic framework that reduces error snowballing in multimodal LLMs by interleaving natural language reasoning with verifiable symbolic constraints and backtracking when contradictions are detected.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Autoregressive multimodal LLMs are prone to error snowballing: once an early reasoning step is wrong, subsequent steps often become corrupted, leading to failure in around 65% of affected traces in current models. Existing mitigations like multiple chain sampling, post-hoc self-verification, or full program synthesis either detect errors too late, lack symbolic grounding, or limit the natural language flexibility of reasoning. A more integrated and efficient approach is needed to catch and correct reasoning errors during generation.

Method: The authors propose Constraint-Anchored Reasoning Traces (CART), in which an MLLM is trained to interleave natural language chain-of-thought steps with symbolic constraint assertions about visual content, such as object counts or attributes. A Constraint Propagation Module, combining a learned neural grounding head with Boolean Constraint Propagation, checks these constraints both against visual features and for logical consistency. When a contradiction is detected, a backtrack controller stops generation and reverts to the last consistent reasoning checkpoint, thereby preventing error propagation. A variable-frequency emission mechanism lets the model adapt how often it emits constraints to balance robustness with efficiency. The authors construct a 218K-example training set by augmenting GQA, CLEVR-CoGenT, and VCR with constraints derived from scene graphs, and fine-tune LLaVA-NeXT and Qwen2-VL using LoRA.

Result: On five benchmarks, the CART-augmented models significantly reduce the snowball rate from 0.65 to 0.14, indicating much less error cascading in reasoning traces. They also improve GQA accuracy by 4.6 percentage points over training-only baselines and achieve 89.1 F1 on the POPE-all benchmark, all while adding at most 18% inference overhead, demonstrating an effective accuracy-robustness trade-off with modest computational cost.

Conclusion: CART shows that embedding lightweight, machine-checkable symbolic constraints directly into the reasoning process of MLLMs, combined with continuous verification and backtracking, can substantially mitigate error snowballing without sacrificing natural language flexibility. The approach offers a practical neuro-symbolic path toward more reliable multimodal reasoning with relatively small training and inference overheads, and suggests that constraint-anchored reasoning traces can generalize across multiple visual reasoning benchmarks and model backbones.

Abstract: Autoregressive multimodal large language models (MLLMs) suffer from error snowballing: a single incorrect inference early in a chainof-thought (CoT) trace corrupts all downstream reasoning. We find that in state-of-the-art open-source MLLMs, once the first error occurs, the reasoning cascades into failure across all remaining steps in 65% of such cases (a metric we term the snowball rate). Existing mitigations-sampling multiple chains, post-hoc self-verification, or full program synthesis-either lack symbolic grounding, catch errors too late, or sacrifice the flexibility of natural language reasoning. We propose Constraint-Anchored Reasoning Traces (CART), a neuro-symbolic framework that trains MLLMs to interleave natural language reasoning steps with symbolic constraint assertions: lightweight, machine-checkable statements about visual content (e.g., count(red_objects) = 3). A dual-pronged Constraint Propagation Module-combining a learned neural grounding head with Boolean Constraint Propagation-continuously verifies these anchors against extracted visual features and checks their mutual logical consistency. When a contradiction is detected, a backtrack controller halts generation and reverts to the last consistent checkpoint, preventing error propagation. A variable-frequency emission mechanism allows the model to adaptively control anchor density, avoiding trace bloat. We construct 218K training instances by augmenting GQA, CLEVR-CoGenT, and VCR with ground-truth constraint annotations derived from scene graphs, and fine-tune open-source MLLMs (LLaVA-NeXT, Qwen2-VL) via LoRA. On five benchmarks, CART reduces the snowball rate from 0.65 to 0.14, improves GQA accuracy by +4.6 percentage points over trainingonly baselines, and achieves 89.1 F1 on POPE-all with at most 18% inference overhead.

### [94] [RELIC: Revealed Principles for Learning Interpretable Composable Skills in Multi-Agent Planning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16745)
*Nguyen Viet Tuan Kiet,Bui Dinh Pham,Duong Quoc Chinh,Dao Van Tung,Tran Cong Dao,Huynh Thi Thanh Binh*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose RELIC, a framework that lets multiple agents learn and share high-level, human-interpretable skills without exposing their private internal policies, enabling coordination under privacy and heterogeneity constraints.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most multi-agent planning methods assume centralized training, shared access to policies, or common internal representations. These assumptions break down when agents are independently developed, have different interfaces, and must keep their internal implementations private (e.g., for IP or security reasons). There is a need for a way to coordinate and transfer skills across such heterogeneous, privacy-constrained agents without sharing executable code or detailed policies.

Method: RELIC introduces a privacy-preserving learning setup with three key components: (1) Each agent maintains its own programmatic skill representation and improves it using a private, LLM-guided search over possible skill programs. (2) A trusted orchestrator observes only team-level performance and evaluates candidate skill updates proposed by agents, without accessing or inspecting their internal code or policies. (3) When useful behaviors are found, they are distilled into abstract, interpretable principles rather than being shared as code; other agents then instantiate these principles within their own interfaces and combine them with their local strategies. This yields interpretable, composable skills that can be transferred across heterogeneous agents without code sharing.

Result: The abstract claims that RELIC enables cross-agent transfer and coordination even when agents have heterogeneous skill signatures and cannot share executable policies. By abstracting successful behaviors into portable principles, agents can benefit from each other’s discoveries while preserving implementation privacy. Empirical or formal results are not detailed in the abstract, but the claimed outcome is effective multi-agent coordination under privacy and heterogeneity constraints.

Conclusion: RELIC provides a new paradigm for multi-agent planning where coordination is achieved via sharing high-level, interpretable principles rather than raw policy code. This separation of coordination from implementation sharing allows agents with different interfaces and private internals to learn, transfer, and compose skills collaboratively while preserving privacy, offering a path toward more scalable and realistic multi-agent systems.

Abstract: Multi-agent planning becomes substantially harder when agents must improve specialized decision-making skills while keeping their internal implementations private. This regime arises when agents are developed independently, expose different interfaces and capabilities, and must nevertheless coordinate without sharing executable policies. Prior research has largely assumed centralized optimization, shared policy access, or common skill representations, making it poorly suited to privacy-constrained cooperation. We introduce RELIC, a framework for learning interpretable and composable skills via revealed principles. Each agent refines its own programmatic skill through private LLM-guided search, while a trusted orchestrator evaluates proposed updates solely through team-level performance. Successful behaviors are not broadcast as code; instead, they are abstracted into portable principles that other agents can instantiate within their own interfaces and recombine with local strategies. This separates coordination from implementation sharing, enabling cross-agent transfer under heterogeneous skill signatures. RELIC thus introduces a new paradigm for privacy-preserving skill learning and coordination in multi-agent planning.

### [95] [FUSAR-R1: A Large-Scale Reasoning Model for Intelligent Interpretation of SAR Images](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16819)
*Yi Yang,Xiaokun Zhang,Yuxuan Li,Ruyi Zhang,Xinpeng Zhou,Haipeng Wang*

TL;DR: The paper introduces FUSAR-R1, a large-scale reasoning vision-language model tailored for SAR image interpretation, combining chain-of-thought supervision and reinforcement learning to achieve more reliable, expert-like reasoning and outperform existing multimodal models on multiple SAR tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing SAR vision-language models benefit from text guidance but still lack human-expert-like stepwise reasoning, logical judgment, and self-correction. Due to SAR’s coherent imaging, complex scattering, speckle noise, and strong target-background coupling, SAR imagery is highly uncertain and specialized. This limits the reliability of current intelligent interpretation systems in complex scenes, motivating a model that can explicitly reason and refine its own outputs.

Method: The authors design FUSAR-R1, a large-scale reasoning model for SAR image interpretation. They first simulate human expert interpretation processes to build explicit chain-of-thought reasoning data, then perform instruction learning so the model acquires basic step-by-step reasoning skills. After that, they apply a reinforcement learning strategy that uses inference results as feedback to optimize the model’s outputs, encouraging self-correction and more robust reasoning for SAR-specific tasks.

Result: FUSAR-R1 is empirically evaluated on diverse SAR interpretation tasks, including target detection, target counting and classification, and land-cover category recognition. Across these tasks, it consistently surpasses existing multimodal large-scale models, indicating superior reasoning quality and task performance under SAR’s challenging imaging conditions.

Conclusion: By integrating explicit chain-of-thought supervision with reinforcement learning-based output optimization, FUSAR-R1 achieves more reliable, human-expert-like reasoning for SAR image understanding. The model advances the state of the art in SAR vision-language interpretation and demonstrates that structured reasoning and self-correction are key to handling the unique complexity and uncertainty of SAR imagery.

Abstract: In recent years, large-scale vision-language models have been driving a paradigm shift in intelligent remote sensing image interpretation. By incorporating textual semantic information, the cognitive expression, semantic understanding, and human-computer interaction capabilities of interpretation models have been significantly improved, achieving initial progress in the field of Synthetic Aperture Radar (SAR) image interpretation. However, SAR images are affected by factors such as coherent imaging mechanisms, complex scattering characteristics, speckle noise interference, and target-background coupling, resulting in complex and variable image features with significant uncertainties and specializations. Existing SAR vision-language models do not yet possess the step-by-step analysis, logical judgment, and self-correction capabilities of human experts, making it difficult to support reliable intelligent interpretation in complex scenarios. To address this issue, this paper proposes a large-scale reasoning model, FUSAR-R1, for intelligent interpretation of SAR images. The model first constructs explicit chain-of-thought reasoning data by simulating the interpretation process of human experts and uses this data to guide instruction learning, thereby endowing the model with basic reasoning capabilities. Subsequently, a reinforcement learning strategy is introduced to optimize the model's outputs based on inference results, enabling self-correction and more reliable reasoning. Experimental results demonstrate that FUSAR-R1 consistently outperforms existing multimodal large-scale models across various SAR interpretation tasks, including target detection, target counting and classification, and land-cover category recognition.

Using a design science research approach, we conducted a rapid literature review, a systematic evaluation of 16 AI tools, multiple interviews, a focus group, and a user study with experts at European XFEL to develop and evaluate two prototypes. Our study identifies key knowledge challenges in scientific data analysis, derives requirements for an AI agent that supports knowledge retrieval and source code generation, and proposes design recommendations for a specialized system adaptable to the evolving AI tool landscape. ### [97] [AgentBrew: Lifelong Knowledge Brewing from Strong Teachers to Weak LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16851)
*Yangqin Jiang,Chao Huang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: AgentBrew distills a strong teacher agent’s interaction experience into an external memory that a weaker student LLM can use at test time, without any training or labels.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In many real-world deployments, we cannot run large, powerful LLM agents at test time due to cost or latency, even though we may have access to them during development. Traditional distillation requires gradient-based training, labeled data, or ongoing teacher access, which can be expensive or infeasible. The authors aim to exploit a strong teacher’s interactive experience to improve a weak student agent, but in a way that avoids weight updates, expert demonstrations, and reliance on ground-truth labels or teacher availability at deployment.

Method: They introduce AgentBrew, a ‘knowledge brewing’ framework that converts a teacher agent’s interaction history into a persistent external memory usable by a weaker student. AgentBrew has two main components: (1) a failure-triggered teacher loop (Ralph Loop), which—when the student fails—asks the teacher to transform those failures into notes that are validated against the environment, thereby overcoming sparse, binary feedback; and (2) student-aware synthesis, which shapes and calibrates the teacher’s notes to match the weaker student’s abilities, producing model-specific, concretely executable instructions that the student can reliably follow at test time, all without changing the student’s weights.

Result: On benchmarks spanning coding, math, and tool use, AgentBrew significantly boosts the capabilities of weaker student LLM agents. Ablation studies show that both the failure-triggered loop and the student-aware synthesis are important, and that the asymmetric, training-free brewing strategy is effective compared to baselines that lack these components or require traditional training/distillation.

Conclusion: Knowledge brewing via AgentBrew allows deployment of capable, low-cost LLM agents by leveraging a stronger teacher only during a pre-deployment phase, without any gradient updates, labeled data, or test-time teacher access. By turning student failures into environment-validated, student-tailored memory, the method addresses sparse feedback and execution gaps between teacher and student, demonstrating a practical pathway to build strong yet deployable agents from weaker models.

Abstract: Deploying LLM agents typically requires a compact test-time student, even if a stronger teacher is available during training. We study knowledge brewing: distilling a teacher's interactive experience into a persistent external memory for the student. Crucially, this requires no weight updates, expert demonstrations, ground-truth labels, or test-time teacher access. This setting poses two challenges: environments provide only sparse, binary feedback, and teacher-authored notes must be inherently tailored to be concretely executable by a substantially weaker student. To address these hurdles, we propose AgentBrew, comprising two coupled components. First, a failure-triggered teacher--Ralph Loop mitigates sparse feedback by transforming student failures into environment-validated notes. Second, student-aware synthesis calibrates teacher knowledge to the weak executor's operational granularity, yielding model-specific, actionable guidance. Extensive evaluations and comprehensive ablations across coding, math, and tool-use tasks demonstrate that this asymmetric, training-free brewing paradigm produces highly capable yet deployable LLM agents.

### [98] [Beyond Semantic Equivalence: Logical Graphs for LLM Uncertainty Quantification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16868)
*Yanni Dong,Minghua Liu,Meiling Zhu,Xiaowei Huang,Lijun Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Proposes Logical Graph Uncertainty (LGU), a new uncertainty metric for LLMs that accounts for logical relationships between answers to better detect unreliable outputs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are overconfident and existing uncertainty metrics like semantic entropy do not consider logical implication or incompatibility between distinct answers. This causes them to overestimate uncertainty when answers differ only in wording, granularity, or specificity but are logically compatible, leading to false flags of hallucinations.

Method: Introduce Logical Graph Uncertainty (LGU), which (1) explicitly models logical relations (entailment, implication, incompatibility) among candidate answers, (2) aggregates probability mass along entailment chains, (3) computes entropy over logically maximal (most informative, non-entailing) hypotheses rather than raw surface forms, and (4) adds a penalty term for mutually incompatible hypotheses. Evaluate LGU on multiple QA benchmarks against existing uncertainty baselines including semantic entropy.

Result: On multiple QA datasets, LGU provides more accurate uncertainty estimation than existing methods, improving over the semantic entropy baseline by up to +7.1 percentage points in AUROC and +3.5 percentage points in AUARC for hallucination and error detection tasks.

Conclusion: Modeling logical structure among candidate answers yields more faithful uncertainty estimates for LLMs. LGU reduces false uncertainty in cases of logically compatible yet diverse answers and achieves consistently better performance than semantic-entropy-based baselines, indicating that future reliability methods should incorporate logical relationships, not just semantic similarity.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) often produce confidently stated yet unreliable outputs, posing critical challenges for deployment in safety-sensitive applications. Existing uncertainty metrics such as semantic entropy capture agreement at the level of semantic equivalence, but largely ignore the logical relationships between distinct answers. As a result, they tend to overestimate uncertainty and falsely flag hallucinations in settings where generated responses are diverse in form yet logically compatible (e.g., differing only in granularity or specificity). We propose Logical Graph Uncertainty (LGU), a framework that explicitly models implication and incompatibility among answers. LGU aggregates probability mass along entailment chains, computes entropy over logically maximal hypotheses, and penalizes mutual incompatibility among them. Across multiple question-answering benchmarks, LGU consistently improves uncertainty estimation over existing methods, and outperforms the semantic entropy baseline by up to +7.1% AUROC and +3.5% AUARC across datasets.

</details>

Method: Using only API specifications, they build a three-stage LLM-based synthetic data pipeline: (1) a task-generator LLM creates diverse tasks solvable with the APIs; (2) a teacher agent solves each task while an LLM simulator acts as a digital world model, producing coherent, stateful API responses conditioned on the task and interaction history; (3) an LLM judge evaluates and filters trajectories for quality, yielding a curated dataset of API-calling traces, all without any executable environment or real backend.

Result: On AppWorld and OfficeBench benchmarks, which cover information-retrieval and state-changing tasks, models fine-tuned on the synthetic trajectories show large performance improvements over baselines that lack this data, indicating that the simulated trajectories provide effective supervision for API-calling behavior.

Abstract: Training API-calling large language model (LLM) agents demands massive amounts of high-quality trajectories. However, collecting such data at scale typically requires fully implemented environments with executable APIs and realistic, pre-populated backend databases, creating a major bottleneck for scalability. To overcome this, we propose an environment-free synthetic data generation approach that leverages LLMs as on-the-fly digital world models. Given only API specifications, our method generates trajectories mimicking interactions between an agent and a stateful environment. Specifically, an LLM first generates diverse tasks solvable with the provided APIs. A teacher agent then iteratively solves each task while an LLM simulator generates coherent synthetic API responses conditioned on the task context and simulation history. Finally, an LLM judge filters the trajectories to ensure the quality of the resulting dataset. We evaluate our approach on the challenging AppWorld and OfficeBench benchmarks, which include both information-retrieval and state-changing tasks. Fine-tuning models on our synthetic data yields significant performance gains, demonstrating that effective supervision for API-calling agents can be generated without any executable environment. Our results establish LLM-based API simulation as a practical, scalable solution for training agents across diverse API ecosystems.

### [100] [Lomekwi: Resource-Bounded Tool Discovery in LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16961)
*Roshan Klein-Seetharaman,Daniel Wang,Andrew Xu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Benchmarks usually give one score for complex tool-use tasks; this paper splits out and studies separate phases of tool discovery and use, finding some abilities get worse as models get larger.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current benchmarks collapse multiple cognitive skills—discovering tools, figuring out how to build them, and using them—into a single metric, hiding where models actually struggle. The authors want a finer-grained framework to understand and evaluate these distinct capabilities, particularly tool discovery, and to investigate how they scale with model size.

Method: They propose a decomposition of tool discovery into: (1) curiosity (finding the components needed for a tool), (2) recognition (discovering the construction procedure), and (3) efficiency (using the tool once built). They then map existing discovery-style benchmarks (e.g., Voyager) into this framework and design new combinatorial games and a separate environment mimicking real-world tasks to systematically measure these components, especially recognition, across model scales.

Result: They show that their curiosity–recognition–efficiency framework can be applied to existing tasks like Voyager. Empirically, they find an inverse scaling trend in recognition—the ability to infer how to construct tools—where larger models perform worse. This inverse scaling is demonstrated both in their combinatorial game suite and in a distinct environment designed to resemble real-world tool-use scenarios.

Conclusion: Tool-use evaluation should be decomposed into distinct phases, especially separating tool discovery from tool use and further breaking discovery into curiosity, recognition, and efficiency. Under this lens, some capabilities, notably recognition, may actually degrade as models get larger, as shown by inverse scaling in both synthetic combinatorial games and more realistic environments. This calls for revisiting how we train and benchmark models for complex tool-related reasoning.

Abstract: Existing tool-use benchmarks report a single success rate for complex, multistep tasks. Inspired by ideas from cognitive science, we distinguish tool use from tool discovery and decompose the latter into curiosity (the model's ability to discover the parts needed to build the tool), recognition (the model's ability to discover the process of creating the tool), and efficiency (the model's use of the tool after creation). We show that this framework can be applied to existing discovery tasks, such as Voyager. In addition, we provide evidence that recognition inversely scales with model size, and we introduce and analyze a class of combinatorial games that demonstrates this. We further observe inverse scaling in a separate environment designed to emulate real-world tasks.

### [101] [Training Continuous Chain of Thought Models: A Tale of Two Regimes](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16972)
*Varun Yerram,He He,Eunsol Choi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes C-MTP, a faster and simpler method for training continuous Chain-of-Thought (CoT) representations by directly supervising condensed latent states, and shows it is competitive on short reasoning traces but fails, like other methods, on long, complex reasoning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Continuous Chain-of-Thought aims to replace long, discrete reasoning traces with short, dense latent representations to make reasoning more efficient. Existing continuous CoT methods often require slow, autoregressive supervision based on full verbose traces, limiting practicality and scalability. There is a need for a simpler, faster training scheme and for understanding how these methods behave on more realistic, longer reasoning tasks.

Method: The authors introduce C-MTP, a direct supervision approach where each latent state in the continuous CoT is trained to approximate the mean of token embeddings from the corresponding segment of the original verbose reasoning trace. This bypasses autoregressive training and previous distribution-matching approaches to compressed tokens. They compare C-MTP against a prior direct method that models the distribution over compressed tokens and against indirect supervision methods that match final states of verbose traces, evaluating on benchmarks with short CoTs (<100 tokens) and then extending to more complex tasks with longer reasoning traces (hundreds of tokens).

Result: On existing benchmarks with simplified, short reasoning chains (<100 tokens), C-MTP outperforms a prior direct supervision method that approximates compressed token distributions and achieves performance on par with slower indirect supervision approaches that rely on autoregressive training. However, when evaluated on complex tasks that require long reasoning traces (hundreds of tokens), both direct (including C-MTP) and indirect continuous CoT methods show a large degradation in performance—about a 65% drop compared to baselines—indicating that none of the current techniques scale well to long reasoning.

Conclusion: C-MTP demonstrates that continuous CoT representations can be learned more efficiently via simple, direct supervision using averaged token embeddings, achieving competitive results on short reasoning tasks while being faster than indirect methods. Nevertheless, the strong performance drop on tasks requiring long reasoning traces exposes a fundamental limitation of current continuous CoT approaches. This suggests that future work must develop new architectures or training schemes to better capture and preserve information over long reasoning chains.

Abstract: Continuous Chain-of-Thought methods replace verbose reasoning traces with a short sequence of dense latent representations. Earlier continuous CoT methods indirectly supervise the latent representations such that its final state match that of verbose reasoning traces, requiring autoregressive, slow generation during training. We introduce C-MTP, a simpler, faster direct supervision approach that models each latent as an average of the embeddings in the CoT traces to be compressed. Our approach outperforms a prior direct supervision method that approximates the distribution of compressed tokens, and performs competitively to slower indirect supervision approaches in existing evaluation setup with simplified CoT traces (less than 100 tokens). Lastly, we extend the evaluation of Continuous CoT methods to complex tasks with longer reasoning traces ($\ge$ few hundreds reasoning tokens). We find both direct and indirect supervision training methods perform poorly (roughly 65\% performance drop) in this setting, revealing the limitations of current continuous CoT methods. The code and checkpoints are released at https://github.com/Varun221/cmtp_research

</details>


### [102] [Expected Free Energy as Belief-Dependent Utility for rho-POMDPs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16981)
*Patrick Cooper,Alvaro Velasquez*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: This paper shows that active inference with Expected Free Energy (EFE) is mathematically equivalent to solving a ρ-POMDP with an information-gain utility and a fixed exploration weight, providing an automatic, task-agnostic way to balance exploration and exploitation in partially observable environments.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In partially observable environments, an agent must decide how much and what kind of information to gather, given that observations are often costly. Standard POMDPs only value information via its indirect effect on future rewards, while ρ-POMDPs explicitly reward uncertainty reduction using a belief-dependent utility ρ. However, in practice, both the form of ρ and the relative weight between information-seeking and reward-seeking behavior must be manually tuned for each task, which is ad hoc and burdensome. The motivation is to eliminate this manual tuning and derive a principled, universal exploration objective that works across tasks, especially in domains where tests are expensive and missing faults is costly.

Method: The authors analyze the Expected Free Energy (EFE) objective used in active inference and prove that minimizing EFE is exactly equivalent to solving a ρ-POMDP whose belief-dependent utility is the expected information gain, with the exploration weight fixed at 1 in units of nats. They first establish this equivalence for observe-then-commit POMDPs, then extend it to factored observation POMDPs, covering interleaved observe-act settings where observations do not alter the hidden state (e.g., non-destructive testing, mobile sensing). They complement the theory with experiments on standard benchmarks such as Tiger, RockSample, and a new Structural Inspection domain with over 65,000 states, comparing EFE-based policies to reward-only planning and tuned exploration bonuses.

Result: The theoretical result is a proof that EFE minimization in active inference corresponds exactly to a ρ-POMDP with utility equal to expected information gain and a fixed, non-tunable exploration weight w = 1. Empirically, across multiple benchmark environments, the EFE-based, untuned exploration objective performs competitively or better than alternatives: it matches or surpasses reward-only planning at the same planning horizon, avoids the excessive exploration often induced by manually tuned bonuses, and tends to operate near the ‘knee’ of the success–reward Pareto frontier, which approximately optimizes the trade-off between task success and cost of exploration.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that Expected Free Energy provides a principled, derived belief-dependent utility for exploration in partially observable settings, removing the need to hand-tune exploration weights and utility functions as in traditional ρ-POMDPs. This yields an exploration objective that works “out of the box” across tasks, offering strong practical advantages in real-world applications such as fault detection and medical screening, where observations are costly and misdiagnoses are expensive, and where a consistent, theoretically grounded treatment of the value of information is crucial.

Abstract: An agent acting under partial observability must decide when to gather information and which observations are worth their cost. Standard POMDPs value information only through its eventual effect on reward. The $ρ$-POMDP framework instead rewards uncertainty reduction directly, through a belief-dependent utility $ρ$, but in practice both the choice of $ρ$ and the weight placed on it are tuned by hand for every task. We show that active inference removes this tuning entirely. Minimizing Expected Free Energy (EFE) is exactly equivalent to solving a $ρ$-POMDP whose utility is expected information gain, and the exploration weight is fixed at $w=1$ because the variational bound expresses pragmatic and epistemic value in the same units (nats). We prove this equivalence for observe-then-commit POMDPs and extend it to factored observation POMDPs, a broader class that covers interleaved observe-act problems such as non-destructive testing and mobile sensing, where gathering information leaves the hidden state unchanged. Experiments support the theory. Across environments ranging from the classic Tiger problem to RockSample and a new Structural Inspection benchmark with over 65,000 states, the untuned weight matches or outperforms reward-only planning at the same horizon, avoids the over-exploration of bonuses tuned per task, and sits near the reward-maximizing knee of the success-reward Pareto frontier. The practical payoff is an exploration objective that works out of the box. In applications such as fault detection and medical screening, where every test has a price and every missed fault has a cost, EFE supplies a belief-dependent utility that is derived rather than tuned.

</details>


### [103] [PriorProof: A Point-in-Time Measure of Technique Novelty for Formal Proofs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.16997)
*Neel Somani*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces PriorProof, a method to quantify how nonstandard a formal proof route is over time by comparing its proof dependencies to those typical in an earlier version of a formal library (Mathlib).


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Mathematicians care about whether a proof route is standard, explanatory, or novel, but such qualitative judgments are hard to formalize and automate. In formalized mathematics (e.g., Lean and Mathlib), there is a need for an automatic, objective measure of how unusual or nonstandard a particular proof route is, anchored to what was available at a given time, without requiring hand-crafted taxonomies or human annotations.

Method: For each Lean theorem, PriorProof mechanically extracts the set of dependencies used in its elaborated proof term (its dependency footprint). It then computes a weighted surprisal score for this footprint under a probabilistic prior learned from an earlier quarterly snapshot of Mathlib. The prior is a retrieval-conditioned, hierarchically smoothed model over proof dependencies. Statement retrieval is trained using proof-derived contrastive pairs, and all scored objects are obtained automatically from proof terms, avoiding any hand-built technique ontology or manual labels. The authors then conduct a blinded study in topology, presenting pairs of proofs to expert raters and comparing their judgments to PriorProof’s scores.

Result: In the blinded topology study, 100 proof presentations reduce to 76 distinct proof pairs (due to repeated canonical contrasts for consistency checks). Among these, PriorProof agrees with the majority judgment of three domain experts on 53 out of 76 pairs (about 69.7%), including 11/12 of the canonical pairs and 42/64 stratified pairs, with confidence intervals reported. Agreement increases toward the largest score gaps, but not monotonically across quartiles after collapsing repeats, suggesting an endpoint-calibration effect rather than a clean stepwise calibration. A strong language model baseline agrees with experts on 60/76 pairs (78.9%). On direct pairwise comparison, PriorProof is uniquely correct on 8 pairs while the model is uniquely correct on 15, and a McNemar test indicates no statistically significant difference at this sample size (p = 0.210).

Conclusion: PriorProof provides an automatic, decomposable, and time-anchored measure of how nonstandard a formal proof route is, based solely on proof-term dependencies and a prior learned from earlier library snapshots. It does not aim to replace expert or language-model judgments but offers a complementary signal whose score gap carries information about reliability. The method avoids manual ontologies and labeling, and shows substantial alignment with expert judgments, particularly for clearly standard or clearly nonstandard cases.

Abstract: Mathematicians distinguish proofs that explain, simplify, or introduce a nonstandard route, but these judgments are difficult to operationalize. We study a deliberately narrower construct: time-relative proof-route nonstandardness in formal mathematics. For a Lean theorem, PriorProof extracts the dependency footprint of its elaborated proof term and scores the weighted surprisal of that footprint under a retrieval-conditioned, hierarchically smoothed prior built only from an earlier quarterly snapshot of Mathlib. The method requires no hand-built technique ontology and no human labels: statement retrieval is learned from proof-derived contrastive pairs, while the scored object is read mechanically from proof terms. In a blinded topology study, 100 presentations collapse to 76 distinct underlying pairs: 12 canonical contrasts shown three times for consistency screening and 64 distinct stratified pairs. Against the majority of three retained domain raters, PriorProof agrees on 53/76 pairs (69.7%, Wilson 95% CI 58.7-78.9%), including 11/12 canonical pairs (91.7%, 64.6-98.5%) and 42/64 stratified pairs (65.6%, 53.4-76.1%). Score-gap quartiles are nonmonotone after repeat collapse; the endpoints are 12/19 (63.2%, 41.0-80.9%) in the smallest-gap bin and 16/19 (84.2%, 62.4-94.5%) in the largest, supporting an endpoint-calibration tendency rather than a resolved staircase. The best language-model condition agrees on 60/76 pairs (78.9%, 68.5-86.6%); on paired outcomes, PriorProof alone is correct on 8 pairs and the model alone on 15 (exact two-sided McNemar p = 0.210), so the difference is not established at this sample size. We therefore present PriorProof not as a replacement for expert or model judgment, but as a decomposable, time-anchored signal whose score gap provides an interpretable reliability indicator.

</details>

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors design an LLM-centered agent framework that synthesizes visual, language, generative, graph, multimodal, reinforcement, and agent intelligence. Core to the method is a POMDP-based routing mechanism that models partial observability and selects actions based on belief states. This is coupled with an internal, self-correcting reward model that scores candidate decision trajectories before execution. The system ingests multimodal inputs, employs reinforcement learning concepts like PPO and value function approximation, and uses graph-based memory to maintain long-term structural context. The work provides an empirically validated, theoretically motivated reference architecture for building scalable autonomous LLM agents that can better plan over long horizons, adapt to dynamic observations, and control hallucinations, with open-source code to support further research and practical deployment.

Abstract: This paper addresses key technical challenges in current large language model (LLM) agent applications, including long-horizon planning, sparse reward attribution, and dynamic environmental interaction, by designing and optimizing an intelligent agent workflow. The proposed architecture is based on the synthesis of core AI paradigms: Visual, Language, Generative, Graph, Multimodal, Reinforcement, and Agent Intelligence. Unlike conventional baseline models that rely on static prompting and lack robust perception-action loops, our approach introduces a Partially Observable Markov Decision Process (POMDP) routing mechanism. This mechanism is augmented with an internal, self-correcting reward model that evaluates decision trajectories before execution. By integrating multimodal inputs and advanced reinforcement learning principles (such as proximal policy optimization and value function approximation), the agent maintains long-term structural memory and dynamically adapts its reasoning pathways to mitigate error accumulation. Empirical experiments on the ALFWorld embodied simulation environment and the WebShop online navigation benchmark demonstrate a 24.5% absolute improvement in task success rate and trajectory efficiency over mainstream baselines like the standard ReAct framework. Comprehensive ablation studies confirm the significant contribution of the reward-driven critique module in suppressing hallucination rates. This research bridges theoretical foundations of reinforcement learning and graph-based memory with autonomous agent workflows. Ultimately, the resulting architecture offers a practical, scalable reference framework for developing artificial intelligence technologies in complex, multi-step autonomous systems. Code is available at https://github.com/01Amez/RLAW_Implementation.

*Ziteng Hu,Jiachi Chen,Wenhao Lv,Huan Zhang,Yingjie Xia*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how well large language models answer hardware description language (HDL) questions, finds they tend to over-answer with redundancy and verbosity, and proposes a multi-agent framework that makes their answers more concise and higher quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Engineers are increasingly using LLMs to answer HDL questions, but mistakes and unnecessary complexity in HDL can cause serious downstream issues in hardware design. Despite this risk, it is unclear how LLM answers compare to human expert answers in quality, faithfulness, and usability. The authors want to systematically assess this and then improve LLM-generated HDL help.

Method: The authors construct a dataset of 6,246 Stack Overflow HDL Q&A pairs with accepted answers and categorize them into a taxonomy of four main question types and ten subtypes. They run a user study with 19 relatively junior HDL engineers to compare LLM answers against expert answers, focusing on correctness, redundancy, verbosity, and preference. Based on issues found, they design a multi-agent LLM framework aimed at reducing redundancy and verbosity while preserving correctness, and evaluate it using an LLM-as-Judge together with structural metrics that count distinct core answers and measure the amount of non-core content.

Result: They observe that LLMs frequently over-answer: in about two-thirds of cases they provide the right content but bury it under redundant alternative solutions and verbose padding, and nearly half of answers do not fully match expert solutions even though participants still often prefer them for readability. Their proposed multi-agent framework significantly improves judged answer quality across four major LLMs, boosting core-answer quality by 0.96 and non-core content quality by 0.51 on a 5-point scale.

Conclusion: LLMs are attractive and readable for HDL question answering but tend to be redundant, verbose, and not always fully aligned with expert solutions, posing risks for hardware design. A targeted, multi-agent answer-refinement framework can mitigate these issues, yielding more concise, higher-quality HDL answers and suggesting a promising direction for safer, more reliable LLM assistance in hardware design workflows.

Abstract: The rapid advancement of large language models (LLMs) has led practitioners to increasingly rely on them for answering questions about hardware description languages (HDLs). Because HDL is ultimately synthesized into physical hardware, an imprecise or redundant answer can propagate into timing violations or non-synthesizable logic that surface only late in the design flow, making the quality of HDL answers especially consequential. However, the quality of LLM-generated responses, particularly in comparison with answers provided by human experts, remains unclear. To investigate this question, we collect 6,246 HDL Q&A posts with accepted answers from Stack Overflow and curate them into a dataset, organized into a taxonomy of four main categories (Conceptual, Debugging, Generation, and Optimization) and ten subcategories. Using this dataset, we design a user study conducted with 19 HDL engineers with one to three years of experience. Our findings reveal a pervasive over answering tendency: LLMs supply correct content but bury it under redundant alternatives (65.7%) and verbose padding (69.1%), while nearly half of answers (49.0%) fail to fully align with expert answers yet participants still preferred LLM responses for readability (58.3%). Motivated by these findings, we propose a multi-agent framework for improving LLM-based HDL question answering. We evaluate answer quality using an LLM-as-Judge and two structural metrics: the number of core answers, which reflects redundancy since LLMs often provide multiple alternative solutions, and the length of non-core content, which reflects verbosity. Evaluated on the four mainstream LLMs, our framework increases the average core-answer quality score from 3.71 to 4.67 (+0.96) and the non-core content quality from 3.72 to 4.23 (+0.51), on a five-point scale.

### [106] [Bridging the Information Gap: Semantic Densification and Hindsight Distillation for Cold-Start Prediction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17070)
*Hao Duong Le,Yifei Gao,Huan Li,Lun Jiang,Chen Bai,Ke Xing,Chen Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: SemRaD is a semantic reasoning-aware distillation framework that combines structured LLM-derived user profiles with hindsight-aware knowledge distillation to improve cold-start LTV and CVR prediction in e-commerce.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: New or sparsely active users in e-commerce have little behavioral data, making it hard to accurately predict lifetime value (LTV) and conversion rate (CVR). Prior attempts either use free-form LLM rationales, which are noisy and hard to deploy, or standard student–teacher distillation with privileged information, which struggles because the teacher has much richer information than the student and this information gap varies across users. The paper aims to make LLM-based semantic signals structured and production-ready, while creating a more robust way to transfer privileged knowledge under heterogeneous information gaps.

Method: The authors propose SemRaD, which has two main components. (1) A Structured Semantic Reasoning Pipeline: instead of free-form LLM rationales, they design a structured schema discovered, curated, and audited offline. This pipeline produces, for each user, (a) a Densified Semantic Profile—a compact structured representation fed to the deployed model via a Semantic-Gated Encoder that highlights the most informative semantic dimensions; and (b) a Hindsight Distillation Target—an LTV/CVR-related teaching signal derived from both pre- and post-conversion reasoning, used only during training. (2) A Hindsight-Aware Distillation Network: a student model learns from the hindsight targets that encapsulate privileged information, and uses Distillation Experts (mixture-of-experts style) to adapt the distillation process to per-user variability in the information gap between teacher and student.

Result: On a large-scale industrial dataset from Keeta, SemRaD improves LTV prediction by +1.9% in Gini and CVR prediction by +1.0% in AUROC over a strong production baseline. In a four-week online A/B test, SemRaD yields +1.0% LTV and +0.43% CVR improvements. Additionally, when trained with only 9% of the data, SemRaD matches the production system’s LTV performance and still improves CVR by 0.8%, indicating better data efficiency.

Conclusion: Structured, audited semantic reasoning combined with hindsight-aware, expert-based distillation can significantly improve new-user cold-start prediction for LTV and CVR, outperforming a production-grade system both offline and online while being more data-efficient. SemRaD demonstrates that turning LLM rationales into structured profiles and tailoring distillation to heterogeneous information gaps makes privileged-information learning practical in large-scale e-commerce settings.

Abstract: New-user cold-start is a critical bottleneck for e-commerce platforms: predicting user lifetime value (LTV) and conversion rate (CVR) for users with sparse interaction history. Two prior directions -- LLM-based semantic augmentation and learning using privileged information (LUPI) -- each face a key limitation. First, LLM augmentation produces unstructured rationales that are noisy and hard to operationalize in production. Second, naive student-teacher distillation can be brittle due to an information gap between the privileged teacher and the sparse student; moreover, this gap is heterogeneous across users. We propose SemRaD, a Semantic Reasoning-aware Distillation framework addressing both limitations. First, a Structured Semantic Reasoning Pipeline replaces free-form rationales with a structured schema built via a discover-curate-audit workflow, producing per user a Densified Semantic Profile (consumed by the deployed student via a Semantic-Gated Encoder that focuses on the most informative dimensions) and a Hindsight Distillation Target reconciled from pre- and post-conversion reasoning (used only at training). Second, to bridge this gap and handle its heterogeneity, a Hindsight-Aware Distillation Network transfers privileged knowledge via the hindsight target, with Distillation Experts improving transfer under per-user variability. On a large-scale industrial dataset, SemRaD lifts +1.9% LTV (Gini) and +1.0% CVR (AUROC) over a production-grade base; a four-week online A/B at Keeta confirms +1.0% LTV / +0.43% CVR. SemRaD also matches the production system's LTV using only 9% of the training data while improving CVR by 0.8%.

### [107] [Otap:Structure-Aware Optimal Transport for Evaluating Planning and Execution in Agent Trajectories](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17082)
*Babak Barazandeh,Subhabrata Majumdar,George Michailidis*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They introduce OTAP, a graph-based optimal-transport metric that robustly scores LLM agent trajectories by comparing execution dependency graphs to valid solution graphs, overcoming brittleness of binary and exact-match metrics.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing ways to evaluate LLM agent trajectories are crude: binary success/fail and exact-match to a reference. Binary success cannot differentiate lucky or low-quality solutions from genuinely sound ones and gives no insight into failure modes. Exact matching wrongly penalizes alternative but valid decompositions, reorderings, or granularities of plans. The authors want a trajectory-level metric that is structurally aware, tolerant of benign variations, and informative about where and how a trajectory deviates from correct solutions.

Method: They represent an agent’s run and reference solutions as attributed dependency graphs capturing steps, their dependencies, and attributes. They then define trajectory evaluation as a graph distance problem: an unbalanced fused Gromov–Wasserstein optimal transport between the execution graph and a set of valid solution graphs. This yields a pseudo-metric, OTAP, that is invariant to dependency-preserving reorderings, softly matches steps across different granularities, and allows mass to remain unmatched to model missing or hallucinated steps. They assess OTAP under controlled perturbations and on several public benchmarks, using both exact and heuristic graph extraction from traces.

Result: OTAP reliably separates valid from invalid trajectories, especially in settings where purely semantic metrics (e.g., embedding-based or text-similarity scores) perform below chance. The metric maintains desirable invariances (reorderings, redundancy) and degrades gracefully when the underlying dependency graph is not perfectly known and must be heuristically inferred from text. When the dependency graph is accurate, OTAP’s classification accuracy is highest; performance drops but remains useful under noisy graph recovery.

Conclusion: Modeling agent trajectories as dependency graphs and scoring them via unbalanced fused Gromov–Wasserstein optimal transport yields a principled, robust evaluation metric for LLM agents. OTAP overcomes the brittleness of binary and exact-match evaluations, is tolerant to reordering, redundancy, and granularity differences, and still works reasonably when dependency graphs are only heuristically inferred. This suggests graph- and structure-aware optimal-transport metrics are a promising direction for evaluating complex agentic behavior.

Abstract: Large language model agents solve tasks by generating trajectories that interleave planning, tool calls, and intermediate results. Current evaluation metrics reduce such a trajectory to a binary success flag or compare it against a reference by exact matching. A success flag cannot distinguish a sound solution from one that succeeds by luck, and says nothing about why a failed run went wrong. Exact matching penalizes plans that are valid but reordered or decomposed differently from the reference. We reframe trajectory evaluation as a distance between the agent's execution graph and a set of valid solution graphs, and instantiate it via an unbalanced fused Gromov-Wasserstein transport problem over attributed dependency graphs. The resulting score, termed \otap{} (Optimal Transport for Agentic Planning), is a pseudo-metric that is provably invariant to dependency-preserving reorderings and has bounded sensitivity to redundant steps. Its unbalanced marginals handle missing or hallucinated steps without forcing a match, and its soft coupling accommodates variation in plan granularity. On controlled perturbations and three public benchmarks, \otap{} separates valid from invalid trajectories in a regime where semantics-only metrics score below chance. Its accuracy is highest when the dependency graph is recovered exactly, and drops only when the graph is inferred heuristically from free-text traces.

</details>

Existing methods typically use either SCADA or weather forecasts, or combine them naively, and they do not properly handle the heterogeneous formats (point vs grid) and geometric properties (wind direction, rotation) of the data, leading to suboptimal performance.

Method: They build a multimodal forecasting framework that fuses historical turbine-level SCADA measurements with grid-based NWP forecasts. Inputs are explicitly decomposed into scalar and vector features, allowing the model to treat site-specific quantities and directional wind information differently. A geometric encoder is used to derive rotation-invariant features from wind vectors, capturing geometric dependencies robustly. On top of this, they employ a Fourier Neural Operator (FNO) architecture that performs global convolutions in the frequency domain, enabling efficient modeling of long-range spatiotemporal dependencies between weather fields and turbine outputs.

Result: On three real-world wind farms with associated weather forecast data, the proposed model consistently outperforms a range of state-of-the-art baselines for short-term wind power forecasting, demonstrating lower prediction error and better capture of spatiotemporal patterns.

Conclusion: Physically-informed multimodal fusion of SCADA and NWP data, combined with geometric encoding of wind vectors and FNO-based global spatiotemporal modeling, significantly improves short-term wind power forecasting. Abstract: Accurate short-term wind power forecasting is essential for grid stability and operational planning, yet remains challenging due to the complex interactions between atmospheric conditions and turbine dynamics. However, existing methods fail to effectively incorporate weather forecasting with wind turbine data (i.e., SCADA), leading to suboptimal solutions. To address this, we introduce a multimodal framework that integrates historical point-based SCADA data with grid-based Numerical Weather Prediction (NWP) forecasts, which is challenging due to heterogeneous input and the complex physical wind-turbine interactions. Our approach first explicitly decomposes inputs into scalar and vector features to better capture both site-specific and geometric dependencies and then incorporates a geometric encoder to extract rotation-invariant features from wind vectors. We further leverages a Fourier Neural Operator (FNO) architecture, which performs global convolutions in the frequency domain to efficiently model long-range spatiotemporal relationships. Extensive experiments on three real-world wind farms, with weather forecasting data, demonstrate that our model consistently outperforms state-of-the-art baselines, highlighting the effectiveness of its physically-informed design. The core implementation of our method is publicly available at: https://github.com/shawn-sypiao/GWPF.

### [109] [Evidence Interfaces Shape How Retrieval-Augmented Readers Use Support](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17108)
*Junchi Liao,Jiawen Deng,Fuji Ren*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper analyzes failures in multi-hop RAG evaluation, distinguishing between missing evidence and poor use of available evidence by the reader model, and shows that accounting for complete-support coverage makes top-k scores interpretable and enables more efficient, higher-quality retrieval.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In multi-hop question answering with retrieval-augmented generation (RAG), standard top-k answer accuracy cannot tell whether errors are due to retrieval missing parts of the necessary support chain or the reader model failing to effectively use the retrieved evidence. This conflation makes it hard to evaluate and improve systems. The authors are motivated to disentangle these failure modes and to better understand how different ways of presenting evidence (evidence interfaces) to readers affect performance and evaluation.

Method: The authors use three multi-hop QA benchmarks with annotated supporting facts. They train and compare matched adapted readers under different evidence interfaces: (1) raw full context, (2) top-k retrieval windows, and (3) gold-support diagnostic renderings that explicitly present the annotated support chain. By checking whether each retrieved window preserves the complete gold support chain, they separate failures due to missing support from reader-interface effects. They also train a support-supervised ranker that prioritizes windows covering gold support, and conduct support-removal ablations to test whether gains come from exposed evidence rather than learned answer priors.

Result: They find that top-k retrieval windows are only interpretable when accompanied by a measure of complete-support coverage: when a window contains the full support chain, short ranked windows can match or even surpass performance with raw context; when the chain is incomplete, missing support explains a large fraction of performance loss. Training readers with gold-support-first renderings improves performance, and on 2Wiki and MuSiQue, a support-supervised ranker increases support coverage and recovers or exceeds raw-context performance at lower prompt cost, while preserving additional potential gains (“gold headroom”). Support-removal experiments show the improvements depend on actually exposing supporting evidence rather than merely exploiting answer priors.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that in multi-hop RAG, evaluating with only top-k answer scores is insufficient and potentially misleading. Evaluations should report complete-support coverage to distinguish retrieval failures from reader-interface issues. Carefully designed evidence interfaces and support-aware ranking can achieve raw-context or better quality with reduced context size, and diagnostic use of gold support reveals remaining room for improvement in how readers exploit evidence.

Abstract: In multi-hop RAG evaluation, a top-k answer score can hide two different failures: the retrieval window may drop part of the support chain, or it may contain support in a form the adapted reader does not use well. We call this reader-facing form of retrieved evidence an evidence interface. Using three support-annotated multi-hop QA benchmarks, we compare matched adapted readers trained with raw context, retrieval windows, and gold-support diagnostic renderings. These comparisons distinguish support-availability failures from remaining reader-interface effects. Top-k windows become interpretable only after checking whether the complete annotated support chain survives: when it does, short ranked windows can match or improve over raw context; when it does not, missing support explains much of the loss. Gold support-first improves matched readers; on 2Wiki and MuSiQue, a support-supervised ranker raises coverage and recovers raw-context quality at lower prompt cost, while retaining gold headroom. Support-removal checks further show that the gains rely on exposed evidence, not only answer priors. On support-annotated evaluations, top-k answer scores should therefore be reported together with complete-support coverage.
