### [110] [A Diagnostic Framework for AI Agent Behavior](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17149)
*Xichen Zhang,Yingjie Zhang,Tianshu Sun*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a layered framework to diagnose the sources of AI agent behavior, distinguishing between computational architecture and behavioral modulation by social and institutional structures, in order to better explain, validate, and govern AI systems.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As AI agents are increasingly deployed in domains traditionally studied by behavioral scientists (clinical, political, scientific, social), the same observed behavior can arise from very different underlying causes: from the AI’s internal architecture or from the external roles, incentives, and governance structures imposed on it. Without a principled way to attribute behavior to its correct source, evaluation, explanation, and governance of AI agents can be misguided or ineffective. The authors are motivated to provide a framework that clarifies how to diagnose where a given behavior originates so that interventions, validations, and comparisons to humans are meaningful.

Method: The paper introduces a conceptual diagnostic framework called ‘layer attribution.’ It decomposes AI agent behavior into (1) a foundational computational layer, encompassing architecture, memory, perception, attention, and representation, which constrains what behaviors are possible; and (2) a behavioral modulation layer, encompassing identity, resources, objectives, social interactions, institutional constraints, and governance rules, which shapes how possible behaviors are actually expressed. The method is theoretical: the authors define these layers, articulate their components, and analyze implications for interpreting observed behaviors and designing evaluations. They then discuss how this decomposition informs notions such as surrogate validity, human–AI behavioral comparisons, and governance strategies.

Result: The main result is a structured framework for attributing AI agent behavior to the right causal layer. The authors show: (a) that surrogate validity (whether an AI model can stand in for humans or other systems) depends jointly on the task and on which layer is being matched; (b) that divergences between human and AI behaviors can be interpreted diagnostically—indicating whether differences stem from computational substrates or from differing roles, incentives, and constraints; and (c) that effective governance must distinguish between problems originating in architecture/representation and those originating in objectives, interaction structures, or institutional rules. These conceptual results reframe how behavioral scientists and policymakers should interpret AI agent behavior in complex systems.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that AI agents should be treated as behavioral actors whose actions must be analyzed with explicit attention to the source layer of behavior. Before explaining, validating, or governing AI systems, evaluators must determine whether a behavior arises from the foundational computational layer or from the behavioral modulation layer. Only with accurate source attribution can we build valid surrogate models, correctly interpret human–AI divergences, and design targeted governance mechanisms. The authors advocate that behavioral research and policy around AI move from treating AI as a monolithic black box to using layer attribution as a standard diagnostic practice.

Abstract: AI agents increasingly act within the same clinical, political, scientific, and social systems that behavioral scientists study. Evaluating these systems requires source-level diagnosis: the same behavioral pattern may arise from an agent representational substrate or from the roles, objectives, interaction structures, and governance rules that shape its expression. This Perspective proposes a diagnostic framework for AI agent behavior: layer attribution. The foundational computational layer defines what behaviors are possible through architecture, memory, perception, attention, and representation. The behavioral modulation layer shapes how those capacities are expressed through identity, resources, objectives, social interaction, institutional constraints, and governance. The framework clarifies three consequences: surrogate validity is a model-task-layer relation, human-AI divergence provides diagnostic evidence, and governance requires source attribution before intervention. Treating AI agents as behavioral actors therefore requires evaluation methods that determine where behavior originates before deciding how to explain, validate, or govern it.

### [111] [Is Your Model Thinking or Just Stagnating? PUMA: Diagnosing Reasoning Pathology via Phase-Momentum Alignment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17188)
*Cheng Yan,Guangyang Ye,Wuyang Zhang,Fan Xu,Zhijun Fan,Xiang Xia,Yanyong Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Paper proposes PUMA, a training-free framework to reduce overthinking in Large Reasoning Models by detecting when extra chain-of-thought is no longer useful.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large Reasoning Models use long chain-of-thought at test time, which improves complex reasoning but causes high computational cost and sometimes redundant or misleading extra steps. Existing efficiency methods either rely on uncertainty signals that can be deceptive, or on post-hoc latent analyses that cannot guide reasoning in real time. The authors aim to create a more reliable, dynamic mechanism to stop or adjust reasoning when it stops being productive.

Method: They introduce the Phase-Momentum Alignment Hypothesis, claiming that correct reasoning depends on temporal alignment between geometric momentum in latent space and reduction in uncertainty. They build a Cognitive-Energy Model with two axes: Geometric Cognitive Effort (measured by latent velocity and path tortuosity) and Entropic Cognitive Uncertainty. Based on this, they design PUMA, a training-free, tiered diagnostic framework that performs lightweight real-time phase monitoring and, when certain events trigger, more detailed geometric analysis. This lets the system differentiate active exploration from passive stagnation in the reasoning trajectory and decide whether to truncate or correct the ongoing chain-of-thought.

Result: On multiple Large Reasoning Models from 1.5B to 32B parameters and across diverse benchmarks, PUMA yields better accuracy-efficiency trade-offs than state-of-the-art test-time scaling and efficiency baselines, and it generalizes well across domains.

Conclusion: Dynamic monitoring of geometric and entropic signals during reasoning allows LRMs to avoid overthinking, reducing computational cost without sacrificing—and sometimes improving—accuracy. PUMA offers a practical, training-free way to control test-time computation and improves robustness across tasks and model sizes.

Abstract: Test-time scaling empowers Large Reasoning Models (LRMs) to tackle complex tasks via extensive Chain-of-Thought (CoT). However, this often induces the "overthinking" paradox, where redundant reasoning increases computational overhead without guaranteeing accuracy. Existing test-time efficiency optimization methods primarily fall into two categories: information-theoretic approaches, which are prone to "deceptive convergence" where low uncertainty masks hallucinations, and latent representation analyses, which are often post-hoc, lacking the real-time sensitivity for dynamic reasoning. To bridge this gap, we first posit the Phase-Momentum Alignment Hypothesis, asserting that reasoning correctness hinges on the temporal synchronization between geometric momentum and uncertainty resolution. We then theoretically formulate the Cognitive-Energy Model to characterize these dynamics through two orthogonal dimensions: Geometric Cognitive Effort, quantified by latent velocity and tortuosity, and Entropic Cognitive Uncertainty. To operationalize this, we introduce PUMA (Phase-Uncertainty Momentum Alignment), a training-free framework employing a tiered diagnostic architecture. By coupling lightweight phase monitoring with event-triggered geometric analysis, PUMA effectively distinguishes active exploration from passive stagnation, enabling precise interventions through adaptive truncation or corrective measures. Extensive experiments on LRMs spanning 1.5B to 32B demonstrate that PUMA consistently outperforms state-of-the-art baselines across diverse benchmarks, achieving a superior accuracy-efficiency trade-off and robust cross-domain generalization.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Prior work often separates generation, evaluation, and alignment, leading to misaligned optimization signals and poor real-world behavior. The paper aims to close this gap by defining anthropomorphic dialogue as a unified problem and building a framework where system design, evaluation metrics, and training rewards are behaviorally consistent.

Method: The authors design AnthroDial as a three-part closed-loop framework: (1) a role-conditioned dialogue runtime that uses persona and scenario cards, long-term memory, virtual time, and single-draft responses to simulate realistic private chats; (2) an executable benchmark that first checks basic validity (L0 gate) and then scores each turn on five behavioral dimensions and the whole dialogue on five more dimensions; and (3) a post-training pipeline that selects over sixteen thousand scheduled-decision examples for supervised fine-tuning and applies GRPO reinforcement learning with a cognitive-diagnostic, ZPD-aware reward. The best unaligned baseline achieves 32.00% strict accuracy, while their Qwen3.6-27B model with both SFT and RL (SFT+RL) achieves 39.00% strict accuracy and an overall score of 98.5. In a constrained 9B “no-think” setting, adding SFT and then RL lifts strict accuracy from 0.00% to 13.00% and 18.37%, showing substantial gains from the AnthroDial training loop.

Conclusion: Aligning the dialogue runtime, evaluation benchmark, and RL reward around the same set of behavioral dimensions yields more human-like private chat behavior than standard training or ad hoc metrics. The AnthroDial framework demonstrates that anthropomorphic dialogue benefits from closed-loop optimization with diagnostic, capability-aware rewards that focus learning on weaker, yet still learnable, skills. This suggests a promising direction for building more consistent, persona-faithful multi-turn conversational agents.

Abstract: Human-like private chat requires more than fluent response generation: a system must preserve persona, relationship, memory, bounded knowledge, medium-specific timing, and a coherent multi-turn arc. We present AnthroDial, a closed-loop framework that formulates anthropomorphic dialogue as a joint problem of system architecture, executable evaluation, and diagnostic alignment. It combines (1) a role-conditioned scheduled dialogue runtime with persona and scenario cards, long-term memory, virtual time, and single-draft message decisions; (2) an executable benchmark with an L0 validity gate, five per-turn dimensions, and five dialogue-level dimensions; and (3) a post-training pipeline that filters 16,436 scheduled-decision examples for SFT and applies GRPO with a cognitive-diagnostic, ZPD-aware reward. The reward maintains Kalman-filtered capability estimates for each behavioral dimension, upweights dimensions with larger capability deficits, and uses rollout scores as task-level ZPD matches to focus optimization on learnable weak skills. On a benchmark with 55 personas, 50 scenarios, 50 persona-scenario bindings, and 100 role-conditioned cases per model, we evaluate 16 systems spanning frontier baselines, open models, thinking/no-think variants, and SFT/RL ablations. The strongest non-trained baseline reaches 32.00% strict ACC, while Qwen3.6-27B-SFT+RL reaches 39.00% strict ACC and a 98.5 overall score. In the 9B no-think setting, SFT and RL improve strict ACC from 0.00% to 13.00% and 18.37%. These results show that anthropomorphic dialogue benefits when generation, evaluation, and reward shaping share the same behavioral dimensions.

### [113] [A Systematic Evaluation of Trajectory Data Curation for LoRA Fine-Tuning of Code Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17205)
*Yunze Han*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: This paper studies how the quality and quantity of expert trajectories affect supervised fine-tuning of open-weight code LLM agents, introducing a two-axis trajectory quality framework and showing that quality-based filtering matters increasingly at larger data scales.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To build strong code agents from open-weight LLMs using supervised fine-tuning, we need to understand how the quality and quantity of collected expert trajectories interact, particularly since collecting high-quality trajectories is expensive and 7B models perform poorly on end-to-end SWE-bench, making direct evaluation difficult.

Method: They fine-tune Qwen2.5-Coder-7B-Instruct with LoRA on subsets of the SWE-trajectory dataset under different filtering strategies guided by a two-axis trajectory quality score (Efficiency and Style). They run 16 controlled experiments varying data scale, selection strategy (e.g., TopQ vs Random), and ablations on quality sub-dimensions, and evaluate models using cross-entropy loss on held-out trajectories, validated via first-action generation and ROUGE-L correlation analysis.

Result: They find a scale-dependent trade-off between trajectory quantity and quality. At small data scales, simply adding more data (e.g., 500→1,000 trajectories) yields large improvements in CE loss, while quality-based selection has minimal benefit. At larger scales (e.g., 2,000 trajectories), quality-based selection (TopQ) outperforms random sampling by a noticeable margin (~3.6% CE-loss gap, statistically significant). Ablations show that the error-retry rate is the most important component of the quality score, nearly matching the full composite metric.

Conclusion: Trajectory-level quality scoring is a useful but scale-sensitive tool for improving SFT of code agents: quality-based filtering helps most once a moderate data size is reached. The paper also proposes using cross-entropy loss on held-out trajectories, validated via its perfect rank-correlation with ROUGE-L on first actions, as a practical evaluation protocol when full SWE-bench resolve rates are too low to be statistically informative.

Abstract: Supervised fine-tuning (SFT) of open-weight LLMs on expert agent trajectories has emerged as a prominent approach to building capable code agents without reliance on proprietary models. A central yet underexplored question is how trajectory quality and quantity jointly shape model performance. We present a systematic empirical study of trajectory data filtering for LoRA fine-tuning of Qwen2.5-Coder-7B-Instruct on the SWE-trajectory dataset (67,074 trajectories, of which 32,161 are resolved). We propose a two-axis quality scoring framework -- Efficiency and Style -- and evaluate it through 16 controlled experiments spanning strategy, scale, and ablation analyses. Since 7B-scale models attain near-zero SWE-bench resolve rates, we adopt cross-entropy (CE) loss on held-out trajectories as the primary metric, validated via first-action generation: CE loss and ROUGE-L are perfectly rank-correlated (Spearman $ρ$ = -1.00), with limited-sample evidence supporting but not conclusively establishing this proxy. Our results reveal a scale-dependent quality-quantity trade-off: at small scales, doubling the dataset (500 to 1,000) yields ~12.7% CE-loss reduction whereas the TopQ-Random gap stays <1% (Mann-Whitney p > 0.10); at 2,000 trajectories this same gap widens to 3.6% (p = 0.016). Ablation further identifies error-retry rate as the dominant sub-dimension, performing comparably to the full composite ($Δ$ < 0.2%). Together, these findings establish trajectory-level quality scoring as a viable but scale-sensitive lever for code-agent SFT and offer a proxy-validated evaluation protocol for the regime where end-to-end resolve rate is statistically infeasible.

</details>


### [114] [Constrained Path Reasoning: Measuring When Committed Stages Earn Their Cost](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17240)
*Honglin Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Constrained Path Reasoning (CPR), a framework for deciding when intermediate commitments in LLM reasoning pipelines are worth their cost, and evaluates it on optimization-style benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM reasoning often uses multi-stage pipelines with intermediate steps (plans, sketches, partial solutions), but it's unclear when committing to these intermediate states improves overall performance relative to their cost and potential error propagation. The paper aims to formalize and measure the value of such commitments.

Method: The authors define Constrained Path Reasoning (CPR), where a reasoning path consists of provisional states, some of which can be hardened into commitments via trusted invariants or validations. They introduce stage-level accounting that tracks effective branching factor, endpoint concentration, cost per usable output, and error propagation. They then experimentally instantiate CPR on quadratic constrained quadratic programs (QCQPs) and degenerate polynomial instances, using LLM-generated states plus various repair, formalization, convexification, and rollback strategies, and compare different commitment policies.

Result: On 1,180 generated QCQPs and 40 engineered polynomial instances (2,140 endpoints), a residual triage strategy recovers 63% of the additional feasible solutions (yield) that a full repair-all strategy would find, while using only 17.7% as many attempts. With a fixed budget of 270 distinct LLM calls shared across pipeline branches, direct LLM solutions yield 41.1% usable outputs, which rises to 90% when solutions are formalized and deterministically executed, 20% under one-shot convexification, and 21.1% for the complete multi-stage path. In 120 paired-condition runs, a simple two-action rollback policy achieves 90% usable yield compared to 36.7% for a more complex feedback-conditioned selector. Endpoint probing experiments further show that mixing trajectories (cross-trajectory transplant) and self-proposals affect entropy, acceptable solution mass, and collision rates, illustrating how source models and validation mechanisms interact.

Conclusion: Intermediate commitments in LLM reasoning pipelines can significantly improve efficiency and solution quality when they are compatible with the task and supported by trusted validation or execution, as quantified by the CPR framework. Committing at the right stages focuses search, reduces entropy, and improves usable yield per cost, while poorly chosen or weakly validated commitments can propagate errors. CPR offers a principled way to design and evaluate multi-stage LLM reasoning systems with explicit accounting for cost, branching, and validation strength.

Abstract: When does a committed intermediate stage in an LLM reasoning pipeline earn its cost? Constrained Path Reasoning (CPR) pairs a source-aware path hypothesis with stage-level accounting. Search generates provisional states; trusted or validated invariants can constrain hard, while other proposals remain soft and revisable. CPR predicts that task-compatible commitments can factor transitions, concentrate candidate mass, induce regularity, and expose feedback when their gains exceed propagated error and execution cost. The formalism covers discrete commitments and continuous flows and measures effective branching, endpoint concentration, and cost per usable output. Across 1,180 generated QCQPs and 40 engineered degenerate polynomial instances (2,140 endpoints), residual triage recovers 63.0% of repair-all's additional feasible yield with 17.7% of its attempts. Fixed-LLM accounting (270 unique calls shared across nested arms) finds usable yield of 41.1% direct, 90.0% after formalization and deterministic execution, 20.0% after one-shot convexification, and 21.1% for the full path. In 120 paired-condition calls, a two-action rollback rule reaches 90% usable yield versus 36.7% for the feedback-conditioned selector. Two endpoint probes separate source from validation: a 72-output cross-trajectory transplant reduces entropy and acceptable mass; a 24-output same-call self-proposal pilot gives unchanged two-repeat collision entropy, 25.0% versus 8.3% usable yield, and 1/8 deterministically confirmed endpoint checks. Model-generated states supply hypotheses; trusted execution earns constraint strength.

### [115] [LenGuard-GPC: Length Guarding with Guided-Prompt Consistency for Spatial Reasoning Reinforce Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17243)
*Xingjian Tao,Yiwei Wang,Yujun Cai,Jing Tang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes LenGuard-GPC, a dense-reward reinforcement learning framework that improves multi-view spatial reasoning in vision-language models by aligning guided and unguided reasoning distributions while controlling response length.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-view spatial reasoning requires models to compare and integrate information across multiple images and long visual contexts. Existing chain-of-thought approaches often become unnecessarily verbose without improving accuracy. Standard GRPO reinforcement learning uses sparse, outcome-level rewards, providing no feedback on where a reasoning trajectory fails and no mechanism to control reasoning length. A better training signal is needed that is both dense (token-level) and length-aware, to improve spatial reasoning while avoiding unhelpful verbosity.

Method: The authors introduce LenGuard-GPC, a dense reward framework for GRPO-style reinforcement learning. For each sampled reasoning trajectory, they run the model under two prompts: a standard prompt and a guided prompt. They compute token-wise predictive distributions for each trajectory and then measure a token-sum KL divergence between the two distributions. This KL serves as a dense reward signal, indicating how closely the reasoning under one prompt aligns with the other. Because summing KL per token inherently favors shorter sequences (less total KL), they add a staged length bonus that encourages responses to fall within a desired length range rather than simply being as short as possible. This combines distributional guidance with explicit control of reasoning length.

Result: On six multi-view spatial reasoning benchmarks, LenGuard-GPC outperforms vanilla GRPO in terms of task accuracy while also producing shorter average responses. This indicates that the method improves both effectiveness (better reasoning) and efficiency (less verbosity) compared with standard sparse-reward GRPO training.

Conclusion: LenGuard-GPC shows that dense, token-level KL-based rewards between guided and standard prompts, combined with a staged length bonus, can effectively train vision-language models for multi-view spatial reasoning. It mitigates the shortcomings of sparse GRPO rewards, yielding higher accuracy and more concise reasoning, and demonstrates that controlling reasoning length and improving correctness can be addressed jointly within a reinforcement learning framework.

Abstract: Multi-view spatial reasoning requires vision-language models to compare visual evidence across images, align object correspondences, and infer spatial relations over long visual contexts, a setting where chain-of-thought reasoning tends to grow verbose without becoming more accurate. Reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards is a natural fit for this task, but standard GRPO reward relies on sparse outcome-level feedback and gives no signal about where a reasoning trajectory goes wrong, nor any control over its length. We propose LenGuard-GPC, a dense reward framework that addresses both problems together. For each sampled trajectory, it compares the token-wise predictive distributions under a standard prompt and a guided prompt, and uses the resulting token-sum KL divergence as a dense reward signal. Since this KL penalty accumulates over tokens and would otherwise reward shorter responses regardless of their quality, we introduce a staged length bonus that keeps reasoning length within a controlled range without simply encouraging brevity. On six multi-view spatial reasoning benchmarks, LenGuard-GPC improves accuracy over vanilla GRPO while reducing average response length.

### [116] [Coordinated Disentanglement with Iterative Mode Discovery Under Hidden Correlations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17264)
*Rong Hu,Ling Chen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces CoDID, a framework that jointly discovers latent modes in data and enforces mode-based conditional independence to achieve more robust disentangled representations, while preventing error amplification via a coordination mechanism with meta-optimization.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing disentangled representation learning methods struggle with hidden correlations: cases where, under a fixed value of an attribute, the data still contain latent modes that correlate with other attributes. These hidden correlations can harm robust attribute prediction and disentanglement, and prior work has not adequately modeled or preserved such mode information. There is a need for a framework that can explicitly discover these modes while still enforcing disentanglement constraints.

Method: The paper proposes Coordinated Disentanglement with Iterative mode Discovery (CoDID), an end-to-end framework that: (1) jointly discovers latent modes within each attribute value and (2) enforces mode-based conditional independence to promote disentanglement. The architecture is dynamic, adapting to the evolving number of discovered modes during training. To avoid error amplification caused by the mutual dependence between mode discovery and disentanglement, CoDID introduces a coordination mechanism based on meta-optimization, which stabilizes the iterative updates of these modules.

Result: Empirical evaluations on diverse tasks show that CoDID achieves state-of-the-art performance, outperforming existing disentangled representation learning methods, particularly in robust attribute prediction under correlated and hidden-correlated settings.

Conclusion: CoDID effectively handles hidden correlations in disentangled representation learning by discovering latent modes and enforcing mode-based conditional independence in a coordinated, meta-optimized fashion. This leads to more robust attribute prediction and improved disentanglement across various benchmarks, highlighting the importance of explicitly modeling latent modes tied to attribute values.

Abstract: Disentangled representation learning is a powerful paradigm for robust attribute prediction. While recent methods address attribute correlations, hidden correlations remain underexplored, where data under the value of a certain attribute exhibit underlying modes correlated with other attributes. To preserve mode information and achieve disentanglement, we jointly discover modes and enforce mode-based conditional independence. Yet, the interdependency between these two modules may lead to error amplification under naive iterations. We propose Coordinated Disentanglement with Iterative mode Discovery (CoDID), an end-to-end framework featuring a dynamic architecture that adapts to evolving number of modes, and a coordination mechanism that mitigates error amplification via meta-optimization. Empirical results demonstrate the state-of-the-art performance on diverse tasks.

### [117] [An Explicit World Model Based on Data-First Ontology: DaoQL Multimodal Storage Validation and Counterfactual Reasoning Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17269)
*Zhanbo Li,Shifeng Wu,Xiangjin Meng,Wenjie Cai*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs currently embed world knowledge implicitly in their parameters, which causes issues in high-precision domains like medicine and finance. These issues include hallucinations, outdated or frozen knowledge, difficulty in explaining answers, and difficulty in updating or constraining the model’s knowledge. The authors want a way to keep the expressive reasoning power of LLMs while obtaining explicit, verifiable, and updatable world models with strong structural guarantees for counterfactual reasoning.

Method: They introduce a “data-first ontology” where the world model is encoded explicitly in DaoQL, a multimodal database that integrates graph, columnar, vector, and full-text stores in a single process. The LLM is used only as a reasoning/language layer on top. Formally, they define conditions (rule independence, deterministic evaluation, fixed conflict resolution) under which explicit world models guarantee composable counterfactual decomposability. They implement DaoQL’s verified storage layer and explicit evaluation (Eval) path, benchmark graph operations, HNSW retrieval, and hybrid queries, and run experiments on LDBC SNB and ANN-Benchmarks. They also run a multi-domain counterfactual reasoning experiment comparing DaoQL+GPT-4o vs GPT-4o alone.

Result: Theoretically, they show that explicit world models with certain properties provide sufficient conditions for composable counterfactual decomposability, whereas implicit LLM-based models lack the atomic read/delta semantics needed for such guarantees. System-wise, DaoQL achieves low-latency graph BFS (~1.20 ms), HNSW (~83.1 µs), and hybrid queries (~105.8 µs) in an embedded same-machine setup, with good coverage and performance profiles on LDBC SNB SF1 and high recall and throughput on ANN-Benchmarks after some engineering fixes. In counterfactual experiments across five domains (n=1250), DaoQL+GPT-4o reaches 94% composable counterfactual decomposability, 49 percentage points higher than GPT-4o alone.

Conclusion: Explicit multimodal databases like DaoQL, when used as the primary world model with LLMs as reasoning front-ends, can provide strong structural guarantees (e.g., composable counterfactual decomposability) that implicit LLM-only approaches cannot. The current implementation demonstrates promising engineering performance but is still incomplete (e.g., KVCache graphs, expert hot updates, and DaoQL-Agent runtime remain future work). The paper emphasizes the distinction between formally provable properties, early empirical evidence, and planned architectural directions, arguing that explicit, data-first ontologies are a promising path to safer, more controllable AI systems in high-precision domains.

Abstract: Large language models encode world models implicitly in neural weights, which exposes four structural risks in high-precision domains such as medicine and finance: hallucination, frozen knowledge, poor explainability, and poor modifiability. This paper proposes data-first ontology: LLMs are treated as reasoning and language engines, while deterministic knowledge is moved into an explicit multimodal database, DaoQL. We formalize an explicit world model and show that, under rule independence, deterministic evaluation, and fixed conflict resolution, explicit models provide a sufficient condition for composable counterfactual decomposability; implicit models lack atomic read/delta semantics and therefore provide no comparable architectural guarantee. The implemented system focuses on DaoQL's verified storage layer and explicit Eval path, integrating graph, column, vector, and full-text engines within one process. KVCache graph nodes, expert hot updates, and the DaoQL-Agent runtime remain future work. On an embedded same-machine setup, DaoQL reports graph BFS at 1.20 ms, HNSW at 83.1 us, and a Fluent hybrid query at 105.8 us; these results indicate engineering potential but must be interpreted with deployment-shape differences from client-server systems. Exploratory measurements on LDBC SNB SF1 and ANN-Benchmarks further show 34/34 query coverage with interactive-class queries mostly in the sub-millisecond to millisecond range, but only 1.8 QPS overall due to long-tail BI/IC queries; ANN-Benchmarks reaches Recall@10 >= 99% at thousand-level QPS after a bridge-edge protection fix. In a five-domain counterfactual experiment (n = 1250), DaoQL+GPT-4o achieves 94% composable counterfactual decomposability, 49 percentage points above GPT-4o alone. The paper explicitly separates provable structure, preliminary empirical evidence, and architectural roadmap claims.

### [118] [Lossless but Not Free: An Empirical Anatomy of Speculative Decoding on Consumer Hardware](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17283)
*Param Chordiya*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper implements and empirically evaluates speculative decoding for large language models on Apple-silicon laptops, showing speedups are achieved only when the verification pass is truly batch-parallel and the draft model is significantly faster than the target.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Single-stream autoregressive decoding is bottlenecked by memory bandwidth and cannot parallelize token generation across time steps. Speculative decoding promises speedups by using a small draft model and batched verification, but its real-world performance and constraints, especially on consumer hardware like Apple silicon with different backends (CUDA/MPS/CPU/Metal), are not well understood.

Method: They implement a from-scratch, device-agnostic speculative decoding framework that works across CUDA, MPS, and CPU, pairing different draft and target models/backends in five configurations. The draft model proposes K tokens in an autoregressive fashion; the target model verifies them in a single batched forward pass using a rejection-sampling rule that preserves the original distribution. They empirically measure speed, acceptance rates, and distributional equivalence, including a chi-squared two-sample test over ~9,200 tokens and checks of greedy sequence agreement. They also analyze backend behavior, especially how Metal executes batched verification.

Result: They empirically confirm that speculative decoding can preserve the target model’s output distribution, with a chi-squared statistic of 162.5 on 200 degrees of freedom (p = 0.976) and exact agreement in greedy decoding. The best draft/target configuration achieves a 1.61x wall-clock speedup at K=6, with acceptance rates dropping from 69.7% at K=1 to 37.8% at K=6. However, in three of the five configurations, speculative decoding slows down generation due to insufficient latency gap between draft and target or because the Metal backend effectively runs the "parallel" verification serially.

Conclusion: Speculative decoding is not universally beneficial: it yields speedups only when the verification pass is truly parallelized in hardware and when the draft model is meaningfully faster than the target. On certain backends and model-size pairings, overheads and pseudo-serial execution can negate or reverse the gains. Their implementation and measurements clarify the practical conditions under which speculative decoding is worthwhile on consumer hardware.

Abstract: Single-stream autoregressive decoding of large language models is bound by memory bandwidth: each generated token requires one full forward pass through the target model, and successive passes cannot be parallelized. Speculative decoding restructures this computation: a small draft model proposes $K$ tokens autoregressively, the target model scores all of them in one batched pass, and a rejection-sampling rule provably preserves the target model's output distribution. We present a from-scratch, device-agnostic (CUDA/MPS/CPU) implementation and an empirical study across five draft/target backend configurations on a consumer Apple-silicon laptop. Distribution equivalence is verified at three levels, culminating in a two-sample test over roughly 9,200 real-model tokens per method ($χ^2 = 162.5$, dof $= 200$, $p = 0.976$) and exact greedy-sequence agreement. The best configuration reaches a measured $1.61\times$ wall-clock speedup at $K=6$, on an acceptance profile declining from 69.7% at $K=1$ to 37.8% at the optimum, while three of five configurations decelerate, either because the draft fails to out-speed a small target or because the quantized Metal backend executes "parallel" verification serially, an effect we isolate and quantify. The failures are as instructive as the successes: speculative decoding pays off only when verification is genuinely batch-parallel and the draft/target latency gap is real.

### [119] [Learning-Driven Adaptive Audit Scheduling: A Sequential Decision Approach to Off-Chain Data Integrity](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17305)
*Changting Lin,Fan Li,Weihang Yu,Keyang He,Mingyuan Yan,Yourong Chen,Meng Han*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper formulates secure auditing of off-chain storage as a constrained POMDP and introduces DRQN-CMDP, a recurrent deep RL method with automatic constraint tuning that achieves low gas cost, low miss rate, and acceptable detection latency, outperforming baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Off-chain data storage (e.g., for blockchains) needs to be audited cryptographically to ensure nodes are not corrupt or misbehaving, but frequent audits are expensive (on-chain gas cost), while infrequent audits increase the risk of undetected corruption and long detection delays. Existing strategies (fixed schedules, simple heuristics, or standard RL without constraints) struggle to jointly optimize economic cost, security (miss rate), and responsiveness (detection latency) in a partially observable setting where the node’s true behavior type and corruption status are hidden. The authors aim to design an auditing policy that balances these conflicting objectives in a principled way while handling partial observability and an explicit security constraint.

Method: The authors formulate the auditing problem as a Constrained Markov Decision Process with partial observability, effectively a POMDP with an added constraint on the miss rate (rho). They model the storage node’s hidden type and corruption state as latent variables. To solve this, they propose DRQN-CMDP, which combines: (1) a Deep Recurrent Q-Network using a GRU layer to maintain an implicit belief state over the unobserved node type; (2) a Lagrangian dual ascent mechanism that adjusts the penalty coefficient lambda associated with violating the miss-rate constraint, thereby enforcing the constraint over time; and (3) a pairing-free homomorphic MAC construction that enables constant-cost (O(1)) on-chain verification of audits. They empirically compare DRQN-CMDP against 12 alternatives, including multiple DQN variants, policy-gradient methods (PPO, A2C), a Lagrangian version of PPO, a Bayesian heuristic, simple fixed-rule baselines, and an oracle-based heuristic.

Result: Empirical evaluation shows that DRQN-CMDP delivers substantially reduced on-chain gas expenditure—about 83% lower than a naive fixed high-frequency auditing strategy—while keeping the miss rate in the single digits (around 7.5%) and maintaining only moderate detection latency. No other tested method achieves comparably strong performance on all three metrics simultaneously; other approaches either cost much more gas, allow higher miss rates, or suffer from larger detection delays.

Conclusion: Treating cryptographic auditing of off-chain data as a constrained, partially observable control problem enables principled policy design. The proposed DRQN-CMDP architecture, which combines recurrent deep RL with Lagrangian constraint handling and efficient homomorphic-MAC verification, achieves a superior trade-off between economic cost, security (miss rate), and detection latency compared with a broad set of baselines. This suggests that constraint-aware recurrent RL is a promising approach for secure and cost-efficient auditing in decentralized storage and similar systems.

Abstract: We model cryptographic auditing of off-chain data as a Constrained MDP (CMDP) under partial observability: the storage node's hidden type and corruption state make the problem a POMDP, while a miss-rate ceiling rho imposes an explicit security constraint. We propose DRQN-CMDP, a Deep Recurrent Q-Network whose GRU layer maintains a belief over the latent node type, paired with Lagrangian dual ascent that adapts the miss-rate penalty lambda automatically. A pairing-free homomorphic-MAC primitive supplies O(1) on-chain verification cost. Across 13 methods--four DQN variants, PPO, A2C, PPO-Lagrangian, a stateful Bayesian heuristic, three fixed-rule baselines, and an oracle-informed heuristic--DRQN-CMDP achieves a favourable balance: 83% lower gas than fixed high-frequency auditing, single-digit miss rate (7.5%), and moderate detection latency--a combination no other method matches across all three objectives simultaneously.

### [120] [Is Progressive Disclosure All You Need for Long-Context Agents?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17598)
*Yifeng He,Yinzhe Zhao,Jicheng Wang,Hao Chen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper empirically evaluates “progressive disclosure” agent skills—structured, on‑demand access to long documents—as an alternative to raw-document navigation and classical retrieval for long‑document QA.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing long-document QA systems either load whole documents into the context window (wasting context and limiting scalability) or bolt on a separate retriever (adding complexity and rigidity). With agentic AI, agents can actively navigate documents, and a common emerging pattern—progressive disclosure via Agent Skills—has been widely used in practice for book‑length understanding, but only supported by anecdotal evidence. There is a need for a controlled, systematic evaluation of whether and when this pattern truly improves performance over standard approaches.

Method: The authors implement and compare several system designs for long‑document QA: (1) direct raw-document navigation by an agent, (2) multiple variants of Agent Skills packs implementing progressive disclosure in one or more levels (from high‑level descriptions down to specific passages), and (3) a traditional hybrid retriever. They test these configurations across three different agent harnesses and three LLM families on InfiniteBench, first on tasks involving a single book and then on tasks spanning multiple books. They also ablate the depth of routing levels in progressive disclosure to study its effect on accuracy.

Result: On a single book, the benefit of progressive disclosure is harness‑dependent: when the agent harness is weak at navigating raw documents, skills-based progressive disclosure yields substantial accuracy gains, but when a strong harness already segments and retrieves well, the improvement is negligible. When scaling to multi‑book tasks, raw-document navigation performance degrades sharply (“collapses”), while a one‑level progressive disclosure design degrades more slowly and surpasses it. Introducing a second, deeper routing level in the skills hierarchy does not improve results and can significantly harm accuracy.

Conclusion: Progressive disclosure via Agent Skills primarily provides better context management rather than new reasoning capabilities: it helps the agent access the right passages when the corpus becomes too large to read directly but adds little benefit when the agent harness already performs strong retrieval over a small document. A single level of progressive disclosure is sufficient and more robust, while deeper routing hierarchies are unnecessary and potentially detrimental. Thus, for scalable long‑document QA—especially over many books—one‑level progressive disclosure is an effective and practical design choice over raw-document navigation or over‑engineered multi‑level skills structures.

Abstract: Long-document question answering usually forces a choice between loading the whole document into the context window and bolting on a separate retriever. Agentic AI suggests a broader option, giving the agent the document path and letting it decide how and what to read. Agent Skills, a standard for packaging expertise into folders an agent loads on demand, supply a ready mechanism: progressive disclosure, which exposes only what a query needs, from a short description down to the specific passages. Practitioners rapidly adopted this pattern for book-length understanding tasks, but the evidence to support such choices has been anecdotal. We run the first controlled study of the pattern, comparing raw-document navigation and several designs of Agent Skills packs against a classical hybrid retriever across three agent harnesses and three model families on InfiniteBench. On a single book, the gain depends on the harness, running large when the agent navigates the raw document poorly but near zero when a strong agent harness already divides and retrieves on its own. When scaling up to tasks that span many books, raw-document navigation collapses while one-level progressive disclosure degrades more slowly and pulls ahead. A second, deeper routing level never helps and sometimes breaks accuracy outright, so one level is enough. Progressive disclosure buys context, not intelligence: it is redundant while a strong agent can locate the right passages itself, and decisive once the corpus grows too large to navigate by reading.

Abstract: Enterprise Resource Planning (ERP) systems record transactions reliably but still delegate almost all operational decision-making to human specialists, because classical rule-based automation cannot reason about exceptions and monolithic AI assistants degrade when asked to coordinate across functional boundaries. Second, a graph-based Planner--Executor--Reflector--Responder orchestration decouples generation from evaluation through externalised grading criteria and sprint contracts, packaging recent harness-engineering principles as inspectable expert-system artefacts. Third, the system is evaluated at three levels: a scenario-based task suite, a comprehensive comparison of six orchestration paradigms on cross-functional crisis tasks, and a 365-day agent-in-the-loop simulation against rule-based RPA and no-intervention baselines. Across these levels the proposed multi-agent method is significantly better than the baseline, and the system sustains a simulated year of operation with zero stockouts while the rule-based baseline accumulates hundreds under the same demand stream. The work shows that role-aligned LLM agents under human oversight can move an ERP system from passively recording transactions to actively executing operational decisions, and it provides a reference architecture and an evaluation protocol for autonomous enterprise resource planning.

### [122] [WorldCupArena: Fine-Grained Evaluation of Language Models and Deep-Research Agents on Football Forecasting](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18084)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing benchmarks rarely test language models in real-time, dynamic, and uncertain environments with external evidence that changes over time; football matches offer a natural testbed for forward-looking probabilistic reasoning beyond static question answering.

Method: Before each World Cup match, models either receive a standardized evidence package or perform their own web-style search, then predict match result and score, players and events, match statistics, and tournament outcomes; after matches finish, predictions are scored with multiple metrics including result accuracy, exact-score accuracy, and a graded scoreline metric, plus task-specific metrics for detailed predictions, across 104 matches and 13 systems.

Result: Across systems with similar win/draw/loss accuracy, the benchmark reveals clear differences in fine-grained predictions; the best model slightly outperforms betting-market and human-fan baselines on result and exact-score accuracy, and more clearly on the scoreline metric.

Conclusion: WorldCupArena offers a reusable, extensible framework for dynamically evaluating present and future language models on real-world sports competitions, with open-source code and assets enabling ongoing, forward-looking assessment without data leakage from already-known outcomes.

Abstract: Predicting a football match before kickoff requires more than knowing past results: a model must use changing information and make a clear prediction before the answer is available. We present WorldCupArena, a dynamic benchmark for language models and deep-research agents. The 2026 FIFA World Cup is its first evaluation, and the same process can be reused for future leagues and cups. Before each match, a model either receives a common evidence package or searches for information itself. It predicts the result and score, likely players and events, match statistics, and the outcome of the competition. After the match, these predictions are compared with the recorded result. We report result accuracy, exact-score accuracy, and a scoreline score that gives some credit when a predicted score is close but not exact, together with scores for the other prediction tasks. Across 104 matches and 13 systems, models with similar result accuracy differ more clearly on detailed predictions. Compared with betting-market and human-fan baselines, the best system shows only small gains in result and exact-score accuracy, but a clearer gain in Scoreline. New schedules can be added as they begin, allowing the benchmark to evaluate future models without using outcomes that are already known. Code, prompts, predictions, and evaluation scripts are open sourced at https://github.com/wzk1015/WorldCupArena.

</details>


### [123] [DeeperRadar: End-to-End MIMO Radar Design and Multi-Modal Fusion for Autonomous Vehicle Perception](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17351)
*Eli Goldenshluger,Barak Pinkovich,Chaim Baskin*

  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need to reduce radar hardware cost and complexity while preserving or improving detection performance in autonomous driving. The authors aim to learn radar MIMO configurations that are tailored to the specific fusion architecture and downstream detection task instead of relying on hand-crafted arrays.

Method: They propose DeeperRadar, a radar-centric framework that integrates a learnable MIMO design module into a multi-modal fusion network. The network consumes raw radar ADC data, camera images, and LiDAR point clouds. After training, the design module is removed and replaced with the learned sparse subsampling mask, while the rest of the detection network remains unchanged.

Result: On the RADIal dataset, DeeperRadar learns sparse, task-aware radar receiver configurations that achieve detection performance comparable to or better than full-array baselines, despite using fewer receiver antennas. Conclusion: The work shows that optimal radar MIMO configurations are not universal but depend on the specific sensor fusion stack and perception task. By co-designing radar sensing and multi-modal 3D detection in an end-to-end fashion, it is possible to discover cost-efficient, task-aware sparse radar designs that maintain or improve performance, suggesting a new paradigm for joint hardware-software design in autonomous mobility.

Abstract: DeeperRadar is a radar-centric, sensor-stack-conditioned framework that co-designs radar sensing and multi-modal 3D detection for autonomous mobility by learning a sparse acquisition pattern end-to-end with the fusion model. A learnable MIMO design module is trained end-to-end within a fusion network that operates directly on raw radar ADC data together with camera images and LiDAR point clouds. During training, the design module is supervised by the other sensors, enabling the system to learn both which receiver antennas to activate and the effective number of them. At deployment, the design module is removed and replaced by the learned sparse subsampling mask, leaving the downstream model architecture unchanged. Evaluated on the RADIal dataset, DeeperRadar discovers sparse, task-aware radar configurations that match or exceed full-array baselines while using fewer receivers, potentially reducing radar cost and integration complexity. These results show that learned optimal MIMO radar design depends on the fusion stack and the downstream perception task.

### [124] [Logical Judgments Under Pressure: Diagnosing Syllogistic Stability with Learned Soft Prefixes](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18228)
*Brian K Chen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how learned contextual signals, implemented as trainable soft prefixes, can disrupt otherwise correct logical reasoning in large language models, revealing model-specific limits in logical stability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although modern language models can solve syllogistic reasoning benchmarks when prompted neutrally, their judgments can be highly sensitive to contextual framing and prompt wording. The authors want to understand how robust these logical abilities really are when the surrounding context pushes toward biased answers, and what kinds of internal changes such contextual pressure induces. This helps clarify whether models are performing stable logical reasoning or merely following surface preferences shaped by context.

Method: The authors keep several LLMs fixed (Qwen3.6-35B-A3B MoE, Qwen3-8B, Gemma 4 31B) and prepend learned soft prefixes—continuous, non-interpretable vectors—to inputs for a labeled syllogistic reasoning benchmark. They train prefixes to push models toward certain outputs (e.g., preferring one logical answer meaning) without changing model weights. They then evaluate how often these prefixes flip previously correct answers, and test generalization to unseen logical forms, wording variations, and interface changes. Diagnostic experiments compare learned prefixes to random controls and use simple score models to see how well prefix-induced behavior can be predicted.

Result: Learned soft prefixes substantially redirect models’ previously correct syllogistic judgments, often generalizing across unseen logical forms and interface variations. For Qwen3.6 MoE and Gemma, trained prefixes outperform random controls by 37–99 percentage points across 16 model–direction–split comparisons. Qwen3.6 MoE shows high flip rates (72–90%) across wordings and prompts, while Gemma’s validity-oriented prefixes still flip 54–56% of answers, versus under 1% for random prefixes. Diagnostic tests indicate that prefixes mainly induce a broad preference for one answer meaning across many items rather than enforcing specific token patterns or portable logical operations. The nature and predictability of this bias differ across models.

Conclusion: Soft prefixes can reliably override correct logical judgments in large language models through contextual pressure alone, without modifying model weights. Their primary effect is to impose a broad answer preference, demonstrating that model reasoning on syllogistic tasks is not logically stable but highly context-sensitive. The behavior and predictability of these biases vary by model family, revealing model-specific patterns of logical instability and challenging the view that strong performance on reasoning benchmarks reflects robust, context-independent logical competence.

Abstract: To test how correct logical judgments respond to learned context, we prepend a soft prefix to an exactly labeled syllogistic reasoning benchmark while keeping the model fixed. Soft prefixes are opaque continuous vectors, so we characterize them through the behavior they induce across controlled variations in logical form and interface. By studying which prefixes succeed and how their effects generalize, we characterize how learned contextual pressure can override correct judgments and expose limits in a model's logical stability. Across Qwen3.6-35B-A3B MoE, Qwen3-8B, and Gemma 4 31B, learned prefixes redirect many correct answers and remain effective across unseen forms and interface changes. In repeated tests with Qwen3.6 MoE and Gemma, they outperform paired random controls in all 16 model--direction--split comparisons by 37 to 99 percentage points. Qwen3.6 MoE flip rates remain between 72% and 90% across wording and prompt changes, while Gemma validity prefixes retain 54% to 56% flip compared with less than 1% for matched random prefixes. Diagnostic tests show that the dominant effect is a broad preference for one answer meaning rather than fixed-symbol forcing or a logical operation that transfers reliably between tasks. The form of this bias differs across models. In both Qwen models, simple score models often predict which judgments will flip but not how far their margins will move, whereas Gemma's overall response is more closely approximated by the same models. These results show that the dominant behavioral effect of successful soft prefixes is a broad answer preference, while the remaining response reveals substantial model-specific differences in logical stability.

### [125] [Self-Modifying Lean Proof Agents with Verifier-Grounded Benchmark Coevolution](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17352)
*Yuqing Li,Zeguan Wu,Yu Gan,Junyu Liu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces a self-evolving Lean proof agent whose workflow, prompts, and tools can rewrite themselves while remaining Lean-verified, and shows that coevolving the agent with its benchmark significantly improves proof success rates.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Designing effective automated agents for the Lean theorem prover is hard not only because proofs are complex, but because the entire workflow—how the agent decomposes tasks, uses tools and feedback, and repairs proofs—matters a lot. Existing systems mostly rely on hand-crafted workflows and optimize models against fixed benchmarks, which may limit adaptability and long-term improvement. Inspired by self-evolving coding agents, the authors want to know if a Lean proof agent can automatically evolve its own workflow while remaining grounded in formal verification, and whether coevolving the evaluation benchmark can lead to stronger provers than training on a static benchmark.

Method: They build a Lean proof agent with a minimal, trusted runtime that wraps a fully mutable workspace. In this workspace, the agent can change its own proof workflow, prompting strategies, and tools across generations. Evolution proceeds across generations where multiple variants are evaluated on proof tasks. Instead of a fixed task set, the system coevolves the benchmark: the best-performing agent (the champion) updates the task distribution via a mastery-throttled curriculum that only introduces harder proofs when easier ones are largely solved. To keep scores comparable as the task set becomes harder, they use a single-anchor recalibration: the champion is re-run on the updated benchmark to adjust scoring. All candidate agents must operate inside a Lean-grounded verification loop: success is counted only when Lean verifies their proofs under a fixed, trusted Lean snapshot, and every proof attempt must output a machine-readable Lean proof context whose format can change but whose semantic grounding in Lean is enforced.

Result: They run two main evolutionary trajectories for 15 generations: one where the agent and benchmark coevolve under the curriculum and recalibration scheme, and a baseline where the benchmark is fixed. Performance is evaluated on a held-out miniF2F test split. The seed agent achieves a 12.7% solve rate. The best fixed-benchmark agent improves this to 32.0%. The coevolving setup performs best: its top agent reaches a 45.1% solve rate on the same held-out test set, indicating a substantial improvement from self-evolution under a coevolving curriculum compared to both the initial agent and the fixed-benchmark evolution.

Conclusion: The study concludes that verifier-grounded self-evolution of a Lean proof agent’s workflow, combined with a coevolving benchmark and curriculum, can significantly improve theorem-proving performance. By tightly coupling agent evolution with Lean’s formal verification loop and gradually increasing task difficulty only after mastery, the system can autonomously discover more effective proof strategies than those obtained under static benchmarks or hand-designed workflows. This suggests a promising direction for building increasingly capable formal reasoning agents whose behavior and evaluation environment co-adapt over time while remaining formally grounded.

Abstract: Designing effective Lean proof agents is a central challenge in formal mathematical reasoning. Beyond building stronger provers, recent work emphasizes the workflow around Lean: how an agent decomposes proof obligations, uses tools and compiler feedback, diagnoses failures, repairs proofs, and maintains structured proof context. Motivated by code-level self-evolving agents, we study whether such workflows can be evolved rather than hand-designed. We present a self-evolving Lean proof agent in which a small fixed, trusted runtime wraps a fully mutable workspace: the proof workflow, prompts, and tools. Unlike most self-evolving systems, which optimize against a fixed external benchmark, our system coevolves the agent and its benchmark. Between generations, the highest-scoring agent (the champion) revises the active task distribution through a mastery-throttled curriculum update that introduces harder proof obligations only after the current level is mastered, and a single-anchor recalibration re-runs the champion on the updated benchmark to keep scores comparable as difficulty rises. All evolution stays inside a Lean-grounded verification loop: however the agent rewrites itself, a success counts only when its behavior yields Lean-verified proofs under a trusted snapshot, and each attempt must emit a machine-readable, Lean-grounded proof context whose representation may evolve but whose groundedness is enforced. We run the coevolving trajectory and a fixed-benchmark baseline for 15 active generations and compare them on a held-out miniF2F test split. The best coevolving agent reaches a 45.1% held-out solve rate, versus 12.7% for the seed and 32.0% for the best fixed-benchmark agent, showing that verifier-grounded self-evolution can improve Lean proof workflows under a coevolving benchmark.

### [126] [Quantifying Diversity of Thought: A Predictive Law of Weighted LLM Ensemble Lift](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17384)
*Junade Ali*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces and experimentally validates a simple law to predict how much performance gain (“uplift”) you get by ensembling LLMs with diverse reasoning, and shows which statistics of the models’ correctness best forecast ensemble lift.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM ensembles often outperform single models, but the factors that determine how much uplift an ensemble will provide, especially due to diversity of thought, are poorly understood and lack a principled quantitative law. Practitioners currently have to rely on brute-force experiments or vague intuitions about correlation and diversity when choosing models and weighting schemes. The authors aim to derive a formal, experimentally grounded relationship between pairwise correctness statistics of models and the resulting ensemble performance, so that ensemble construction can be predicted and optimized without exhaustive trial-and-error.

Method: From first principles, the authors derive an exact decomposition of the performance difference between an LLM ensemble and its constituent models into two components: “rescue mass” (cases where the ensemble corrects an individual model’s error) and “damage mass” (cases where the ensemble introduces an error). This yields a compact heuristic law for ensemble uplift expressed in terms of a few measurable quantities: an accuracy-adjusted correctness correlation metric φ_adj, the accuracy gap between models, and their collective accuracy. They compute these metrics on large-scale experiments: 767,520 inferences from ten open-weight models across two graduate-level science benchmarks (SuperGPQA and GPQA Diamond), plus a new agentic cybersecurity benchmark where models perform multi-turn digital forensics with tools in a sandbox (23,520 graded trials including abstentions). They calibrate the heuristic on SuperGPQA at a specified vote split (40:60), then freeze the coefficients and test predictive performance on unseen datasets, assessing correlations (Spearman’s ρ) and variance explained (R^2) between predicted and realized uplift and swap masses.

Result: The derived law yields a simple heuristic that, once calibrated on SuperGPQA with a 40:60 ensemble vote split, predicts ensemble lift on that calibration set with Spearman’s ρ = 0.84. With coefficients frozen, the same heuristic transfers to two unseen datasets, achieving ρ = 0.51 on GPQA Diamond and ρ = 0.84 on the forensic cybersecurity benchmark. The measured swap mass (difference between rescue and damage) aligns very closely with realized lift across datasets, with R^2 ≥ 0.96. The widely used raw correctness correlation φ has very low predictive power for uplift (R^2 ≤ 0.09), whereas the proposed accuracy-adjusted φ_adj is substantially more informative (R^2 = 0.67 on SuperGPQA). Combining φ_adj with accuracy gap and collective accuracy in the authors’ heuristic provides the most stable pre-pooling predictor of ensemble performance across all three datasets.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that the uplift from LLM ensembles can be accurately and compactly characterized by a formal law that decomposes ensemble performance into rescue and damage masses, leading to a practically useful heuristic for predicting ensemble gains. Simple pairwise correctness correlation φ is inadequate as a predictor of ensemble benefit, but an accuracy-adjusted version φ_adj, together with accuracy gap and collective accuracy, reliably forecasts ensemble lift both in conventional QA benchmarks and in more complex agentic cybersecurity tasks. This establishes a principled, empirically validated framework for selecting and combining LLMs based on measurable diversity-of-thought statistics, reducing reliance on ad hoc experimentation when constructing ensembles.

Abstract: This paper provides an experimentally verified formal law for calculating the uplift that diversity of thought provides in Large Language Model (LLM) ensembles. From first principles, we derive an exact decomposition of LLM ensemble lift into rescue and damage masses, which yields a compact heuristic for calculating uplift. From this we extract the metrics which predict ensemble performance: an accuracy adjusted correctness correlation, $φ_{\mathrm{adj}}$, together with the accuracy gap and collective accuracy of the pair. We test the law on 767,520 inferences from ten open-weight models over two graduate-level science benchmarks, together with a novel agentic cybersecurity benchmark in which each model conducts digital-forensics investigations by multi-turn tool use in a network-isolated sandbox (23,520 graded trials including abstentions); all votes are released openly. Calibrated once on SuperGPQA at a 40:60 vote split, the heuristic predicts lift on the calibration set with Spearman's $ρ=0.84$ and, with its coefficients frozen, transfers to two datasets never used in calibration ($ρ=0.51$ on GPQA Diamond and $0.84$ on the forensic tasks), whilst the measured swap mass tracks realised lift with $R^2\ge 0.96$ throughout. Raw $φ$ has almost no predictive power ($R^2\le 0.09$ throughout); the accuracy-adjusted $φ_{\mathrm{adj}}$ is markedly superior ($R^2=0.67$ on SuperGPQA), and the heuristic combining these metrics is the most stable pre pooling predictor across the three datasets.

### [127] [Intermittent Control Is Not Diluted Control: A Switching Effect in Artificial Agency](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17432)
*Veronique Ziegler*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that in adaptive agents with memory, intermittently losing and regaining anticipatory control can lower long-term regulatory cost beyond what is predicted by simply averaging fixed control modes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand how the timing and ordering of disturbance and recovery, in agents that retain state history, affect the overall regulatory burden, especially when control alternates between anticipatory and reactive modes.

Method: The authors simulate an adaptive agent whose internal state retains history and which can operate in anticipatory or reactive control modes. They compare fixed-mode operation against schedules where access to anticipatory control is periodically or stochastically switched, running high-statistics simulations (N = 1000 matched replicates per schedule) to measure mean regulatory burden and its distribution.

Result: Across all tested switching schedules, the mean regulatory burden under intermittent access to anticipatory control is consistently lower than predicted by a simple weighted mixture of fixed anticipatory and reactive operation. The observed effect is small (~0.5% of the mean gain) but robust, with 63–68% of simulation replicates showing lower-than-mixture burden and no evidence of hidden buildup of regulatory cost over late windows.

Conclusion: In history-dependent adaptive systems, regulatory burden depends not only on the fraction of time spent in anticipatory vs reactive modes but also on their temporal ordering relative to disturbances. Intermittent anticipatory control can function not as partial regulatory failure but as a strategy that reorganizes state trajectories to reduce long-term recovery costs, offering a timing principle for designing adaptive agents.

Abstract: Adaptive agents do not always regulate under the same timing conditions. Sometimes stabilization can begin before a disturbance has fully entered the internal state; at other times the agent can only recover after disruption has taken hold. A simple expectation is that an agent moving between these conditions should behave like a weighted average of the two fixed cases: the more time spent in reactive recovery, the greater the regulatory burden.
  This paper shows that expectation can fail. In a simulated adaptive agent with retained state history, at an operating point where sustained reactive control is more costly than sustained anticipatory control, intermittent access to anticipatory control reduces the mean regulatory burden below the value predicted by a fixed-mode mixture. The effect appears under both periodic and stochastic switching schedules: losing anticipatory access does not simply dilute its benefit, and restoring it intermittently can reorganize the later regulatory burden. High-statistics runs (N = 1000 matched replicates per schedule) resolve a negative nonlinear switching penalty across every tested schedule. The effect is small but consistent: about half a percent of the mean gain, with 63-68% of replicates falling below zero. Late-window diagnostics reveal no unresolved upward accumulation of regulatory burden.
  The result identifies a design-relevant timing principle. In history-dependent adaptive systems, the burden of remaining organized is not set only by how much time an agent spends in each mode; the order in which disturbance and recovery enter the state can change the subsequent burden. Intermittent anticipatory control may therefore act less like a partial failure of regulation than like a mechanism for reducing the long-term burden of recovery.

Conclusion: Empirically grounding LLM agents with demographic, time-use, and spatial data markedly increases the statistical realism of simulated population behavior in both normal and disruptive conditions. This strengthens the case for using such models in disaster and infrastructure-disruption planning while also highlighting that, even with grounding, current LLM agents still only partially capture real-world adaptive responses and need further refinement.

We evaluate whether empirical grounding improves the statistical realism of LLM-agent simulations during disruptions. Specifically, we develop an empirically grounded LLM-agent framework that embeds demographic profiles from the American Community Survey, baseline routines from the American Time Use Survey, and urban spatial context into agent initialization, memory, decision prompts, and activity execution. Under heatwave conditions, the grounded model better reproduced survey-derived activity profiles, increasing mean correlation from 0.349 to 0.836 and reducing mean squared error from 0.098 to 0.012. The grounded model captured 46.4% of observed heatwave response amplitude, compared with 20.6% for the ungrounded baseline. ### [129] [AEC-DS: Adaptive Erasure Coding with PDP-Triggered Reputation and QoS-Aware Migration for Decentralized Storage](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17460)
*Shuaiwen Li,Weihang Yu,Ke Wang,Meng Han*

Main category: cs.AI

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing decentralized storage systems typically treat audit results from integrity-checking schemes (like PDP) as isolated signals, not as active inputs to guide redundancy level, node selection, or shard migration. This disconnect can cause inefficient resource usage, suboptimal shard placement on unreliable nodes, slow reaction to node instability, and increased recovery operations. The paper is motivated by the need to make storage systems self-healing and QoS-aware by using integrity feedback directly to adapt redundancy and placement, especially under heterogeneous node reliability and file priority.

Method: The paper introduces AEC-DS, a closed-loop adaptive erasure coding framework integrated with Provable Data Possession (PDP). PDP audits are used to continuously update node reputation scores based on their reliability. A QoS-aware migration policy then uses these scores and data priority classes to decide where to place shards and when to migrate them. High-priority shards on unstable nodes are proactively migrated to more reliable nodes, especially within a designated cold tier, while unstable nodes are penalized in future placement decisions. The system is evaluated via simulations on a network of 800 nodes and 500 files, comparing against Static-EC, Dynamic-EC, and DRD-EC baselines, and also via ablation studies to isolate the effect of class migration.

Result: Under the tested fault model, AEC-DS achieves 100% data durability with a redundancy factor of only 1.25x, indicating efficient redundancy utilization. Relative to Static-EC, Dynamic-EC, and DRD-EC, AEC-DS reduces cumulative recovery operations by 66.8%–75.2%, demonstrating significantly lower repair overhead. Ablation experiments show that the class migration component is crucial, increasing loss-prevention capability by 176.8%, highlighting the importance of QoS-aware shard movement guided by PDP-based reputations.

Conclusion: The study concludes that feeding PDP audit feedback directly into redundancy and shard-placement logic enables an effective closed-loop control mechanism for decentralized storage. AEC-DS can simultaneously ensure high data durability, significantly cut recovery operations, and prioritize protection for high-value data through targeted migration. The integration of integrity auditing with adaptive coding and placement offers a practical route to self-healing decentralized storage systems that explicitly balance durability, reliability, and the cost of data migration.

Abstract: In decentralized storage systems, audit results are often not used directly to guide later redundancy and shard-placement decisions, which can lead to inefficient resource allocation and delayed recovery. We propose AEC-DS, a closed-loop adaptive erasure coding mechanism driven by Provable Data Possession (PDP) feedback. PDP audits continuously update node reputation, while a QoS-aware migration policy adjusts shard placement according to node reliability and data priority. The policy moves high-priority shards from unstable nodes to more reliable nodes in the cold tier and penalizes unstable nodes in subsequent placement decisions. Simulations with 800 nodes and 500 files show that AEC-DS maintains 100% data durability under the evaluated fault model with a redundancy factor of 1.25x. Compared with Static-EC, Dynamic-EC, and DRD-EC, AEC-DS reduces cumulative recovery operations by 66.8%-75.2%. Ablation results further show that class migration plays a major role in preventing data loss, improving the measured loss-prevention capability by 176.8%. These results indicate that PDP feedback can connect integrity auditing with redundancy and placement adaptation, providing a practical path toward self-healing decentralized storage while accounting for the additional cost of migration.
