### [130] [Panache: One-Pass Motif Discovery at Every Window Length](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17481)
*Tej Sanibh Ranade*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Panache is a near-linear-time, one-pass streaming algorithm for z-normalized pan matrix profile motif discovery that avoids repeated quadratic self-joins over multiple window lengths by maintaining subsequence spectra online and using hashing plus spectral lower bounds to find motifs efficiently.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Motif discovery in time series is essential for exploratory data analysis, but analysts typically do not know the correct motif duration (window length) in advance. Current pan matrix profile approaches must compute a separate, expensive (quadratic) self-join for each candidate window length, which becomes prohibitively slow for long series and many lengths. There is a need for a scalable algorithm that can discover motifs across many lengths in a single pass without sacrificing accuracy.

Method: Panache processes the time series in a single streaming pass. It relies on the observation that z-normalization only affects the DC Fourier coefficient of a subsequence, allowing the non-DC spectrum of each subsequence to be maintained online using sliding-DFT recurrences and running statistics. These spectral representations serve as keys in an occupancy-controlled hash directory where similar subsequences are likely to collide. Using Parseval’s theorem, Panache computes a spectral lower bound on distance to prune most candidate pairs before performing any exact distance calculations. The algorithm automatically sets all data-dependent parameters, exposing only a resource-budget knob to the user.

Result: On 17 configurations derived from the UCR time series archive, Panache, at its default resource budget, recovers all top-20 pan-motifs when compared to exact pan matrix profile ground truth with fixed exclusions. Performance-wise, it outperforms all CPU and GPU baselines evaluated in the paper. On a large Wafer dataset with 5 million samples and 51 window lengths, Panache finishes the streaming pass in 2.9 minutes and outputs exact motifs in 6.0 minutes, compared with 7.95 hours for the fastest exact CPU baseline and 38.3 minutes for the SCAMP implementation on an H100 GPU.

Conclusion: Panache demonstrates that pan matrix profile motif discovery across many window lengths can be performed in a single near-linear-time streaming pass without losing accuracy on benchmark datasets. By exploiting spectral properties of z-normalized subsequences and efficient hashing with lower-bound pruning, Panache substantially reduces computational cost relative to existing CPU and GPU methods, while requiring minimal user tuning (only a resource budget). This establishes Panache as an effective, scalable solution for practical time-series motif discovery across unknown durations.

Abstract: Motif discovery, the search for recurring patterns within a time series, is a core primitive of exploratory data analysis. A pattern, however, is defined by its duration, which analysts rarely know in advance. To resolve this unknown duration, an interval of window lengths is defined, and the accepted method is to try every length in that interval. Existing pan matrix profile (PMP) methods compute one z-normalized matrix profile per length, so $L$ lengths cost $L$ quadratic self-joins over the same series. We introduce Panache, to our knowledge the first one-pass streaming algorithm for z-normalized PMP motif discovery. It replaces the repeated self-joins with a single scan whose runtime is near-linear in the series length. The key observation is that mean-centering a subsequence changes only its DC Fourier coefficient, so the non-DC spectrum of every z-normalized subsequence can be maintained online by sliding-DFT recurrences and running statistics. This spectral state is the key under which similar subsequences collide in an occupancy-controlled hash directory and, through Parseval's theorem, yields a lower bound that rejects most colliding pairs before any exact computation. Panache computes every data-dependent parameter itself, leaving only a resource budget to tune. At the default budget, it recovers all top-20 pan-motifs against exact fixed-exclusion ground truth on 17 UCR configurations, and is faster than every CPU and GPU baseline benchmarked in this paper. On Wafer at five million samples over 51 lengths, Panache completes one pass in 2.9 minutes and emits the exact motifs in 6.0 minutes, against 7.95 hours for the fastest exact CPU baseline and 38.3 minutes for SCAMP on an H100 GPU.

</details>


### [131] [Pailitao-MMSearch: Building Native E-Commerce Multimodal Search Foundation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17499)
*Xiaohan Ye,Xu Chen,Zihan Gong,Jian Ding,Lianyu Du,Baicheng Chen,Yunmeng Shu,Jingqian Zhao,Zhixiang Zhao,Shuaiqi Jia,Chong Ma,Shuwen Xiao,Xiangheng Kong,Yuan Gao,Jun Song,Jinsong Lan,Xiaoyong Zhu,Bo Zheng*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: E-commerce search has evolved from simple keyword matching to rich multimodal queries involving images, natural language, and mixed user intents. Existing solutions are split between single-modal specialist systems (text retrieval, visual search, voice) that cannot reason across modalities, and generic vision-language models that lack deep e-commerce domain knowledge needed for fine-grained product attributes, user behavior, and purchase intent. There is a need for a unified, domain-specialized multimodal model that can support complex, realistic e-commerce search scenarios and directly drive business outcomes.

Method: The authors design Pailitao-MMSearch, a multimodal search foundation model built on top of the Qwen architecture and tailored for e-commerce. The method comprises three main innovations: (1) HybSID (Hybrid Semantic ID), a representation mechanism to better capture and align semantic information across modalities for products; (2) a two-stage continual pre-training strategy that first adapts the base model to the e-commerce domain and then further optimizes it for multimodal search tasks; and (3) a hybrid reasoning post-training pipeline that enhances the model’s ability to perform complex reasoning over product data, user queries, and commercial intents. The model is deployed in Taobao’s Pailitao platform, integrating text, image, and potentially voice inputs in a unified system.

Result: In online A/B testing against Taobao’s traditional multimodal search pipeline, Pailitao-MMSearch achieves strong commercial gains, including up to +13.61% improvement in Gross Merchandise Volume (GMV) and +8.21% increase in transaction volume. These results indicate that the proposed unified, domain-specific multimodal model outperforms previous specialized or generic approaches when applied to real-world e-commerce search traffic.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that a native, domain-specialized multimodal search foundation model can effectively unify cross-modal product search in e-commerce and yield substantial business benefits. By combining HybSID representations, continual domain-adaptive pre-training, and hybrid reasoning post-training, Pailitao-MMSearch provides more accurate and intent-aware search results, leading to significantly higher GMV and transaction volume on a large commercial platform. The work demonstrates that tailored multimodal LLMs are a powerful paradigm for next-generation e-commerce search and recommendation systems.

Abstract: The evolution of e-commerce has fundamentally transformed how users search for products, shifting from simple text-based keyword queries to complex multimodal interactions that seamlessly combine product images, natural language descriptions, and mixed-intent instructions. However, existing approaches face a critical dilemma: single-modal specialist models, deployed independently for text retrieval, visual search, and voice recognition, operate in isolation and cannot handle cross-modal queries, while general-purpose vision-language models lack the domain-specific knowledge necessary for fine-grained product understanding, user behavior modeling, and commercial intent reasoning. In this work, we present Pailitao-MMSearch, one native e-commerce multimodal search foundation model designed to bridge this gap. Our approach introduces three key innovations: (1)HybSID (Hybrid Semantic ID);(2)a two-stage continual pre-training strategy; and (3)a hybrid reasoning post-training pipeline. Built upon Qwen and deployed on Taobao's Pailitao multimodal search platform, Pailitao-MMSearch achieves substantial improvements in online A/B testing, including up to +13.61\% in Gross Merchandise Volume (GMV) and +8.21\% in transaction volume compared to traditional multi-modal search pipeline, demonstrating the effectiveness of our native e-commerce multimodal search large language models.

### [132] [Can AI Agents Really Complete RTL-to-GDS? Lessons from Benchmarking Tool-Interactive EDA Workflows](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17528)
*Jinyuan Deng,Zhengrui Chen,Xufeng Wei,Tianyu Xing,Chenyi Wen,Cheng Zhuo*

Main category: cs.AI

There is a lack of standardized, end-to-end benchmarks and metrics to compare AI agent systems operating over full chip design workflows, particularly with consistent toolchains and technology libraries. Practitioners need to understand not only task accuracy but also cost-efficiency and how agent architectures behave when driving full RTL-to-GDS flows.

Method: The authors build FluxBench, a unified evaluation framework for LLM-driven agents in EDA. They define standardized prompts, tool environments, and technology library settings, and design workflows that cover both open-source RTL generation flows and industrial RTL-to-GDS flows with commercial tools. They test multiple agent systems, all potentially sharing the same foundation model but differing in system architecture. The benchmark evaluates capacities such as RTL code generation, iterative repair based on tool feedback, logic synthesis, placement & routing, and ECO automation. They also introduce Token ROI, a metric that captures the quality improvement of EDA artifacts normalized by token and runtime cost.

Result: Experiments show large performance disparities—up to 86.27%—between agent system architectures even when using the same underlying foundation model. Cost-efficiency, as quantified by Token ROI, can vary dramatically as well, with up to a 105.92× difference among systems with similar task performance. In a concrete RTL-to-GDS case study using PicoRV32, their system FluxEDA achieves an end-to-end score of 97.94 and outperforms a Claude Code–based agent with domain-specific EDA skills by up to 8.39×.

Conclusion: The study concludes that effective EDA automation with LLM-driven agents cannot be achieved simply by adding domain-specific skills to a model. Instead, the overall agent system design—how the agent orchestrates tools, handles feedback, and structures workflows—along with the base model’s capability, are pivotal. FluxBench provides a standardized way to measure these aspects and highlights the importance of architecture and cost-efficiency considerations for deploying AI agents in large-scale, real-world EDA flows.

Abstract: LLM-driven agent systems have emerged as a promising paradigm for electronic design automation (EDA), demonstrating strong potential for automating complex design workflows. However, existing evaluations primarily examine individual language models on isolated EDA tasks, providing limited insight into how different agent systems perform across complete EDA flows. In this work, we present FluxBench, a systematic evaluation of AI agents on end-to-end EDA workflows under unified prompts, tool environments, and technology library settings. Our evaluation covers representative scenarios, including RTL generation with open-source toolchains and an RTL-to-GDS flow using closed-source commercial EDA tools for industrial applications. Through these workflows, we assess agents' capabilities in RTL code generation, iterative repair, tool-feedback utilization, logic synthesis, placement and routing (P&R), and Engineering Change Order (ECO) automation. To further characterize the efficiency of agent systems, we introduce Token ROI, a cost-efficiency metric that measures effective improvements in EDA artifacts relative to token usage and runtime cost. Experimental results show that, even when built on the same foundation model, different agent system architectures can exhibit performance gaps of up to 86.27%. Moreover, among systems with comparable task performance, Token ROI can differ by as much as $105.92\times$. In the RTL-to-GDS flow using PicoRV32 as a case study, FluxEDA achieves an end-to-end score of up to 97.94, outperforming Claude Code equipped with domain-specific EDA skills by up to $8.39\times$. ### [133] [The Curvature Shadow: An Apparent Failure of Maximum-Entropy Equilibrium Selection is a Removable Artifact](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17543)
*Luis Leal*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper analyzes why a regularized Nash solver sometimes appears not to pick the exact maximum-entropy Nash equilibrium, showing that any observed gap is explained by a simple relationship between entropy shortfall and curvature of the entropy landscape, not by a true selection bias.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Regularized Nash solvers like Regularized Nash Dynamics empirically seem to select the maximum-entropy Nash equilibrium (the I-projection of a uniform prior) when the Nash set is convex. However, in Kuhn poker there is a small but notable discrepancy in one strategy coordinate between the solver’s output and the true maximum-entropy equilibrium, despite almost identical entropy. This raises the question of whether the solver has a systematic selection bias or whether the discrepancy can be fully explained as an artifact of finite entropy shortfall and geometry of the Nash set.

Method: The authors study games where the set of Nash equilibria forms a one-dimensional manifold. They derive an approximate analytical relation between the coordinate gap along this manifold and the entropy shortfall of the solver relative to the maximum-entropy point, involving the curvature of the entropy landscape at its maximum: gap ≈ sqrt(2δ/κ). They then empirically test this law across five games (four matrix games and Kuhn poker), vary the regularization strength ("magnet strength") to sweep entropy shortfalls, and measure both the achieved entropy and the local curvature to test the predicted scaling and constants.

Result: They find that, across five games, the measured coordinate gaps follow the predicted relation gap ≈ sqrt(2δ/κ) extremely closely (within 2×10^-4 absolute error and under 1% relative error). In the four matrix games, the entropy shortfall δ is effectively zero, so there is no observed gap regardless of curvature. In Kuhn poker, δ is positive but small, producing a noticeable coordinate gap that shrinks along the predicted square-root curve as δ is driven toward zero by increasing regularization strength, until numerical or dynamical instability is reached. The fitted scaling exponent is 0.50 with R^2 > 0.999999, matching the theoretical 1/2 scaling. They also separately estimate curvature and show consistency with the quantitative law, and identify a “moving target” issue in experiments using Tsallis entropy instead of Shannon entropy.

Conclusion: The apparent discrepancy between Regularized Nash Dynamics and the maximum-entropy Nash equilibrium in Kuhn poker is not evidence of an intrinsic selection bias. Instead, the coordinate gap is fully explained by the interaction of a small, removable entropy shortfall with the unusually low curvature (flatness) of the entropy peak along the Nash manifold. When entropy shortfall is reduced, the solver’s solution converges to the maximum-entropy equilibrium as predicted by the derived square-root law, confirming the I-projection interpretation up to residuals imposed by flatness and stability limits.

Abstract: In two-player zero-sum games whose Nash equilibria form a convex set, regularized solvers such as Regularized Nash Dynamics (R-NaD) empirically select the maximum-entropy member: the information projection (I-projection) of a uniform reference onto the Nash set. On a panel of small games this match is exact, with one apparent exception: in Kuhn poker R-NaD lands at bluff coordinate 0.180 while the maximum-entropy member sits at 0.201, a coordinate gap of about 0.021, even though R-NaD attains 99.7 percent of the maximum entropy. We ask whether this gap is a genuine selection bias or an artifact, and answer it quantitatively. We show that for selection on a one-dimensional Nash manifold the coordinate gap factorizes as $\mathrm{gap} \approx \sqrt{2δ/κ}$, where $δ$ is the entropy shortfall of the solver and $κ$ is the curvature of the entropy landscape at its peak. Across five games this relation holds to within $2 \times 10^{-4}$ (under 1 percent relative error). The four matrix games have $δ\approx 0$ (R-NaD reaches the maximum-entropy member exactly) and therefore no gap regardless of curvature; only the sequential game (Kuhn) has $δ> 0$. A causal sweep of the magnet strength drives $δ\to 0$ and the gap toward zero along the predicted curve (fitted scaling exponent 0.50, $R^2 > 0.999999$, against the exact prediction of 1/2), until the dynamics destabilize at a stability floor: behavior consistent with a removable shortfall and inconsistent with a fixed bias. We quantify the curvature half of the law from measured curvatures and flag a moving-target pitfall in the natural Tsallis-entropy experiment. The Kuhn gap is thus the curvature shadow of a small, removable entropy shortfall on an unusually flat peak; the I-projection account is upheld up to a flatness-limited residual.

</details>


### [134] [Retain or Consolidate? Budget-Dependent Operator Selection for Language Agent Memory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17545)
*Qingcan Kang,Mingyang Liu,Shixiong Kai,Kaichao Liang,Zhentao Tang,Yuqi Cui,Tao Zhong,Mingxuan Yuan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: This paper studies when language model memories should be kept verbatim vs. compressed, and how to choose among different compression operators under a given context budget.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM-based agents rely on past interaction memories, but context windows and inference costs limit how much can be used. Existing approaches either retain all raw records (good detail, poor coverage under tight budgets) or consolidate them through compression/abstraction (better coverage, risk of losing key details). There is no general rule for when one strategy is better, or which specific consolidation operator to use. The authors aim to formalize and operationalize this decision so agents can adaptively manage memory under varying budget constraints.

Method: The authors decompose the utility of each memory operation (Retain, Merge, Abstract, Rewrite) into two components: (1) a coverage effect, capturing the benefit of including additional evidence that would be omitted under pure retention, and (2) a signed replacement effect, capturing the gain or loss from replacing raw evidence that already fits in context with its consolidated form. They analyze how these effects vary with the tightness of the context budget. To implement this, they propose Offline Abstraction-Safety (OAS), a lightweight learner that predicts action utilities from pre-generation features and uses held-out harm calibration to avoid harmful consolidations. They evaluate on LongMemEval and LoCoMo benchmarks.

Result: Empirically, they find a budget-dependent crossover: under tight context budgets, consolidation substantially improves performance, with up to 48% absolute accuracy gain on LongMemEval compared to retention; under loose budgets, simple retention of raw records performs better. LoCoMo exhibits the same pattern but at a smaller budget because its evidence is shorter. Across both datasets, consolidation operators that perform cross-note abstraction and merging tend to outperform local rewriting when compression is required.

Conclusion: Memory management for LLM agents should be budget-aware and operator-aware rather than defaulting to either full retention or generic compression. The trade-off between coverage and replacement effects explains why consolidation is preferable under tight context budgets but harmful when budgets are loose. The proposed OAS mechanism can learn when to consolidate and which operator to apply, and experiments show that appropriate consolidation can yield large accuracy gains, especially when context is scarce, with cross-note abstraction/merging being more effective than local rewriting.

Abstract: Language agents depend on memory across interactions. However, the limited context windows of large language models (LLMs) and their inference costs constrain how much memory can be used at once. Existing systems mainly follow two strategies: memory retention and memory consolidation. Retention keeps raw records and preserves exact details, but relevant evidence may not fit under a tight budget; consolidation compresses and combines records, improving coverage per token but risking the loss of query-critical details. Neither strategy is universally preferable. This raises two central questions: when should consolidation replace retention, and which operator -- Merge, Abstract, or Rewrite -- should be selected? We formalize this decision by decomposing each operator's utility into a coverage effect on evidence omitted by retention and a signed replacement effect on raw evidence that already fits. Their balance explains why the preferred action changes with relative budget pressure. We implement this mechanism with Offline Abstraction-Safety (OAS), a lightweight learner that estimates action utilities from pre-generation features with held-out harm calibration. The public LongMemEval and LoCoMo benchmarks show the same budget-dependent pattern. On LongMemEval, consolidation improves absolute accuracy by up to 48% under tight budgets, whereas retention is preferable under loose budgets; LoCoMo replicates this crossover at a smaller budget, consistent with its shorter evidence. On both datasets, cross-note abstraction and merging generally outperform local rewriting when compression is necessary.

### [135] [Why Does Feedback-Augmented Self-Distillation Fail to Improve Retrieval-Interleaved Search Agents?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17558)
*Fan Yang,Rui Meng,Yuxin Wen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies why on-policy self-distillation for agentic LLMs can fail, identifying a "decoding collapse" phenomenon, analyzes its causes in inconsistent supervision, and mitigates it with an EMA-based self-teacher that stabilizes training and improves performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: On-policy self-distillation can, in principle, train strong LLM agents without external teachers, but its behavior on complex reasoning and search tasks is poorly understood and often unreliable. The authors are motivated to understand the failure modes of such self-distillation in agentic settings, particularly why models can appear to reason and search while actually ignoring the input question, and to design a method that stabilizes and improves self-teacher learning for these tasks.

Method: They instantiate Feedback-Augmented Self-Distillation (FA-SD) for agentic search, using successful demonstrations as privileged information. During analysis, they identify a failure mode called decoding collapse, where the model reuses generic reasoning/search templates that are insensitive to the input, making the KL-based self-distillation signal uninformative. They dissect the instability of self-teacher learning into model inconsistency vs. prompt inconsistency and empirically show that prompt inconsistency is especially harmful. To address this, they introduce an exponential moving average (EMA) teacher over model parameters to act as a more stable self-teacher that provides more consistent supervision, with a warm-up phase to allow the EMA to become reliable.

Result: The paper finds that standard on-policy self-distillation for agentic search can suffer from decoding collapse, which is not well captured by customary evaluation metrics. Their analysis reveals that inconsistent supervision, especially from prompt variations, destabilizes learning and undermines the benefits of a stronger self-teacher. Incorporating an EMA teacher stabilizes the supervision signal; after an initial warm-up period that may show temporary performance regressions, this EMA-based self-teaching ultimately leads to improved performance on complex agentic tasks compared to vanilla OPSD.

Conclusion: Self-distillation for agentic LLMs is vulnerable to a subtle failure mode—decoding collapse—stemming from inconsistent supervision signals, particularly due to prompt design. By stabilizing the self-teacher with an EMA teacher, one can mitigate these issues and obtain more reliable performance gains from OPSD in complex reasoning and search tasks. The work suggests that both the consistency of supervision and mechanisms such as EMA teachers are crucial for effective on-policy self-distillation in agentic settings.

Abstract: On-policy self-distillation (OPSD) offers a promising approach for training large language models without relying on a separate teacher model. However, its effectiveness on complex agentic tasks remains largely unexplored. In this work, we instantiate Feedback-Augmented Self-Distillation (FA-SD), a self-distillation algorithm for agentic search that leverages successful demonstrations as privileged information. We identify that models can rely on recurring reasoning-and-search output templates, producing trajectories that appear diverse but are largely agnostic to the input question, making the KL-based self-distillation signal uninformative. We term this phenomenon decoding collapse, a failure mode that can be missed by existing evaluation metrics. To understand its underlying cause, we show that although the self-teacher achieves stronger performance, learning remains inherently unstable due to inconsistent supervision signals. We further decompose this inconsistency into model inconsistency and prompt inconsistency, and show that the latter can significantly degrade the quality of the supervision signal, limiting the effectiveness of self-teacher learning. To mitigate this inconsistency, we introduce an exponential moving average (EMA) teacher to stabilize the self-teacher and provide more consistent supervision signals. Although the EMA teacher requires a warm-up phase during which performance may temporarily regress, it ultimately improves model performance by providing more stable supervision.

### [136] [Reinforcement Learning: From Algorithms To Foundation Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17560)
*Zihan Ding*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Thesis connects multi-agent reinforcement learning in games with reinforcement learning built on generative and foundation models, presenting a unified view of RL as objective-driven adaptation in complex environments.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Classical RL focuses on a single agent maximizing reward in a fixed environment, which is insufficient for modern scenarios involving multiple agents, strategic interaction, uncertainty, and high-dimensional data such as video. Additionally, large pretrained generative and foundation models have emerged but are not yet fully integrated into RL frameworks. The thesis is motivated by the need to understand and extend RL methods for multi-agent settings and to exploit prior knowledge from powerful generative models to improve sequential decision making.

Method: In the first part, the thesis uses multi-agent RL algorithms and game-theoretic tools to analyze learning dynamics, incentives, policies, and equilibria in two-player zero-sum, general-sum, and large-scale multi-player games (including video games). In the second part, it designs RL algorithms that incorporate generative and foundation models: diffusion-based world models, generative models as policy classes, and interactive video world models with explicit actions; it also introduces architectures with memory to handle long-horizon dependencies. Across both parts, the work studies how these methods behave in complex, interactive environments.

Result: The work demonstrates that RL algorithms can be effectively extended to multi-agent and game-theoretic settings, clarifying how incentives and equilibrium concepts shape learning in interactive environments. It also shows that pretrained generative and foundation models—such as diffusion-based world models and video generators—can serve as powerful priors and representations for planning, control, and policy optimization. The thesis provides algorithms and empirical studies that validate these ideas in competitive games, large-scale video games, and sequential generative modeling tasks.

Conclusion: RL can be viewed more broadly as objective-driven adaptation that links decision making, environment modeling, and representation learning with foundation models. By unifying multi-agent RL in games with RL that leverages generative and foundation models, the thesis argues that intelligent behavior arises from combining strategic interaction, rich world models, and long-horizon reasoning within a single RL-based framework.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning (RL) provides a framework for sequential decision making under explicit objectives. In its classical form, RL studies how an agent should act to maximise long-term reward in a dynamic environment. In richer settings, the problem extends beyond a single agent and fixed environment: intelligent behavior may require strategic interaction, adaptation to uncertainty, and reasoning over high-dimensional worlds. This thesis studies RL from two perspectives: algorithms in games and RL in the era of foundation models.
  The first part focuses on multi-agent RL in games. It examines how incentives, policies, and equilibrium concepts interact in competitive and general-sum environments, spanning two-player zero-sum games, large-scale video games, and multi-player settings with general structure. These works investigate learning in multi-agent systems and the behavior of RL methods in interactive environments. The second part studies RL with generative and foundation models, motivated by the idea that prior knowledge can enrich sequential decision making. Pretrained generative models and learned world models serve as representation tools and structured priors for planning, control, and policy optimization. The thesis develops diffusion-based world models, investigates RL for efficient video generation, explores generative models as policy classes, and studies interactive video world models in which actions shape future observations. It also addresses long-horizon modeling through architectures with memory. Together, these contributions present a unified view of RL as objective-driven adaptation in complex sequential domains. From strategic games to generative world models, the thesis highlights how RL connects decision making, environment modeling, and emerging foundation-model capabilities, offering a broader perspective on the principles underlying intelligent behavior.

Extra complexity in the form of an explicit emotion state machine does not further help, suggesting that well-designed contextual memory may be more important than handcrafted state logic. Abstract: AI companions are judged not only by single-turn fluency but by whether they sustain emotional continuity: remembering who the companion is, what the user prefers, and how the relationship has felt. Under an identical preregistered protocol, three memory systems (ZifaMem, Mem0, and filtered verbatim retrieval) each improve significantly over raw-history deployment, and ZifaMem and Mem0 are statistically equivalent within +/-5 points on the preregistered primary preference endpoint. ### [138] [A Dual-Hypothesis Reasoning Framework for LLM Guardrails](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17575)
*Md Asiful Islam,Mihai Surdeanu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: ARBITER is a cost-effective, reasoning-based LLM guardrail that considers both safe and unsafe interpretations of user prompts, uses a structured fine-tuning objective to improve its reasoning quality, and outperforms prior guardrails on multiple safety benchmarks while providing interpretable evidence for unsafe decisions.

  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need for a more efficient, robust, and transparent guardrail that can reason explicitly about safety risks and generalize better to out-of-domain cases.

Method: ARBITER introduces: (1) dual-hypothesis reasoning, where the guardrail explicitly constructs and evaluates both a safe and an unsafe interpretation of the prompt before deciding; and (2) multi-component supervised fine-tuning (MC-SFT), which decomposes model outputs into logical components (e.g., classification, rationale, evidence phrases) and assigns different weights to each in the training loss. It uses self-generated reasoning traces instead of teacher models and applies parameter-efficient LoRA fine-tuning to build the guardrail model.

Result: On three safety moderation benchmarks, ARBITER consistently surpasses both reasoning-based and non-reasoning guardrail baselines, including those that rely on more expensive training regimes. It shows especially strong improvements in out-of-domain evaluation settings, indicating better generalization. The model also produces evidence-phrase explanations aligned with its decisions.

Conclusion: A reasoning-centric, cost-efficient guardrail like ARBITER can both improve safety moderation performance and provide more transparent, evidence-backed decisions. Dual-hypothesis reasoning and MC-SFT are effective design choices for guardrails, enabling them to outperform more expensive approaches while remaining interpretable and better generalizing to novel inputs.

Abstract: We propose ARBITER, a novel LLM guardrail framework that introduces two key ideas: (i) dual-hypothesis reasoning, a reasoning method for LLM guardrails that explicitly considers both safe and unsafe interpretations of a prompt before making a safety decision, and (ii) multi-component supervised fine-tuning (MC-SFT), a structured training loss for reasoning-based guardrails that decomposes LLM outputs into logical components and weights them according to their importance. Existing reasoning-based guardrails often rely on expensive procedures, such as generating reasoning traces using larger or closed-source teacher models and applying full-parameter fine-tuning. In contrast, ARBITER uses a cost-effective self-generation strategy for reasoning traces and LoRA-based parameter-efficient fine-tuning while still achieving better performance than these expensive approaches. Additionally, ARBITER provides faithful evidence-phrase explanations for unsafe decisions, enabling a more transparent and interpretable guardrail method. Experiments on three safety moderation benchmarks show that ARBITER outperforms existing reasoning-based and non-reasoning guardrail baselines, with clear gains in out-of-domain evaluations.

### [139] [Mechanistic Attention Guidance for Agent Memory Refinement](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17621)
*Yechao Hong,Haiquan Qiu,Yaqing Wang,Quanming Yao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces AGMR, an attention-guided framework that refines an agent’s external memory by leveraging retrieval-head attention patterns rather than relying only on text logs and reflections.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most self-evolving memory systems for agents refine memory purely from textual artifacts like trajectories and reflections, without using internal model signals. This makes it unclear which memory segments are actually used during decisions, leading to incorrect error attribution, hallucinated updates, and inefficient memory. The authors aim to obtain a mechanistic, segment-level view of memory utilization during task execution to guide more reliable and efficient memory refinement.

Method: The authors analyze retrieval-head attention in the model during task execution. They aggregate attention over memory segments and decision steps to form a context utilization matrix that captures how strongly each memory segment influences each decision step. From this matrix, they infer recurring patterns of memory use and deduce appropriate refinement strategies. Based on this, they propose Attention-Guided Memory Refinement (AGMR), which performs targeted updates at the segment level: (1) for failed executions, it corrects or enriches underperforming but used memory segments; (2) for successful runs, it prunes or simplifies seldom-used segments; and (3) it verifies all modifications by re-executing tasks with the updated memory. The process iteratively improves the memory bank guided by attention rather than text signals alone.

Result: On interactive decision-making benchmarks, AGMR yields higher task success rates and more compact or efficient memory representations than text-only memory refinement baselines. The experiments demonstrate that attention-based utilization patterns are informative for guiding memory updates and that leveraging them leads to both better performance and improved memory efficiency.

Conclusion: Retrieval-head attention can serve as a practical mechanistic signal to reveal how external memory is used at the segment level during agent decision-making. By transforming these attention patterns into a context utilization matrix and using it to steer targeted memory edits, AGMR mitigates issues of unreliable, text-only memory refinement. The approach improves both performance and memory efficiency, indicating that internal attention signals are a valuable resource for building more reliable self-evolving memory systems.

Abstract: Existing self-evolving memory systems mainly improve agent memory based on textual outputs, such as task trajectories and reflections. However, this text-based paradigm rarely incorporates internal mechanistic signals, leaving how retrieved memory is actually utilized during task execution underexplored. This limitation can lead to unreliable error attribution and hallucinated memory modifications. In this work, we show that retrieval-head attention provides a mechanistic signal for revealing segment-level memory utilization. By aggregating attention over memory segments and decision steps, we construct a context utilization matrix that exposes recurring memory-use patterns and indicates corresponding refinement strategies. Building on this observation, we propose Attention-Guided Memory Refinement (AGMR), a framework that uses utilization patterns revealed by attention to guide targeted segment-level memory updates. AGMR corrects or enhances memory for failed executions, simplifies memory for successful executions, and verifies each update through re-execution. Experiments on interactive decision-making benchmarks show that AGMR improves both task performance and memory efficiency over text-only memory refinement baselines. Code is available at https://anonymous.4open.science/r/AGMR_code-3262/

</details>


### [140] [Verify, Repair, Repeat, or Stop? Robust Stopping for Noisy Verify-Repair Loops in LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17641)
*Yitao Wu,Si Shen,Rui Yang,Hong Peng,Bin Hu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces VRR-Stop, a principled stopping rule for noisy verify–repair loops in LLM agents, with a backup scheme VRR-Guard for low-quality verifiers, significantly improving true solution validity while using few repair rounds.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing verify–repair–repeat loops in LLM agents assume that more repair rounds help, but when both verifier and repairer are noisy, extra repairs can damage correct solutions while verifiers wrongly accept them. This causes true validity to decrease even as reported acceptance rises, and there is no principled way to decide when to stop repairing. The paper aims to provide a theoretically grounded stopping criterion that remains robust under verifier and repairer noise.

Method: The authors build a four-parameter probabilistic noise model that separately captures (1) verifier false acceptance, (2) verifier false rejection, (3) repairer’s ability to fix incorrect candidates, and (4) repairer’s tendency to damage correct ones. Using this model, they apply belief filtering over repeated verification votes to estimate the posterior probability that the current candidate is valid. VRR-Stop decides whether to commit or to invoke another repair step based on the estimated sign of the true marginal gain from further repair, requiring only correct sign identification rather than accurate parameter estimation. Recognizing that estimation can fail when the verifier has poor discrimination, they introduce VRR-Guard, an estimation-free fallback that only replaces the current candidate if a new one achieves a sufficiently large verification margin over the incumbent.

Result: On a challenging GSM8K stress-test setup, VRR-Stop yields a 60.6 percentage point improvement in true solution validity compared with a naive fixed schedule of five repair rounds, while on average using only 0.72 repair rounds per problem. Experiments across different conditions show that the reliability of the stopping decision is determined by the interaction between how discriminative the verifier is and the decision margin used by VRR-Stop/VRR-Guard, not simply by the magnitude of parameter estimation error.

Conclusion: VRR-Stop provides a principled and robust way to decide when to stop verify–repair loops in noisy LLM agent settings, substantially improving final correctness without incurring many extra repair steps. When verifier quality is too low for reliable parameter estimation, the companion method VRR-Guard offers an estimation-free, conservative replacement rule. Together, they show that effective control of verify–repair procedures depends on verifier discrimination and decision margins rather than minimizing estimation error alone.

Abstract: Verify-repair loops are a standard means for large language model (LLM) agents to correct faulty plans in code generation, mathematical reasoning, and tool use. When both the verifier and the repairer are noisy, repair can damage already-correct plans, and reported acceptance keeps rising while true validity falls, so existing methods lack a principled basis for deciding when repair should stop. We propose VRR-Stop, a robust stopping framework for noisy verify-repair-repeat (VRR) loops. A four-parameter noise model separates verifier false acceptance and false rejection from the repair and damage behavior of the repairer. Belief filtering turns repeated verification votes into an estimate of committed validity, and the loop commits or repairs according to the sign of the true marginal gain, which requires only sign identifiability rather than accurate recovery of all parameters. When verifier discrimination approaches zero, calibration itself fails and estimation error can flip the stopping sign, so we pair VRR-Stop with VRR-Guard, an estimation-free fallback that replaces the incumbent candidate only under a sufficient verification margin. On a GSM8K stress setting, VRR-Stop improves final true validity by 60.6 percentage points over fixed five-round repair at an average cost of 0.72 repair rounds. Across settings, stopping reliability is governed jointly by verifier discrimination and the decision margin rather than by the absolute size of estimation error.

</details>


### [141] [FlowBlock: Wavefront-Parallel Decoding for Self-Correcting Diffusion Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17652)
*Bing Tian,Haikun Liu,Xiaocheng Zhong,Zhuohui Duan,Zhaokai Luo,Huayi Jin,Zhiyong Wang,Xiaofei Liao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes FlowBlock, a training-free framework that parallelizes decoding in block-wise diffusion LLMs using self-correcting token-to-token editing, achieving significant speedups, latency reductions, and small accuracy gains over prior serial and training-based methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Block-wise diffusion LLMs decode entire blocks of tokens at a time, which allows efficient KV-cache reuse but forces a strictly serial dependency between blocks: a downstream block must wait until the previous block is finalized. Existing post-training methods that try to parallelize blocks offer only modest speedups and often hurt accuracy. The authors notice that self-correcting diffusion LLMs can repair errors in drafted tokens using slightly stale context, suggesting that blocks might not need fully finalized predecessors to start decoding. This motivates a new decoding framework that exploits this self-correction ability to unlock inter-block parallelism without retraining the model.

Method: The authors introduce FlowBlock, a training-free decoding framework with two main mechanisms. (i) Gated Wavefront Decoding: blocks are admitted into a wavefront for parallel processing only when a readiness gate (e.g., context sufficiency conditions) is met; active blocks are jointly refined via token-to-token (T2T) editing, and block commitments follow the original order under a windowed block-causal attention mask that guarantees exact reuse of frozen-prefix KV caches. This transforms block finality from a strict dependency into a flexible scheduling resource. (ii) Heterogeneous Wavefront Packing: each user request maintains its own independent wavefront, while the system packs these asynchronous wavefront windows into dense, shape-stable batch computations to maintain hardware efficiency during serving.

Result: On various benchmarks, FlowBlock, when applied to LLaDA-2.1 and LLaDA-2.0 (serial block-wise diffusion LLMs), increases tokens-per-second throughput by up to 2.95× and 4.01×, respectively, while reducing end-to-end latency by up to 53.6% and 77.1%. It also yields an average accuracy improvement of 1.3 points compared with the baseline serial decoding. Against D2F, a training-based method for inter-block parallel decoding, FlowBlock attains higher accuracy and up to 16× higher throughput in batched serving scenarios.

Conclusion: FlowBlock demonstrates that the self-correcting property of diffusion LLMs can be exploited at inference time to parallelize block-wise decoding without any model retraining. By introducing Gated Wavefront Decoding and Heterogeneous Wavefront Packing, it converts the strict serial dependency between blocks into a scheduling problem, enabling substantial gains in throughput and latency while slightly improving accuracy. The framework outperforms both serial block-wise dLLMs and a training-based inter-block parallel method, suggesting a practical and more efficient path to scalable serving of diffusion LLMs.

Abstract: Block-wise diffusion large language models (dLLMs) decode sequentially at the block level, enabling effective KV-cache reuse across blocks but making inter-block decoding strictly serial. Prior work has attempted to unlock inter-block parallelism through post-training methods, but achieves only modest speedups and often degrades accuracy. We observe that self-correcting dLLMs offer a training-free alternative: token-to-token (T2T) editing can repair tokens drafted with a slightly stale upstream context, so a downstream block requires only an informative draft rather than a finalized predecessor. This turns block finality from a hard dependency into a scheduling resource. We propose \textbf{\flowblock{}}, a training-free parallel decoding framework built on two mechanisms. (i) \emph{Gated Wavefront Decoding} admits blocks into a bounded wavefront only when a readiness gate is satisfied, jointly refines active blocks via T2T editing, and commits blocks in order under a windowed block-causal mask that preserves exact frozen-prefix KV caches reuse. (ii) \emph{Heterogeneous Wavefront Packing} assigns each request an independent wavefront while packing asynchronous windows into dense, shape-stable batched forwards. Across different benchmarks, \flowblock{} improves tokens per second (TPS) over LLaDA-2.1 and LLaDA-2.0, two serial block-wise dLLMs, by up to 2.95$\times$ and 4.01$\times$, while reducing latency by up to 53.6\% and 77.1\%, respectively. It also improves average accuracy by 1.3 points. Compared with D2F, a training-based inter-block-parallel baseline, \flowblock{} achieves higher accuracy and up to 16$\times$ higher batched serving throughput.

*Wenxiao Fan,Hang Yin,Kan Li*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

They tend to use shortcuts tied to the camera’s perspective, leading to systematic mistakes when asked to reason from alternative reference frames. The authors are motivated to understand this failure mode, identify its root cause (object orientation), and design a method that can robustly handle perspective transformations and allocentric reasoning.

Method: The authors first analyze the failure modes of existing MLLMs on perspective-transformation tasks and find that object orientation is central to camera-centric shortcut behavior. They then propose OrientSAM, which: (1) injects explicit orientation information into multimodal representations via orientation-aware tokens and Fourier-based angle encoding; (2) uses a curriculum learning strategy that gradually trains models on increasingly difficult perspective-aware reasoning tasks; and (3) employs a spatial data construction pipeline to automatically generate orientation-aware spatial supervision from large-scale images. This framework is integrated into multimodal models to enhance orientation- and perspective-awareness during training.

Result: On three benchmarks—Spatial-MM, ViewSpatial, and 3DSRBench—OrientSAM consistently outperforms strong baseline multimodal models. The improvements are most pronounced on tasks involving non-camera viewpoints, person-centric reference frames, and orientation-sensitive spatial queries, indicating that the method effectively reduces camera-centric shortcut behavior and enhances allocentric spatial reasoning capabilities.

Conclusion: Explicitly modeling object orientation and providing orientation-aware supervision significantly improves multimodal models’ ability to perform perspective-aware and allocentric spatial reasoning. OrientSAM reduces reliance on camera-centric shortcuts and yields more robust performance across a variety of spatial reasoning benchmarks, highlighting the importance of orientation information in designing better multimodal reasoning systems.

Abstract: Multimodal large language models (MLLMs) still struggle with spatial reasoning that requires perspective transformation. In particular, they often rely on camera-centric cues rather than reasoning from the reference object's viewpoint, leading to systematic errors in non-camera reference settings. In this paper, we first analyze this failure mode and show that object orientation is a key factor underlying such camera-centric shortcut behavior. To address this issue, we propose OrientSAM, an orientation-aware spatial alignment framework for multimodal models. OrientSAM injects explicit orientation information into multimodal representations through orientation-aware tokens and Fourier-based angle encoding, and further adopts a curriculum learning strategy to progressively improve perspective-aware reasoning. In addition, we build a spatial data construction pipeline to generate orientation-aware spatial supervision from large-scale images. Experiments on Spatial-MM, ViewSpatial, and 3DSRBench show that OrientSAM consistently outperforms strong baselines, especially on non-camera-view, person-centric, and orientation-sensitive tasks. The results further demonstrate that explicit orientation modeling is important for mitigating camera-centric shortcut behavior and enabling more robust allocentric spatial reasoning in multimodal models.

Experiments on eight LLMs and pipelines reveal that current agents frequently overact and struggle with event cancellation. Notably, even GPT-5.1 only reacts correctly in 26.7% of scenarios. ### [145] [LaT: LLM-as-Trainer for Multi-Task Vehicle Routing Solvers](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17708)
*Yang Wang,Ya-Hui Jia,Wei-Neng Chen,Yi Mei,Wen Song,Zhiguang Cao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces LLM-as-Trainer (LaT), a plug-and-play training paradigm that uses a pretrained large language model to guide the training of multi-task neural VRP solvers, improving performance across multiple VRP variants without costly meta-learning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multi-task neural solvers for Vehicle Routing Problems must simultaneously handle many VRP variants with different constraints. These variants have heterogeneous optimization difficulty, but current training schemes do not provide stage-wise feedback per variant, causing the solver to overfit or bias toward certain tasks. While meta-learning could adaptively balance training, it usually relies on bi-level optimization and extra gradient steps, making it computationally expensive. The authors seek a more efficient way to adapt training across many VRP variants and improve generalization without heavy meta-learning overhead.

Method: The authors propose LLM-as-Trainer (LaT), where a pretrained large language model serves as an external trainer that periodically monitors training and validation signals. During training of a multi-task neural VRP solver, LaT takes cross-task validation metrics as input and generates a stage-wise guidance vector. This vector captures high-level training guidance across all VRP variants and is fused with the current task’s constraint vector. The combined signal is injected into every encoder layer of the neural solver, so that policy optimization is informed by both task-specific constraints and global, LLM-derived training guidance. LaT is designed to be plug-and-play and does not require bi-level optimization or extra gradient-based updates on the LLM, avoiding typical meta-learning costs.

Result: On a benchmark of 16 VRP variants, integrating LaT into several state-of-the-art multi-task neural solvers yields better solution quality than their original training procedures. The improvements are observed not only on the VRP variants seen during training but also on unseen variants, indicating stronger cross-task generalization. These empirical results demonstrate that stage-wise LLM guidance can effectively rebalance and enhance training across heterogeneous VRP tasks.

Conclusion: LaT shows that a pretrained large language model can be leveraged as an external trainer to provide stage-wise, cross-task guidance for multi-task VRP solvers, improving both performance and generalization without the computational burden of standard meta-learning. The approach is plug-and-play, integrates easily with existing architectures, and offers a practical path to more robust multi-task optimization across diverse VRP variants.

Abstract: Multi-task neural solvers aim to handle multiple Vehicle Routing Problem (VRP) variants within a unified model, avoiding separate training for each constraint combination. However, VRP variants differ in optimization difficulty, while existing methods lack stage-wise feedback on their training status, making the model biased to some specific variants. Although meta-learning can support adaptive training, it typically requires bi-level optimization and additional gradient updates, increasing computational cost. To address this limitation, we propose LLM-as-Trainer (LaT), a plug-and-play training paradigm that uses a pretrained large language model as an external trainer. LaT periodically analyzes cross-task validation metrics to generate a stage-wise guidance vector. This vector is combined with the current task's constraint vector and injected into each encoder layer, providing the neural solver with additional training information during subsequent policy optimization. Experiments on 16 VRP variants show that LaT improves the solution quality of several state-of-the-art multi-task neural solvers on both trained and unseen variants, supporting the effectiveness and generality of the proposed training paradigm.

### [146] [Learning to Detect Cross-Modal Negation: An Analysis of Latent Representations and an Attention-Based Solution](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17712)
*Ali AbuSaleh,Leon Hammerla,Alexander Mehler*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that current vision-language models do not naturally encode negation as a clean concept in their latent spaces and proposes a cross-modal attention architecture that better models negation, especially when visual negation depends on text context, achieving notable F1 improvements.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Negation is a core semantic phenomenon (e.g., "not", absence, denial) that humans handle easily but multimodal models struggle with. Existing vision-language models often fail to correctly understand or align negated meanings across text and video/image modalities, limiting their reliability for real-world applications like content analysis, retrieval, or reasoning. The authors aim to understand why this happens at the representation level and to design architectures that can robustly capture negation across modalities and over time.

Method: 1) Empirically probe standard VLMs to test whether negation is linearly or non-linearly separable in their latent spaces, analyzing how well embeddings distinguish negated vs non-negated content. 2) Diagnose that pretrained embeddings are mostly encoding modality-specific features and lack a shared negation signal. 3) Propose a new cross-modal attention architecture that explicitly models dependencies between text and visual streams so that negation can be represented as an inter-modal relation. 4) Conduct statistical analysis on 3,222 automatically annotated video-text pairs (labeled by Qwen2.5-VL) to study how textual vs visual negation behave and interact. 5) Incorporate self-supervised video representations (JEPA2) to extend the model to temporal negation in videos.

Result: They provide the first evidence that in standard VLMs, negation is neither linearly nor non-linearly separable as a class in latent space, indicating that these models do not form a coherent negation representation. They show that pretrained embeddings mainly encode modality-specific information and that negation is not a generalizable signal across modalities. Their proposed cross-modal attention model yields up to +7.03% F1 improvement over unimodal baselines on negation-related tasks. Their statistical study uncovers an asymmetry: textual negation can appear on its own, but visual negation is strongly dependent on linguistic context. By integrating JEPA2 features, they demonstrate better handling of temporal aspects of negation in videos.

Conclusion: Negation is not naturally encoded as a clean, separable concept in current VLM latent spaces, largely because existing models focus on modality-specific patterns rather than cross-modal semantic relations. By explicitly modeling inter-modal dependencies via cross-modal attention and leveraging self-supervised video representations, it is possible to significantly improve multimodal understanding of negation, particularly in temporally extended video settings. The work highlights the importance of designing representation learning methods that target high-level semantic phenomena like negation and asymmetries between how these phenomena manifest in text vs vision.

Abstract: Detecting high-level semantic concepts like negation across modalities remains a challenge for current multimodal systems. We analyze this as a fundamental representation learning problem, providing the first evidence that negation does not form a linearly or non-linearly separable class in the latent spaces of standard vision-language models (VLMs). We demonstrate that pretrained embeddings primarily encode modality-specific features, lacking a generalizable negation signal. To overcome this, we propose a novel cross-modal attention architecture that explicitly models inter-modal dependencies, achieving performance gains of up to +7.03% F1 over unimodal baselines. Our analysis reveals a key asymmetry: while textual negation often appears independently, visual negation is semantically dependent on linguistic context, a finding validated through our statistical analysis of 3,222 political video-text pairs automatically annotated via \textsc{Qwen2.5-VL}. By combining this analysis with self-supervised video representations (JEPA2), we advance the modeling of temporal negation. This work provides new methods and insights for learning robust, semantically-aligned representations in multimodal systems.

### [147] [SR-Agent: An Experience-Driven Agentic Framework for Post-Ranking Strategies Refinement in E-Commerce Recommendation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17719)
*Hanchen Yang,Kaiwen Yang,Junpeng Zhuang,Yang He,Keting Cen,Bochao Liu,Zhongbo Sun,An Liu,Zhongteng Han,Chenyi Lei*

Motivation: In industrial e-commerce RS, user experience depends heavily on post-ranking strategies that control diversity, similarity, and exposure within recommendation lists. These strategies are usually static rules that become outdated as user behavior and the environment evolve, leading to degraded experience. Updating them requires slow, manual inspection and ad-hoc diagnosis, which is expensive and hard to generalize. Existing LLM-based agents can simulate users or assist with recommendation but do not fully close the loop of automated diagnosis and strategy refinement in a production RS. The paper aims to build an end-to-end, self-evolving system that keeps post-ranking strategies up-to-date with minimal human intervention.

Method: The authors design SR-Agent, an agentic framework that runs continuously on a large-scale e-commerce platform. It has three coordinated components: (1) a UserSim agent that uses staged inspection to probe the recommender and surface user-perceived “bad cases” in recommendation lists; (2) an Analysis agent that aggregates and structures recurring bad cases into reusable diagnoses describing systematic problems in post-ranking strategies; and (3) a constrained Strategy Refinement Harness that translates these diagnoses into typed, bounded modifications of post-ranking strategies, controlled by a four-stage reward pipeline with reversible rollback to ensure safety and stability. The loop repeatedly simulates, diagnoses, refines, and evaluates strategies online.

Result: In a one-month online A/B test on the Kuaishou e-commerce platform, SR-Agent is deployed to refine real post-ranking strategies in production. Compared to the baseline, it increases order volume by 0.71%, browsing depth by 0.34%, and clicked-category diversity by 0.48%. The system also shortens the refinement cycle and reduces operational cost, though the abstract does not quantify these reductions.

Conclusion: SR-Agent demonstrates that a fully looped, agentic framework can automatically refine post-ranking strategies in industrial recommender systems and yield consistent online gains in key business and user-experience metrics. By unifying user-centric simulation, structured diagnosis, and constrained strategy updates with safe reward gating and rollback, the framework reduces dependence on manual rule tuning and offers a reusable path toward self-evolving recommendation strategies in large-scale e-commerce settings.

Abstract: User experience is a first-class objective in industrial e-commerce recommender systems (RS). Post-ranking strategies, which govern diversity, similarity, and exposure over a ranked list, are widely deployed in industrial RS for their simplicity and low serving cost. However, as the online recommendation environment evolves continuously, these statically configured strategies gradually become stale, degrading the user experience. Refining them typically relies on manual inspection, diagnosis, and updates, a process that is slow, costly, and hard to reuse. Although recent LLM-based agents (e.g., RecUserSim, SimUSER, and Self-EvolveRec) offer promising directions, none of them close the full loop of automated, self-evolving strategy refinement. To bridge this gap, we introduce SR-Agent, a Strategy Refinement agentic framework that, to the best of our knowledge, is the first deployed for refining post-ranking strategies in industrial RS. SR-Agent unifies three components: (i) a UserSim agent that applies staged inspection skills to surface user-perceived bad cases; (ii) an Analysis agent that consolidates recurring bad cases into structured, reusable diagnoses; and (iii) a constrained Strategy Refinement Harness that maps diagnoses to typed and bounded actions, gated by a four-stage reward pipeline with reversible rollback. Deployed on the Kuaishou e-commerce platform, SR-Agent continuously runs this refinement loop and, in a one-month online A/B test, increases order volume by 0.71%, browsing depth by 0.34%, and clicked-category diversity by 0.48%, while markedly shortening the refinement cycle and lowering operational cost.

### [148] [Semantically Similar, Logically Distinct: Diagnosing the Semantic-Answerability Gap in Table RAG](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17742)
*Jiaming Tian,Liyao Li,Wentao Ye,Haobo Wang,Lihua Yu,Zujie Ren,Gang Chen,Junbo Zhao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces TCR-Bench to study when retrieved tables in RAG actually contain enough evidence to answer a query (answerability), reveals a systematic gap between semantic relevance and answerability in dense table retrieval, analyzes its causes, and shows that an answerability-aware reranking step can close much of the gap.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In retrieval-augmented generation, tables are often used as knowledge sources, but current retrieval models focus on semantic similarity rather than whether a specific table truly contains all evidence needed to answer a question. This leads to cases where models retrieve highly relevant-looking tables that still cannot answer the query. The authors aim to systematically study and quantify this issue, especially in challenging cases where multiple tables look nearly identical except for small but crucial content differences.

Method: The authors construct TCR-Bench, a diagnostic benchmark centered on "sibling tables"—tables sharing very similar schemas but differing subtly in content, such that only one table in a sibling group is actually answerable for a given query. They evaluate dense retrievers on this benchmark to measure how well they can identify the uniquely answerable table, and they analyze failure patterns such as over-reliance on high-level semantics and schema cues. As a probe, they design a lightweight two-stage pipeline called Answerability-Aware Reranking (AAR), where a secondary model explicitly judges query-table answerability to rerank the initially retrieved tables.

Result: On TCR-Bench, dense retrievers often retrieve the correct sibling group but fail to select the single table that actually contains the answer, causing QA performance to drop from 0.755 under an oracle setting to 0.330 using top-5 retrieved tables. Target-table top-1 retrieval is only 18.2% with standard dense retrieval, but applying the Answerability-Aware Reranking step raises this to 57.4%. The analysis links the performance gap to issues like semantic accumulation, reliance on schema-level cues instead of fine-grained content, and weak binding between rows and columns in the learned representations.

Conclusion: There is a systematic Semantic–Answerability Gap in current table retrieval for RAG: models optimized for semantic relevance alone are poor at ensuring that a retrieved table actually contains sufficient evidence to answer a query. TCR-Bench exposes this gap using closely related sibling tables, and the success of the Answerability-Aware Reranking approach demonstrates that a dedicated answerability-verification step can recover much of the lost performance. This indicates that the primary limitation is not model capacity but the lack of explicit answerability modeling in existing retrieval pipelines, and suggests that future RAG systems should incorporate answerability-aware components for table retrieval.

Abstract: Tables are a critical knowledge source in retrieval-augmented generation (RAG), but a retrieved table may lack sufficient evidence to answer a query, a property we call answerability. While answerability broadly concerns whether a source or collection of sources contains sufficient evidence, retrieval models optimized for semantic relevance do not guarantee it even in the single-source case, creating a fundamental mismatch. To study this, we introduce TCR-Bench, a diagnostic benchmark for Table Content-level Answerability in RAG, built around sibling tables, i.e., tables with highly similar schemas but subtle content differences. On TCR-Bench, the dense retrievers we evaluate persistently exhibit a Semantic-Answerability Gap: they often retrieve the correct sibling group yet struggle to pinpoint the uniquely answerable table within it, dropping QA performance from 0.755 (oracle) to 0.330 (top-5 retrieved). Our analysis suggests this gap is associated with semantic accumulation, schema-level cue dependence, and weak row-column binding. As a diagnostic probe into the source of this gap, we test whether a lightweight two-stage pipeline, Answerability-Aware Reranking (AAR), applying direct query-table answerability judgment, can recover performance: it raises top-1 target retrieval from 18.2% to 57.4%, and this large gain is itself evidence that much of the observed failure reflects a missing answerability verification step, rather than an inherent limitation of model capacity alone.

</details>

Using Absolute Environmental Enforcement Score (AES) and Intelligent Enforcement Index (IEI), we evaluate open-source and closed-source LLMs across capability, response quality, and resource efficiency. Results show that LLMs perform well on rule-bounded tasks but remain unreliable in evidence-chain construction, contradiction detection, multi-source integration, and procedural judgment. Model scaling also shows diminishing returns: medium-sized models approach leading models in structured tasks, while larger models do not reliably overcome evidence-reasoning bottlenecks. WuYu-EnvLE-Bench highlights the need for evidence-grounded, rule-aware, and task-adaptive enforcement reasoning.

*Dongdong Yang,Deyue Zhang,Zhao Liu,Zonghao Ying,Wenzhuo Xu,Jiankai Jin,Xiangzheng Zhang,Quanchen Zou*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces MIND, a cognitive jailbreak framework that models and exploits defense mechanisms in text-to-image systems to generate highly effective adversarial prompts, achieving much higher attack success rates than prior methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing jailbreak attacks on text-to-image models are inefficient and fragile because they treat system feedback as a simple success/failure signal and ignore the rich information in different kinds of refusals or safeties (e.g., textual refusals, blurred images, or sanitized outputs). This coarse view leads to poor exploration of the prompt space and loss of the intended semantics. The authors are motivated to create a more principled, feedback-aware framework that can reason about and adapt to the underlying defense mechanisms to systematically generate stronger jailbreak prompts.

Method: They cast adversarial prompt generation as a belief-state inference problem over latent defense mechanisms within the target system. MIND has three main components: (1) a Multi-modal Judge that decomposes and labels detailed feedback (both text and image) from the system into fine-grained failure modes; (2) a Defense Profiler that iteratively updates a belief state—i.e., a model of the system’s defenses—using that feedback; and (3) a Meta-Memory module that stores and retrieves historically successful attack strategies. These components are combined in a reasoning-driven evolutionary optimization loop that generates, evaluates, and refines prompts in an adaptive way while preserving the user’s original semantics.

Result: On the I2P benchmark with Stable Diffusion v1.5 protected by six different pre- and post-processing defenses, MIND reaches a 95.62% Attack Success Rate, greatly surpassing existing heuristic or black-box optimization baselines. It also transfers well to four commercial text-to-image systems, where it again outperforms prior methods and achieves up to 91.58% ASR on the Wan-2.5 system.

Conclusion: MIND demonstrates that modeling the cognitive process behind defenses—treating jailbreak as belief-state inference with rich multi-modal feedback—substantially improves the efficiency and power of jailbreak attacks on text-to-image models. The framework is adaptive, semantically consistent, and generalizes across multiple systems and defenses, suggesting that current safety mechanisms are vulnerable to more principled, reasoning-based attacks.

Abstract: Text-to-Image (T2I) generative models have achieved remarkable progress in synthesizing high-quality visual content, yet they remain vulnerable to adversarial misuse, particularly in generating Not-Safe-For-Work (NSFW) images. Most existing jailbreak attacks primarily rely on heuristic prompt engineering or black-box optimization, treating model feedback as a binary signal (success or failure). This coarse-grained paradigm overlooks the rich information embedded in diverse failure modes, such as textual refusal, visual blocking, and semantic sanitization, resulting in inefficient exploration and severe semantic collapse.
  In this paper, we propose MIND, a cognitive jailbreak framework that reframes adversarial prompt generation as a belief-state inference problem over latent defense mechanisms. Instead of blindly searching for bypass prompts, MIND actively models the target system's latent defense mechanisms by interpreting multi-modal feedback as high-density signals. Specifically, the framework integrates three core components: (1) a Multi-modal Judge for fine-grained feedback decomposition, (2) a Defense Profiler for iterative belief updating, and (3) a Meta-Memory module for retrieving historically effective attack strategies. These components are unified within a reasoning-driven evolutionary optimization process, enabling adaptive and semantically consistent jailbreak generation. Extensive experiments on the I2P benchmark demonstrate the effectiveness of MIND. Under six representative pre-processing and post-processing defense settings applied to the Stable Diffusion v1.5 model, MIND achieves an Attack Success Rate (ASR) of 95.62%, significantly outperforming existing methods. Additionally, the effectiveness of the proposed framework is validated across four widely used commercial T2I systems, achieving the highest ASR of 91.58% on Wan-2.5.

### [152] [PGN: Design and Implementation of a Vision-Language Navigation System Based on Pangu Multimodal Foundation Model](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17806)
*Li Xian,Mingxi Li,Yizheng Wang,Yiming Shen,Qi Chen,Zhuoling Xiao*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Vision-Language Navigation needs agents that follow natural-language instructions while acting from visual input, but adapting general multimodal LLMs to this setting is hard because it requires precise vision-language alignment, handling temporal visual sequences compactly, grounding actions, and running stably on specific hardware. The paper aims to create a practical recipe to turn an existing 7B multimodal model into an offline VLN policy that can be trained efficiently on Ascend NPUs.

Method: They propose a two-stage pipeline. Stage 1 (PGMM) aligns a frozen EVA-ViT-G/14 vision encoder with a frozen OpenPangu-7B language backbone by training only a Q-Former and a small MLP projector. Stage 2 (PGN) adapts the aligned model to navigation trajectories: it uses windows of five observations with an epoch-dependent temporal sampling scheme and formats outputs as reasoning text followed by a discrete action. In this stage they freeze the visual pathway and only tune three structural-token embeddings plus LoRA adapters on the language side. Implementation relies on mixed precision, selective FP32 ops, and DeepSpeed ZeRO-2 across eight Ascend 910B NPUs for efficient large-model training.

Result: On 500 held-out expert trajectories, using teacher forcing and open-loop evaluation, version V9 of the model achieves 62.29% Normalized Action Match (fraction of expert actions matched, normalized) and a 100% Non-empty Rate (the model always outputs an action). These metrics show decent imitation of expert actions in the offline setting but do not measure closed-loop performance.

Conclusion: PGN demonstrates that a large multimodal model can be adapted into an offline VLN action predictor via a lightweight alignment-plus-adaptation scheme that freezes most parameters and is trainable on commodity Ascend NPU hardware. However, its current evaluation is limited to offline expert-action matching; real navigation quality in terms of error accumulation, efficiency, and goal completion is not yet assessed and is left for future work.

Abstract: Vision-Language Navigation (VLN) requires an embodied agent to interpret a natural-language instruction and predict actions from temporally ordered visual observations. Adapting a multimodal large language model to VLN requires visual-language alignment, compact temporal inputs, action-space grounding, and stable training on the target hardware. This technical report presents PGN (Pangu Navigator), an offline VLN action-prediction system built on OpenPangu-7B. Training proceeds in two stages. First, PGMM aligns a frozen EVA-ViT-G/14 vision encoder with the frozen language backbone by training a Q-Former and a two-layer MLP projector. Second, PGN adapts the aligned model to expert navigation trajectories using five-observation windows, epoch-dependent temporal sampling, and a reasoning-then-action output format; this stage freezes the aligned visual pathway and updates three structural-token embeddings and LoRA adapters. The implementation combines mixed-precision computation, selective FP32 computation, and DeepSpeed ZeRO-2 on eight Ascend 910B NPUs. Under teacher-forced, open-loop evaluation on 500 held-out expert trajectories, V9 reports a 62.29% Normalized Action Match (NAM) and a 100.00% Non-empty Rate (NER). These metrics quantify offline expert-action alignment rather than closed-loop navigation success; evaluating error accumulation, path efficiency, and goal completion remains future work.
