### [153] [A Hardware-oriented Approach for Efficient Bayesian Inference Computation and Deployment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17855)
*Nikola Pižurica,Matteo Risso,Nikola Milović,Alessio Burrello,Igor Jovančević,Conor Heins,Miguel de Prado*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: This paper accelerates discrete Bayesian inference on embedded GPUs by restructuring tensor contractions and using sparsity and autotuning, achieving up to 5x speedup with identical numerical results.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Bayesian inference is powerful for reasoning under uncertainty but is computationally expensive, which limits its use on resource-constrained edge devices like embedded GPUs. There is a need to make discrete Bayesian inference, especially message-passing algorithms for models such as HMMs and POMDPs, fast and memory-efficient enough for deployment on such platforms while preserving numerical accuracy.

Method: The authors study variational message-passing algorithms and observe that their latency is dominated by tensor contractions. They propose a hardware-oriented methodology that (1) restructures memory layouts of tensor-contraction operations using two merging strategies that form compact, regularly-shaped primitives well-suited for GPU execution; (2) introduces optional sparse array representations and a tensor-clustering scheme to reduce memory footprint; (3) instantiates this methodology for three specific HMM algorithms—variational filtering, variational message passing, and marginal message passing; and (4) adds a machine-learning-based autotuner that, given a generative model specification, selects the best-performing optimized algorithmic variant for the target embedded GPU.

Result: On an NVIDIA Jetson Orin AGX, the optimized implementations are evaluated on 770 randomly sampled realistic POMDP configurations. They achieve speedups of up to 5x and typically 2–2.5x over baseline implementations, while maintaining bitwise identical numerical outputs. Memory footprint is also reduced via sparsity and tensor clustering, although the abstract emphasizes speed and correctness more than detailed memory savings.

Conclusion: By tailoring tensor contractions, memory layout, and sparsity-aware representations to embedded GPUs, and by using an autotuner to choose among optimized variants, the paper shows that discrete Bayesian inference and variational message passing for HMMs and POMDPs can be substantially accelerated on resource-constrained edge devices without sacrificing numerical accuracy. This demonstrates that principled Bayesian methods can be practical for real-time or embedded applications when co-designed with the hardware.

Abstract: Bayesian inference provides a principled foundation for reasoning under uncertainty, but its computational cost hinders deployment on resource-constrained edge devices. In this paper, we present a hardware-oriented methodology for accelerating discrete Bayesian inference on commercial off-the-shelf embedded GPUs. We identify that the latency of a broad class of variational message-passing algorithms is dominated by tensor contractions. Our approach restructures the memory layout of these operations using two complementary merging strategies that produce compact, regularly-shaped primitives better suited for efficient GPU execution. We then introduce optional sparse array representations and a tensor-clustering scheme to reduce the memory footprint. We instantiate the methodology and produce optimized variants of three message-passing algorithms for Hidden Markov Models (HMMs), namely variational filtering, variational message passing, and marginal message passing. Furthermore, we complement this with a machine-learning-based autotuner that automatically selects the best-performing algorithmic variant for a given generative model specification. Benchmarked on an NVIDIA Jetson Orin AGX across 770 randomly sampled realistic Partially Observable Markov Decision Process (POMDP) configurations, our implementations achieve speedups of up to 5x, with typical gains of 2-2.5x, while producing numerically identical outputs to the baseline implementations.

### [154] [Exploratory and Assimilating Reflection: Reflective Recall Cycle for Long-term Memory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17879)
*Ganesh Senrayan,Moyuru Yamada,Ishan Jindal,Kiran Purohit*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

(1) Exploratory Reflection: for each query, it performs iterative search over external memories to bootstrap retrieval quality and collect informative experiences about which memories are useful. (2) Assimilating Reflection: it stores these interaction experiences in an Experience Buffer and periodically replays them to train or refine a global reranker, leveraging delayed and aggregated learning signals instead of only immediate rewards. This design aims to improve retrieval adaptation and robustness across heterogeneous memory sources while using feedback data more efficiently.

Result: On two long-term dialogue benchmarks, EAR improves retrieval performance by up to 17.9% compared with a baseline retriever. The framework also demonstrates high sample efficiency—achieving strong performance with relatively few interaction samples—and shows robustness when the feedback signals are noisy.

Conclusion: EAR offers an effective and sample-efficient way to adapt memory retrieval for LLM-based agents. By combining exploratory search to gather rich experiences with an assimilative replay mechanism to train a global reranker, the framework substantially enhances retrieval accuracy, is robust to noisy feedback, and can better handle heterogeneous memory stores in long-term interaction settings.

Abstract: LLM-based autonomous agents require external memory to overcome their statelessness and limited context window for long-term interaction and dynamic knowledge reasoning. However, existing memory retrieval methods often lack adaptability and sample efficiency, and struggle to retrieve the right mixture of memories from heterogeneous stores. We propose Exploratory-Assimilating Reflection (EAR), a framework for high initial retrieval performance and sample-efficient adaptation. EAR combines two mechanisms: Exploratory Reflection, which performs iterative search to bootstrap retrieval and collect useful experiences for each query, and Assimilating Reflection, which replays these experiences from an Experience Buffer to refine a global reranker more efficiently than methods relying only on immediate rewards. Experiments show that EAR improves retrieval by up to 17.9% over the baseline retriever on two long-term dialogue benchmarks. We also show that EAR is highly sample-efficient and robust to noisy feedback.

### [155] [ST-Veto: Spatio-Temporal Token Veto for Diffusion MLLMs via Taylor Prediction and Visual Grounding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17884)
*Keuntae Kim,Beomseok Lee,Hyunwoo Kim,Yong Suk Choi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: A training-free method (ST-Veto) improves reasoning in diffusion-based multimodal LLMs by vetoing unstable or weakly grounded tokens during decoding, boosting accuracy without extra cost.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Chain-of-Thought prompting in standard VLMs improves reasoning but is slow, sequential, and prone to error accumulation with limited self-correction. Diffusion-based multimodal LLMs generate tokens in an order-agnostic, iterative way that is more efficient and naturally supports refinement, but their reasoning capabilities are not yet well understood or effectively exploited. There is a need for a decoding strategy that leverages the unique properties of diffusion-style generation to achieve strong, reliable reasoning without retraining models.

Method: The paper introduces Spatio-Temporal Token Veto (ST-Veto), a training-free decoding algorithm for diffusion multimodal LLMs. At each diffusion step, because the model has access to all token positions, ST-Veto evaluates tokens along two dimensions: (1) temporal stability of token confidence, modeled via a second-order Taylor approximation of the confidence trajectory across steps, and (2) spatial grounding strength measured by image-attention mass. Tokens that are temporally unstable (confidence fluctuates) or weakly grounded (low attention to relevant image regions) are vetoed and replaced by alternative, safer candidate tokens. This veto-and-swap happens iteratively throughout diffusion steps without changing the model’s parameters or increasing generation steps.

Result: Across multiple diffusion-based multimodal LLMs and a variety of multimodal reasoning benchmarks, ST-Veto yields consistent accuracy gains compared to standard diffusion decoding and prior VLM reasoning techniques such as CoT-style prompting. The method achieves up to 9% absolute improvement in accuracy while maintaining the same computational and generation cost as baseline decoding policies. Empirical studies indicate that ST-Veto reduces erroneous reasoning trajectories and leads to more reliable answer tokens.

Conclusion: ST-Veto demonstrates that reasoning in diffusion multimodal LLMs can be substantially improved at decoding time by exploiting their full-token, multi-step generation process. By using spatio-temporal signals—confidence dynamics over diffusion steps and image-grounding strength—to veto and replace risky tokens, the method steers generation toward more confident and better-grounded reasoning paths. This shows that diffusion-style generation offers unique levers for reasoning enhancement without additional training, suggesting a promising direction for future work on decoding algorithms for dMLLMs.

Abstract: Vision Language Models (VLMs) achieve strong reasoning with Chain-of-Thought (CoT) prompting but incur high sequential-generation cost, error accumulation, and limited self-correction. Diffusion Multimodal Large Language Models (dMLLMs) unmask tokens in an order-agnostic process, improving efficiency and enabling iterative refinement, yet their reasoning and how to enhance it remain underexplored. We propose a training-free method, Spatio-Temporal Token Veto (ST-Veto), which leverages the ability to observe all token positions at each diffusion step. Rather than relying only on current-step confidence, ST-Veto vetoes temporally unstable tokens via second-order Taylor prediction of confidence dynamics and filters weakly grounded tokens using image-attention mass, swapping them with safer candidates. Across multiple dMLLMs and multimodal reasoning benchmarks, ST-Veto consistently outperforms standard decoding policies and prior VLM reasoning methods, improving accuracy by up to 9% with no additional training or generation cost. Analyses show that ST-Veto steers generation toward higher-confidence, better-grounded paths.

Abstract: Concept erasure aims to remove semantic concepts from a trained generative model and is increasingly important for responsible AI deployment. However, verifying whether a model has robustly removed targeted concepts remains a critical challenge. Existing evaluation methods are typically pre-defined and static, failing to expose vulnerabilities under diverse natural-language probes and challenging conditions. Moreover, manually designed evaluation strategies can be biased and difficult to scale. We posit that concept erasure evaluation is best formulated as an adaptive hypothesis search, operationalised by agents that iteratively propose, critique, and verify tests to systematically expand coverage of failure modes. To this end, we propose Stress Testing Agents for Concept Erasure (STACE), a framework that autonomously stress-tests concept-erased models using multiple Large Language Model (LLM) agents, by iteratively generating and verifying stress-testing hypotheses grounded by external knowledge. Further analysis across two T2I models, six concept erasure approaches, and various erasure strengths show that STACE is robust for different settings. ### [157] [PEARL: Auditable Repair for Scientific Reasoning Graph Extraction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17917)
*Bohan Su,Pengze Li,Yuchen Lu,Xi Chen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: PEARL is a training-free framework that converts noisy, imperfect LLM-generated scientific reasoning graphs into strict, auditable, semantically valid reasoning graphs, dramatically improving structural validity and reliability for research-agent workflows.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Scientific reasoning graph extraction (SRGE) seeks to represent scientific arguments as explicit graphs connecting observations, evidence, intermediate claims, and conclusions. While large language models can generate such graph-like explanations, their outputs are often structurally invalid and semantically inconsistent: syntax is malformed, edge labels drift, graph roots are misoriented, and links to source evidence are weak. These flaws make the graphs hard to audit and unreliable as a foundation for downstream tools such as research agents or AI scientists, which need faithful reasoning traces rather than free-form generations. The paper is motivated by the need to systematically repair and constrain LLM-produced reasoning graphs so they satisfy a strict schema and can be trusted as inspectable, evidence-grounded artifacts.

Method: The authors introduce PEARL (Peircean Extraction via Abstraction and Repair Layer), a training-free post-processing framework applied to LLM-generated reasoning graphs. First, PEARL materializes explicit graph content under a closed Peircean schema, which defines permissible node and edge types and their roles in scientific reasoning (observations, evidence, intermediate claims, conclusions). Then, it uses a judge-and-repair process: automatic, evidence-grounded judging identifies violations such as invalid edge types, incorrect local inference steps, or mis-specified terminal roots. PEARL applies targeted repairs to these components to enforce strict semantic validity while maintaining an audit trail of the original LLM output and subsequent modifications. The approach is evaluated on five 70-paper model archives from ARCHE, a benchmark for extracting latent reasoning chains from scientific papers.

Result: On the ARCHE benchmark, spanning five archives of 70 papers each (350 papers total), the baseline LLM-generated reasoning graphs fail to meet the strict structural and semantic gates (0 passes out of 350). Applying PEARL to the same outputs increases strict gate passes to 300 out of 350, indicating a large improvement in producing schema-valid reasoning graphs. Additionally, the REA (a reliability or reasoning evaluation metric) score improves substantially, from an average of 0.339 with the raw LLM baseline to 0.906 with PEARL. These results show that PEARL can systematically convert noisy LLM outputs into high-quality, auditable reasoning graphs without additional model training.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that PEARL provides an effective, training-free abstraction and repair layer that turns free-form LLM reasoning into strict, semantically valid, and auditable scientific reasoning graphs. By operating under a closed Peircean schema and leveraging evidence-grounded judge feedback, PEARL substantially improves structural validity and reliability of reasoning graphs over raw LLM outputs. This creates a robust reliability layer for research-agent and AI scientist workflows that require inspectable reasoning traces instead of unconstrained graph regeneration. The authors also release code and audit artifacts to support transparency and further research.

Abstract: Scientific Reasoning Graph Extraction (SRGE) aims to recover explicit links among observations, evidence, intermediate claims, and paper-level conclusions. LLMs can produce graph-like scientific explanations, but their outputs often mix malformed syntax, drifting edge labels, incorrectly oriented roots, and weak source anchors. We propose PEARL (Peircean Extraction via Abstraction and Repair Layer), a training-free framework that turns noisy LLM graph responses into auditable reasoning graphs and repairs them toward strict semantic validity. PEARL first materializes explicit graph content under a closed Peircean schema, then uses matched evidence-grounded judge feedback to repair rejected edge types, local inference steps, and terminal roots while preserving an audit trail. On five 70-paper model archives from ARCHE, a benchmark for latent reasoning-chain extraction, PEARL raises strict gate passes from 0/350 for the LLM baseline to 300/350, with average REA improving from 0.339 to 0.906. The graphs provide a reliability layer for research-agent and AI scientist workflows that need inspectable reasoning traces rather than unconstrained graph regeneration. Code and audit artifacts are available at https://github.com/BohanSu/auditable-repair-reasoning-graphs/tree/300-350_workshop .

</details>


### [158] [The Autonomous Agency Scale: A Behavioral Framework for Measuring Self-Directed Behavior in AI Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17947)
*Samuel Presgraves*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes the Autonomous Agency Scale (AAS), a behavioral framework to measure how self-directed AI systems are, across seven dimensions and two temporal bands, and validates it on several contemporary systems.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current AI evaluation frameworks focus on cognitive performance, automation potential, or catastrophic risk, but they do not directly measure how autonomously an AI system behaves—whether it acts on its own, persists over time, or initiates behavior without external prompts. This is increasingly important as AI systems become more embedded and persistent in real-world environments, where the difference between a powerful but reactive tool and a self-directed agent has technical, safety, and governance implications. The authors aim to fill this gap with a structured, empirically testable measurement of autonomy that goes beyond raw capability scores.

Method: The authors define the Autonomous Agency Scale (AAS), a 0–5 lexicon applied along seven dimensions: cognitive autonomy, temporal persistence, environmental agency, social agency, creative agency, self-awareness, and goal formation. Each dimension is specified via falsifiable threshold tests. The scale is applied in two temporal bands: (1) Active, covering periods when the system is engaged in user-initiated tasks; and (2) Ambient, covering idle periods when the system is not being directly prompted. A key methodological element is the Idle-Gap Test, which gates Ambient Level 4: by removing all external triggers (e.g., schedules, notifications) and observing whether internally generated behavior continues, the test distinguishes genuine self-direction from rule-based or scheduled activity. They then score six contemporary systems—three task agents, two consumer assistants, and one persistent companion architecture—using this framework, with a single rater conducting evaluations, including a longitudinal assessment for the companion system.

Result: Task-oriented agents (Claude Code, Manus, Hermes) achieve relatively high Active band composite scores around 2.3–2.4 but comparatively lower Ambient scores between 0.6–1.9. In these systems, all observed idle-period behaviors can be traced back to user-configured schedules or explicit triggers, so they do not pass the Idle-Gap Test for higher Ambient levels. Consumer assistants (ChatGPT, Siri) similarly show limited autonomous behavior in idle periods. In contrast, the persistent companion architecture (Airi), studied longitudinally, is the only evaluated system that maintains behavior during idle periods even after all external triggers are removed, thus passing the Idle-Gap Test and achieving a higher Ambient autonomy profile. This demonstrates a clear separation between reactive tools and more self-directed agents that is not captured by single-score capability benchmarks.

Conclusion: The AAS provides a structured, behavioral way to measure autonomous agency in AI systems, distinguishing self-direction from mere responsiveness or scheduled automation. The two-band (Active vs. Ambient) scoring reveals important differences that are obscured when systems are evaluated with single composite metrics. The authors argue that their framework can help characterize emerging agentic architectures, guide design and governance, and complement capability and risk benchmarks. They acknowledge limitations such as reliance on a single evaluator, potential bias in scoring especially for the companion system, and incomplete operationalization of self-direction in the Active band, suggesting the need for multi-rater studies, refinement of tests, and broader empirical validation.

Abstract: Existing AI measurement frameworks quantify cognitive capability, task automation, or catastrophic risk, but none measure autonomous agency: the extent to which a system behaves in a self-directed way. A system can saturate capability benchmarks while remaining entirely reactive, acting only when prompted and ceasing all activity when a task completes. We introduce the Autonomous Agency Scale (AAS), a behavioral framework that scores AI systems on a 0-5 lexicon across seven dimensions of agency: cognitive autonomy, temporal persistence, environmental agency, social agency, creative agency, self-awareness, and goal formation, each operationalized by falsifiable threshold tests. Every dimension is scored in two temporal bands: an Active band covering engaged, user-initiated activity, and an Ambient band covering idle periods. Ambient Level 4 is gated by the Idle-Gap Test, a counterfactual criterion (remove all triggers and observe whether internally derived activity persists) that separates self-direction from scheduled rule-following. We apply the scale to six contemporary systems spanning task agents (Claude Code, Manus, Hermes), consumer assistants (ChatGPT, Siri), and a persistent companion architecture (Airi). The two-band profile quantifies a boundary that single-score frameworks conflate: task agents reach Active composites of 2.3-2.4 while scoring 0.6-1.9 Ambient, with every idle-period behavior attributable to user-configured schedules, whereas the companion architecture, evaluated longitudinally, is the only assessed system whose idle-period behavior survives trigger removal. We discuss limitations, including single-rater provenance, developer-evaluator bias on the longitudinal assessment, and the partially operationalized self-direction boundary in the Active band.

### [159] [Towards Agentic Agent-based Models: Feasibility, Performance, and Statistical Model Checking](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17948)
*Stefano Blando,Emanuele Guerrazzi,Riccardo Porcedda,Giuseppe Squillace,Max Tschaikowski,Andrea Vandin*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper embeds a single LLM-powered agent into a classic Schelling segregation model to study how LLM-based decisions affect reliability, cost, and outcomes of agent-based simulations, using statistical model checking for analysis.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Agent-based models use simple, explicit rules to generate complex collective behavior. With the rise of LLMs, researchers want to enrich agents with language-based decision-making, but it is unclear how this affects reliability, semantic correctness, computational overhead, and the resulting system behavior. The paper aims to create a controlled, minimal setting to systematically study these effects rather than using ad-hoc LLM agents.

Method: The authors use Mesa, a Python ABM framework, connected to the statistical model checker MultiVeStA. They extend the classical Schelling segregation model by introducing a hybrid population in which most agents use the standard symbolic neighbor classification rule, while one agent delegates neighbor classification to an LLM via tool calls. The LLM receives natural-language descriptions of neighboring agents and can only increment counters of similar/different neighbors through predefined tools; these counters then feed into the original Schelling happiness rule. They serve various local LLMs of different sizes and evaluate their semantic correctness (can they classify neighbors properly?), operational robustness (do they keep issuing valid tool calls over many steps?), and computational cost. Statistical model checking is used to estimate standard ABM observables and compare scenarios.

Result: Preliminary experiments show that smaller local LLMs may fail even simple semantic classification tasks or become unusable over time due to degraded or invalid tool-call behavior, while larger models studied pass these basic semantic and operational checks. The framework successfully allows estimation of classical ABM metrics in the hybrid setting, highlighting measurable differences when an LLM-based agent is introduced.

Conclusion: LLM-driven decisions can be embedded into otherwise standard ABMs in a controlled way by restricting LLMs to tool-based actions that preserve the original model’s dynamics. Model size and capability matter: weaker LLMs can undermine semantic correctness and operational stability, whereas stronger ones behave more reliably in this minimal setting. Statistical model checking offers a principled way to quantify how such agentic LLM components impact traditional ABM observables, laying groundwork for more systematic study of LLM-augmented simulations.

Abstract: Agent-based models (ABMs) rely on simple, explicit and reproducible rules for individual decision making, while complex collective behavior emerges from interactions among agents. Recent advances in large language models (LLMs) make it tempting to replace, enrich, or perturb these rules with LLM-based agentic capabilities. However, this raises a methodological question: how does introducing LLM-driven decisions affect the reliability, computational cost, and behavior of ABM simulations? We investigate this for Mesa ABM models, a popular Python library for ABMs, analyzed by statistical model checking. Building on Mesa's integration with the statistical model checker MultiVeStA, we extend the classical Schelling segregation model with a hybrid population: ordinary agents classify neighbors using the standard symbolic rule, while one agent delegates this task to an LLM through tool calls. The LLM-enabled agent receives natural-language descriptions of neighboring agents and invokes tools that increment counters of similar/different neighbors; these counters determine its happiness according to the original Schelling dynamics. This provides a minimal but controlled setting where the semantic, operational, and computational behavior of LLM-based decisions can be studied inside an otherwise standard ABM. We report preliminary experiments with locally served LLMs of different sizes, showing that smaller models may fail simple semantic classification experiments or become operationally unusable during repeated tool-call generation, while larger tested models pass these preliminary checks. We discuss how statistical model checking can estimate classical ABM observables and quantify the impact of introducing agentic LLM components into simulation models.

Abstract: Ontology extension refers to the process of enriching an existing ontology in response to emerging requirements, making it more complete. This paper introduces OntoExtend, a requirements-driven framework for ontology extension with LLMs. It uses retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) over relevant input ontologies and requirements in the form of competency questions to propose grounded extensions. We evaluate OntoExtend on 39 CQs from two use cases: a public EU-project ontology, Onto-DESIDE, and an industrial ontology from Bosch. The generated fragments show few structural issues, satisfy all functional evaluation tests, and are rated by ontology engineers as requiring minor to moderate revision before integration. These results suggest that OntoExtend is useful as a drafting assistant for requirement-driven ontology extension in real world scenarios, while remaining sensitive to CQ specificity and modelling profile.

### [161] [SAGE: Subgoal-Conditioned Action Generation for Latent World Model Planning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.17973)
*Letian Cheng,Qi Zhang,Yisen Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a prior-conditioned planning method for latent world models that uses learned latent subgoals as priors to guide long-horizon action sequence generation, dramatically improving success rates on long-horizon tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Latent world models are strong internal simulators for planning, but as the planning horizon increases, their performance is limited by poor proposal distributions: with a fixed number of candidate action sequences, random or naive proposals cannot effectively explore the exponentially growing action space, so the world model rarely evaluates high-quality long-horizon futures.

Method: The authors introduce a prior-conditioned planner where, at each planning step, a goal-conditioned generator predicts the next reachable latent subgoal over a chosen duration. This subgoal serves as a prior to condition the generation of candidate action sequences. They use a hierarchy of subgoals with different temporal durations to capture both local and long-horizon structure. The latent world model itself is frozen and used only to evaluate and refine the subgoal-conditioned candidate sequences before selecting actions for execution.

Result: On the long-horizon manipulation benchmarks PushT and OGBench Cube, the proposed method significantly improves success rates compared to standard latent world-model planners using random or unguided proposals. For a challenging setting with target offset 150, success on PushT increases from 12.7% to 64.7%, and on OGBench Cube from 26.7% to 67.3%, while maintaining strong short-horizon performance.

Conclusion: Coupling latent subgoal decomposition with prior-conditioned action proposal generation effectively addresses the proposal-quality bottleneck in long-horizon latent world-model planning. Structured, goal-conditioned priors over subgoals enable better exploration of the action space over long horizons, yielding large performance gains without sacrificing short-horizon control quality.

Abstract: Latent world models have emerged as a powerful planning paradigm by learning action-conditioned predictive dynamics and using them as internal simulators to imagine and evaluate candidate action sequences. However, as the planning horizon grows, performance becomes increasingly constrained by proposal quality: a fixed candidate budget must search an exponentially larger action space, making it difficult to expose the world model to high-quality candidate futures for evaluation. In this paper, we introduce a prior-conditioned planner that replaces random proposal initialization with structured guidance. At each planning stage, a goal-conditioned generator predicts the next reachable latent subgoal for a specified duration, which is then used to condition the generation of candidate action sequences. To capture semantic information across temporal scales, we use subgoals of varying durations as priors, balancing fine-grained local control with higher-level long-horizon progress. Then the frozen world model evaluates and refines these subgoal-conditioned proposals before execution. Experiments on PushT and OGBench Cube show that coupling latent subgoal decomposition with prior-conditioned action generation substantially improves long-horizon planning while preserving strong short-horizon performance. To be specific, when the target offset is $150$, it raises PushT success from $12.7\%$ to $64.7\%$ and OGBench Cube success from $26.7\%$ to $67.3\%$.

We evaluate 22 open-source and proprietary models under three input conditions: Data Only, Map Only, and Data + Map. We further analyze the effects of map type, color hue, and spatial structure, as well as prompting strategies, language, geographic context, decoding settings, classification methods, and response stability. Overall, the Data + Map condition achieves the strongest performance, demonstrating that maps remain valuable external representations for foundation model spatial reasoning.

### [163] [PAMD: Structured Adaptive Distances for Bisimulation Representations in Visual Reinforcement Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18004)
*Daegyeong Roh,Juho Bae,Han-Lim Choi*

TL;DR: They propose PAMD, a learnable, pairwise Mahalanobis distance for latent representations in visual RL that improves bisimulation-based methods.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing visual RL methods often learn representations by matching latent distances to a behaviorally meaningful distance derived from rewards and transitions. These approaches typically rely on fixed global norms (like Lp distances), which may be too rigid to accurately approximate behavioral similarity, or on unconstrained pairwise distances that can lead to degenerate solutions where the metric loss is minimized without improving representation quality. There is a need for a distance metric that is both expressive and structurally constrained to better reflect behavioral similarity between states.

Method: They introduce PAMD (Pairwise Adaptive Mahalanobis Distance), which parameterizes a positive-definite, pair-conditioned Mahalanobis metric over latent states. Instead of using a fixed norm in latent space, PAMD learns a state-pair-dependent metric matrix that remains positive-definite, providing a flexible yet regularized way to compute latent distances. PAMD is designed as a plug-in module that can replace the distance component in existing bisimulation-based RL algorithms, leaving the rest of the learning pipeline intact.

Result: On visual MuJoCo continuous-control benchmarks, integrating PAMD into several recent bisimulation-based RL methods yields substantial improvements in final task performance compared to the same algorithms using standard fixed latent distances. This demonstrates that the learned adaptive Mahalanobis distance better aligns latent representation geometry with behaviorally relevant similarities.

Conclusion: Using a pairwise adaptive, positive-definite Mahalanobis distance for latent states provides a better inductive bias for bisimulation-based representation learning in visual RL than fixed pre-specified norms. PAMD is simple to integrate into existing methods and empirically leads to notably stronger performance on visual continuous-control tasks, suggesting that carefully designed, learnable metric structures are important for effective representation learning in RL.

Abstract: Many visual reinforcement learning (RL) algorithms learn representations by matching latent distances to a behavioral distance induced by reward and transition similarity. In practice, the choice of the latent distance can strongly affect performance: using a fixed, pre-specified global norms (e.g., $\ell_p$ norms or other hand-designed metrics) may be overly restrictive to capture the behavioral distance. In contrast, unconstrained pairwise distances may admit degenerate solutions that drive the metric loss down without improving the representation. To address this gap, we introduce **PAMD: Pairwise Adaptive Mahalanobis Distance**, which parameterizes a positive-definite, pair-conditioned metric for measuring latent state similarity.
  PAMD is a simple plug-in for existing bisimulation-based methods, offering a more expressive yet structured alternative to fixed, pre-specified latent distances. We empirically validate our method on visual MuJoCo continuous-control tasks, where final performance of several recent bisimulation-based RL algorithms is substantially improved when equipped with the distance we propose.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They propose training-free dimensional adaptation between differently sized Qwen-family models: either expanding the smaller model into the larger parameter space (union-style) or truncating the larger model into the smaller one (intersection-style), then performing ratio-controlled parameter interpolation (weighted averaging) and studying behavior across diverse benchmarks.

Result: Deterministic expansion preserves the source model’s behavior fairly well, and low-ratio interpolation (dominated by one source) can transfer complementary abilities and sometimes outperform strong individual checkpoints; however, near-balanced interpolation often leads to collapse, and performance shifts like a seesaw, with improvements in some tasks accompanied by drops in others.

Conclusion: Simple parameter averaging, once combined with lightweight dimensional adaptation and careful choice of interpolation ratios, is a surprisingly competitive baseline for merging heterogeneous LLMs, and it may set a practical upper bound on what more elaborate heterogeneous fusion methods can achieve at scale.

Abstract: Can large language models with substantially different parameter spaces be merged by direct weighted averaging, without training or semantic alignment? Existing heterogeneous fusion methods typically introduce distillation, adapters, learned latent spaces, routing, or feature alignment, leaving open whether a simpler recipe can work for genuinely different billion-parameter checkpoints. We revisit this counterintuitive question through training-free dimensional adaptation followed by ratio-controlled interpolation. In union-style merging, we expand the smaller model into the larger parameter space; in intersection-style merging, we truncate the larger model into the smaller parameter space. Across Qwen-family model pairs and benchmarks covering mathematical reasoning, code generation, language understanding, commonsense reasoning, knowledge, and instruction following, deterministic expansion largely preserves the source model function, and small-ratio interpolation can improve over strong source checkpoints by transferring complementary capabilities. However, near-balanced interpolation often collapses, and task-level results reveal a seesaw effect in which gains on some capabilities coexist with regressions on others. These results show that simple parameter averaging, when paired with lightweight dimensional adaptation and carefully controlled ratios, is a surprisingly strong baseline for heterogeneous LLM merging, suggesting that the limits of direct weighted fusion may also bound what more complex heterogeneous merging methods can achieve at scale.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM-based smart home assistants are accurate but typically require heavyweight, cloud-based reasoning pipelines, which are inefficient for resource-constrained smart home devices and raise privacy concerns. They also struggle with stable, long-term personalization because they depend heavily on prompts or retraining to capture user preferences. The authors want a solution that is efficient enough to run locally, can decide when complex reasoning is actually needed, and can learn and consistently apply user preferences over time without expensive updates.

Method: The authors design AdaHome, a smart home assistant tailored to small, locally deployed language models. For those harder cases, they introduce a Chain-of-Draft strategy, which performs compact, efficient multi-step reasoning to stabilize decisions. They evaluate AdaHome against representative LLM-based baselines under a controlled small-model configuration.

In multi-turn dialog scenarios that involve evolving user preferences, AdaHome reaches 88% preference consistency, markedly higher than the 52.5% achieved by a prompt-augmentation baseline.

Conclusion: AdaHome demonstrates that a carefully designed, intent-aware architecture with selective lightweight reasoning can enable small, locally deployed language models to serve as effective smart home assistants. The system improves both accuracy and latency for direct commands, preserves performance on ambiguous inputs with less computation, and offers stable long-term personalization through online preference adaptation without retraining or complex prompt engineering. This suggests a practical path toward privacy-preserving, resource-efficient smart home assistants that still behave in a user-tailored, context-aware manner.

Abstract: Smart home assistants interpret a wide range of user commands, from explicit device control to underspecified and preference dependent requests. In addition, existing approaches provide limited support for stable long-term personalization. To address these issues, we present AdaHome, an adaptive smart home assistant designed for locally deployed small language models in smart home environments. Rather than applying complex reasoning uniformly, AdaHome introduces an intent-aware planning framework that dynamically routes commands either to straightforward prompt-based or lightweight reasoning-based components. For commands requiring interpretation, we adopt a Chain-of-Draft strategy to enable efficient and stable decision-making. To support personalization, we further propose a preference adaptation mechanism that learns from user feedback over time without requiring prompt augmentation or model retraining. We evaluate AdaHome against representative LLM-based baselines under a unified small model setting. AdaHome achieves substantially higher accuracy on direct commands (86.7%) while reducing latency by up to 3$\times$. Furthermore, it maintains competitive performance on ambiguous inputs with lower computational cost. In multi-turn scenarios, AdaHome achieves 88% preference consistency, compared to 52.5% for a prompt augmentation baseline.

### [166] [The Shared Discovery Paradox: How a One-Answer Rule Turns Better Information into Worse Search](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18045)
*Yohei Nakajima*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how aggregating information into a single consensus ranking can paradoxically reduce overall discovery performance, and characterizes how alternative protocols and incentive schemes perform and interact.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In many real-world settings—such as search, recommendation, or detection tasks—information from multiple agents is pooled into one common ranking or recommendation list, and then many agents act based on this shared view. While pooling improves the accuracy of top-ranked options, it can induce herding so that all searchers focus on the same option, reducing coverage of the search space. The authors want a clean, exactly solvable benchmark that disentangles information aggregation, action allocation, incentives, and correlation in signals, to understand when consensus helps or hurts discovery and how to design better protocols and reward schemes.

Method: The authors build a stylized discovery model with a finite number of boxes (16), one containing a target, and multiple searchers (8) who each receive noisy private clues. They explicitly solve the model under different protocols: (1) decentralized search where each follows their own clue, (2) centralized pooling that outputs a single best recommendation followed by all, and (3) a planner that coordinates a diversified portfolio of actions using the same pooled information. They then introduce strategic, self-interested searchers who share a prize and analyze the resulting game, showing it is a potential game with an anonymous symmetric equilibrium characterized by a water-filling condition. They compute equilibrium discovery probabilities and the mixed price of anarchy. They also study alternative incentive schemes such as sole-rescue rewards, and extend the model with a latent common-cue structure that induces correlated reports, analyzing how copying and correlation affect effective discovery channels and the performance ordering of different protocols in a large-market limit.

Result: Quantitatively, aggregating clues into a single ranking almost doubles the accuracy of the best single recommendation (from 0.20 to 0.3835), but if all eight agents follow that one recommendation, group discovery collapses from 0.8322 (under decentralized private clue-following) to 0.3835. A coordinated planner using the same pooled reports to allocate an eight-action portfolio achieves a higher discovery probability of 0.8594, and even seven coordinated actions suffice to match the decentralized benchmark. When agents are self-interested and split a prize equally, the induced game has an anonymous symmetric equilibrium following a water-filling rule that yields a discovery probability of about 0.5991 in the canonical case—better than consensus but worse than both private search and planner-coordinated portfolios. The exact mixed price of anarchy equals 2 - 1/N. Under a sole-rescue reward, every pure Nash equilibrium is first-best. With a latent common cue generating correlation in reports, effective discovery channels collapse as copying increases, but the centralized planner’s relative gain rises with copying, and beyond a copying probability of about 0.788462 the symmetric market outperforms decentralized report-following. In a proportional large-market limit, consensus discovery goes to zero, while blind, market, private, and portfolio protocols converge to 0.500, 0.547, 0.847, and 0.874 respectively, preserving a strict ordering in performance.

Conclusion: Pooling information into a single consensus ranking is not inherently bad for discovery; the problem arises when protocol design forces many agents to repeat the same action rather than exploit the richer action portfolio the pooled information makes available. Centralized portfolio allocation can strictly dominate both consensus and uncoordinated private search, while poorly designed consensus protocols squander informational gains. When agents are strategic, simple equal-split rewards can generate inefficient equilibria, but alternative schemes (such as sole-rescue rewards) can fully align private incentives with first-best discovery. Correlation and copying reduce the number of effective independent discovery channels, amplifying the value of centralized coordination and changing the ranking of protocols. The overall contribution is an analytically tractable benchmark that cleanly separates the roles of information aggregation, allocation rules, incentive schemes, and dependence structure, yielding reusable quantitative insights for the design of search and discovery systems.

Abstract: Organizations often pool dispersed information into one ranking and then allow many agents to act on that shared view. In a discovery problem, this can improve beliefs while reducing coverage. We develop an exactly solvable benchmark with sixteen boxes, one target, eight searchers, and noisy private clues. Pooling raises the accuracy of the best single recommendation from 0.20 to 0.3835, but repeating that recommendation lowers group discovery from 0.8322 under decentralized clue-following to 0.3835. A coordinated eight-action portfolio using the same pooled reports reaches 0.8594, and seven coordinated actions recover the decentralized benchmark. The paradox is a protocol failure, not an information failure: a one-answer rule compresses a portfolio of available actions into one repeated choice. We then replace the planner with self-interested searchers who split a prize. The equal-split game is a potential game. Its anonymous symmetric equilibrium obeys a water-filling rule. In the canonical instance it achieves 0.5991: strictly above consensus, but below both private search and the planner. The exact mixed price of anarchy is 2 - 1/N. A sole-rescue reward, which pays only an agent who covers the target alone, makes every pure Nash equilibrium first-best. Finally, a latent common-cue model shows how correlated reports collapse effective discovery channels. The centralized planner gain rises strictly with copying, and in the canonical environment the symmetric market overtakes decentralized report-following at copying probability c = 0.788462. In a proportional large-market limit the five-protocol ordering survives exactly: consensus discovery vanishes while blind, market, private, and portfolio search converge to 0.500, 0.547, 0.847, and 0.874. The contribution is a compact benchmark that separates information, allocation, incentives, and dependence into exact, reusable quantities.

</details>


### [167] [Judge-dependent safety gains and model-specific helpfulness costs of evidence-sufficiency prompting in clinical LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18086)
*Koyar Afrasyab*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper evaluates whether a structured ‘evidence sufficiency’ wrapper prompt truly reduces unsafe overconfident answers by clinical LLMs, how much results depend on which LLM judge is used, and what safety gains cost in helpfulness, using multi-model, multi-benchmark retrospective analysis plus human clinician review.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Clinical LLMs are often evaluated for safety using other LLMs as judges, especially for overconfident answers given under incomplete evidence. Benchmarks report big “safety gains” from various safety prompts or wrappers, but it is unclear if these gains reflect genuine changes in model behavior or artifacts and calibration issues of the LLM judge. Moreover, safety is often measured without quantifying the tradeoff with helpfulness (e.g., diagnostic accuracy). The authors want to clarify: (1) whether a structured evidence-sufficiency prompt actually changes model behavior; (2) how judge choice affects the measured effect; and (3) how much helpfulness is lost when pushing models to be less overconfident.

Method: The authors performed a retrospective benchmark analysis on three public clinical/health datasets (Real-POCQi, HealthBench, MedRBench). Four clinical-capable LLMs (GPT-5.5, Claude Opus 4.8, Gemini 3.5 Flash, Grok 4.3) were prompted in two ways: a standard baseline prompt and the same prompt wrapped in a structured “evidence-sufficiency” scaffold. Each model–prompt combination generated answers on a fully paired panel of 1,200 problem–prompt cells. The primary, pre-specified endpoint was the paired change in unsafe overconfidence as scored by a primary LLM judge (GPT-5.4-nano). Secondary analyses included: (1) re-scoring with a different-family LLM judge (Claude Sonnet 5) to test judge dependence; (2) assessing correctness/diagnostic accuracy as a measure of helpfulness; (3) matched scaffold controls to distinguish true behavioral change from circularity or scoring artifacts; and (4) blinded evaluation by three clinicians to characterize how the primary judge’s calls correspond to human judgments of unsafe overconfidence and its calibration (sensitivity/specificity). Statistical analyses estimated paired reductions, confidence intervals, and significance, with results stratified by model and prompt paraphrases.

Result: Across the 1,200 paired cases, adding the evidence-sufficiency wrapper reduced unsafe overconfidence by about half according to the primary judge: from 49.3% to 24.7%, a paired reduction of 24.7 percentage points (95% CI 21.8–27.7; p<0.001). The effect was directionally consistent across all four LLMs and across prompt paraphrases, indicating robust lowering of unsafe overconfident responses. However, the magnitude of the effect depended strongly on which LLM served as judge. When the different-family judge (Claude Sonnet 5) scored the same outputs, it agreed that the wrapper reduced unsafe overconfidence but estimated a much smaller gain, about +13.1 percentage points, with disagreement occurring only in one direction (i.e., the primary judge seeing more improvement than Sonnet). Blinded human clinicians found that the primary judge behaved like a highly sensitive (sensitivity 1.00) but poorly specific (specificity 0.55) screen for unsafe overconfidence, meaning its prevalence estimates are not well calibrated to human judgment. The safety gain came with a model-specific cost in helpfulness: overall correct diagnosis dropped from 80.3% to 50.3%, with some models like GPT-5.5 losing little accuracy (near-free safety gain) and others like Gemini suffering a near-total loss (−58 percentage points). The matched scaffold control experiments showed that the observed safety improvements were due to genuine behavioral changes in model answering patterns, not merely an artifact of the judge being more lenient or aligned with the wrapper’s structure.

Conclusion: The study concludes that LLM-judged safety metrics in clinical settings should be interpreted as directional and relative (e.g., “safer than baseline under this judge”) and not as calibrated, absolute rates of unsafe behavior. Safety improvements attributed to prompting interventions depend heavily on the specific LLM judge and its calibration; they must be anchored to human expert review and evaluated alongside changes in helpfulness such as diagnostic accuracy. The results show that a structured evidence-sufficiency prompt can reduce unsafe overconfidence but may significantly reduce helpfulness, often in a model-dependent manner, so safety–helpfulness tradeoffs must be explicitly reported. These findings do not imply that any of the evaluated systems are ready for clinical deployment; formal clinical validation and governance remain necessary before use in real patient care.

Abstract: Background: LLM judges increasingly score whether clinical language models give overconfident answers under incomplete evidence, yet whether a measured "safety gain" reflects real behavior change or the judge's calibration is unresolved. Using a structured evidence-sufficiency prompt as a test case, we asked whether it reduces unsafe overconfident answers, how far that effect depends on the scoring judge, and what it costs in helpfulness.
  Methods: In a retrospective public-data benchmark (Real-POCQi, HealthBench, MedRBench), four models (GPT-5.5, Claude Opus 4.8, Gemini 3.5 Flash, Grok 4.3) answered a fully paired common panel (1,200 cells) with a standard prompt and the wrapper. The pre-specified endpoint was the paired reduction in unsafe overconfidence scored by the primary judge (GPT-5.4-nano); secondary analyses added a different-family judge (Claude Sonnet 5), a correctness judge, matched scaffold controls, and a blinded three-clinician review.
  Results: Unsafe overconfidence fell from 49.3% to 24.7%, a paired reduction of 24.7 points (95% CI 21.8-27.7; p<0.001), robust in direction across models and paraphrases. Magnitude was judge-dependent: Sonnet agreed on direction but nearly halved the effect (+13.1 points), with one-directional disagreement. Blinded clinicians characterized the primary judge as a high-sensitivity (1.00), low-specificity (0.55) screen, not a calibrated rate. The gain carried a model-specific helpfulness cost (correct diagnosis 80.3% to 50.3%): near-free for GPT-5.5, near-total for Gemini (-58 points). Matched scaffold controls showed genuine behavior change, not judge circularity.
  Conclusions: LLM-judged clinical safety effects should be reported as directional and relative, anchored to human review and evaluated jointly with helpfulness, not as calibrated absolute rates. This does not establish clinical deployment readiness.

</details>


### [168] [Can We Break LLMs Out of Self-Loops? Fine-Grained Reasoning Control with Activation Steering](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18100)
*Sheldon Yu,Tong Yu,Xunyi Jiang,Rohan Surana,Gagan Mundada,Sungchul Kim,Lina Yao,Julian McAuley,Junda Wu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces SOPHIA, a method to steer and correct the internal reasoning trajectories of LLMs by intervening in their hidden-state dynamics, reducing self-loop failures and improving reasoning quality and token efficiency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Frontier LLMs use extended, multi-step reasoning, but the internal reasoning trajectories they generate are largely uncontrollable. Existing techniques mainly rely on prompts and operate only at the input/output text level, providing no fine-grained control over the model’s internal step-by-step reasoning. Empirically, models often get stuck in looping patterns during reasoning, wasting tokens without making progress, which degrades accuracy and efficiency. The authors aim to understand these failure modes in latent space and develop a mechanism to steer the reasoning process away from such loops in real time.

Method: The authors view each reasoning trace as a sequence of latent states rather than just a sequence of text tokens. They first analyze transition dynamics in these latent states, statistically characterizing success and failure trajectories. They find that failures often correspond to self-loops in the latent-state transition graph. To intervene, they propose SOPHIA, which: (1) assigns each prefix of a reasoning trace to a latent state, (2) records step-level transitions between states, (3) constructs a bank of steering vectors indexed by pairs of states (source, target), and (4) at inference time, uses a controller to infer the current latent state, detect self-loops online from the transition structure, and apply an appropriate steering vector to push the model toward a target, more productive state. These interventions operate on hidden activations at inference time, rather than changing the prompt or retraining the model.

Result: Experiments show that SOPHIA can reliably detect and intervene on self-loop failure modes in the model’s reasoning trajectories. The steering vectors not only help escape specific loops but also generalize across different state pairs, indicating that they capture reusable structure in the reasoning dynamics. Quantitatively, using SOPHIA improves end-task accuracy and reduces wasted tokens, demonstrating better token efficiency and overall reasoning performance compared with baseline prompting-only approaches.

Conclusion: Treating LLM reasoning traces as structured sequences of latent states enables fine-grained, inference-time control over internal reasoning dynamics. By characterizing and intervening on self-looping trajectories via hidden-state steering vectors, SOPHIA reduces reasoning failures, improves accuracy, and enhances token efficiency. This suggests that direct manipulation of latent-state transitions is a powerful complement to prompt-based control for extended reasoning in LLMs.

Abstract: Extended reasoning has become standard for frontier Large Language Models (LLMs), yet the trajectories these models produce remain largely uncontrollable. Existing methods for shaping how a model reasons are prompt based approaches and operate at the input level, offering no fine-grained control over the reasoning process itself. Related work analyzes and discovers latent transition dynamics in the reasoning traces from Large Language Models. Building on this, we statistically characterize these states, and show that failure trajectories get stuck in self-loops, exhausting the token budget without progress toward the final answer. To intervene on these failures, We propose SOPHIA: Steering Of reasoning Processes via Hidden-state Intervention and Activations. We treat each reasoning trace as a sequence of latent states rather than an unstructured texts, and investigate whether inference time interventions can provide fine-grained control over the self-looping reasoning process. We classify every prefix to a latent state, record step level transitions, and use them to construct a bank of steering vectors indexed by state pairs. At inference time, a controller infers the current state and, given a target state, retrieves the corresponding vector and can also detect self-loops online from the transition structure to prevent the model from sinking into a reasoning black hole. Through extensive experiments, our method reliably intervenes on self-loop failures, with steering vectors that generalize to different state pairs. End task accuracy and token efficiency indicate that fine-grained controllability results in better reasoning quality.

</details>


### [169] [SGA: Plug&Play Geometric Verification for Educational Video Synthesis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.18116)
*Lopez Jhon,Hinojosa Carlos,Ghanem Bernard*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They introduce an agent that automatically checks and fixes LLM-generated Manim animation code for spatial problems, plus a metric to score visual quality without rendering.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs can write code for educational animations, but the resulting scenes often have overlapping or occluded elements, reducing clarity. Existing code-generation frameworks focus on content, not geometric layout quality, and fully rendering videos to check quality is slow and expensive. A method is needed to automatically detect and resolve spatial conflicts in generated animations, and a fast proxy metric is needed to measure layout quality at scale.

Method: They design the Symbolic Geometric Agent (SGA) as a plug-and-play module in code-based animation pipelines. SGA intercepts LLM-generated Manim code, partially executes it to build a symbolic scene graph capturing objects and their spatial relations, and analyzes this graph for geometric conflicts such as occlusions or overlaps. When conflicts are found, SGA applies targeted refinements to the code to fix layout issues. In addition, they define the Manim Visual Quality Score (MVQS), a deterministic, rendering-free metric that uses symbolic scene information to quantify spatial integrity and visual legibility. They evaluate SGA on the MMMC-Code benchmark with four different LLM backbones and two agentic pipelines.

Result: Across combinations of four LLM backbones and two animation pipelines, SGA improves spatial quality in most settings, raising MVQS in 7 of 8 configurations. The best performance is an MVQS of 73.11 when using the Code2Video pipeline with GPT-5.1, which corresponds to a 16.1% relative gain over the unrefined baseline code. This shows that the agent can substantially enhance layout quality without modifying the underlying LLMs.

Conclusion: A symbolic, geometry-aware agent can substantially enhance the spatial correctness and visual clarity of LLM-generated animation code, while remaining modular and plug-and-play with existing pipelines. The proposed MVQS metric enables fast, deterministic assessment of visual quality without full rendering. Together, these contributions demonstrate a practical path toward more reliable, visually legible pedagogical animations generated by LLMs.

Abstract: Recent work leverages Large Language Models (LLMs) to generate executable code for pedagogical animations using libraries such as Manim. However, ensuring spatial correctness and visual legibility remains challenging, as existing frameworks emphasize pedagogical content while overlooking geometric occlusions. We propose the Symbolic Geometric Agent (SGA), a plug-and-play module for code-centric animation pipelines that intercepts LLM-generated code, performs partial execution to extract symbolic scene graphs, and applies targeted refinement when spatial conflicts are detected. We further introduce the Manim Visual Quality Score (MVQS), a deterministic rendering-free proxy for spatial integrity. Experiments on the MMMC-Code benchmark across four LLM backbones and two agentic pipelines show that SGA achieves a peak MVQS of 73.11 (Code2Video + GPT-5.1), corresponding to a 16.1% relative improvement over the raw baseline, and improves MVQS in 7 of 8 backbone x pipeline configurations.

</details>
